Africa is emerging as a rapidly growing region, still facing major challenges, but with a potential for significant progressùa transformation that necessitates vigorous efforts in research and policy thinking. This book series focuses on three intricately related key aspects of modern-day Africa: economic, social and political development. Making use of recent theoretical and empirical advances, the series aims to provide fresh answers to AfricaÆs development challenges. All the socio-political dimensions of todayÆs Africa are incorporated as they unfold and new policy options are presented. The series aims to provide a broad and interactive forum of science at work for policymaking and to bring together African and international researchers and experts. The series welcomes monographs and contributed volumes for an academic and professional audience, as well as tightly edited conference proceedings. Relevant topics include, but are not limited to, economic policy and trade, regional integration, labor market policies, demographic development, social issues, political economy and political systems, and environ- mental and energy issues.

Preface







It is not by augmenting the capital of the country, but by rendering a greater part of that capital active and more productive than would otherwise be so, that the most judicious operations of banking can increase the industry of the country.ùAdam Smith
  Banking is an important area as far as financial market development is concerned, especially in developing countries, which tend to have bank-based financial systems. The trends and challenges confronting banks in developing countries are completely different from what affect banks in developed markets. For this reason, it is neces- sary for finance students, economics students as well as accounting students in Africa to have a good appreciation of these unique issues. Considering the critical role banks play in most developing countries, it is particularly useful to understand the peculiar environments within which they operate. At the core of the financial system to drive growth in Africa is the banking system. A holistic exploration and appreciation of the banking systems as operated in Africa is key to designing relevant policies tailored to Africa and the developing world; this is what this textbook seeks to provide.
  This book approaches money and banking from an African perspective, with a view to provide a unified and analytic framework that explains the core principles and issues of money, banking and bank management in a manner that is simple for students to understand while retaining practicality and relevance to the times. One of the core strengths of this book is the specificity with which it addresses banking issues that are relevant to developing countries and African banking systems in particular. Built on significant Africa-based research and years of teaching experi- ence, this book sets out the appropriate background and then presents a functionalist approach that also explores the various dynamics of money and banking that separate Africa from the rest of the world. The textbook also makes a contribution to the literature by providing relevant scenarios that are set in the African and developing world context but are nonetheless relevant for the international commu- nity. Furthermore, the book approaches the issues from a developmental perspective as well as drawing out the issues in a way that stimulates discussions relevant to the quest for growth in Africa and the developing world. While the book is not

vii

viii	Preface

completely exhaustive of money and banking issues in Africa, it seeks to provide a relatively more nuanced view of the core issues while dealing with the importance, opportunities and challenges of the monetary and banking systems in Africa.
  The book is uniquely organised into five parts with 17 chapters. Part I focuses on monetary systems and the business of banking, and it is made up of three chapters. In Chap. 1, æOverview of the Monetary SystemÆ, we provide an overview of the financial system and then explain the barter system of trade and the nature and evolution of money. We examine the functions and characteristics of money. We also look at modern money and monetary aggregates, the demand for money and supply of money. Chapter 2 focuses on æIntroduction to the Business of BankingÆ. We discuss the products and services of banks and explain banking operations and the risks in banking. We also examine recent developments and trends in banking, globalisation and the impact of the global financial crisis as well as non-classical banking techniques. In Chap. 3, æOrganisation and Structure of the Banking Indus- tryÆ, we explain the internal organisation of a banking firm, the trends in organisation and organisational structures in banking. We also examine different types of banks operating in developing countries and the functions and behaviour of cross-border banks. Chapter 4 looks at æDetermination of Interest RatesÆ. We discuss theories of interest rates and the economic factors that affect interest rates. We also examine the risk and term structure of interest rates.
  Part II discusses asset-liability management and it covers three chapters: Chap. 5 focuses on æAsset-Liability Management Strategies: Interest Rate Risks, Maturity and Duration GapsÆ. We explain the asset-liability management strategies of banks, interest rate risk and the gap analysis. We also look at market value-based models and explain the difference between maturity and duration analysis. Chapter 6 also discusses æAsset-Liability Management: Using Hedging TechniquesÆ. In Chap. 7: æOff-Balance Sheet ActivitiesÆ, we explain the importance of off-balance sheet activities of banks operating in Africa. We also discuss management and regulation of off-balance sheet activities.
  Part III centres on managing banksÆ loan portfolio and capital. This part of the book has five chapters. In Chap. 8, we examine æLoan Portfolio and Credit Man- agementÆ. We accordingly discuss how banks in Africa provide loans. We further explain the regulation of bank lending, the lending process and the credit analysis processes of banks. We also explain loan agreements, loan reviews and the resolving of problem loans. æCorporate LendingÆ is discussed in Chap. 9. Here we explain the types of corporate loans banks provide to business firms. We also discuss the evaluation of corporate loan applications and the pricing of corporate loans. Chapter 10, æConsumer and Retail LendingÆ, discusses types of consumer loans, growth of consumer loans, the consumer loan application process, consumer loans and economic development, default risk and default risk management and current trends in the consumers loans market. In Chap. 11, æMortgage BankingÆ, we discuss the primary market for mortgages, the different types of mortgages and the factors affecting the value of mortgages. We also look at the secondary market for mort- gages, the risks associated with mortgage origination, lending and investing, chal- lenges confronting the mortgage/housing markets in Africa and how to improve the

Preface	ix

mortgage and housing markets. In Chap. 12, æManagement of Bank CapitalÆ, we examine bank capital and risk, regulation and bank capital and the size of bank capital. We also explain risk-based capital ratios and the Basel agreements and how banks strategise to meet their capital needs.
  Part IV focuses on bank performance, strategies and sustainable banking. Chapter 13 looks at æMeasuring and Evaluating the Performance of BanksÆ. We explain financial statement of banks, focusing on statement of financial position and statement of comprehensive income. We end the chapter by explaining the mea- surement and evaluation of bank performance. Chapter 14 focuses on æStrategy in the Banking IndustryÆ. We explain bank corporate strategy and international strategy in banking. We also deliberate on innovations in banking as well as on mergers and acquisitions in banking. We examine æSustainable BankingÆ in Chap. 15 by looking at ethics and the need for ethics in banking, sustainable banking and social respon- sibility in banking. We also discuss corporate governance of banks.
  The last part (Part V) of the book focuses on æCentral Banking, Monetary Policy and RegulationÆ. Chapter 16 looks at æCentral Banking and Monetary PolicyÆ by examining the concept of central banking, the role of the central bank in developing countries and monetary policy. It also discusses monetary policy transmission mechanisms and the concept of inflation targeting. In Chap. 17, æBank RegulationÆ, we explain the importance of bank regulation in developing countries, the rationale for bank regulation and the forms of bank regulation. We also discuss the challenges of bank regulation.

Part I
The Monetary System and the Business
of Banking

Chapter 1
Overview of the Monetary System







Learning Objectives
By the end of this chapter, you should be able to:
ò Discuss the financial system and its components
ò Explain what money is
ò Discuss the barter system and the evolution of money
ò Discuss the functions of money
ò Identify the characteristics of money
ò Explain money supply and demand for money
ò Explain the theories of money demand


1.1 Introduction

In a typical economy, some people have more money than they can use (surplus economic units), while others need more money than they have (deficit economic units). If these two groups can get together so that the idle funds can be put to use, we can have a better economy. Financial markets and financial institutions are able to bring these two economic units together. Of course, financial markets and institu- tions cannot operate smoothly without money, financial instruments and a regulator. These five (i.e. money, financial instruments, financial markets, financial institutions and financial regular) are the main components of a financial system.
  In this chapter, we examine the five components of the financial system. We also discuss issues related to introduction of money and the barter system, evolution of money, functions of money, characteristics of money, money supply and demand of money.





® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019	3
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_1

4	1  Overview of the Monetary System

1.2 Overview of the Financial System

A financial system is a system that allows for financial transactions and exchange of money between surplus economic units and deficit economic units. It involves the complex, related financial services, markets and institutions aimed at linking surplus units with deficit units. Financial systems operate at the global, regional, national and firm levels. A typical financial system is made up of money, financial instruments, financial markets, financial institutions and financial regulators that facilitate the interactions between the surplus units and the deficit units (see Mishkin 2013; Madura 2014). We now discuss the five main components of the financial system.



1.2.1 Money

In this section, we try to clear the confusion that exists in providing an appropriate definition of money. Everyone may have a definition for money as it is used in everyday life. However, it is important to distinguish between how economists define money and how money has been defined conventionally. To the economist, anything that is generally accepted as a medium of exchange for goods and services or used to repay debt is considered money. Currency (which is made up of notes and coins) is a typical example. Yet, restricting the definition of money to notes and coins makes the definition very narrow. This is because cheques, credit/debt cards and electronic transfers are also forms of money and are therefore considered as mediums of payment as well. We can broaden the definition of money by including savings account deposits so long as they can easily be converted into currency or current account deposits. This means that even for economists, money does not have a precise definition. A product forms part of the definition of money, if in a particular context it can easily be used as a substitute for the notes and coins.
  The confusion on the definition of money becomes even more complicated when people cannot clearly distinguish between money and wealth. Every now and then, we find people using wealth and money interchangeably. When we say someone is rich, we often do not just mean he or she has a lot of currency sitting somewhere. We mean that, in addition to the currency, that person also has assets such as cars, buildings, land, cattle and investment in financial assets. Therefore, although we recognise that defining money just in terms of currency is too narrow, defining money to also include these other items becomes too broad. Economists therefore try to distinguish between money and wealth. To them, wealth goes beyond money to include other assets such as buildings and land.
  People also use money and income interchangeably. For instance, we often hear people say æ.. .he earns a lot of moneyÆ. In actual fact, this statement refers to the income people earn. These two are certainly not the same. Money is a stock concept in the sense that it refers to the amount a person has at a given point in time. Income is, however, a flow concept, which refers to the total amount of money an individual

1.2 Overview of the Financial System	5

or household earns during a specific period of time. It is what money is worth in a particular time frame (a month, a quarter, a year). It does not make sense to say æI earn $500Æ. What will come into someoneÆs mind immediately is whether the $500 is for a month or a year. Certainly, there is a great difference between earning $500 in a year and in a month. This means we always should have a time frame attached to income.


1.2.2 Financial Instruments

Financial instruments are usually legal agreements that give rise to a financial asset of one entity and a financial liability or equity of another entity. They are legal obligations of one party to transfer something of value, usually money, to another party at some future date, under certain conditions. Financial instruments can be hard copy documents or virtual documents. Examples of hard copy financial instruments are currency and cheques, and examples of virtual financial instruments are fund transfers between bank accounts. Since financial instruments involve contracts, they are also pieces of paper issued by deficit economic units to surplus economic units. Financial instruments may be standardised or customised. Financial instruments that are standardised are known as financial securities. Although customised financial instruments have the advantage of meeting the needs of the two parties, standardised financial instruments (securities) are those that can be traded and are easily transfer- able. Examples of standardised financial instruments are cash instruments.
  Financial instruments are useful as a means of payment for goods and services and repayment of debt. They are also used to store value and used for trading risk. Examples of financial instruments used to store value include bank loan, mortgages, bonds and shares. Insurance contracts and derivative instruments such as futures, forwards, options and swaps are examples of financial instruments used to transfer risk. Derivative instruments derive their value from an underlying asset such as financial assets, interest rates, indices and commodities.


1.2.3 Financial Markets

Financial markets are platforms where securities are traded. Financial markets play a major role in channelling funds from surplus units (savers) to deficit units (bor- rowers). This is known as direct financing because surplus units provide funds directly to deficit units. Why is the channelling of funds from savers to borrowers important for an economy? It is important for every economy because it promotes efficiency. Usually, those who have surplus funds are not the same people who want more funds. Therefore, on a personal level, even if you had $500 extra and financial markets were not present, you would not be able to put this extra money to use if you did not have any investment ideas. This means that your $500 will not be able to

6	1  Overview of the Monetary System

yield you any interest. However, if you or a borrower could take this $500, you will be able to undertake a productive activity with it so you can earn some interest. When financial markets are absent, both you and the borrower will not get together and both of you will be worse off. More often than not, households are the main savers. Sometimes, however, firms, governments and foreigners become surplus units as well. The primary deficit units are firms and government, but can include households and non-residents. It is clear that unlike financial institutions that channel funds indirectly from surplus units to deficit units, the main role financial markets play is to channel funds from surplus units to deficit units directly.


1.2.3.1 Structure of Financial Markets

Financial markets are broadly classified as money markets and capital markets. Money markets are markets in which financial instruments that have maturity of less than 1 year are traded. Examples of such instruments are negotiable certificates of deposits (NCDs), treasury bills, commercial papers, bankerÆs acceptance, etc. Capital markets, on the other hand, are markets in which financial instruments that have maturity of more than 1 year are traded. Typical examples of such instruments are bonds and shares.
  In financial markets, financial instruments are initially issued in the primary market and they are subsequently traded on secondary markets. In other words, primary markets allow for newly issued financial instruments to be traded, whereas secondary markets allow for already issued financial instruments to be traded. Financial markets are also classified as organised markets and over-the-counter (OTC) markets. Organised markets are those with a comprehensive regulatory framework of traders linked to one platform. They can easily be identified because they have trading floors or electronic platforms, where only authorised members can trade in the securities listed. An over-the-counter market is a largely unregulated market, whereby geographically dispersed traders, who are linked to one another via telecommunication systems and computers, trade in securities.


1.2.4 Financial Institutions

Financial institutions are firms that serve as intermediaries by facilitating the transfer of funds from surplus economic units and deficit economic units. Thus, we can analyse their behaviour just like the way we do for any other firm. As firms, financial institutions such as banks produce goods but the goods they produce are in the form of loans with inputs coming from deposits. Just like any other firm, we can also assume that their ultimate goal is profit maximisation, and so financial institutions exist to maximise profit. Thus, the condition for profit maximisation for firms which is that marginal revenue (MR) equals marginal cost (MC) pertains here too. There are major differences among the products offered by financial institutions. These

1.2 Overview of the Financial System	7

differences become very clear when we group financial institutions under their major constituents: deposit-taking institutions (DTIs) and non-deposit-taking institutions (NDTIs).


1.2.4.1 Deposit-Taking Institutions

Like their names suggest, these financial institutions have the core mandate of accepting deposits and making loans. Examples are banks, savings and loans institutions, building societies and credit unions. Although deposit-taking institu- tions include savings and loans institutions, building societies and credit unions, banks tend to dominate these institutions. Therefore, the term DTIs is almost synonymous to banks. We discuss briefly the types of banks.
  Previously, it was easy to classify banks according to the activities they are involved in. We could therefore classify them into retail banks and wholesale banks. While retail banks provide loan and deposit facilities for individuals and small and medium-sized firms, wholesale banks provide loan and deposit facilities and financial advice to large corporate bodies. Wholesale banking activities also include the services provided by investment banks whose activities do not involve core banking activities, but operate only on the securities market.
  Financial liberalisation in many developing countries has resulted in intense competition amongst banks, and this has in turn influenced the nature and activities of banks. Most banks are currently involved in universal banking activities. They combine both retail and wholesale activities by performing core banking activities for individuals, small firms and large firms, as well as performing investment- banking activities.


1.2.4.2 Non-deposit-Taking Institutions

Non-deposit-taking institutions are broadly classified into contractual savings insti- tutions and investment intermediaries, which include investment companies and finance companies.


Contractual Savings Institutions

These are non-deposit-taking financial institutions that obtain funds at periodic intervals on a contractual basis and promise their clients future compensation. Examples are insurance companies and pension funds. Since these institutions, especially life insurance companies and pension funds, to some extent can predict how much they would have to pay out as benefits, they are more able to plan their activities without thinking so much about losing their funds. Thus, these institutions are able to invest in illiquid assets and still meet their obligations whenever they have

8	1  Overview of the Monetary System

to. We discuss below briefly the activities of insurance companies and pension funds.
Insurance Companies are financial institutions that provide cover to entities for future contingencies and in exchange take premiums from these entities. Their cover can be in the form of life insurance or general insurance. While life insurance provides financial protection against death, disability and medical bills, general insurance provides cover for theft, accidents, fire, fraud and natural disasters. Insurance companies, whether life or general, are able to perform their functions by collecting premiums from their policyholders in exchange for an agreement to cover them in the event that any mishap should occur. So long as enough funds are gathered, they can invest them and meet unexpected claims easily.
  The level of premium paid varies from one individual to another, as it depends on the likelihood of the event occurring. Yet, identifying the risk level of each individ- ual is often difficult because of asymmetric information. Asymmetric information refers to a situation where there is inequality in the information between parties involved in a financial transaction. This gives rise to two problems: adverse selection and moral hazard. Adverse selection occurs when insurance companies are unable to distinguish between more risky and less risky policyholders and end up charging a premium that will drive out the less risky clients from the market. Moral hazard, on the other hand, occurs when policyholders take the policy and become reckless because they know they are covered. While the former can be resolved by screening, the latter can be resolved by restrictive covenants and deductibles. Although insur- ance companies perform an important role of protecting entities against unforeseen circumstances, the insurance penetration rate in most developing countries is very low.
Pension Funds are financial institutions that receive pension contributions and invest these funds in order to provide pension benefits to pensioners in the future. Pensions in emerging and developing countries are more needed than in developed countries, as they are a good way to reduce the dependency ratio and alleviate poverty. When people retire, they can earn some form of income, which comes from contributions they would have made earlier on in life. Pension funds generally come in the form of state pensions, occupational pensions and private pensions.
  With the state pensions, each generation of workers pays for their predecessorsÆ pensions. As they do this, they hope that the next generation will pay for theirs. This scheme may not be suitable for private firms as private firms cannot be relied upon to raise funds to take care of employeesÆ pensions. As a result, the occupational pensions are able to better support the private sector. With this, both employers and employees contribute into a fund. The funds are then invested, out of which pensions are paid. There are two main arrangements under this scheme: defined benefits and defined contribution. The defined benefit promises to give a definite amount to the pensioner, whereas the defined contribution determines the regular contributions of workers, but how much they get in pensions depends on the value of the invested funds. The private pensions are meant to supplement the other forms of pensions.

1.2 Overview of the Financial System	9

Investment Intermediaries

Investment intermediaries are also non-deposit-taking institutions, and they include investment companies and finance companies.
Investment Companies are financial institutions that issue their own shares and they use the funds generated to invest in diversified portfolios of financial assets. They typically take small contributions from investors, create a fund out of these and invest them in institutions that need the funds. The fund that is created may be open ended or close ended. Open-ended funds are funds that allow investors to join or leave at any time. They stand ready to issue fresh shares and buy back their shares at any time. Close-ended funds are those that once issued, additional trading can only be done on the secondary market. Investment companies include unit trusts, invest- ment trusts and mutual funds. Unit trusts are open-ended funds and as such encour- age regular investment by the public. At any point in time, if the inflow of funds exceeds the rate at which people want to surrender their units, new units are created by the trust. Under unit trusts, the value of a unit is determined by demand and supply and the value of the underlying asset. Investment trusts, on the other hand, are close-ended funds. At any point in time, the total number of shares is fixed, which means that new investors can buy shares only from existing shareholders. With investment trusts, the value of the shares is determined by the value of the underlying assets. Mutual funds are open-ended investment companies that issue their own shares and invest the funds in diversified portfolios of shares, bonds and money market instruments.
  Investment companies are important for a number of reasons. First, they pool funds from surplus units and give these funds to deficit units. Second, if an investor holds units or shares, they can be liquidated easily. Also, risk is also minimised through pooling of funds and investment in diversified portfolios of financial assets and also through professional management.
Finance Companies are non-deposit-taking institutions that acquire funds from banks and other financial institutions. They also raise funds by issuing securities to the public and use the funds acquired to provide loans to firms and individuals. Finance companies include consumer finance companies, commercial finance com- panies and sales finance companies. Consumer finance companies provide loans to individuals for acquiring home durable items. Commercial finance companies lend to small businesses and are also involved in accounts receivable financing, factoring and leasing equipment. Sales finance or captive companies also provide loans for the purchase of items from a particular supplier or manufacturer.
  Apart from distinguishing between DTIs and NDTIs on the basis of the products they offer, there are some other major differences that should be emphasised. First, compared to NDTIs, the liabilities of DTIs form the bulk of a countryÆs money supply and is therefore of great interest to policymakers. Thus, the activities of DTIs are subject to more scrutiny than that of NDTIs. Second, unlike NDTIs, the failure of DTIs affects the economy enormously. This is because almost all entities including the NDTIs themselves make use of DTIs. Finally, the motive for using DTIs differs

10	1  Overview of the Monetary System

from the motive for using NDTIs. EntitiesÆ decision to use DTIs depends on changes in their income and the fact that they may want to buy other assets in future. As a result, people hold more deposits when they have spare income and less when their income level declines. Therefore, the amount entities would want to hold as deposits is optional. However, in the case of NDTIs, especially contractual saving institu- tions, the contracts indicate how much entities should contribute and what future compensation they are entitled to.


1.2.5 Financial Regulators

In the wake of the global financial crisis, many countries are beginning to realise that stability of the financial system is important. As a result, these countries have started tightening the regulations of their financial systems. Financial regulation is made up of two main categories: safety and soundness regulation and compliance. The main goal of safety and soundness regulation is to protect creditors, who offer fixed amounts, from becoming insolvent as a result of losses coming from banks. Finan- cial institutions with fixed amount creditors include banks, insurance companies, credit unions and so on. There are also other investors who are not fixed amount creditors. These include investors in the bond and stock markets. Compliance, on the other hand, offers individuals protection from unfair practices of the different financial institutions and markets and also prevents crimes such as money laundering.
  Since there are several activities performed in the financial system, there are several regulatory bodies that exist to oversee the activities of the different aspects of the financial system. The different activities including banking, financial markets, insurance and pension funds thus require different regulators. In Ghana, the Bank of Ghana oversees the activities of the banks, the National Insurance Commission oversees insurance companies, the National Pensions Regulatory Authority oversees pension funds and the Securities and Exchange Commission regulates the capital markets. In India for instance, the Reserve Bank of India (RBI) is responsible for regulating the banks, the Securities and Exchange Board of India (SEBI) is respon- sible for the securities market, the Insurance Regulatory and Development Authority of India (IRDAI) is responsible for the insurance industry and the Pension Fund Regulatory and Development Authority (PFRDA) is responsible for pensions.
  However, in other jurisdictions, the regulatory regime is such that, besides the central bank regulating the banking system, there is one financial regulator oversee- ing the non-banking financial services industry. An example is South Africa which until 2017 had two sector-specific regulators: the South African Reserve Bank (SARB) for regulating the banking sector and the Financial Services Board (FSB) which oversaw the non-banking sector, including short-term and long-term insur- ance companies, funeral insurance schemes, retirement funds, collective investment schemes (unit trusts and stock market) and financial advisors and brokers. South AfricaÆs regulatory framework was changed in 2017 with the implementation

1.3 Introduction of Money and the Barter System	11

of a new financial sector regulatory framework: the Twin Peaks model. The new regulatory model will lead to the establishment of two regulators: a prudential regulator (Prudential Authority) and a dedicated market conduct regulator (Financial Sector Conduct Authority). The Prudential Authority will operate within the SARB and will be responsible for promoting and enhancing safety and soundness of financial institutions, while the Financial Sector Conduct Authority will be in charge of supervising market conduct with the aim of protecting financial consumers. The South African model prior to 2017 was similar to what existed in England before 2013, while the current regulatory model in South Africa follows that of post-2013 England. Prior to 2013, England had the Financial Services Authority (initially known as Securities and Investments Board) in charge of regulation of the financial services industry in the UK. Following the global financial crisis, the responsibilities of the Financial Services Authority were split in 2013 and assigned to two new entities: the Financial Conduct Authority and the Prudential Regulation Authority of the Bank of England.
  Now that we have discussed briefly the different components of the financial system, let us try and understand the main effects money has on the economy. Before that, we discuss the evolution of money in the next section.
Quick Quiz
1. Identify the components of the financial system.
2. Distinguish	between	deposit-taking	institutions	and	non-deposit-taking institutions.



1.3 Introduction of Money and the Barter System

Before money evolved, exchange was done through barter, which involved the direct exchange of one good/commodity/thing for another good/commodity/thing or one good/thing for a service. For instance, in a barter system, a cow may be exchanged for 20 chickens or 5 goats, or a lecturerÆs two-hour service may be exchanged for 1 goat. Hence, a barter economy is an economy where money is not used. It can be successful mainly in areas where people produce goods for subsistence, which means that the barter system is for very simple and primitive societies. Although barter has been phased out as many societies have become more complex, it can still be found in many rural areas.


1.3.1 Problems of the Barter System

The barter system could not survive the complexities of modern societies because it had the following problems.

12	1  Overview of the Monetary System

1.3.1.1 Lack of Double Coincidence of Wants

For a barter system to be successful, it requires that individuals who want to engage in an exchange have a double coincidence of wants. This means that any individual who wants to trade his or her goods does not only have to find someone who wants his or her goods or service but also someone who has the good or service, which the former has. For example, an individual who wants some lectures in economics but had goats does not only need to find someone who wants his goat, but someone who wants his goat and can deliver the lectures in economics at the same time. The probability of finding such a person to trade with is almost impossible as this process is often time-consuming and arduous. At best, the individual will have to go through a series of intermediary transactions before getting what he or she wants, but this is even practically impossible.


1.3.1.2 Lack of a Common Measure of Value

A second problem associated with the barter system is the lack of a common unit in which the value of goods and services can be measured. The two individuals who want to trade may have a double coincidence of wants but there could still be a problem of what proportion of one good is equivalent to another. For instance, if two individuals, one with lecture hours and the other with goats, agree to trade because they both have the goods each the other wants, without a common unit of measure, it will be difficult to determine how much of the lecture hours will be equivalent to one goat. With this, for an exchange to take place, it will depend greatly on the intensity of demand by the two parties. Whoever needs the good most would have to sacrifice more in the course of the transaction. Accordingly, one party will lose out in the transaction. Also, barter will require that the value of each good be expressed in as many quantities as there are types and qualities of goods. Even if we assume there are only two goods in the world, those goods may have different qualities. Under the barter system, an exchange rate will have to be expressed for all these qualities of goods. This is why a barter system can only be useful for simple societies.


1.3.1.3 Indivisibility of Some Goods

Remember that the barter system is based on the exchange of goods with other goods. Some goods are just indivisible and thus using these goods for trade makes operation under barter very difficult. Fixing exchange rates for goods that are indivisible is often difficult. Consider two individuals, one with sheep and the other with tables. Even if these two agree to exchange, one table may be equivalent to half a sheep. The owner of the sheep may be unwilling to give out a whole sheep in exchange for one table. Similarly, if one and a half tables is equivalent to one

1.4 Evolution of Money	13

sheep, the owner of the table will also be reluctant to give out two tables for one sheep. Accordingly, trade cannot be complete without a loss in value.


1.3.1.4 Difficulty in Storing Value

Under the barter system, it is difficult to store the value of goods and services, making savings a difficult task. Some goods may be perishable, and others may become obsolete when stored over a long period of time. Since barter involves the exchange of goods for goods, anyone wanting to store goods over a long period will either experience deterioration or loss of value.


1.3.1.5 Difficulty in Making Deferred Payment

It is difficult to honour debt contracts under the barter system. The question will always arise as to whether the payment being made exactly matches what was borrowed. When payment is due, disagreement arises on whether the same quality of the good being used to make the settlement with is the same as what was borrowed. Another thing that may bring disagreement may be the increase or decrease in the value of the good being used in making the payment. For instance, maize may lose value when payment is due to the advantage of the borrower but may also increase in value when payment is due to the advantage of the creditor. Whichever way, one party may feel cheated when payment is due.
These problems of the barter system led to the introduction of money.



1.4 Evolution of Money

The evolution of money has gone through some stages.



1.4.1 Commodity Money

In prehistoric times, early man used some sort of money. The type of money used in these societies depended on what type of commodity was available in that society. Thus, the pastoral society used sheep, cattle, etc., as money; the hunting society used bows and arrows, spears, etc., as money; and the agricultural society used grains as money. The use of commodities as money obviously had similar disadvantages as those that persisted under the barter system. For instance, storage was a problem, commodities were not portable and commodities were not uniform in quality.

14	1  Overview of the Monetary System

1.4.2 Metallic Money

As societies became more advanced, stones or shells or metallic money came to replace commodity money. Several African societies used the cowrie as money. Many societies started using silver, gold, copper, tin, etc., as money, either to be weighed in small units (as gold dust in several parts of Ghana) or later as coins. But again, metal was difficult to use as money. It was heavy, it was difficult to ascertain its quality and it was not easily divisible. This led to the introduction of coins with predetermined weights and values. Then some criminals started reducing the quality of the coins by clipping the edges of these coins if they were made of real and precious metals. People who had full-bodied coins started hoarding their coins, leaving only the debased coins in the system. Soon, coins with rough edges became accepted in the system. As gold started rising in value, people preferred melting the gold coins to earn more. Government had no option but to mix copper and silver into the gold coins. Eventually, gold became more scarce and silver coins replaced gold coins, first in the pure state and later the silver was mixed with other metals. However, metallic money also had some defects. They were too heavy; it was difficult to change or replace its supply and not safe and convenient to carry around.


1.4.3 Paper Money

Because of all these developments, goldsmiths, who were thought to be trustworthy, were given the responsibility by many individuals to keep their gold for them. Once people gave their gold to them, the goldsmiths in turn gave them receipts to prove they had their gold with them. These individuals keeping their gold with the goldsmith could give the receipts to people who sold things to them and these sellers could in turn take these receipts to the goldsmiths for their payments. Consequently, these receipts became substitutes for money. This paper money was backed by gold and could be converted to gold upon the demand of the seller. This was how bank notes were developed. The central bank of countries now acts as the former goldsmiths. Sellers upon receiving payments from buyers can present the bank notes to the central bank, which will be obliged to give something back. However, as gold and silver rose in value, governments could no longer sustain the conversion of bank notes into gold and silver, as the demand for money greatly increased.


1.5 Functions of Money

Though many people on a daily basis use money, just a few of them may be able to define it and explain its functions. Theoretically, money is defined by the functions it performs, and for that reason, it is often defined as a legal tender used as a medium of

1.5 Functions of Money	15

exchange, a store of value, a unit of account and a standard of deferred payment. Unlike gold or silver money, modern money as a physical entity has no real value (intrinsic value).
  The problems associated with barter led to the introduction of money. Hence, the functions of money are derived from the weaknesses of the barter system. The functions of money include medium of exchange, store of value, unit of account and standard of deferred payment. Of all these functions, money as a medium of exchange represents the core function of money, and it is really what separates money from other assets.



1.5.1 Medium of Exchange

Money as a medium of exchange implies that money is generally accepted as a means of payment. One is not afraid to pay for something in the grocery shop with money because one is confident the cashier will accept it. In fact, most paper money (e.g. the Ghanaian Cedi, the Nigerian Naira, the Botswana Pula, the South African Rand, the Kenyan Shilling and the Mauritius Rupee) has the inscription æThis note is legal tender.. .Æ. This means governments back the use of money in the exchange of other goods. Using money as a medium of exchange is beneficial as it reduces the time needed for an exchange to take place. The function of money as medium of exchange helps to resolve the problem of the double coincidence of wants. The time spent searching for someone who wants your product and also has what you want is costly. This type of cost is transaction cost. Under barter, transaction costs are high. If money is introduced, anyone with it can have whatever he or she wants without having to find someone who wants what he or she has. Money as a medium of exchange promotes efficiency by minimising time spent in exchange and allows people to specialise in the things they do best. For money to perform its medium of exchange function, it has to be easy to determine its value and it should be divisible, portable and durable.



1.5.2 Unit of Account

Another function that money performs is that it serves as a unit by which the value of goods and services is measured, the records of debt are kept and calculations are made. A unit of account can be divided in such a way that when its different components are brought together, they will equal the original value. For instance, if $1 is split into 5, each representing 20 cents, when brought together, no value is lost. You still get $1. Money serving as a unit of account also means that one unit has the same value as any other one unit.

16	1  Overview of the Monetary System

1.5.3 Store of Value

Money also serves as a store of value. It maintains the purchasing power over time. Consequently, money as a store of value saves the purchasing power from the time of receipt of income to the time it is spent. This is a very useful function of money because most of us do not spend all the money we have all at once. It is, however, important to recognise that money is not the only asset that can perform the function of store of value. Other assets like bonds, stocks, land, houses, etc., also perform the store of value function. Interestingly, these assets can even fetch a higher interest rate or have their value appreciate more over time than money. Why then would people want to hold money? The answer rests on the advantage that money possesses, which is liquidity, which measures how easy it is for an asset to be readily converted into a medium of exchange. Money represents the most liquid asset because you do not have to convert it before you can use it to make any purchases. For you to be able to convert the other assets into cash, you would have to incur a cost. For instance, if you need cash immediately and you sell your bonds, you will incur a brokerage fee. The store of value function that money performs depends greatly on prices. When prices increase, it reduces the value of money. This is the reason why we are so concerned when inflation is high.



1.6 Characteristics of Money

The characteristics of money include scarcity, durability, divisibility, recognisability, acceptability, portability and liquidity, and it has no intrinsic value.
Scarcity Money is supplied in limited quantities such that it is difficult to come by. The limited supply of money ensures the preservation of its value. If the scarcity characteristic of money is lost, it leads to hyperinflation and consequently a drastic loss in value. The difficult-to-find nature of money serves an incentive for people to work hard before they can get it. The hard work leads to productivity, which further enhances the value of money. The monetary authority (central bank or reserve bank) of any economy is responsible for regulating the money supply in an economy and for designing money in such a way that it is difficult to counterfeit.
Durability Unlike most of the commodities used as money under the barter system (e.g. perishable commodities and livestock), modern money has the capacity to last for a fairly long period of time. It is easy to store modern money for a long time without æspoilageÆ. The emergence of electronic cash has further enhanced the durability of money by emphasising cashless transactions.
Divisibility Money is printed and distributed in several denominations to facilitate different volumes of transactions. One problem of the barter system, for instance, was the difficulty in dividing a live cow without killing it. Divisibility, as a

1.7 Money Supply	17

characteristic of money, ensures that the amount paid for a product or service is equal to its worth.
Recognisability Money is designed in such a way that it is easy to identify. School children, the young and elderly and literates and non-literates should all easily recognise money when they see one. Also, money comes with certain features to facilitate the identification of proper money from counterfeits.
Acceptability People must have confidence in money so as to agree to use it as a medium of exchange, a store of value, a unit of account or a standard for deferred payments. To provide this assurance, modern money is backed by law (fiat). Therefore, the refusal to accept money in any jurisdiction of its issuance constitutes a breach of the law.
Portability In the barter days, it was difficult, costly and inconvenient to carry, say, a bag of maize or a cow around for exchange. Modern money solves these problems associated with the barter system by being light and easy to carry around.
Liquidity Liquidity has to do with the ease with which an asset can be converted into cash without substantial loss in value. The modern money of various forms can easily be converted into cash to facilitate transactions. What this means is that assets such as stocks and bonds can be easily converted into cash, indicating the liquidity property of money. Thus, money itself is liquid and also facilitates the creation of liquidity.
No Intrinsic Value Modern money is designed in such a way that besides performing its functions, it has no further use (value). This contrasts with items used as money in the barter days. Barter items such as cowries, cattle, cola, gold, silver, among others, had value on their own (intrinsic value), and for that matter if they could not be used to serve the purpose of money they could be put to alternative uses.


1.7 Money Supply

Empirically, money supply is defined as the amount of money in circulation in an economy at any point in time. It includes the notes, coins and any form of deposit, which can easily be converted into money. Money supply is defined based on different measures, including monetary base, M1, M2, M3, etc. However, the specific definition or measurement of money supply depends on the country and what products can be easily converted into money. For instance, in Tanzania (measured in millions of Tanzanian Shillings), money supply is measured as follows:
ò M1 (narrow money) = C + Dd
ò M2 (broad money) = M1 + TD + SD
ò M3 (Extended Broad Money) = M2 + FCD
ò M0 (base money/HPM) = C + Cash reserves of commercial banks

18	1  Overview of the Monetary System

where
C = Currency in circulation outside banks Dd = Demand deposits
TD = Time deposits SD = Savings deposits
FCD = Foreign currency deposits
In Ghana, money supply is measured as follows:
ò M1 = CP + Dd
ò M2 = M1 + Quasi-money (SD + TD + CD)
ò M2+ = M2 + FCD
where
CP = Currency with non-bank public Dd = Demand deposits
SD = Savings deposits TD = Time deposits
CD = Certificates of deposits
FCD = Foreign currency deposits of deposit money banks
  Further, the definition of money supply in different countries has evolved over time. For instance, in Ghana, before 1997, M1 was the official definition of money, although M2 was recognised. Since 1997, M2+ has been used as the official definition of money.
  Measures of money supply usually show a close link to economic variables such as GDP and inflation. Consequently, money supply provides information about the course the economy is taking in the short term and determines the level of prices and inflation in the long run. Many central banks have used measures of money supply to guide them in the conduct of monetary policy. In recent times, however, the relationships between different measures of money supply and the economic vari- ables have not been very stable. Hence, the relevance of money supply in directing the course of monetary policy has been de-emphasised in many economies. Although most central banks still review money supply figures, they see it as part of the usual review done by policymakers.
  Money supply is determined not just by the central bank but by the behaviour of the non-bank public and the banking system as well (Mishkin 2013). Here, the central bankÆs role as an overseer of the banks is very critical. By the banking system, we are referring to commercial banks that are responsible for accepting deposits and making loans. The non-bank public refers to anyone in an economy who is outside the banking system and the central bank. We will return to this later in Chap. 16.

1.8 Demand for Money	19

1.8 Demand for Money

Demand for money refers to how much money people want to hold at any point in time. People demand money for two main reasons: first, because it is a medium of exchange and, second, because it acts as a store of value. Therefore, economic agents will hold money in both cash and assets. Why do individuals change their demand for money? They change their demand for money because of the scale view and the substitution view. The scale view relates to the view that changes in income can affect the demand for money, while the substitution view relates to the fact that there could be other assets apart from money which may give a higher rate of return than what money can give. The different theories of money demand illustrate the scale view and the substitution view.
  An important question we usually would ask is the extent to which money demand is influenced by interest rate changes. This is because whether money demand is influenced by interest rate or not, it determines the effectiveness of interest rate on monetary policy. In this discussion, we focus on the role interest rate plays in the demand for money by discussing some relevant theories. These include:
1. The classical theory (made up of the Fisherian theory and the Cambridge theory)
2. The Keynesian theory
3. BaumolÆs theory
4. TobinÆs theory
5. FriedmanÆs restatement theory


1.8.1 The Classical Theory of Money Demand

The classical theory constitutes the Fisherian theory and the Cambridge theory.


1.8.1.1 The Fisherian Theory of Money Demand

FisherÆs theory of money demand was developed by Irvin Fisher in 1911. It was originally not a theory of money demand, but it was developed to explain what determines prices in the economy. Thus, the Fisherian theory was based on the quantity theory of money. The quantity theory of money was also based on the equation of exchange, which explains that for every transaction, there is a buyer side and a seller side and the value of expenditure will always be equal to what the seller gets, which is the value of output. To illustrate this view, suppose M represents money stock, V the velocity of money (where velocity of money refers to how fast money changes hands), P prices and Y output, then, the value of expenditure = MV, and the value of output = PY. This gives an identity called the equation of exchange. According to this equation, MV = PY. Since this is an identity, it will always hold. Fisher was able to turn this into a theory by making certain assumptions. According

20	1  Overview of the Monetary System

to Fisher, the quantity of money is not determined by any of the variables in the model, so it is therefore constant. He also assumed that the velocity of money was also constant and was determined by external factors like communication and technology. So, for instance, if technology made debit cards readily available, then velocity of money will be low. Output, according to Fisher, was assumed to be constant too. From his argument, prices were the only ones determined within the model and this was determined by the quantity of money in the economy. This means that with Fisher, inflation is always a monetary phenomenon and can only be resolved by monetary policy and not fiscal policy.
  It is this quantity equation that was later converted into a theory of money demand (Laidler 1993). He assumed that because in the money market, equilibrium required money demand to be equal to money supply, we could replace money stock by money demand so that we end up with Md = 1/v Î PY. His conclusion was then based on the assumption that money demand is determined by the value of trans- actions in the economy, which then depends on income.


1.8.1.2 The Cambridge Approach

The Cambridge approach represents another popular approach to understanding demand for money. Some of the proponents of this theory who were at Cambridge University in England included Alfred Marshall in 1923 and Arthur Cecil Pigou in 1917. We concentrate on PigouÆs dimension of the theory. Pigou was generally concerned about what he called the titles to legal tender and defined these titles as currency and demand deposits in banks, which is currently what we call M1. In PigouÆs proposition, a person holds currency and demand deposits for two main reasons: first, to provide convenience and, second, to provide security. With regard to the former, people would hold currency and demand deposit for the medium of exchange function, and with the latter, people would hold currency and demand deposit because of the store of value function. Yet, money demanded for its store of value function was somewhat silent, leaving Pigou (1917) and Marshall (1923)æs conclusion to be similar to that of the Fisherian. That is, money is demanded for transactionary purposes.


1.8.2 KeynesÆ Theory of Money Demand

Essentially, KeynesÆ theory of money demand propounded in 1930 is an extension of the Cambridge cash balance approach that stresses the store of value function of money. Similar to the cash balance approach, KeynesÆ theory of money demand emphasises that the demand for money is determined by what individuals or firms wish to hold and not what they have to hold. In essence, money, being the most liquid asset, can serve not just as a medium of exchange but also as a store of value. Relating to this argument, we can say that people do not only wish to hold money for

1.8  Demand for Money	21

other reasons but that they demand money for moneyÆs own sake. KeynesÆ idea suggests reasons why people prefer to hold liquid cash or money instead of other assets as a store of value. This desire for holding money is what Keynes calls liquidity preference. Following this, we can say that the demand for money is liquidity preference. This view is perceived as the liquidity preference approach by the modern approach theorists. Looking at the modern approach of demand for money, we ask the question: why should individuals or firms hold money? A straightforward answer to this question is that individuals or firms will hold money to satisfy their desire to hold money. Keynes, however, gives three motives for this: transactionary motive, precautionary motive and the speculative motive. Keynes further suggested that the total demand for money implies total cash balances. He classified total cash balances into two main parts: active cash balances and idle cash balances.
  Keynes believed that active cash balances should comprise the demand for money for transactions and precautionary motives.


1.8.2.1 Transactionary Demand for Money

According to Keynes (1930), transactionary demand for money arises from the need of individuals or firms to hold money to take care of their daily activities. Since there is a gap between when money is received and when money has to be spent, or say payments and receipts are not properly synchronised, economic agents will have to hold money to bridge this gap. Hence when people have to receive income at fixed periods and yet payments would have to be made too often, it becomes obvious for people to hold some stock of money all the time to take care of their day-to-day activities. According to Keynes (1930), how much money people would want to hold for this purpose depends on how much income they have, on price levels, their habits and the time between when they receive income and when they make payments. The larger the incomes of people, the more money they would wish to hold for transactionary purposes and vice versa. That is, when income is received less frequently, people hold more money for transactions, but when incomes are received less frequently, people hold less money for transactions. Thus, a rich man would want to hold more money for transactionary purposes than a poor man. The transactionary demand for money is also influenced by the price level of goods and services. Higher prices require the use of more money to buy a given quantity of goods. Thus, individualsÆ or business transactionsÆ demand for money rises during inflation. Also, when the gap between receipts and expenditure is wide, individuals demand more money for their transactions and the opposite is true for when the gap is narrow. Spendthrifts also hold more money for transactions than people who are thrifty. If we hold the price level, habits, time interval and all other factors constant, we can say that the transactionary demand for money depends on the individualÆs income. Similar views hold for businesses.

22	1  Overview of the Monetary System

1.8.2.2 Precautionary Demand for Money

This view suggests that individuals hold money to take care of unforeseen circum- stances like accidents, illnesses, car breakdowns, etc. The need to hold money to take care of such circumstances is referred to as a precautionary demand for money. Like a transactionary demand for money, a precautionary demand for money largely depends on income, in that the higher an individualÆs income, the more money that individual holds for precautionary purposes.


1.8.2.3 Speculative Demand for Money

Speculative demand for money arises when individuals need to hold money for transactions other than for a living. Households and firms, after keeping enough for transactionary and precautionary purposes, would like to benefit by investing in bonds. According to Keynes, it is the expectations about changes in the bond prices or in the current market rate that determines the speculative demand for money. Keynes (1930) believed that a speculative demand for money is associated with individuals having what we call a critical rate of interest. This is to say that every individual has an idea of what the interest rate should be. Let us call this interest rate rc. When the market interest rate goes above rc, the individual will expect the interest rate to fall. Given that there is a negative relationship between the price of bonds and interest rates, if an individual or firm expects interest rates to fall, it will mean they expect the price of bonds to rise. Individuals expecting the price of bonds to rise will buy bonds and sell them in future when the price rises, leading to a fall in the speculative demand for money. Conversely, if interest rates go below the critical value, and they expect interest rates to rise, this will mean that they will expect bond prices to fall. They would sell bonds in the present and the speculative demand for
money will rise. Thus, when r > rc, an investor holds all his/her liquid assets in bonds, and when r < rc, his/her entire holdings will be in money. However, when r = rc, he/she will be indifferent to holding bonds or money. This means that the
speculative demand for money is a decreasing function of the interest rate.
  KeynesÆ demand for money idea combines all three motives for holding money and concludes that demand for money is a function of Y (output) and interest rate. Figure 1.1 shows that when incomes rise (fall), demand for money rises (falls), but when interest rates rise (fall), demand for money falls (rises).
  Thus, unlike the classicals who suggest that demand for money is a function of just income, Keynes in his liquidity preference theory suggests that demand for money is a function of both interest rate and income.

2. Explain precautionary demand for money
3. What is the difference between speculative and transactionary demand for money

1.8.3 The BaumolÆs Inventory Theoretic Approach

BaumolÆs (1952) theory made two important additions to KeynesÆ theory of money demand, particularly to the transactions demand for money. First, Keynes assumed that the transactions demand for money had a proportional and linear relationship with income. Contrary to this view, Baumol (1952) suggested that the relationship between income and money demand was neither linear nor proportional. Second, Keynes assumed that the transaction demand for money was also interest inelastic. However, Baumol proved that this is not so, as he suggested that the transactions demand for money depends not just on income but also on interest rate.
  According to Baumol (1952), every individual, after receiving an income Y, does not spend all at the same time. As there exists a gap between how much an individual receives and how much he/she spends, there should be an optimal amount of money needed to be held in a specific period of time. He explained that holding money allowed individuals to bridge the gap between the time incomes are received and the time expenditure had to be made. However, holding money is costly, as one would have to forfeit the interest, which could have been earned from investing that amount of money. Hence, there is an alternative to holding money, which is holding bonds. Yet, although holding bonds gives the individual some form of interest, when the time comes for expenditure to be made, the individual would have to convert the bonds to cash, which will also come at a cost (brokerage fee). With these complex- ities, an individual would always need to think carefully about how much money to hold to minimise the transaction cost.
  Baumol (1952) therefore assumes in his theory that if an individual receives Y income a year, and spends it at a constant rate, he/she will not spend all at the same time. It will therefore be prudent for that individual to spend the idle funds on buying bonds to earn some interest. For instance, if an individual receives an income of US
$1500 per year and spends this at a constant rate every four months, the individual can spend US$500 in each period. Out of the US$1500, the individual can keep US
$500 in cash for transactions for the first four months and buy bonds with the

24	1  Overview of the Monetary System

remaining US$1000. Half of the US$1000 invested in bonds will mature in the next period, which the individual can sell and use for transactions. The remaining half can also be liquidated in the next periods for use.
  In resolving this problem, the individual would have to minimise the cost of holding cash balances over a particular year. As already discussed, holding cash balances involves both interest costs and non-interest costs. The interest costs are in the form of opportunity costs because when an entity holds cash balances, it forgoes the income it would have earned from investing the cash. The non-interest income includes items such as brokerage fees, administrative expenses and so on. Let r be the interest rate and b be the brokerage fee. Assume that Y is also the income of the entity at the beginning of the year, which is equal to the value of the transactions performed by it, and K the size of the withdrawal. This means that  Y is the number of withdrawals made over the year and the brokerage fees during the year will equal b Y  . Given that the average cash withdrawal is K, the interest cost of holding cash
balances can be given by rK. Thus, the total cost of making transactions can be

written as:

1.8  Demand for Money	25

  Another important factor, which determines the decision of how much cash balances to hold, is the amount of money involved in the transactions. Since brokerage fees of buying and selling bonds are usually fixed, when the money used for the transactions is larger, the brokerage cost will be relatively smaller. Hence, the larger the amount involved in the transactions, the smaller will be the brokerage cost. This means that there are economies of scale in undertaking large transactions. Thus, although increased income can lead to increased transactions demand for money, the fact that an entity will enjoy economies of scale means that even if income increases, the demand for money will increase by a smaller proportion.



1.8.4 TobinÆs Portfolio Selection Model: The Risk Aversion Theory of Liquidity Preference

James Tobin in his seminal article in 1958 æLiquidity Preference as Behaviour Towards RiskÆ formulated the risk aversion theory of money demand based on portfolio selection. He criticised KeynesÆ speculative theory of money demand that assumed that individuals had knowledge on what the critical rate of interest is and that individuals will hold either bonds or money. According to Tobin (1958), since money does not fetch any interest it has no risk attached to it. Bonds, on the other hand, give some form of interest but are risky to hold. Bearing this in mind, he suggested that there are three types of people in every economy:
1. Those who like to take on so much risk that they will always hold bonds so long as the interest rate is high
2. The plungers, that is, those who will either hold all their portfolio in bonds or all in money
3. The risk averters, that is, those who do not like to take risk but will be prepared to take a little risk if they are compensated with enough returns
  In his view, many individuals fall within the third category. The return on bonds is given by the fixed return, which is the coupon to either capital gain or loss. That is, e = r + g, where e represents return, r the coupon rate and g the capital gain or loss which can either be negative or positive depending on whether a gain or loss is made. Since the coupon rate is determined at the beginning of the investment period, its value is certain. However, the value of the capital gain or loss cannot be predicted with certainty. Thus, the expected value of the return on bonds is r. That is E(e) = r. Since both money and bonds come with advantages and disadvantages, individuals will consider holding part of their wealth in bonds and part in money. The question now is: how much of the personÆs wealth will be held in money and how much will be held in bonds? Tobin (1958) uses an indifference curve analysis to arrive at the optimal amount of money individuals should hold. Indifference curves show the preferences that individuals have. Remember that bonds, although they fetch

26	1  Overview of the Monetary System

Fig. 1.2 Optimal portfolio construction








O


P

W	C


interest, they are risky to hold, while money is not risky to hold but does not fetch any interest. Thus, the individual will have to balance his/her expected returns against risk. In this case, the indifference curve shows the different combinations of risk and return that will yield the same level of satisfaction. Unlike the normal indifference curve that slopes downwards from left to right, this particular indiffer- ence curve will slope upwards from right to left because the individual is a risk averter and would be prepared to take on a little bit of risk in exchange for some return. This means that the satisfaction of the individual will be higher as we move to the north west of the plane. Without any constraints, an individual will want to move as far as he can towards the north west of the indifference curve plane because that indifference curve at the farthest point will yield the highest level of satisfaction. However, in a world of scarcity, individuals face constraints. In this particular instance, the individual is constrained by his/her wealth and that constraint is given by W=Wo + W1r. If we assume that Wo is constant, then W = W1r. This equation is linear with a positive slope r. Where the indifference curve is tangential to the constraint, the individual shows the optimal amount of risk and return he/she wants to take. Since the idea is to get the optimal amount of bonds and money an individual has to hold, it is important we relate the optimal amount of risk to the bonds (Fig. 1.2).
We thus draw a 45-degree line below the main diagram and trace the optimal risk
point to that line. That gives the optimal amount of bonds an individual would want to hold which is OP. The rest PW becomes the optimal amount of money the individual would want to hold. As the interest rate increases and the slope gets steeper (rotates from r1 to r2) as shown in Fig. 1.3, individuals will be willing to hold more bonds but not as much as they wanted to before, as shown by the increase from B1 to B2 compared to the distance OB1 before.

  This clearly shows how risk-averse individuals are, as the rate at which they would want to increase (decrease) their holdings for bonds (money) reduces (increases) as the interest rate rises.



1.8.5 FriedmanÆs Restatement Theory

FriedmanÆs (1956) reformulation is a complex attempt to get rid of the earlier crude version of the demand for money theory propounded by the classicals and to bring on a more articulate theory of demand for money. As has been seen above, the original theory propounded by the classicals appeared to be a tautology. It was based on the crudest form of the demand for money function that did not give any chance for substitution between money and other non-money assets. With the above criti- cism, Friedman pointed out that the quantity theory of money is a theory of money demand and not a theory of output, income or prices. Further, he distinguished between two types of demand for money: first, the demand for money for transactionary purposes, which is similar to the old quantity theory, second, because the demand for money on the part of people who hold wealth is similar to that of the demand for consumption services, he saw real money balances as a commodity which is demanded for the services it provided for the person who held it. Thus, money is considered an asset, which is more basic than the medium of exchange function it performs. Friedman, therefore, treats money as a part of the wealth theory. Friedman (1956) further argues that demand for money is stable, but it does not mean it is constant. By stability, he means functional stability, which establishes a stable relationship between income, price level, relative rates of return and then demand for money. Therefore, an acute rise in the velocity of money during

28	1  Overview of the Monetary System

hyperinflation should still be consistent with a stable functional relationship. This argument, which appears strong, has received a lot of criticism.
  Money demand being stable also means that the variables that are to be included should be limited and clearly specified. The earlier statement of the quantity theory of money had neglected any discussion on the channels through which monetary influences flow to other sectors of the economy, the commodity market in particular. This made the original quantity theory of money too mechanical, according to Friedman. The interest rate channel also suggested by Keynes was also too narrow. Thus, Friedman (1956) widened the range of substitution between money and non-money assets, with the latter not just being made of financial assets but including physical goods as well.
  Given all these criticisms, Friedman (1956) considers the demand for money to be a function of total wealth to be held in various forms: the price or return from these various assets and the tastes and preferences of the asset holders. With this, Friedman (1956) considers five ways in which wealth could be held, namely: in money, bonds, equities, physical non-human goods and human capital. Broadly speaking, total wealth is made up of all forms of income which is the aggregate of permanent income calculated as the expected yield from wealth during its lifetime. Wealth holders distribute their total wealth among these different assets to maximise their satisfaction. This is done in such a way that however much individuals can substitute one form of wealth for another is equal to how widely they are willing to disperse their wealth. Also, the cost of holding different forms of assets, except human capital, can be measured by the rate of return on the different assets. Following this, Friedman suggests four factors which influence the demand for money. These are price level, real income, rate of interest and the rate of increase in the price level (inflation).
  The relationship between the demand for money and income is direct but not proportional. Changes in income cause a more than proportionate change in the demand for money. This is similar to the relationship between demand for money and prices. However, this relationship is proportional. The relationship between demand for money and interest rate is inverse. At higher interest rate, the demand for money will be less. The rate of increase in the price level also has an inverse relationship with demand for money. The faster the rate of increase in the price level, the more expensive it becomes for one to hold money.



1.9 Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we provided an overview of the monetary system by discussing financial systems, the barter system, the evolution of money, functions of money and the characteristics of money as well as money supply and demand for money. We learnt that:

1.9 Summary and Conclusions	29

1. The financial system of any economy allows for financial transactions and exchange of money between surplus economic units and deficit economic units. The financial system is made up of money, financial instruments, financial markets, financial institutions and financial regulators.
2. Before money evolved, exchange was done through barter, which involved the direct exchange of one good for another good or one good for a service. The barter system could not survive the complexities of modern societies because it was associated with the problems of lack of double coincidence of wants, lack of a common measure of value, indivisibility of some goods, difficulty in storing value and difficulty in making deferred payment.
3. The problems associated with barter led to the introduction of money. The evolution of money has gone through some stages, including commodity money, metallic money and paper money.
4. Money is different from wealth, as wealth encompasses more assets than money. Money is anything used as a medium of exchange, a store of value, a unit of account and a standard of deferred payment.
5. The characteristics of money include scarcity, durability, divisibility, recognisability, acceptability, portability and liquidity, and it has no intrinsic value.
6. Theoretically, money can be defined by the functions it performs, but the empir- ical definition differs from one country to another and depends greatly on what serves as substitutes to currency in the respective country.
7. Money supply is defined as the amount of money in circulation in an economy at any point in time. It includes the notes, coins and any form of deposit, which can easily be converted into money.
8. The different theories of money demand and their relationship with interest rates. On one hand, the classical theory of transactions demand for money suggests that money demand depends on income, while Keynes, Baumol and TobinÆs theories of money demand see interest rate as a factor playing a critical role in the demand for money analysis. The Friedman restatement theory considers real income and rate of interest in addition to price level and inflation as the determinants of demand for money.
