2.1 Introduction

The business of banking is financial intermediation involving deposit taking and granting of loans. Banks are important financial intermediaries since they serve all types of surplus and deficit units. They provide opportunity for surplus units to deposit any amount of their funds for the length of time they want. They also repackage the funds received from the depositors in order to provide loans of the amount and duration that the deficit units require. Banks typically evaluate the credit worthiness of deficit units that apply for loans in order to determine the amount of loans the borrower is capable of repaying. This exercise is important to the extent that it minimises the credit risk the bank would have been otherwise exposed to in granting the loan.
  This chapter provides an overview of the banking system. It discusses the products and services of banks, the sources and uses of banksÆ funds and banking operations. It looks at the risks confronting the business of banking and the changes taking place in the banking industry. It also discusses globalisation and the impact of the global financial crisis and non-classical banking techniques.
® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019	31
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_2

32	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

2.2 Overview of the African Banking System
2.2.1 Evolution of Banking in Africa

The banking sector in Africa has witnessed significant changes in the past 20 years (read about the history of the African Banking system in Box 2.1). In the 1980s, the African banking system was dominated by state-owned banks and was subjected to restrictive regulations including interest rate ceilings and credit quotas (Laeven and Valencia 2012; Barth et al. 2013). However, the African financial landscape had changed, largely due to financial liberalisation, reforms, institutional upgrades and regulatory capacity (McDonald and Schumacher 2007). In addition, the expansion is also believed to have been triggered by cross-border banking activities and the rapid development of the Pan-African groupsÆ networks. Even though there are still challenges such as concentration, limited competition, high costs, short-term matu- rities and limited inclusion, most countries within the African continent have witnessed a deeper and more stable financial system. The African banking system survived the turmoil of the global financial crisis despite the indirect pressures through international trade linkages. This created the ambience for the banking system in Africa to be more robust and able to withstand the economic shocks to the fragile African economies (Beck et al. 2011; Nyantakyi et al. 2015).

(continued)


1This work is based on Beck et al. (2014) and its cited source document Brownbridge and Harvey (1998).

2.2 Overview of the African Banking System	33


Box 2.1 (continued)
foreign banks did not serve the needs and development goals of the new states. Banks were criticized for discriminating against Africans and African-owned businesses and for lending almost exclusively to foreign companies for the purpose of trade finance and other short-term purposes. Thus there was political pressure for governments to intervene in the financial sector. While the extent of intervention varied from country to country, the overall goal was to rectify the perceived financing gaps by issuing directives to reduce the cost of credit and creating development institutions to provide longer-term finance. Most governments established state-owned banks in order to serve those parts of the economy that had been denied loans by the foreign banks. Locally owned private banks also entered the market in some countries, especially in Kenya and Nigeria, where entry requirements were low and banks saw an opportunity to compete on service or to make high returns, such as on foreign exchange trading or insider lending. Throughout this period, foreign banks mostly maintained their presenceùsometimes with governments taking minority shares, such as in Uganda and Ghana, or majority shares, such as in Nigeria and Malawiùalthough their relative importance decreased due to the emergence of new state-owned and private local banks.
  By the 1980s, many African economies were experiencing economic crises due to a combination of external shocks and failing domestic policies. In the financial sector, the interventionist policies came at considerable cost and provided little, if any, benefit. With the exception of Ethiopia, countries with the most extensive government interventions suffered the most damage. Many state-owned banks became insolvent due to political pressure to make unsustainable loans, but also because of lack of technical expertise and poor management. In several state-owned banks across different countries, non-performing loans accounted for up to 80% of the loan portfolio. However, not all state-owned banks failed. In countries where government intervention was less heavy-handed, resulting in relatively less distorted credit markets, as in Kenya, Ethiopia and Zimbabwe, state-owned banks remained functional. This was often because those banks had private sector participation or man- agement provided by a foreign bank. As part of a more general economic liberalization agenda imposed on countries as part of the structural adjustment process in the 1990s and early 2000s, the banking sectors in many Africa countries underwent financial liberalization. The objective was to restore credit allocation based on commercial criteria, relying on the credit risk assessment skills of the private sector, and return to market-determined interest rates. The liberalization of the financial sector in turn provided opportunities for entry and expansion of banks across the continent.
  While many African countries experienced a significant increase in foreign bank participation during this period, Kenya, Nigeria and South Africa saw
(continued)

34	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking


  The progress made in the banking system might have been significant, but it is not enough to sustain future growth. Several countries within the African continent continue to exhibit weak banking systems with few financial instruments and lack of sufficient depth. Thus, financial inclusion remains low in the region. The 2017 World BankÆs financial inclusion database indicates that only 43% of the adults in Sub-Saharan Africa have bank accounts, but this was up from 34% in 2014. This means that even though access to finance is expanding in the African sub-region, it still remains among the lowest in the world. One of the key obstacles to this is the activities and growth of enterprises, especially micro, small and medium-sized enterprises. The constraint to low financial inclusion has also been attributable to cost, distance, religious reasons, regulations and fragile financial systems.
  The African continent is divided into several sub-regions and geopolitical, eco- nomic and geographical regions. In terms of geography, the African continent is divided into Southern Africa, North Africa, West Africa, East and Central Africa. Each of these sub-regions have their own forms of banking system with the Southern and North Africa having a relatively robust banking system as compared to the West, East and Central Africa. The differences in the banking systems are influenced by competition and entry regulations. In the case of West Africa, it is relatively easy for new banks to enter the banking industry as compared to South and North Africa, where regulation regarding entry is rigid. The banking systems in the Southern African countries and in North Africa tend to be fairly more developed compared to those in the West, East and Central Africa. More specifically, the banking system in North Africa is much deeper, well penetrated and efficient than those in other parts

2.2 Overview of the African Banking System	35

of Africa. The main challenge, however, is that it lacks innovations and is not competitive compared to banking systems in other regions in Africa. This might be so because Islamic banking is more dominant in the North African countries, a system which does not believe in recent banking innovative products that are contrary to Islam. Also, the less competitive nature of the North African banks is largely due to the over-concentration in Islamic banking models and the neglect of the universal banking system, which is driven by high interest rates.
  The depth of the African financial system shows the extent to which agents are able to use financial markets. Savings and investments have a strong link with long- term economic growth, and it enhances firms and businessesÆ ability to invest in long-term risky financial instruments. Financial deepening is measured using domes- tic credit to the private sector as a percentage of GDP. This measure captures the claims on the private sector by deposit-taking financial institutions relative to economic activity. This reflects the role of financial intermediaries in channelling savings to private sector investors. When the financial sector deepens, it increases the amount of credit to the private sector, and this results in higher productivity and enhances the use of financial services by the unbanked population. However, with this measure, the Sub-Saharan AfricaÆs financial system is seen as the shallowest in terms of financial depth. Allen et al. (2014) suggest that most SSA countries experience a higher financial development gap relative to other peer developing economies. For instance, in 2014, Africa recorded 37.3% for domestic credit to the private sector and SSA recorded 24.4% below the African average. The figure for SSA was also half the average ratio of North Africa (48.1%) and Latin America and the Caribbean (45.5%) and less than quarter of the OECD (134.3%) countries. Within the region, West Africa and East Africa recorded the lowest domestic credit to the private sector at 20% and 21%, respectively, while Southern Africa recorded a relatively high ratio of 43%. The high ratio in Southern Africa is the result of the financial depth of South Africa. In addition, the ratio of liquid liabilities to GDP, a measure of monetary resources, serves as a comprehensive indicator of the level of financial intermediation by the financial players in the market. Similarly, bank deposit as a percentage of GDP, which measures financial depth, has been low in West and Central Africa recording 27.2 and 27.4%, respectively, which is below the African average of 45.5% (Nyantakyi et al. 2015).
  In the 1980s and 1990s, many banks in Africa moved from the manual banking system to office digital services. The banks have spent almost a decade to invest in banking infrastructure, which include online banking and electronic transactions platforms. This has not only allowed banks to increase their coverage, but it has also allowed domestic banks to speed up competition with their foreign counterparts and has exceedingly reduced transaction cost while increasing their profitability. The expansion of telecommunication networks and access to mobile phones in rural communities in East Africa have made the use of mobile money very common for the rural poor who cannot access ATMs and electronic payment systems. Banks have been able to save on the cost of installing ATMs, maintaining and balancing liquidity by partnering with telecommunication providers to provide financial ser- vices at low cost. This has helped to save the shoe-leather cost of visiting long-

36	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

distance ATMs by banksÆ clients and also allows for real-time money transfer. In the case of Kenya, M-PESA comes to mind. M-PESA is a money transfer system developed by Safaricom in 2007, which now serves over 25 million clients with more than 261,000 agents across 11 countries. In addition, across Africa, banks have introduced SMS alerts for clients, online banking, mobile banking technology and so forth, which were hitherto not part of the manual banking system. This has helped to improve the efficiency of African banks, reduce cost of transaction and free up customers movements to long-distance branches for transactions.
  Banks play an enormous role in an economy as financial intermediaries. They are confronted with risks and challenges in carrying out their banking operations, hence the need to regulate and supervise them. BanksÆ regulation and supervision in Africa are mainly done by central banks. The independence of central banks plays a critical role in the supervision and regulation of banks. Countries with weak central bank independence may tend not to provide proper supervision and regulation of banks because of unnecessary interferences. Banks need to be regulated because they cannot overcome information asymmetries. Central banks safeguard unsophisticated depositors from banksÆ risky operations and assure the stability of the financial system by mitigating excessive risk taking that can damage the interest of creditors and by preventing systematic banking crises that can impair the entire economic system. Central banks or reserve banks in Africa use three tools to regulate banks. These include regulating the severity of banksÆ activities, the capital requirements of banks and the degree of competition of the banks. Similarly, three indicators are used to measure the degree of bank supervision in Africa: first, the degree of indepen- dence of the central bank from political influence; second, the extent to which supervisory authority is protected by the legal system from the banking industry; and third, an aggregate measure of central banksÆ independence and a legal system. International rules on banking supervision and regulation are important for Africa. The Basel accord of banking supervision is a classical mediation. The Basel committee on bank supervision had developed three frameworks. Basel I, which was developed in the late 1980s and launched in December 1992, purposely to regulate the new global banking and its capital requirements, is known as the Cook ratio. This ratio recommends that the banksÆ commitments cover 8% of the capital. The Cook ratio was to allow banks to absorb unexpected shocks without damaging the economic system. In 2004, Basel II was launched which is organised under three pillars, namely equity, risk monitoring and transparency. The Cook ratio became the McDonoughÆs ratio and remains at the recommended 8% of total capital require- ments. The subprime crises of 2007 forced the Basel committee to release Basel III to take care of the inherent risk caused by Basel II. Basel III aimed at increasing the ratio of total capital from 8% to 10.5% by 2019. Basel III makes two significant differences from Basel II. First, it strengthens the micro-prudential regulation that existed in Basel II, and second, it introduces macro-prudential regulation to avoid systemic crisis. However, most African countries had focused on the implementation of only the Basel I framework, basically because the African financial system is seen as too small to be affected by financial crises and also because the African financial system deals very little with derivatives, so too much emphasis is not placed on risk

2.2 Overview of the African Banking System	37

mitigating supervisions. Similarly, high liquidity and recently experienced good governance seem to be sufficient for the stability of the African banking system. Currently, South Africa and Mauritius are the only two countries which seem to be engaged in the implementation of the Basel II framework.


2.2.2 Cross-Border Banking in Africa

What Is Cross-Border Banking? A bank is said to be a cross-border bank if it is engaged in banking activities outside its home country through at least one branch or subsidiary. Thus, cross-border banking as the name implies is banking conducted across borders (that is, commercial banking activities conducted in at least two countries). Note that a subsidiary is different from a branch. While both operate as a bank, a subsidiary has a separate legal status and may be fully owned or partly owned by a bank in another country, but in the case of a branch, it does not have a separate legal status from the parent bank. A cross-border bank with the controlling shares owned by a shareholder or a group of shareholders outside the country of operation is regarded as a foreign bank.
  Cross-border banking has been an integral part of AfricaÆs banking sector history since colonial times. In the aftermath of independence, a lot of the colonial banks were acquired and nationalised by the newly independent African states leading to the exit of most colonial banks. This trend was however reversed in the 1980s following the implementation of financial liberalisation policies in many African countries. Later on, non-performing state-owned and private banks were sold mostly to global investors or multinational banks. Rapid social, economic, political and financial international globalisation together with deregulation further increased the number of foreign banks, and by the mid-2000s many African banking systems were once again dominated by foreign banks. As at 2014, Africa had about 104 active cross-border banks with at least one subsidiary outside the country of origin (Beck et al. 2014). Up to 71 of these cross-border banks have their presence in one to four African countries, while 34 are present in at least five African countries and operate through branches or subsidiaries.
  An important development in cross-border banking is the dominance of banks of African origin in the African cross-border banking landscape. Only one-third of the cross-border banks in Africa are of non-African origin. Thus, of the 104 cross-border banks operating in Africa, 69 are of African origin. Africa continues to witness an expansion of branch network by these Africa headquartered banks. For instance, Ecobank tripled its affiliate network in Africa between 2000 and 2013 from 11 to 32 countries, NigeriaÆs United Bank for Africa increased its footprint from 1 to 19 countries, MoroccoÆs Attijariwafa Bank extended its footprint from 1 to 12 coun- tries and MoroccoÆs BMCE went from 2 to 18 countries within the same period.
  The presence of foreign banks has increased competition and good corporate governance and triggered innovative banking technique spillovers to domestic banks. There is enough empirical evidence that suggests that the presence of foreign

38	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

banks has led to greater access to finance by small and medium enterprises on the African continent. Also, unfair competition can arise due to the dominance of foreign banks in the industry in terms of assets and branches. Research has shown that foreign banks have a competitive edge when it comes to access to credit information of borrowers, and therefore they can engage in æcherry-pickingÆ bor- rowers, while worsening the remaining credit pool for domestic banks. This will have a negative effect on small businesses and mitigate the positive effect of foreign bank entry (Nyantakyi et al. 2015). Generally, the degree to which a countryÆs banking and economic system will profit from cross-border banking will depend on the financial infrastructure, particularly the availability and efficiency of credit reference bureaus; the market structure, particularly the degree of state participation in the banking sector; and the availability of supportive regulatory and supervisory framework (Beck et al. 2014). Cross-border banks in Africa largely focus on the corporate sector, especially on large firms with little focus on the lower end of the market. Particularly, the extractive sector has been of interest to African cross-border banks.
Cross-border banks of African extraction can be classified into three main groups:
1. Regional Banking Groups: These are cross-border banking groups that operate in only one economic block in Africa.
2. Multi-regional Banking Groups: These are cross-border banking groups that operate in at least two different economic blocks in Africa.
3. Pan-African Banking Groups: These are cross-border banking groups that oper- ate in more than two economic blocks in Africa and more commonly operate across the entire continent.
  There are eight economic blocks in Africa, namely AMU (Arab Maghreb Union), CEN-SAD (the Community of Sahel-Saharan States), COMESA (Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa), EAC (East African Community), ECCAS (Eco- nomic Community of Central African States), ECOWAS (Economic Community of West African States), IGAD (Intergovernmental Authority on Development) and SADC (Southern African Development Community). The African Union has man- dated these regional economic blocks to serve members states in their quest to integrate.


2.2.3 Mobile Phone Banking

The vast majority of Africans lack access to formal banking services due to lack of supportive infrastructure, dispersed geographic spread, low levels of financial liter- acy and macroeconomic stability which exacerbate the cost of delivering banking services. Estimates from World Bank data from 2011 to 2014 show the number of bank accounts per 1000 adults is an average of only 150.3 in SSA compared to a world average of 511.7. Again, the average private credit provided by banks and other financial institutions over the same period was 16.5% of GDP for SSA

2.2 Overview of the African Banking System	39

compared to a global average of 42.3% of GDP. This means that, left to traditional banking alone, the greater number of Africans would have been left out of the banking systems.
  In this situation, technology can offer a means to innovate out of this quagmire. In particular, mobile phone banking and the use of smartcards can change the face of banking in Africa by allowing a large segment of the population to have access to banking services at their doorstep. Perhaps, over the past four decades three tech- nological products have revolutionised the world the most in the ICT arena, viz. the Internet, personal computers and mobile phones. But among these, the mobile phone has penetrated Africa the most. This makes the mobile phone a viable tool around which financial services can be developed to increase banking penetration in Africa. The mobile phone can function as a virtual bank card, as a point of sale terminal,
as an ATM and as an Internet banking terminal. Mobile phone banking therefore entails the use of a mobile phone to access banking services such as withdrawals, deposits, transfers, payments of bills, transaction alerts and bank statements, among others. Typical mobile phone banking may involve linking your mobile phone to your bank account to enable the performance of banking services via the mobile phone or online and without the need to go to a brick-and-mortar bank. It may also involve mobile money services where money is stored mostly in local currency as a credit on smart card or in the books of the service provider that enables transactions through a mobile phone. Thus, mobile money platforms allow individuals to trans- form cash into electronic cash which is then stored in an electronic wallet. It is this alternative form of mobile banking that has revolutionised the African banking landscape.
  Mobile phone banking has the potential to reduce transaction costs, deepen the intermediation process and improve financial services delivery in Africa. Mobile money systems permit the transfer of funds from one mobile user to the other, can be linked to a bank account to freely allow for the movement of funds, can be used to pay electricity, water and other bills, can be used to make online and offline purchases, can be used to receive remittances and so on. Mobile money services are also now being linked to ATMs such that a token can be generated from the electronic wallet to enable the individual to withdraw cash from an ATM machine. The most celebrated example of mobile money services is M-PESA which was pioneered by Commercial Bank of Africa and Safaricom in Kenya in 2007. The mobile money service providers in Kenya have a customer base over 20 million, and this population (which is about half of the entire population) handle over $19.9 billion worth of transactions per year, representing 47% of the countryÆs GDP (Mwega 2013). Mobile money systems have spread to several other African coun- tries and are gaining increasing usage especially by the poor. Estimates for Ghana show that mobile money wallets have expanded astronomically from 2.5 million in 2011 to 13 million in 2016, a 420% growth outturn. The mobile money service providers currently have in excess of 80,000 merchants across the country, with the value of mobile money transactions growing from GHS 400 million in 2011 to GHS 35 billion (US$9 billion) in 2016 (Ghana Chamber of Telecommunications 2016).

  Table 2.1 shows that Africa outperforms the rest of the world in the use of mobile phone-based financial services. As at 2014, about 11.5% of the adult population in SSA had a mobile money account compared with just 2.6% of the adult population in Europe and Central Asia having the same. The African countries leading the mobile money revolution include Kenya, Somalia, Uganda, Tanzania, Cote dÆIvoire, Zimbabwe, Rwanda, South Africa, Ghana and Zambia.
  With mobile money accounts in Sub-Saharan Africa capable of helping to rapidly expand and scale up access to financial services, the fight against poverty in the continent has received a new boost. Already, the literature shows that mobile banking services have helped in reducing poverty, increasing agricultural produc- tivity and promoting pro-poor growth. Again, data also show big opportunities for boosting financial inclusion among women and the poor (Demirguc-Kunt et al. 2015). In addition, mobile money systems hold great potential for extending finan- cial inclusion to private sector enterprises, especially micro and small enterprises, which often find it difficult to access funds from formal financial markets and institutions.



2.2.4 African Banking Perspective on the Global Crisis

Its impact will depend on initial conditions, degree of globalisation and financial integration with advanced countries.
  The 2007û2009 global economic and financial crises (GEFC), which had the USA as its epicentre, particularly its housing market, adulterated the broader finan- cial system, spread through other parts of the advanced world and ultimately affected developing countries through various transmission mechanisms (Essers 2013). Indeed, the crises began as a subprime mortgage turmoil in the USA in 2007, metamorphosed into a global financial crisis and economic recession in the advanced world in 2008 and then ruptured into a development crises in Africa in 2009

2.2 Overview of the African Banking System	41

(Maswana 2009). While the transmission mechanism of the crises to the industrialised world was mainly through financial integration, in the developing world it was through trade, foreign direct investment (FDI) flows, tourism revenue, migration and remittances, foreign aid and to some extent the financial system (Sigwele Howard 2009; Aryeetey and Ackah 2011). The crises had different impact on different parts of the world, with advanced countries being the hardest hit. Even though the impact on developing countries was not as drastic as it was in industrial countries, here too, the effects are not negligible (Maswana 2009). Rough calcula- tions indicate that the GEFC increased the number of poor people in 2009 (those living with less than $1.25 a day) by 50 million, compared to a no-crises scenario and about 64 million by the end of 2010 (World Bank 2010). A more gloomy projection by Friedman and Schady (2009) showed that in Sub-Saharan Africa, the crises might have left an additional 30,000û50,000 infants dead.
  Initially, it was thought by most analysts that the crises would not have much impact on African economies. The explanation that was offered was that African financial systems were not as leveraged as those of the USA and the UK (Te Velde 2011). According to Te Velde (2011), by the end of 2008 the effects of the financial contagion on Africa were clear with international bank lending, which had grown at a very fast rate in the pre-crises period, declining by 10% in the post-crises period. Furthermore, owing to the crises, 10 African countries experienced declines in real GDP (compared with forecasts made before the crisis) of more than 5% in 2009, 11 countries of between 3% and 5% and 19 of between 1% and 3%; others have been affected minimally (Te Velde 2011). The author projects that financing shortfall in Africa increased by at least $134 billion due to challenges from funding sources such as bank lending, trade, portfolio flows and foreign direct investment (FDI) and remittances.
  The banking sector in Africa is said to be a major channel through which the global crises impacted Africa. The degree of impact of the crises on a particular countryÆs banking system was dependent on the countryÆs degree of global integra- tion. The crises impacted African banks to whatever degree through cross-border fund flow (Aryeetey and Ackah 2011). Generally, the African banking system is quite insulated from the complex financing instruments in advanced markets and relies largely on domestic deposits. Consequently, no African country recorded a banking failure during the GEFC (UNESC/ECA/AUC 2009). The relative resilience of the banking system in Africa stemmed from the following factors (IMF 2009):
ò Most African banking systems do not have well-developed interbank markets through which contagion could spread.
ò The African banking system generally relies on low-cost domestic deposits and less on wholesale funding from abroad.
ò Prior to the crises, treasury bills and other liquid assets composed a large portion of the balance sheets of most African banks.
ò The African banking system lacks sophisticated financial instruments which were at the centre of the crises in the USA and other advanced countries.
ò Limited transfers of funds from subsidiaries in Africa to parents.

42	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

  But this does not mean that all African countriesÆ banking systems were insulated from the GEFC. Countries that were most hurt were those that had high presence of foreign banks in their banking system. The African countries with high foreign bank occupation include Botswana, Cape Verde, Central African Republic, Chad, C¶te dÆIvoire, Equatorial Guinea, Lesotho and Zambia (Aryeetey and Ackah 2011). There are four main ways by which subsidiaries of foreign banks in Africa could have been affected by the crises (IMF 2009):
ò Parent bank withdrawing capital from African subsidiaries
ò Parent bank calling back loans extended to their subsidiaries in Africa
ò Parent bank refusing to invest local profits in local subsidiaries
ò A combination of these
  Even in those countries with high foreign bank presence, the impact of the crises on their banking system was at best modest. As shown in Table 2.2, in about 20 countries in Africa, the majority of banking sector assets are foreign owned. There is also the large presence of French banks in the CFA Zone and pre-eminence of UK banks (Barclays and Standard Chartered), especially in southern Africa. From Table 2.1, two countries in Africa (Mozambique and SÒo TomÚ & PrÝncipe) had their entire banking sector assets owned by foreigners, and for these countries, the fate of their banking institutions rested entirely on their foreign sponsors. And in four other African countries (Botswana, the Comoros, the Democratic Republic of Congo, Lesotho), foreign ownership of banking sector assets was not less than 90% at the time of the crises.
  In a number of countries the impact of the global crises was manifested in terms of squeeze on bank lending which was manifested through banks raising the criteria for lending and focusing on high-quality borrowers, limited appetite of banks for new business and hikes in lending rate (IMF 2009). These tight credit conditions led to a decline in credit to the private sector in some African countries. For instance, Aryeetey and Ackah (2011) have documented that in Kenya, the growth of credit to the private sector in the first quarter of 2007 was as high as 9.6%, but by the last quarter of 2008, this had declined to 2.5%. It has also been reported that loan syndications in Africa were affected during the crises period with syndication on the continent declining sharply by 23% since the last quarter of 2008 (Bandara 2010). In some cases, non-performing loan portfolios increased marginally. For example, in Ghana the loan loss provisions to gross loans ratio rose from 5.9% in December 2008 to 6.3% in December 2008 (Aryeetey and Ackah 2011).



2.3 Products and Services Provided by Banks

Banks in Africa provide the following categories of banking products:
ò Bank Liability Products. These include current account, savings account, fixed deposit account, foreign deposit account and call account.

ò Transactional products including remittances, drafts and travellersÆ cheques.
  In very general terms, banks provide various services, which can be classified into individual banking, institutional banking, global banking and investment banking.
Individual Banking covers consumer lending, residential mortgage lending, credit card financing, consumer instalment loans, car loans, student loans, brokerage services and other individual-oriented financial investment services such as personal trust and investment services. Banks generate income from interest charged on the individual loans and fees for maintaining customersÆ accounts.

2.3 Products and Services Provided by Banks	45

Institutional Banking includes loans to businesses and government entities. It also covers commercial real estate financing, leasing activities and factoring. Banks generate income from interest charged on loans given out and fees for services such as management of pension fund assets, fiduciary and custodial services and cash management services including account maintenance, cheque clearing and electronic fund transfers.
Global Banking covers corporate financing, capital market and foreign exchange products and services. Global banking operations usually generate fee income instead of interest income. Corporate financing involves assisting a bankÆs corporate customers to raise funds. Banks provide letters of credit, bankersÆ acceptances and other types of guarantees to help their customers obtain funds. For instance, if a customer borrows funds supported by a letter of credit or other guarantee, the lenders can look up to the customerÆs bank to fulfil the loan obligation. The capital market also involves transactions in which the bank acts as a dealer or broker on behalf of its customers. Foreign exchange products and services entail maintaining foreign exchange operations which involve the buying and selling of foreign cur- rencies. Customers of a bank requiring foreign exchange can therefore rely on the services of the bank.



2.3.1 Functions of the Banking System

The banking system provides a number of functions including, financial intermedi- ation, enhancing payment systems and acting as agents of government policy.
Financial Intermediation Banks provide an intermediation role by bringing sup- plier of funds and users of funds together. They transfer funds between suppliers and users of funds, who need them for carrying out productive activities or investment. Banks have access to large amounts of funds, and they are able to attract these funds by selling bank products such as demand deposits, savings and time deposits, which they give out as loans to those in need of funds for production.
Trust and Advisory Services Banks provide financial advice to customers and offer trust services to both individuals and businesses. They manage the financial affairs of their customers such as investing funds, safe keeping of valuable assets, acting as agents for companies, selling shares on behalf of companies and selling/ buying bills on behalf of government for a fee.
Enhancing Payment Systems Banks play an important role in the payment system by keeping peopleÆs money in safety and ensuring the payment of goods and services. They also facilitate effective working of the payment system when pay- ments are made by cheques, electronic funds transfer, debit/credit cards, etc.
Acting as Agents of Government Policy Banks give out loans to government so that governmentÆs activities can get going, even if government is short of funds.

46	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

Government can increase or decrease the amount of money in the hands of cus- tomers and investors in order to try to control price levels. This is done through the use of monetary policy tools. Banks therefore aid in the implementation of monetary policies.
Banks and Economic Activity Banks are very important in facilitating economic activities in the sense that they facilitate the expansion of economic activity by granting credit to businesses, they facilitate trade by acting as guarantors to their customers when they open letters of credit, they facilitate fundraising by underwrit- ing bankersÆ acceptances (acting as guarantors to their customers in the home country) and they also help in the efficient allocation of resources by transferring funds from surplus units to productive deficit units.
Quick Quiz
1. What are the functions of the banking system in Africa?
2. Describe AfricaÆs banking system.




2.4 Sources and Uses of Bank Funds
2.4.1 Sources of Bank Funds

The sources of bank funds include deposits, non-deposit borrowing and equity and retained earnings. These major sources of funds are described as follows.


2.4.1.1 Deposits Accounts

These include transaction deposits, savings deposits, time deposits and money market deposit accounts.
Transaction Deposits These include demand deposit accounts or current accounts. They are offered to customers who want to make withdrawals by writing a cheque. Customers can also make withdrawals by using the ATMs or make payments through Internet banking or debit cards. Transaction deposits may also involve electronic transactions by bank customers. Traditionally, demand deposits do not earn interest and they involve a small minimum balance. Demand deposits provide a source of funding for the bank until such time that customers withdraw their funds.
Savings Deposits The traditional savings account is the passbook savings account, which does not allow customers to write cheques to make withdrawals. Savings accounts do not usually require a minimum balance to be kept in the account, and customers are supposed to give some daysÆ notice in order to make withdrawals.

2.4 Sources and Uses of Bank Funds	47

Time Deposits These are deposits for which the customer cannot make withdrawals until a specified maturity date. The two most common types of time deposits are certificate of deposit (CDs) and negotiable certificate of deposits (NCDs). CDs are a type of time deposit, which requires a specified minimum amount of funds to be deposited for a specified period of time. CDs cannot be sold before maturity. NCDs are another type of time deposit offered by some large banks to firms. NCDs are similar to CDs in that they require a specified maturity date and require a minimum deposit. They can be sold before maturity.
Money Market Deposit Accounts (MMDAs) These are different from conventional time deposits in the sense that they do not have maturity dates. MMDAs are more liquid than CDs from the depositorÆs point of view. Because banks prefer to know how long they will have a depositorÆs funds, they normally pay a higher interest rate on CDs.
  The other sources of funds we are going to discuss are of a non-depository nature. Such sources are necessary when a bank temporarily needs more funds than what customers have deposited with it. Some banks tend to rely on non-depository funds as a permanent source of funding.


2.4.1.2 Borrowed Funds

Banks also raise funds by borrowing from the central bank, by borrowing on the inter-bank market, by repurchase agreements or by Eurodollar borrowings.
Borrowing from the Central Bank Another temporary source of funds for banks is borrowing from the central bank. The central bank serves as a ælender of last resortÆ for banks in the country. The central bank often provides short-term loans to banks and other depository institutions. This form of borrowing from the central bank by banks is often known as borrowing at the discount window. The interest rate charged on these loans is referred to as the discount rate.
Borrowing on the Inter-Bank Market Banks sometimes borrow from other banks on a short-term basis. In Africa, banks borrow overnight from other banks for a day or few days. Such overnight borrowing and lending take place in what is popularly known as the inter-bank market or overnight bank market.
Repurchase Agreements A repurchase agreement (repo) is the sale of securities by one party to another with the agreement of repurchasing the securities at a specified price on a predetermined future date. Banks often use ærepoÆ as a source of finance when they expect they will need funds for just a few days. For instance, a bank in need of short-term funds may decide to sell its treasury bills to another bank or firm for the funds needed with the understanding that it will buy back its treasury bills from this firm by refunding the funds it received when it sold the treasury bills.
Eurodollar Borrowings Banks in the USA in need of short-term funds could borrow in dollars from banks outside of the USA that take dollar-denominated

48	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

deposits or Eurodollars. Some of these Euro banks are foreign banks or foreign branches of US banks that participate in the Eurodollar market by accepting large short-term deposits and making short-term loans in dollars. Some large US banks often obtain short-term funds from Eurodollar deposits.


2.4.1.3 Long-Term Sources of Funds

Other sources of bank funding include issuing long-term debt and equity securities.
Bonds Issued by Banks Banks may finance their fixed assets by issuing bonds to the general public. However, banks do not finance with bonds as much as manufacturing or industrial firms that require heavy machinery and equipment for production.
Bank Capital Banks may also raise funds by issuing shares or relying on their retained profits. Bank capital is basically the equity or net worth of the bank. Unlike other sources of funds, which represent a future obligation by the bank to make payments out of the funds, the use of equity does not require the bank to make any such payments. Capital can be classified as primary or secondary. Primary capital is obtained from issuing ordinary or preference shares or relying on retained profits, while secondary capital is raised from issuing subordinated notes and bonds.



2.4.2 Uses of Funds by Banks

Banks put their funds into various uses including holding cash, granting loans, investing in securities, lending on the inter-bank market, reverse repurchase agree- ments and acquiring fixed assets.
Holding Cash Banks hold cash, first of all to satisfy the reserve requirements by the central bank and, secondly, to meet the withdrawal requests of their customers. Banks are very particular about the amount of cash they hold, given that such amounts held do not attract interest income. Therefore, they will hold just enough cash to meet these requirements. Banks do not feel pressured to hold excess cash because they can always raise temporary funds from other sources (as we have discussed earlier). Cash held for the purpose of meeting customersÆ withdrawal requests is kept in a vault, while cash meant for satisfying the reserve requirement is kept with the central bank. The central bank or reserve bank uses the reserve requirement as a tool for controlling the money supply in the economy.
Giving Loans Banks utilise the funds they raise to give out loans to their customers. There are different types of loans that banks can grant. An example is working capital loan (also known as self-liquidating loan), which is meant to support ongoing business operations. Working capital loans are short-term loans used to support a

2.5 Banking Operations	49

business until sufficient cash inflows are generated. Another example is term loans, which are used to finance the purchase of fixed assets.
Investment in Securities Banks also use their funds to invest in government treasury securities as well as other securities such as shares and bonds issued by other institutions and government agencies.
Lending on the Inter-Bank Market Banks often lend on a short-term basis to other banks. In Africa and elsewhere, banks lend overnight to other banks for a day or few days. Such overnight lending and borrowing take place on the inter-bank market.
Reverse Repurchase Agreement A bank can lend to another institution by agreeing to buy Treasury bills sold by that institution with the understanding of selling the Treasury bills back to the institution at a specified price at a future date.
Acquiring Fixed Assets Banks also invest in fixed assets such as office buildings and office equipment in order to carry out their normal business operations.



2.5 Banking Operations

Banks accept deposits and give out loans. The deposits received from depositors represent the bankÆs liability and the loans given out represent the bankÆs assets.
  The banking law in any particular country specifies the reserve requirement, which is the percentage of deposits banks are required to keep with the central bank. The percentage set as reserve requirement affects how much is left as loanable funds for the banks. A high reserve requirement increases the banksÆ cost of funds since it does not attract interest. After maintaining the required reserves with the central bank and lending the excess reserves or remaining portion of the deposits, the process of credit creation then begins (Rose and Hudgins 2013).
  One of the important functions of the banking system is the creation of credit, which involves the expansion of banksÆ deposits as multiples of their excess cash reserves. A single bank cannot create credit. It is the banking system as a whole, which can expand loans by many times of its excess cash reserves. A loan advanced to a bank customer is not provided in the form of physical cash. The bank credits the loan amount to the customerÆs account, allowing the customer to make withdrawal as and when necessary. The loan advanced becomes the gain of deposit by some other bank.
  Banks aim to earn a profit by granting loans to customers from the deposits they mobilise. All bank customers do not make withdrawals at the same time; some withdraw while others deposit simultaneously. So, banks encourage credit creation by giving loans to customers and keeping some cash in reserve for day-to-day transactions.
Credit creation is based on a series of assumptions including:
ò The publicÆs holdings of currency remain unchanged.

50	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

ò The quantities of time and savings deposits are fixed, and we concentrate only on demand deposits.
ò The banking systemÆs desired level of excess reserves is constant. We will discuss credit creation in greater detail in Chapter Sixteen.
Quick Quiz
1. Identify products provided by banks in Africa.
2. Identify banksÆ sources of fund and how they used these funds.
3. Describe how banks operate.



2.6 Risks in Banking

Banks are confronted with several risks. They have to identify and manage these risks; otherwise they may be taken unawares when these risks crystallise, leading to serious consequences. Some of these risks include credit risk, currency risk, interest rate risk, liquidity risk, strategic risk, operational risk and reputational, legal and compliance risks, among others.
Credit Risk Credit risk may result from the clients of the bank failing to pay interest and loans granted them on time or at all. Since banks give out a lot of loans, they are exposed to credit risk. Banks therefore need to do a proper credit analysis and good loan evaluation before giving out loans. Banks are relationship- based organisations and should therefore develop long-term relationships with their clients to reduce information asymmetry between the bank and these clients. They should also lend across different business sectors and geographies in order to diversify the loan book and reduce the probability of default. These tactics will reduce the probability that loans given out will go bad.
  The performance of the economy may also affect the ability of borrowers to repay loans taken. An economic slowdown may lead to a delay in the payment of loans by most clients.
  We looked at the loan loss ratio, which is one of the indicators of the credit risk of a bank. Other measures include:
ò Relating provisions on loans to the bankÆs equity capital
ò Relating non-performing loans to the bankÆs equity capital
ò Relating non-performing loans to total loans and leases
Market Risk Market risk could occur because the cash flows of a financial instru- ment could change due to changes in market prices. Market risk therefore reflects exposures such as currency risk, interest rate risk, liquidity risk and other price risks.
Currency Risk Banks experience currency or foreign exchange risk arising from changes in value of a currency in relation to other currencies. It is a risk associated with fluctuation in exchange rates. Banks give out loans in foreign currency and also

2.6 Risks in Banking	51

invest in some foreign currency-denominated assets. Therefore, fluctuations in exchange rates affect their investments in loans and other assets. Banks that are involved in multicurrency transactions are affected by currency risk.
Interest Rate Risk One of the important risks that confront banks is interest rate risk. Interest rate changes affect the value of a bankÆs asset as well as its liabilities. It also impacts on the value of a bankÆs equity. Large changes in interest rates could easily wipe out the equity capital of a bank. Interest rate changes also affect the interest incomes and interest expenses of a bank.
Liquidity Risk Banks also face liquidity risk, which arises because they may not have sufficient funds to meet depositorsÆ withdrawal needs or to meet loan requests by their prime customers. However, keeping too much liquid resource comes at a cost of forgone return that the bank could have earned by investing these funds in other assets. Some of the more liquid resources on the bankÆs balance sheet include cash and cash balances with the central bank, items in the course of collection (uncollected cheques), investments in government securities and amounts due from other banks and other financial institutions. Liquidity management is usually done by the Asset and Liability Committee (usually referred to as ALCO) of a bank. If a bank runs out of liquidity, it can borrow from the inter-bank market but that will cost the bank more money and reduce its profitability.
Strategic Risk Strategic risk arises from the products and markets in which the bank operates. It results from the strategic positioning of the bank. This is a high- level risk, and its management will usually lie with the board of the bank. It is a risk associated with the strategic decisions of the board. These risks may occur because the board makes strategic mistakes. These risks are costlier compared to operational risks and have a major effect on the organisation. An example of a strategic risk is a product that is developed by the bank but not accepted by the market.
Operational Risk Operational risks occur because of inadequate or failed internal processes or controls, which lead to losses arising from business operations. Oper- ational risks arise from the day-to-day activities of the bank. Operational risk may arise due to technological failure. When you walk into a bank and the bank cannot serve you because its systems are down, that is a clear example of operational risk. Another source of operational risk is fraud perpetrated on the bank by its employees as well as other criminals. Employees may also make mistakes that are very costly to the bank. Because banks are service organisations, unfriendly staff may drive away clients and potential clients by their attitudes. Another example of operational risk is when the bankÆs premises catch fire.
Reputation Risk Banks rely very much on their reputation in conducting business. Adverse publicity may therefore damage the bankÆs reputation and reduce its income for several years or even lead to its failure. If a bank receives adverse publicity about the state of its financial health, it may experience a bank run, which will worsen its problems and lead to a possible collapse.

52	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

Legal and Compliance Risk Banks write several contracts every day. If these contracts are not well structured, it could lead to legal problems and losses being incurred on the part of the bank. The bank also faces the risk that it may not comply with regulations that pertain to it. Most banks will have a legal and compliance department to mitigate this risk.
Capital Risk The risks discussed above could all have a profound effect on a bankÆs equity capital. Therefore, bank executives need to constantly monitor and manage the bankÆs equity position. One of the factors they can look at is the capital adequacy ratio, which we looked at earlier. Banks are being pressured to hold more capital, especially in the light of a possible current financial crisis.



2.6.1 Managing Risk

Risks must be identified before they can be managed. The following is an outline of the risk management process:
ò Risk identificationùthis occurs when management tries to identify the risks that are peculiar to the bankÆs business.
ò Risk analysisùin analysing risk, management assesses the probability and impact of the risks identified. High probability and high impact risks certainly require more attention. Therefore, risk analysis helps to prioritise risk.
ò Risk planningùthis involves the actual management of risk. Thus, at this stage, a policy may be put in place to manage the risks identified based on their likelihood and impact.
ò Risk monitoringùthe business environment is very dynamic and fast changing, which requires that identified risks are properly monitored. Risk monitoring could lead to new risks being identified as well as to identifying the changing nature of already identified risks.
  Risk management aims at reducing the likelihood (probability) and the impact of identified risk. Risk is a normal part of the business of banking, and if banks do not take risks they cannot earn the returns that they desire. Entering into the banking business is in itself a risk since the bank may fail. What is important is that banks identify and adequately manage the risks that they are exposed to.
  Risk management must be given the consideration that it deserves. This may be done by including risk management within the systems and culture of the bank. This means that risk management is seen as an integral part of the bankÆs systems and controls and not as a separate activity. This also means that risk management is performed at all levels and not just at the managerial and board levels. This also means that all employees are made aware of the risks. To effectively manage risks, banks need not only design policies but they also need to implement them.
  There are several approaches that can be used to manage risk. We will look at the TARA model, which simply stands for:

2.7 Recent Developments and Trends in Banking	53

ò TùTransference
ò AùAcceptance
ò RùReduction
ò AùAvoidance
So how can the TARA model be used to manage risks? LetÆs have a go.
ò Tùtransferenceùfor risks with high impact but low likelihood; they can be transferred through insurance to a third party.
ò Aùavoidanceùfor risks with high likelihood and high impact; they are best avoided to prevent them from materialising.
ò Rùreductionùthis applies to risks with high likelihoods and low impact, for example operational risks. The best way to manage these risks is to reduce them through strengthening internal controls.
ò Aùacceptanceùapplies to risks with both low likelihoods and impact. A bank may decide to accept these risks without, for instance, taking insurance.
Quick Quiz
1. Identify and explain the different types of risks that banks face.
2. Explain the risk management process.
3. How can banks manage risks identified?



2.7 Recent Developments and Trends in Banking

The banking industry in Africa and elsewhere is constantly in a state of dynamic change and the factors responsible for the process of change include technological change, service proliferation, increasing competition, government deregulation, interest rate risk, consolidation and branch expansion and globalisation (see Rose and Hudgins 2013).


2.7.1 Technological Change

The high cost incurred by financial service firms has led to the automation and installation of electronic systems to replace the older, labour-intensive production and delivery system. From the banking customersÆ perspective, two of the practical purposes of banking are convenience (accessibility to funds) and account informa- tion. Technological advancements in financial-services delivery and production have made it more convenient and efficient for customers to access funds and information. The ongoing structural changes in the financial service industry can be attributed to electronics, automation and telecommunication and the changing internal production processes, product lines and the organisational structure of depository institutions.

54	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

2.7.2 Service Proliferation

It is common to find banks and other financial service firms expanding and increas- ing the number of services and products they offer to their customers. This has given customers more options in terms of the menu of financial services provided. It has also given banks the opportunity to earn more income.



2.7.3 Increasing Competition

The competitive environment within which banks operate has become intense. When banks were few, there was little need to go after customers by developing and marketing new products and services. However, in todayÆs competitive envi- ronment, product development and marketing are critical factors in the degree of success experienced by financial service firms. With the inherent potential of competition for customers, financial service firms will have to find alternative ways of increasing their efficiency to maintain their profit levels.



2.7.4 Government Deregulation

Deregulation involves the removal or relaxing of barriers to competition such as price or interest rate controls, product restrictions, organisational constraints, geo- graphic restrictions and tax regulations. Financial sector deregulation policies adopted two decades ago by many governments all over the world have resulted in an improved competition and an increased number of financial service providers. Deregulation has led to customersÆ ability to earn higher and flexible returns on their savings.



2.7.5 Interest Rate Risk

Since banks hold portfolios of financial assets and liabilities, their net asset values are sensitive to unanticipated changes in interest rates. The management of interest rate risk is thus one of the key tasks of banksÆ or financial service firmsÆ financial management, especially during a period of rising and volatile interest rates.

2.8 Globalisation and Impact of the Global Financial Crisis on African Banks	55

2.7.6 Consolidation and Branch Expansion

The technological innovations in the banking industry require banks or financial service firms to expand their services into new geographical areas in order to increase their revenue levels. It has been necessary therefore for these firms to establish more branches and increase bank branching activities.


2.7.7 Globalisation

Globalisation refers to the internationalisation of financial institutions and markets. In the past, only the largest banks entered foreign markets. Today, most banks of various kinds and sizes are venturing into international markets. We now see banks going beyond the boundaries of one country into foreign markets to look for lowest- cost sources of funds and for the most profitable investment opportunities. For example, major banks such as JP Morgan Chase in the USA, Barclays in the UK and Deutsche Bank in Germany have strong presence in the global marketplace. In Africa, we have witnessed banks such as Ecobank, Bank of Africa, Standard Bank of South Africa, Zenith Bank and Bank of Nigeria registering good cross-border presence, especially on the African continent. UBA Bank and GCB Bank headquartered in Ghana also have a presence in the UK.


2.8 Globalisation and Impact of the Global Financial Crisis on African Banks

It is also important to appreciate how globalisation and the global financial crisis have impacted banks in Africa. The African banking system has undergone a series of transformations over the past decades. Globalisation and other macro-economic issues and events persistently affect the financial system and serve as major causes of the banking sectorÆs transformation. In as much as these factors affect the perfor- mance of the banking sector in Africa, they also serve as major opportunities to the sector.
  Globalisation promotes the efficiency of the banking system through its associ- ated competition, formalised governance structures and the building of quality institutions. Globalisation is the assimilation of markets, cultures, economies, indus- tries and the act of policymaking around the world. In recent times, globalisation basically focuses on the development of the socio-economic environment through foreign direct investment, foreign capital inflow, international trade, political and cultural assimilation, technological transfer, etc. In the banking sector, globalisation is seen in the creation of subsidiaries of parent banks in other countries. Currently, there are a lot of foreign-owned banks across the various countries in Africa. These

56	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

banks help link the international financial market and also help expand the banking sector to facilitate the flow of funds from surplus and deficit units across the globe. In this case, liquidity challenges in some parts of the globe are remedied by funds from other parts of the globe. In the African financial market, the presence of foreign- owned banks has enormous positive effects on economic development and growth. This is seen in the development of sophisticated banking products and services. As such, there is an increased complexity and effective diversification of financial risks in the banking sector (Raluca 2012).
  Contrary to the above, globalisation of the financial system exposes banks to numerous risks. For instance, the financial system of a country is likely to be affected by crises in its trading partnersÆ economy. The global financial crisis affected most economies as a result of globalisation of the economic system. Hence, it has become imperative for banks to constantly review their credit management and risk man- agement strategies due to the dynamic nature of the financial system (Reinhart and Rogoff 2013).
  The global financial crisis of 2007û2008, which started in the USA, affected the performance and stability of the global banking sector. The consequential global economic recession affected the productivity of goods and services, which led to the accumulation of huge non-performing loans by banks, especially those in lower income countries. Additionally, the financial crises led to a decline in foreign remittances inflow and foreign direct investment, which were major sources of funds for most banks in Africa. In the commodities market, the prices of crude oil fell, leading to a decline in the export revenue and the inflow of capital to most oil-rich economies in Africa. Another means through which the global financial crisis affected the financial system was the crashing of most stock markets and as such this put a lot of pressure on the banking system for financing. With the underdeveloped nature of most stock markets in Africa, the banking system is seen as the backbone of the economy. With the collapse of most stock markets across the globe, coupled with currency crises and higher non-performing loans, the stability of most banks within most developing economies was affected. In order to remedy the situation, most banks in Africa refrained from investing in highly volatile assets from the USA as well as other parts of the world. This arguably helped limit the effect of the global financial crisis on most banks in Africa (Adamu 2009; Raluca 2012; Reinhart and Rogoff 2013).



2.9 Non-classical Banking Techniques

We will now discuss two important non-classical banking techniques that some banks seem to be focusing on. These include Islamic banking and microfinance.

2.9 Non-classical Banking Techniques	57

2.9.1 Islamic Banking

In recent times, Islamic banking has grown from a mere theory to a reality. Over the last two decades, Islamic banking has achieved a tremendous growth and expansion amidst the mismatch in the existing business operations and financial systems. It applies the principles of æshariaÆ (law of Islam) in its management and operations. The practice of Islamic banking has become one of the potential remedies to the worldÆs socio-economic demands. The motivation behind the finance and manage- ment of Islamic banks is Islamic economics. Islamic economics refers to the commercial jurisprudence of Islam, which is based on the teachings of Islam and takes a middle ground between capitalism and Marxism. In a practical sense, the sharia law prohibits the charging of interest (riba) on investing or lending activities. Hence, the conventional banking system, which is based on interest rate, is deemed unlawful in the perspective of Islam. It was therefore necessary to create the Islamic banking system in order to address the financial needs of the Islamic communities (Haiwad 2008; Hussain et al. 2015).
  Interest-based finance has been the dominant banking practice during the colonial period and even continued after independence and even in predominantly Muslim countries. However, based on the persistent research and writing of Muslim econ- omists on the effect of interest-free finance on the socio-economic development of the society, Islamic banking emerged. Per their analysis, the existing interest-rate- based financing stresses business growth and development and as such affects socio- economic development. Islamic banking and finance have been in existence since the 1990s and beyond, and it has become one of the major models of finance. This system of banking emerged, as a remedy to the challenges identified by Muslim philosophers in the existing global socio-economic system (Haiwad 2008; Hussain et al. 2015).


2.9.1.1 Principles of Islamic Banking

Islamic banking, as a system, serves as a form of reaction to the conventional banking system, which is forbidden by sharia law. Some common questionable forbidden practices of the conventional banking that motivated the creation of Islamic banking are interest-based transactions, absolute risk of outcome (Gherar), the winning of a party which is linked to the loss of the other (Myser and Qimar), financing for unlawful purposes (Haram) and the absence of the sharing of profit and loss. In order to be effective, Islamic banks work on the principle of justice. This forms the basic requirement of all manner of Islamic financing. Islamic banks do not charge interest on loans but rather share in the risk and profit of the projects they finance. According to sharia law, fair play is required in the sharing of profit on projects financed by Islamic banks. No specific rate of profit is required for the sharing of returns on projects financed by Islamic banks. Islamic banking is based on the principles of the QurÆan, which is the document of the word of God in the

58	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

perspective of Muslims, as revealed to Prophet Mohammed (Neienhaus 2011). These principles are outlined below:


Prohibition of Interest

The basic concept that distinguishes Islamic banks from conventional banks is the strict prohibition of charging and receiving interest. Interest is known in sharia law as riba or usury. This view is based on the negative implications of interest rates on the entire economy. When Riba infects an entire economy, it jeopardises the well-being and standard of living of everyone in that society. Also, the persistent interest of investors in interest rates and secured rates of returns rather than how money is used could have a negative impact on the economy.


Ethical Standards

Another key principle governing Islamic banks is the strict adherence to ethical standards. Muslims are very interested in the appropriateness and the legality of what they invest their money in. They also ensure that they do not invest in an activity that is forbidden in the QurÆan. For instance in the trading of shares, Muslims examine the activities of a firm in their investment decisions. They strictly examine the policies, products and services provided by an entity before the investment process is concluded. Additionally, the impact of the investment on the society and the entire environment is also considered.


Moral and Social Values

In addition to the maintenance of ethical principles, the moral and the values of the society guide the investment decisions of Islamic banks. The QurÆan implores Muslims to be interested in the needs of the weak, the destitute and the poor in society. In this regard, Islamic banks, as well as other Islamic financial institutions, are to design special products and services that meet the needs of the needy. This is not limited to a mere donation of charity but rather the granting of profit-free loans. For instance, an Islamic bank might consider granting profit-free loan to an individ- ual who needs money for medical attention or to further somebodyÆs education. These forms of loans are to be given for a maximum of a year.


Liability and Business Risk

The concept of fairness is also one of the basic principles governing Islamic banking. This principle is based on the idea that all parties involved in any transaction are to share in its risk and profitability. In order to benefit from the returns of an endeavour,

2.9 Non-classical Banking Techniques	59

Table 2.3 Balance sheet of an Islamic Bank

AssetsLiabilitiesCash and liquid securitiesInterbank MudÔrabahInterbank MudÔrabahDemand deposits (wakala, etc.)Inventory of real estate, vehicles, commodities, etc.Unrestricted profit sharing investment accounts such as MudÔrabahAsset-backed transactions such as MudÔrabah, ij?rah, istisnaÆ and salamRestricted profit sharing investment accountsFee-based services such as wakalah, kafalahReserves in the form of PER and IRREquity capital of shareholders
a party should either provide an asset as a form of service provision or share in the business risk. Failure to abide by this is sinful and makes a party an economic parasite. This principle is based on the saying æprofit comes with liabilityÆ.


2.9.1.2 The Islamic Banking Model

Theoretically, the Islamic banking model is made up of a two-tier concept: mudÔrabah and wakalah. MudÔrabah is explained as the contract between the bank and the investment account holder where the account holder provides capital and the bank acts as the manager of the funds in the account. Wakalah, on the other hand, is a contract between the bank and a client where the bank finances the client (entrepreneur) who is the business manager. In addition to these, profit equalising reserves (PER) and investment risk reserves (IRR) both serve as sources of funds to Islamic banking. Table 2.3 illustrates the balance sheet of a typical Islamic Bank.



2.9.2 Microfinance and Banking

For a time, banks have been known to offer financial services to the middle and upper classes. But this trend is gradually changing, as some banks are gradually entering into the microfinance space. This is particularly common in developing economies. Since all banks have a similar structure, it allows them to sell their services in the microfinance sector without any major adjustments, should they penetrate this sector. The fact that microfinance has been shown to be a profitable venture, by proponents like Muhammad Yunus and Joseph Blatchford, the concept is enticing to these banks. Already in the early 1990s, banks were seen venturing into the microfinance market in developing economies. This is because they saw that lending small amounts at shorter periods to a large number of low-income earners at reasonable interest rates that these borrowers could pay is profitable. Again, the banking sector itself has become saturated, and there are less innovation coupled with more competition, as most banks offer the same or similar kinds of services at

60	2  Introduction to the Business of Banking

almost the same rates. Microfinance, therefore, provides an avenue for these banks to be innovative in a new environment. Also, the deregulation of the financial system globally has sped up the process. NGOs, savings and loans and other non-bank financial institutions have been seen to operate and develop in the microfinance market and have graduated to become full-fledged banks.
  With global consensus for the need to reduce the poverty gap, banks entering the microfinance market are generally welcomed. The idea is that banks are in a better position to help reduce poverty among low-income earners. Banks ideally have a huge capital base and can therefore lend more than the conventional MFIs. They can provide these funds directly by setting up branches or indirectly by giving direct loans to MFIs, which in turn lend to low-income earners. It is expected that increase in competition in the microfinance environment will also drive down interest rates and cost of services. Additionally, banks, in order to meet their regulations, invest in infrastructural development, technological systems and human capacities to help them operate efficiently. Such infrastructure may not be readily available in existing MFIs, and therefore, if banks are brought into the microfinance space it can be of immense benefit to the banksÆ low-income clients. Again, as mentioned earlier, banks by nature and set-up offer retail banking services, a model, which is common to MFIs, hence banks can successfully get integrated into the microfinance environ- ment (Valenzuela et al. 1997; Young 2004; Abor 2017). Likewise, the integrity and the reputation of banks become an added advantage, as they are less prone to fraud and malpractices. However, it must be stated that these advantages, if not properly incorporated, could turn into a disadvantage. For instance, a bankÆs culture may not work well in the microfinance environment.
  It must also be added that some pro-poor international institutions encourage banks that enter into microfinance services because they envisage the banks are better positioned in tackling poverty issues and promoting financial independence of women, equity and human rights. In addition to financial support, these international institutions also provide technical and advisory support to these banks that enter the microfinance industry (Valenzuela et al. 1997; Young 2004).



2.10 Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we provided an overview of the African banking system and identified the products and services provided by banks as well as the sources and uses of banksÆ funds; we further described banking operations, identified the risks in banking and explained the changes taking place in the banking industry. We also discussed globalisation and the impact of the global financial crisis as well as non-classical banking techniques used/operated by banks. We learnt that:
1. The African banking system has gone through a number of changes from the period of state ownership of major banks and restrictive regulations to the era of

2.10 Summary and Conclusions	61

financial liberalisation, reforms, institutional upgrades and regulatory capacity. The banking system seems to be expanding as a result of cross-border banking.
2. Banks are financial intermediaries that accept deposits and give out loans. Financial intermediaries are firms that specialise in borrowing funds from savers and lending those funds to investors and others.
3. The banking system provides a number of functions, which include financial intermediation, enhancing payment systems and acting as agents of government policy.
4. Banks in Africa provide (the) different categories of banking products, including bank liability products, consumers assets products, trust services, guarantor services, international trade services and transactional products.
5. The sources of banksÆ funds include deposits, non-deposit borrowings and equity and retained earnings.
6. Banks put their funds into various uses, including holding cash, granting loans, investing in securities, lending on the inter-bank market, reverse repurchase agreements and acquiring fixed assets.
7. Banks accept deposits and also give out loans. The deposits received from depositors represent the bankÆs liability and the loans given out represent the bankÆs assets.
8. Banks are confronted with several risks. They have to identify and manage these risks; otherwise they may be taken unawares when these risks crystallise, leading to serious consequences. Some of these risks include credit risk, currency risk, interest rate risk, liquidity risk, strategic risk, operational risk, reputational, legal and compliance risk, etc.
9. The banking industry is constantly in a state of dynamic change and the factors responsible for the process of change include technological change, service proliferation, increasing competition, government deregulation, interest rate risk, consolidation and geographical expansion and globalisation.
  Two important non-classical banking techniques banks are focusing on are Islamic banking and microfinance. Islamic banking has achieved a tremendous growth and expansion amidst the mismatch in the existing business operations and financial systems. It applies the principles of æshariaÆ (law of Islam) in its manage- ment and operations. Banks in recent times are beginning to consider microfinance as a viable area of business.
