3.1 Introduction

We discussed in Chap. 2 that the business of banking involves banks acquiring deposits and other funds and lending them to borrowers as well as providing other services. Banks by their operations provide services and earn profit by charging their clients a fee for that service. Subject to both internal and external constraints, banks operate to maximise profits. They obtain funds from depositors at a cost and lend those funds to borrowers through the process of offering liquidity, risk sharing and information services. The difference between the interest that the bank earns from lending and the cost of acquiring the funds is the spread. In Africa, banks dominate the financial system and mostly engage in retail activities. Many African and developing countries have undergone financial sector reforms since the mid-1980s. These reforms, which were part of the IMF and World Bank Structural Adjustment Policies (SAP), generally had the aim of restructuring and privatising state-owned banks. In addition to these reforms were supplementary policies that reduced the entry and exit restrictions, eased interest and capital and also overhauled banking supervision and regulation.
  In this chapter, we provide an overview of the organisational structure of the banking industry with a particular focus on Africa and other developing countries. Specifically, the chapter looks into the internal organisation of banks, trends in the
® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019	65
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_3

66	3  Organisation and Structure of the Banking Industry

organisation of banks, the organisational structures of banking in Africa, different types of banks operating in developing countries and the functions and behaviour of cross-border banks.


3.2 Internal Organisation of Banks

Banks offer financial services to their customers to earn interest and to get fee incomes. Banking is dynamic; therefore, since the mid-1980s, many banks in many African countries have undergone several internal organisational restructurings to meet the competition and technological innovations. Organisational structure, as defined by Wilson and Rosenfeld (1990), is the recognised pattern of links between the constituent parts of an organisation, defining communications, controls and authority patterns. Banks, by their functions, are internally structured to suit their size and target customers. Banks whose activities are mainly oriented towards household and small business funds mobilisation and firms and individual loans are referred to as retail banking. In other words, retail banking or consumer banking, as it is also called, is the provision of financial or other related services by a bank to individual consumers as opposed to corporations or other banks. Unlike wholesale banks, whose activities are mainly centred on offering large loans at the corporate level, retail banks commit basically to markets for smaller, locally centred deposits and loans (Rose and Hudgins 2013). The success or otherwise of a bank providing these services depends on its organisational structure.
  A typical organisational structure of a community or commercial bank is depicted in Fig. 3.1. The structure is not complex. It is generally made up of four different blocks. The first is the executive (including the board of directors) who sees to specific business areas. The executives are normally in charge of specific business areas. They normally keep their attention on the local branches and offices at the local level. Another executive may also oversee consumer lending, which is mainly positioned to provide consumer loans. This executiveÆs activity is normally sepa- rated from commercial or corporate lending. Bank executives need to understand the complexities in monetary instruments in order to make sound decisions based on the bankÆs risk exposure.
  There is a separate chief among the executives in charge of wealth management, whose mandate is to give advice on tax, estate planning and investment. Normally, the bank will appoint a chief operating officer among the executives who will run the day-to-day operations within the bank. The executives work hand in hand with the board.
  Next to the executives are the directors, who closely manage business functions under the executives. The executives usually assign one senior leader to manage distribution of products and another to focus on the business development in community banking. Home loans (i.e. mortgage loan) and credit cards form part of consumer lending. Commercial lending will allocate some leaders purposefully for small start-ups and others to large corporate customers. However, wealth

3.2 Internal Organisation of Banks	67

Fig. 3.1 Organisational structure for commercial banks. Source: AuthorsÆ own compilation

management branches off into trust services and asset management. Directors are mandated to own and implement the strategic vision of their units. Although the directors determine the direction and the objectives of a business unit, they need managers for implementation. Usually, community banks have managers for areas like new accounts, branch operations and teller services. Functions such as credit management, direct consumer loans and home loan are allocated managers by the consumer lending department. The planning service under the wealth management division is further divided into specific departments such as securities, mutual funds and commodities, as pertaining to South Africa. Commercial banks also have front liners who are often the face of the bank to the clients and potential employees alike. Like any other industry, banks can boast of operational staff. This may include but is not limited to human resource, customer service and sales staff. Community banks, by their operations, may offer a broader range of direct consumer services and therefore have more front-line staff, like tellers, personal bankers and courier services.

3.3 Developments in Bank Organisation

Deregulation and financial liberalisation, privatisation, mergers and acquisitions (M&As), foreign banksÆ entry and various trends have shaped the current state of banking structures in African and other developing countries.
  Previous banking regulations in developing economies were characterised by restrictions on borrowing, interest rates, foreign entry and high reserve requirements. Over the years, global, economic and technological pressures have resulted in the relaxation of such strict supervision and have caused authorities to deregulate the banking sector. This has led to banks being more market oriented and has expanded the financial markets, increased competition and enhanced consumer welfare and economic growth.
  Furthermore, some institutions such as banks, utilities, telecommunications and transport were considered too dire to be left in the hands of individuals. As such, these services were provided by the state only until the late 1990s. In most African and developing countries, state-owned enterprises suffer from poor management and underperformance and as such hinder economic development. Therefore, the privatisation of these organisations and especially banks helped restore stability in the economy. Deregulation and financial liberalisation coupled with privatisation of previously state-owned banks have led to an exponential increase in competition. The result of the increase in competition is more mergers and acquisitions in the banking sector.
  Cross-border activity typically characterises mergers and acquisitions. Over the years, Africa and other developing countries have seen an influx of foreign banks while domestic banks have diversified into foreign markets. While this comes with attendant regulatory risks, cross-border banks are able to take advantage of their diversification to protect them against unsystematic risks and to benefit from scale economies.



3.4 Organisational Structures in Banking

Business organisations, including banks all over the world, must be directed and controlled in such a way as to protect the interest of all interested parties or stakeholders. Being extremely pivotal in world economies, banks must be structured in a way that facilitates the protection of such stakeholders. Furthermore, Africa and developing countries are rife with unsophisticated customers and, as such, the organisational structure of banks serves to increase credibility of banks and the financial system as a whole. Banks in Africa and other developing countries gener- ally share similar organisational structures with those of developed countries.
  The board of directors are typically responsible for formulating the strategy of the bank, setting the strategic direction and implementing the strategies to ensure that objectives are met. The board also monitors management and helps align the interest

3.5 Different Types of Banks Operating in Developing Countries	69

of management with that of shareholders. An effective board is crucial to mitigate the agency problem that exists in the bank. The most common type of board is the unitary board, comprising both executive and non-executive directors and that make decisions as a united body. Another board structure is the two-tier board. These have one supervisory board, which serve to include the interests of various stakeholders. It is made up exclusively of non-executive directors and headed by the board chair- man. The management board, which consists of executive directors, mainly addresses the major operational matters of the bank and is led by the chief executive officer. In addition, the board operates in committees to address various areas such as remuneration, nomination, audit, assets and liabilities, risk and compliance, corpo- rate social responsibility and so on. Some of these committees are required by corporate governance codes or listing requirements.
  Managers or heads of departments are directly under the board of directors. While the board sets the strategic direction of the bank, managers champion its implemen- tation. These managers head the different departments such as corporate banking, finance, human resources, credit, operations, information technology, branches and many more and guide them to ensure that objectives and targets are met.
  Banks in developing countries, like their more developed counterparts, provide employment to a large section of the population. These employees represent the bank to customers, and potential employees may include administrative staff, cus- tomer service representatives, tellers, drivers, accountants and many more.



3.5 Different Types of Banks Operating in Developing Countries

Different types of banks exist to serve the different needs or varying demands of consumers in developing countries. Central banks, retail banks, commercial banks, investment banks and universal banks serve these developing economies. At times, the differences between the various banks are not clear-cut, as one bank may offer the services of more than one type of bank.
  The central bank in every country is established to supervise the banking sector of the economy and to ensure that the monetary system functions effectively. This role is especially necessary in developing countries, as these require higher levels of regulation and supervision. The central bank serves as a banker to other banks and manages the economy through the monetary policy, which is used to control money supply.
  Retail banks are the most common type of banking organisation in Africa and developing countries. Individual consumers are the main focus of retail banks. Every day banking needs such as current accounts, savings accounts, debit and credit cards and loans are examples of services offered by retail banks to the general public. These services provide individuals in developing countries with the support they need to effectively and efficiently manage their finances.

70	3  Organisation and Structure of the Banking Industry

  Similar to retail banks are commercial banks. The major difference is that commercial banks usually cater to the needs of businesses. In addition to the services that individuals require, businesses operate using larger sums of money and are often involved in more complex transactions. In developing countries, it is necessary that these businesses obtain the requisite support in order to help drive the economy forward. As has been mentioned earlier, the distinctions between the various types of banks are not always clear, and in developing countries, commercial banks focus on providing banking services to both individuals and business organisations.
  Investment banks provide businesses with assistance in the financial markets. Africa and other developing countries are characterised by growing capital markets to facilitate the flow of surplus funds to deficit units. There is therefore the need for financial institutions to specialise in the raising of these funds and in providing advice to the relatively unsophisticated investors found in developing countries. Investment banks are typically involved in mergers and acquisitions, corporate finance, investment management and compliance among other services. Merchant banks are similar to investment banks, but focus on helping companies that may not be large enough to raise funds through initial public offerings.
  Universal banks are entities that combine several financial services, including commercial and investment banking services. Universal banking provides banks with the opportunity to diversify their operations and minimise their risk. While the universal bank may benefit from the synergy to be gained from engaging in various services, there is an increase in regulation risk, since all the regulations applicable to banks providing a single service will have to be complied with.
  Development banks are also included in the types of banks that finance develop- ment and economic activities in developing countries. These financial institutions are dedicated to providing funds for development of particular business sectors and economic undertakings. Such banks are especially necessary in developing countries where investment capital is relatively scarce.


3.6 Functions and Behaviour of Cross-Border Banks

A cross-border bank is one with business presence extending beyond its home jurisdiction in the form of a branch or subsidiary. Foreign-owned cross-border banks are those controlled by a shareholder or group of shareholders outside the authorising jurisdiction. The result is that cross-border banks are subject to the various regulations applicable in the countries in which they are present.
  Cross-border banks perform the same functions as banks that only operate in one jurisdiction. In that, they accept deposits, advance loans and overdraft facilities, discount bills of exchange, facilitate payments and so on. Having an international presence enhances cross-border banksÆ ability to provide international services to individual consumers and business organisations. By diversifying into different geographical areas, cross-border banks are able to take advantage of the different risk profiles of developing countries and hence are better hedged against shocks and

References	71

failure. They, thus, contribute to the stability of the banking systems in developing countries. The entry of foreign banks into Africa and developing countries brings with them increased competition, introduction of new technologies and other inno- vations, improves efficiency and elevates the quality of the financial system.

The chapter presents an overview of the organisational structure of the banking industry with a particular focus on Africa and other developing countries. 2. Deregulation and financial liberalisation, privatisation, M&As, foreign banksÆ entry and various recent trends have shaped the current state of banking structures in developing countries.
3. Banks in developing countries share similar organisational structures with those of developed countries.
4. Different types of banks exist to serve the different needs or varying demands of consumers in developing countries.
5. A cross-border bank is one with its business presence extending beyond its home jurisdiction in the form of a branch or subsidiary.

Chapter 4
Determination of Interest Rates

4.1 Introduction

Understanding interest rates is crucial to our general appreciation of the banking sector. This is because interest rates are an important macroeconomic variable that affects economic activities and have a direct impact on the banking industry. They also affect individualsÆ and householdsÆ decisions regarding whether or not to defer present consumption by saving and whether to invest in short-term or long-term financial assets. The business decisions of firms are also affected in the sense that they would have to ascertain whether to invest available funds in an expansion project or place such funds in financial instruments. Also, governmentsÆ borrowing from the banking sector has huge implications for interest rates. In many African countries where governments tend to crowd out the market by issuing treasury bills to borrow from the money market, this drives interest rates up, as banks will opt to lend to government.
  In this chapter, we discuss interest rates by focusing on the theories of interest rates and the factors affecting interest rates. We also explain the risk and term structure of interest rates.
® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019	73
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_4

74	4  Determination of Interest Rates

4.2 Theories of Interest Rates

Interest rate is the price paid for borrowed funds for a specified time period. The loan amount provided by the bank or lending institution constitutes the principal, and the additional amount paid by the borrower to the lending institution is the interest amount. Interest rate is typically expressed as a percentage of the principal or remaining balance of the loan. It is sometimes referred to as the price of credit. There are a number of theories that tend to explain interest rates, and these include FisherÆs classical theory, the loanable funds theory, KeynesÆ liquidity preference theory and McKinnonÆs complementarity theory.


4.2.1 FisherÆs Classical Theory

This theory, which was proposed by Irving Fisher, suggests that the level of the interest rate in an economy is determined by two forcesùthe supply of funds through savings, mainly by household, and the demand for funds for investment by business firms. The interaction of the supply of funds and the demand for funds determines the equilibrium interest rate (Madura 2014).
  The supply of funds is influenced by the marginal rate of time preference, the level of income and the reward for savings (Madura 2014; Abor 2018).
Marginal Rate of Time Preference IndividualsÆ time preference affects their sav- ings pattern, which again influences the supply of funds. For example, if you want to buy a house 10 years from now, you would probably defer your consumption for the next 10 years to meet your financial target in 10 yearsÆ time. On the other hand, if you do not have any pressing need to save for, you would probably spend all you earn. Therefore, how much you decide to save or forgo current consumption is dependent on your preference to spend now or later, which is properly termed as the marginal rate of time preference. A more technical definition for the marginal rate of time preference is putting aside todayÆs spending for the future, or delaying current consumption for the future. When more people save, there will be more funds for others to borrow, and as supply exceeds demand, all things being equal, the equilibrium interest rate falls. On the other hand, when people save less, there will be less supply of funds. So, when the supply of funds falls short of the demand for funds, the equilibrium interest rate rises, all things being equal.
Level of Income Income refers to how much a person earns from a paying job or activity. Income generally is linked with a personÆs purchasing power. If people earn more, they will probably save more. If they earn less, they are likely to save less. Therefore, a higher income level is in itself an incentive to increase savings. But a lower level of income will discourage savings.
Reward for Saving Individuals, as rational human beings, tend to save more when interest rates provide enough incentive for them to do so. When interest rates are

4.2 Theories of Interest Rates	75

high enough, individuals would rather defer present consumption and save to take advantage of or seek benefit from the high interest rates. Therefore, high interest rates will lead to more savings, while reduced interest rates suggest low savings.
  With respect to the demand for funds by business firms, this is influenced by the marginal productivity of capital and the rate of interest (Madura 2014; Abor 2018).
Marginal Productivity of Capital If borrowing firms can be more productive with additional funds, all things being equal, they would demand more funds. This is termed as the marginal productivity of capital. If firms were able to make more profits from extra funds borrowed, then they would borrow more. However, if by borrowing more, the firms end up undertaking projects that result in losses, then it would not make economic sense for them to borrow. So, as a rule of thumb, firms will only invest if their marginal productivity of capital exceeds their cost of capital. Immediately their cost of capital exceeds the marginal productivity of capital, the firms will make losses, and therefore as a rational economic unit, it would not borrow under such a situation. Therefore, the higher marginal productivity of capital, the higher the demand for funds. The reverse is also true.
Rate of Interest The level of the interest rate is a major determinant of the demand for funds. While savers want higher interest rates, borrowers prefer lower interest rates so they can pay less on the amount borrowed. A higher interest rate is associated with higher cost of capital, which discourages business firms from borrowing and vice versa.
  We have so far seen that savers and borrowers are on opposite sides of the spectrum. With high interest rates, savers save more, while borrowers borrow less. When the tides turn and interest rates are low, savers will rather save less, while borrowers borrow more to take advantage of the low rates. Supply of funds (savings) represents the upward sloping supply side, while demand for funds (borrowings) represents the downward sloping demand side. The meeting point of the upward sloping supply curve and the downward sloping demand curve is the equilibrium interest rate.
  Irving Fisher also distinguished between a nominal interest rate and a real interest rate. The nominal interest rate is the rate quoted on a loan, a savings account, bonds and others. It represents how much is earned or paid at the end of the period of the investment or loan. We know at this stage that inflation always consumes part of an individualÆs purchasing power; therefore, the real interest rate is the nominal interest rate after adjusting for inflation. It means that when the inflation factor is removed from the nominal interest rate, what is left is the real interest rate. The real interest rate is the nominal interest rate minus inflation. It measures the rate at which oneÆs purchasing power of an investment increases, while inflation reduces oneÆs purchas- ing power. So, while the nominal interest rate measures how fast your investment appreciates over time, the real interest rate measures how fast your purchasing power appreciates over time. Therefore, the higher the level of inflation relative to the nominal interest rate, the lower the real interest rate, hence the lower the purchasing power.

76	4  Determination of Interest Rates

  To understand what purchasing power means, let us consider this simple illus- tration. Assume Axelia earns US$1000 per month. With this money, she is able to buy 20 bags of millet at a price of US$50 per bag. Let us assume Axelia invests in treasury bills with her salary at 10% per annum. At the end of the year, Axelia will
have US$1100 (US$1000 Î 1.10). Now, with US$1100, she can now buy 22 bags of millet. Furthermore, assume the price of a bag of millet increases to US$54. At this
new price pegged at US$54, Axelia can now buy only 20.37 bags of millet (US
$1100/US$54) instead of the 22 bags she could buy before the price increase. Note that due to an increase in the price of the millet (inflation), AxeliaÆs purchasing power has reduced. Even though her money had increased from US$1000 to US
$1100 due to the investment in the treasury bills, her purchasing power had reduced to 20.4 bags of millet. The reason for this is inflation (i.e. the increase in the price of a bag of millet). Though her money had increased by 10%, the price of a bag of millet had also increased by 8% (i.e. increased from US$50 to US$54).
  It stands to reason that when the inflation rate is zero, all things being equal, the nominal interest rate would be equal to the real interest rate. Thus, in the absence of inflation, Axelia would have been able to buy 22 bags of millet with the US$1100. However, in the presence of inflation, the nominal interest rate will be higher than the real interest rate. The reasoning here is that savers or suppliers of funds will demand a premium higher than the real interest rate as a way of compensating them for the expected loss in purchasing power of their ready money (interest income and principal amount) arising from inflation.
  Irving Fisher came up with a way of calculating the real and the nominal interest rate based on the observed relationship between the two variables. According to him, the nominal rate is equivalent to the sum of the real and the inflation rate. The reason being that the investors would require a rate higher than the inflation rate to save. If the rate were lower, they would be making losses even though their monies would grow, as their purchasing power would fall. The nominal rate has to be higher, not only to offset the inflation rate but also to provide a premium to investors for deferring present consumption. This is expressed in an equation, popularly known as the Fisher equation. This is given as follows:
(1 + i) = (1 + r) Î (1 + p)	(4.1)
where
i = the nominal interest rate
r = the real interest rate
p = the inflation rate (or expected price change in the level of goods and services)
Using AxeliaÆs case to compute the real rate, we have:
(1 + i) = (1 + r) Î (1 + p)

r = (1.10) ù 1
(1.08)
r = 1.0185 ù 1
r = 0.0185 = 1.85%
This explains why Axelia can buy 20.37 (20 Î 1.0185) bags of millet. An approximation definition of the Fisher equation is given as:
i = r + p r = i ù p
Applying the approximation formula in AxeliaÆs case, we have:
r = 0.10 ù 0.08 = 2%
  FisherÆs theory is criticised on the grounds that governmentÆs demand for loan- able funds may be independent of the interest rate. For instance, the government can demand loanable funds by issuing treasury bills, but these may not be dependent on the prevailing interest rates. Also, individuals and business firms may hold cash balances as a precaution against unforeseen circumstances and therefore the desire to hold money is not dependent on the interest rate.



4.2.2 The Loanable Funds Theory

The loanable funds theory, which is commonly used among practitioners, was introduced to overcome the limitations with FisherÆs theory. This theory goes beyond FisherÆs theory. It considers the role of monetary factors and incorporates the demand and supply of money in the analysis of interest rate determination. The modelÆs concentration is on the flow of demand and supply of loanable funds,
i.e. new bonds. The loanable funds theory suggests that the market interest rate is determined by the interaction of demand for loanable funds and supply of loanable funds. The demand for loanable funds consists of borrowing in the domestic market by households, businesses and governments as well as by borrowing in the domestic market by foreign entities. The supply of loanable funds is also made up of domestic savings (by households, firms and governments), lending in the domestic market by

78	4  Determination of Interest Rates

foreign entities, negative hoarding of money balances (dishoarding) and credit creation by banks. Negative hoarding or dishoarding occurs when the public demand for money is less than the money supply in the economy. The opposite is termed positive hoarding (Rose and Marquis 2009).
  Households typically demand loanable funds to finance the purchase of goods and services and other household durable items. HouseholdsÆ demand for loanable funds may not exactly respond to changes in interest rates, as the demand for loanable funds is mostly considered interest rate inelastic. A number of factors could influence householdsÆ demand for loanable funds, and this would shift the demand curve. For instance, with a reduction in personal income tax by govern- ments, all things being equal, peopleÆs disposable income will increase. This increase means households can demand more loanable funds and the demand curve will then shift to the right. When income taxes are high, the effect is reversed, and the demand curve moves to the left. Households are also suppliers of loanable funds in the financial market. They are usually net suppliers of loanable funds through their savings, because their aggregate savings often exceed their aggregate borrowing.
  Business firms also demand loanable funds to grow their businesses. The demand for loanable funds by firms is generally more responsive to changes in interest rate than householdsÆ demand for loanable funds. When interest rates are low, businesses will demand more of the loanable funds for investments in their businesses. Low interest rates also translate into more positive net present value (NPV) projects, which suggests that there will be more projects that businesses can undertake. High interest rates reduce the returns on projects firms can undertake, and if the returns fall short of the cost of funds, it renders the project completely unprofitable. There is an inverse relationship between demand for loanable funds and interest rates, meaning: with a decline in interest rates, the demand curve shifts outward to the right. The opposite is a truism. Though business firms may also supply loanable funds in the financial market, they are often regarded as net demanders, as they demand more loanable funds than they supply in the market.
  Governments also demand loanable funds to finance their budget deficit. Usually when the government spends more than the revenue it generates, it goes to the financial market to raise more loanable funds. For instance, governments of many African countries have been active in the financial market through issuing treasury bills and bonds to acquire the necessary funding to finance their budget deficit. A governmentÆs demand for loanable funds is not significantly influenced by the prevailing interest rates and may, thus, be considered interest rate inelastic. Govern- ment may also have a budget surplus, in that case it generates more revenue than it spends and therefore may be in a position to supply funds in the financial market. In general terms, governments usually experience a budget deficit and therefore are considered net demanders of loanable funds.
  Foreign entities also demand loanable funds from the domestic financial market. For example, the government of a foreign country may issue securities to attract funds from the domestic market. Let us say, if the Turkish government acquires funds by issuing treasury notes to Ghanaian investors and places them in the

4.2 Theories of Interest Rates	79

Ghanaian financial market, this will represent the Turkish governmentÆs demand for Ghanaian funds. In recent years, many foreign entities such as multinationals and foreign banks have also entered the African market to raise funds from local investors. Foreign entitiesÆ demand for domestic funds is dependent on the spread between the interest rates in the domestic market and the interest rates in the foreign market, holding the exchange rate constant. If interest rates in the domestic market fall relative to interest rates in foreign markets, foreign borrowers will demand more loanable funds from the domestic market than in the foreign markets. In such a situation, domestic suppliers of funds will lend to entities in the foreign markets and reduce their supply of funds to domestic borrowers in the domestic market. On the other hand, if the domestic interest rates inch up, foreign borrowers will reduce their demand for funds from the domestic market. Such foreign borrowers would rather borrow from the foreign markets. At the same time, foreign suppliers of funds will increase their supply of funds in the domestic market by investing in securities issued by domestic borrowers. In many African countries such as Ghana, we often see non-resident holdings of government securities increasing when interest rates in Ghana increase.
  Now, the demand curves of the various economic units can be aggregated into the aggregate demand curve. The aggregate demand curve consists of the summation of the demand for loanable funds by households, business firms, government and foreign entities. The aggregate supply curve is also the summation of the supply of loanable funds by households, business firms, government and foreign entities, as well as the negative hoarding (dishoarding) and credit creation by banks.
  The aggregate equilibrium is the point at which the aggregate demand curve and the aggregate supply curve meet. At that point, the aggregate demand is equal to the aggregate supply. If the interest rate rises above the equilibrium, the amount of loanable funds supplied by economic agents will exceed the amount of loanable funds demanded and this will drive the interest rate down till it gets to the equilib- rium interest rate again. On the other hand, if the interest rate falls below the equilibrium, total demand of loanable funds will exceed the total supply of loanable funds and this will move the interest rate up until it moves back to the equilibrium interest rate. This interaction of demand and supply allows for market participants to set the equilibrium interest rate, which is the rate at which aggregate demand for loanable funds is equal to aggregate supply loanable funds.


4.2.3 KeynesÆ Liquidity Preference of Theory

The liquidity preference theory, also known as the cash balances theory of interest rates, is credited to the economist John Maynard Keynes. The theory, which is considered more relevant for policymakers, examines the equilibrium interest rates based on the interaction of the publicÆs aggregate demand for holding money and the supply of money. Keynes explained that people hold their wealth in money or cash balances (including demand deposits) or in bonds. Individual and firms will hold

80	4  Determination of Interest Rates

money to take care of their daily transactions and also for precautionary purpose, even when the rate of return is low. Investors in bonds also hold money as a way of cushioning themselves against declining asset prices. With low interest rates, people prefer to hold more money because it is more favourable for them to do so (and the opportunity cost of holding money is low). Further, holding money is advantageous because an increase in interest rates will cause investors to incur losses on any bonds that they purchase. However, with high interest rates, the opportunity cost of holding money is higher. Consequently, investors are more likely to hold bonds. Further, a reduction in interest rates will be beneficial since it would cause the prices of their bonds to increase.
  Though bonds pay interest, they are associated with low liquidity. If interest rates rise, bond prices reduce, and the bond investors may incur capital loss if the bonds are liquidated or sold on the secondary market. Hence it would be better for them to keep their money in cash. On the flip side, if interest rates decline, bond prices will rise, resulting in capital gains for the bondholder. Therefore, it is more favourable to hold bonds rather than hold cash.
  Based on the liquidity preference theory, the publicÆs demand for money has three different motives (as discussed in Chap. 1), and these include the transactions motive, the precautionary motive and the speculative motive. The transaction motive for holding money involves the demand for money or cash balances to undertake daily transactions. The precautionary motive for holding money represents the need for taking care of future uncertainties. The speculative motive involves holding money to take advantage of investment opportunities in the future. The aggregate demand for money or cash balances in the economy is therefore the summation of the transaction, precautionary and speculative demands. According to the liquidity preference theory, the supply of money is under the control of government and is not influenced by the level of interest rates. Therefore, to Keynes, the supply curve will be inelastic. The interaction of the aggregate demand for money and the supply of money will determine the equilibrium interest rate, at least in the short run.


4.2.4 McKinnonÆs Complementarity Theory

The development of McKinnonÆs (1973) theory is based on the constraint of internal financing to capital formation in developing countries and that high deposit rates encourage savings for financing physical capital. McKinnon (1973) suggests, æmoney and investment are complementary due to a self-financed investment, and that a real deposit rate is the key determinant of capital formation for financially constrained developing economiesÆ. He explains that that demand for real money (savings) is directly dependent on the average real return on capital, and the ratio of investment to GDP increases with an increase in a real deposit rate. It stands to reason that investment has a positive relationship with deposit rates.
  The model for self-financed capital formation for financially constrained devel- oping economies by McKinnon (1973) is given as:

4.3 Economic Factors Affecting Interest Rates	81

M/P = ?[Y, Rc, Rd ù ?e]	(4.2)
where
M/P = demand for real money, which is a function of real income, Y Rd = deposit rate
?e = expected rate of inflation (so Rd ù ?e represents real deposit interest rate)
Rc = real average returns on capital
  The equation makes two important assumptions: (1) the demand for money is allocated to finance capital formation in economies, which have constrained bank credit; and (2) there is a need to hedge against inflation so that the real value of savings can be preserved (McKinnon 1973).
  The theory was criticised by Shaw (1973), who held the view that debt finance was rather the relevant constraint and not self-finance, though they both agreed that high interest rates could encourage savings and stimulate investment in developing countries.
  The theory, which was developed for developing economies, which are consid- ered financially constrained, assumes that increasing deposit rates will enhance capital formation to promote investments. It is seen as an important theory that explains financing conditions in developing economies. However, others like Sayinzoga et al. (2014) and Allen et al. (2014) have argued that this position may not hold in Sub-Saharan African, which is characterised by low levels of financial inclusion. With a high percentage of the population operating outside the formal financial system, raising deposit rates may not result in increase in savings.
Quick Quiz
1. What is interest rate? Distinguish between nominal interest rate and real interest rate.
2. Explain the Fisher equation.
3. Briefly explain the loanable funds theory, KeynesÆ liquidity preference of theory and the McKinnonÆs complementarity theory.


4.3 Economic Factors Affecting Interest Rates

We mentioned that interest rates are determined by the interaction of demand and supply of loanable funds. Changes in the demand and supply of funds are affected by changes in economic factors, including inflation, economic growth, monetary pol- icy, fiscal policy and external flow of funds (Madura 2014; Abor 2018). In this section, we discuss each of these factors.

82	4  Determination of Interest Rates

4.3.1 Inflation and Interest Rates

Inflation is one major problem that confronts economies in the world. It is the persistent rise in the general prices of all goods and services in the economy. Though prices of some goods and services may be rising, while others may be falling in an economy, inflation is said to occur when there is an increase in the average level of prices of all goods and services in the economy. Inflation expectations (i.e. the rate of inflation economic agents believe will exist in the future and therefore will consider in making their decisions) tend to influence the level of spending of economic agents, which affects the supply of loanable funds, thus influencing the equilibrium interest rate. When households and firms expect prices to rise, they tend to spend more in order to take advantage of lower prices now. Increased spending reduces savings and low savings mean that there will be low supply of loanable funds, therefore creating a deficit. On the demand side, economic agents will want to borrow more to be able to spend now in order to avoid the expected increase in prices. Therefore, demand for loanable funds will exceed supply, all things being equal, causing interest rates to rise. The opposite is also true. If economic agents expect prices to fall, they will spend less in the interim and wait for prices to fall so they can spend more in the future. The behaviour of less spending will lead to more savings, leading to increased supply of loanable funds. When supply of funds exceeds demand of funds, it will drive interest rates down. Also, on the demand side, since economic agents are spending less now, their demand for loanable funds will fall and that will also cause interest rates to decline.



4.3.2 Economic Growth and Interest Rates

When economic conditions change, the demand and supply of funds are affected, causing a change in the equilibrium interest rate. In an economic boom, where there is growth through industrialisation, there will be increase in demand for funds for investment projects. The increased aggregate demand for loans will lead to a rise in interest rates. On the other hand, when the economy goes bust, growth slows down and not much investment can take place. The effect is that there will be less demand for loans and that will drive down interest rates. This interaction of demand and supply determines the equilibrium interest rate. Changes in economic conditions may also affect the supply of funds, but it may be difficult to ascertain how the supply of funds will change. Usually, the shift in the demand curve is more than the shift in the supply curve, meaning demand for loanable funds usually increases more than supply. It is important to note that economic expansion could lead to enhanced standards of living and improved wages, which could lead to more savings. On the other hand, if the economy were suffering, there would be less demand for goods and services, which will lead to less projects being undertaken, resulting in less demand for loanable funds. The result of less demand for loanable funds is a fall in interest

4.3 Economic Factors Affecting Interest Rates	83

rates. We could say from the relationship observed between economic growth and interest rate that they are positively correlated. It stands to reason that when the economy thrives, interest rates rise and vice versa.



4.3.3 Monetary Policy and Interest Rates

Monetary policy involves the actions taken by the central bank or the reserve bank to influence money supply and interest rates to achieve macroeconomic objectives such as price stability, exchange rate stability, growth and employment. The central bank can influence the supply of funds through its monetary policy by changing the reserve requirement or by using open market operations. The central bank uses expansionary or contractionary monetary policies to control the supply of money in the economy so as to avoid a recession or an inflationary gap. When the central bank uses contractionary policies to reduce the money in circulation, this reduces the amount of loanable funds available to deficit economic units. This may involve increasing the reserve requirement for banks or selling bills to the public through (the) open market operations. When the supply of loanable funds reduces, interest rates rise, all things being equal. On the other hand, when the central bank embarks on an expansionary policy, it increases the money supply in the economy. This may also involve reducing the reserve requirement or buying back bills from the public. Increase in money supply translates into increased supply of loanable funds in the economy, which leads to a fall in interest rates.



4.3.4 Fiscal Policy and Interest Rates

Fiscal policy entails using government revenue and expenditure to influence eco- nomic conditions. Whenever revenue exceeds the expenditure, then we have a surplus budget, while a deficit budget occurs when revenue falls short of expendi- ture. A deficit budget requires that government find ways of financing the deficit. In many African countries, government usually goes to the local financial market to borrow by demanding more loanable funds through the issue of treasury bills and bonds. The increase in the demand for loanable funds pushes interest rates upwards. GovernmentsÆ demand for loanable funds tends to æcrowd outÆ the private sector, limiting how much funds the private sector can borrow from the financial market. Lenders generally prefer lending to the government compared to other investors because government is considered a default-free borrower. On the whole, govern- mentsÆ increased borrowing on the local financial market increases the demand of loanable funds, leading to increase in interest rates.

84	4  Determination of Interest Rates

4.3.5 External Flow of Funds and Interest Rates

The interest rate for the currency of any country is determined by the demand for funds in that countryÆs currency and the supply of funds in that currency. When there is an increase in the inflow of foreign or external funds into a local economy, it increases the supply of loanable funds in that economy, and this in effect reduces interest rates in the local economy. External flow of funds may come in the form of portfolio investments, foreign direct investments and remittances, and all these have the tendency to increase the supply of loanable funds in the receiving country, which then tend to have an impact on the level of interest rates in that country. In the foreign country where the funds are coming from, its supply of loanable funds is going to dwindle, thus leading to an increase in the interest rate in that foreign country. Foreign investors will typically invest in markets or countries with relatively higher interest rates in order to benefit from such high rates. However, if the currencies of these receiving countries depreciate, then the interest rate advantage to the investor may be offset by the depreciation of the currencies of these countries. We have seen in recent times a number of foreign portfolio investors place their funds in African countries with fairly stable exchange rates promising high interest rates on certain government debt instruments. This has resulted in an increase in the supply of loanable funds in these African countries, thus driving down interest rates.
Quick Quiz
1. How are interest rates determined?
2. Identify the economic factors that affect interest rates.




4.4 Risk Structure of Interest Rates

The risk structure of interest rates refers to the relationship that exists among interest rates of securities or bonds that have the same maturity. It depicts the interest rate behaviour of securities with the same maturity. The various types of bonds have different interest rates in any particular year, and the difference (spread) between these interest rates tends to vary over time. For instance, the interest rate on a 5-year corporate bond is likely to lower than the interest rate on a 5-year government bond, and the spread between these rates may vary depending on the period. During periods of economic downturns, the spread is likely to be wider than in periods of steady growth. The question we ask is: why do interest rates on securities or financial assets with the same maturity period vary? The risk structure of interest rates can be explained by three important factors: the default or credit risk, liquidity and income tax status (Mishkin 2013; Cecchetti and Schoenholtz 2014).

4.4 Risk Structure of Interest Rates	85

4.4.1 Default Risk

Default is a major risk that investors face and it refers to the risk that the bond issuer may be unable to make timely payments of principal and/or interest obligations. Issuers of bonds have different default risk profiles. A firm that has suffered major losses is likely to default on honouring its debt obligations in terms of payments of the bond principal and/or the interests to investors. Therefore, the default risk for this firm will be high. Government bonds are, however, considered to have no or zero default risk (also known as default-free bonds). This is because government is always in the position to raise tax revenue to honour its debt obligations. Investors are willing to invest in risky bonds provided the risk premium is adequate to compensate them for that level of risk. The risk premium is the spread or difference between interest rates on bonds with default risk and the default-free bonds with the same maturity. The risk premium is a function of the entity-specific factors and macroeconomic factors.
  Considering the importance of default risk to potential investors, independent rating institutions have been established to provide ratings for government and corporate bonds. They generally provide an evaluation of the creditworthiness of borrowers or issuers of securities. Examples of rating institutions are MoodyÆs Investors Service, Standard & PoorÆs, Fitch Investors Service and Duld and Phelps Credit Rating Co. A higher rating suggests a better credit rating, and the issuer or borrower is more creditworthy (therefore a lower credit risk), and a lower rating represents a relatively worse credit rating. The ratings typically range from A to
D. Bonds that are rated as Baa or better by MoodyÆs or BBB or better by Standard & PoorÆs are termed investment grade bonds and those with ratings below investment grade are known as junk bonds; they offer a high yield and are associated with high default risk.
  Overall, less creditworthy issuers of bonds or securities have higher default risk and lower credit ratings. Therefore, investors assuming the risk of investing in such risky securities must be compensated in the form of higher yield. The risk premium is generally correlated with the ratings. All else being equal, a lower rating will generate higher risk premium and therefore a higher interest rate and vice versa.



4.4.2 Liquidity

Liquidity is another factor that affects interest rates. Liquidity refers to how quick an asset or security can be converted into cash without losing value. Investors generally prefer to hold more liquid assets than to hold less liquid ones. Generally, a good secondary market facilitates liquidity of securities and thus lowers the required interest rates. Government bonds tend to be more liquid than corporate bonds because they are easily traded on the capital market. Corporate bonds are less liquid as it may be more difficult to offload them on the market in the event that the holder

86	4  Determination of Interest Rates

needs cash immediately. Therefore, the lower liquidity of corporate bonds will influence interest rates differently from that of government bonds. The lower liquidity of corporate bonds, the wider the spread between the interest rates on corporate bonds and government bonds. Investors therefore expect securities with lower liquidity to provide higher interest rates, considering the additional liquidity risk premium. Securities that are associated with higher liquidity will offer lower interest rates.
  Generally, there is an inverse relationship between liquidity and interest rate. Other factors being equal, a lower degree of liquidity will lead to higher liquidity risk and therefore higher interest rates and vice versa. For example, mortgaged-backed securities had low liquidity during the period of the financial crisis (2007û2009) and that explained the high interest rates on these securities.


4.4.3 Income Tax Status

The income tax status is also an important factor that influences interest rates on securities. Bonds or securities in terms of their interest payments are not taxed equally. Interest income of some bonds is taxable, while others, like municipal bonds, are non-taxable. The differences in the tax treatments on interest income will certainly affect the required interest rates. Investors are more concerned about the after-tax yield as opposed to before-tax income.
  We can use this simple formula to illustrate the relationship between after-tax interest rate (Iafter tax) and before-tax interest rate (Ibefore tax):
                 Iafter tax = Ibefore tax (1 ù Tax rate)	(4.3)
  Consider that a 1-year taxable security has a before-tax interest rate of 14%. If the tax rate is 20%, then the after-tax interest rate will be:
Iafter tax = Ibefore tax (1 ù Tax rate) = 0.14 (1 ù 0.20) = 11.2%
  Let us consider two securities with the same characteristics in terms of maturity, default risk and liquidity, except that one is a local government bond and the other a corporate bond. The interest income on the local government bond is tax exempt, while that on the corporate bond is taxable. Therefore, for these two securities, the taxable security (corporate) should offer a higher before-tax yield compared to the tax-exempt security (local government bond) for it to be preferred.
  Following from the computation above, the local government bond can afford to offer interest rate of 11.2% in order to stay competitive with the taxable corporate bonds offering 14% interest rate. Investors in high tax brackets usually prefer tax-exempt securities.
  All things being equal, a higher tax rate will lead to a wider difference in the interest rates between tax-exempt securities and taxable securities.

4.5 Term Structure of Interest Rates	87

Quick Quiz
1. What is the risk structure of interest rates?
2. How do default risk, liquidity and tax characteristics influence the risk structure of interest rates?




4.5 Term Structure of Interest Rates

We saw in the previous section how default risk, liquidity and income tax status affect interest rates. It is important to mention that these are not the only factors that influence interest rates. In fact, another factor that affects interest rates is the term to maturity (or time to maturity). Bonds or securities that have the same default risk, liquidity and income tax status but have different terms to maturity may have different interest rates. This means, if we hold default risk, liquidity and income tax status constant, bonds with different maturities will exhibit different interest rates.
  The relationship between the yield on bonds or securities with different maturities but the same default risk, liquidity and income tax status is known as the term structure of interest rates. A plot of the term structure is referred to as the yield curve. The yield curve can take various shapes. It could be normal, flat, humped or inverted. Figure 4.1 depicts the various shapes the yield could assume.
  Panel A represents a normal sloping yield curve, which is upward sloping. A normal yield curve suggests that long-term interest rates are higher than short-term interest rates. In Panel B, the yield curve is flat, indicating that long-term interest rates and short-term interest rates are the same. In Panel C, the yield curve is humped, which indicates that interest rates or yields initially rise, then fall and almost become flat. Panel D depicts an inverted (abnormal) yield curve, which is downward sloping. An inverted yield curve suggests that short-term interest rates are rather higher than long-term interest rates.
  Let us attempt plotting the yield curve for treasury securities for four African countries: Ghana, Kenya, Egypt and South Africa for May 2018. Figure 4.2 shows the yield curves of the selected countries. Apart from Kenya, which exhibits a normal yield curve, the shape of the yield curves for the other countries is not normal. The shape of the yield curve for Egypt is even inverted.
  It is important at this stage to discuss the theories which economists have proposed to explain the term structure of interest rates and for that matter the shape of the yield curve. These include the pure expectations theory, the segmented markets theory and the liquidity premium and preferred habitat theory (see Mishkin 2013; Madura 2014; Cecchetti and Schoenholtz 2014; Mishkin and Eakins 2014).

88	4  Determination of Interest Rates


PANEL A - Normal	PANEL B - Flat

Fig. 4.1 Different shapes of the yield curve

4.5.1 Pure Expectations Theory

The pure expectations theory argues that the term æstructure of interest ratesÆ is explained purely by expectations of future interest rates. The theory indicates that the interest rate on a long-term security will be equal to the average of short-term interest rates that are expected to occur over the life of the long-term security. In other words, long-term interest rates are expected to be equal to the geometric averages of short- term interest rates over the same time period. For instance, if market participants expect the interest rate on a short-term security to be 15% on average over the next 4 years, the pure expectations theory envisages that the interest rate on a security with 4 years to maturity will also be 15%. Assuming short-term rates are expected to increase more over the 4-year period than the average, such that the average of short- term rates in the next 10 years is 19%, then the interest rate on a 10-year security will also be 19%, and this is higher than the interest rate on the 4-year security of 15%. According to pure expectations theory, if interest rates are expected to increase in the future, interest rates on long-term securities will be higher than the interest rates on

4.5  Term Structure of Interest Rates	89



























Fig. 4.2 Yield curve of selected African countries. Source: AuthorsÆ own compilation

short-term securities, thus the yield curve will be upward sloping. It also means that a rise in short-term interest rates will also result in rise in long-term interest rates; hence, short-term and long-term interest rates tend to move in the same direction. The theory also explains why long-term interest rates are less volatile than short-term interest rates.
  The pure expectations theory suggests that securities of different maturities are perfect substitutes provided that the geometric average returns on short-term secu- rities are equal to what longer-term securities are offering during the same time period. The implication of the theory is that if securities with different maturities are perfect substitutes, the expected returns on such securities will be equal. This means investors would not invest in any security if the expected returns for holding that security to be less than the expected returns of another security with different maturity. To illustrate how securities with different maturities are perfect substitutes for each other, we attempt constructing investment strategies that give the same expected returns. Let us consider two possible investments strategies:
1. Invest in a 1-year bond, and at maturity in yearÆs time, invest in another 1-year bond.
2. Invest in a 2-year bond and hold it for the entire maturity period of 2 years.
  Based on the pure expectations theory, recall that these bonds are perfect sub- stitutes, so an investor should be indifferent to variance between the two investment strategies. Since both investment strategies will have the same expected returns, if an

90	4  Determination of Interest Rates

investor invests in both the one- and the 2-year bonds, the interest rates on the 2-year bond should be equal to the average interest rate on two 1-year bonds. For instance, the prevailing interest rate on a 1-year bond is 14% and the expected interest rate on a 1-year bond for the next year is 16%. Going for the first investment strategy would mean investing in the two 1-year bonds, and therefore, the expected returns over the
2 years will be the average of the two interest rates (14% + 16%)/2 = 15% per annum. This means an investor will be prepared to invest in both the 1- and 2-year
bonds provided the expected returns per annum for holding the 2-year bond are equal to the same return of 15%.
  The indifference between the two investment strategies suggests that the expected returns must be the same; therefore, we can express it mathematically as:
(1 + i2t)(1 + i2t) = (1 + i1t)(1 + iet+1)	(4.4)
where
i2 = todayÆs or known interest rate on a 2-year security as of time t i1 = todayÆs or known interest rate on a 1-year security as of time t

e
t + 1 

= interest rate on a 1-year security that is expected 1 year ahead (time t + 1).

Equation 4.4 can also be expressed as:

(1 + i2t)2 = (1 + i1t)(1 + iet+1)	(4.5)
  The equation can be stated more generally to reflect that fact that the interest rate on a security with n years to maturity is the average of the n expected future 1-year interest rates:

  The pure expectations theory explains the different shapes of the yield curves. With the normal yield curve, the theory indicates that short-term interest rates are expected to increase in the future. Under this, as seen from above, the prevailing long-term interest rate is higher than the short-term interest rate. This means we expect the average of future short-term interest rates to be higher than the prevailing short-term interest rate. This can happen once we expect short-term interest rates to increase in the future. With the inverted yield curve, we expect the average of future short-term interest rates to be less than the prevailing short-term interest rate, suggesting that the average of future short-term interest rates is expected to decrease. When the yield curve is flat, it means the average of future short-term interest rates will remain unchanged.



4.5.2 Segmented Markets Theory

Gauging from the name, the segmented markets theory indicates that markets for short-term securities and long-term securities are entirely distinct and segmented. This means investors and users of funds would have to stay in one market: either in the market for short-term funds or securities or in the market for long-term funds or securities. An investorÆs decision to stay in any of these markets will not depend on expectations of interest rates but rather on his/her needs or preference. Depository institutions like banks and savings and loans companies are likely to opt for short- term securities given their liability structure in terms of having to stay liquid to meet depositorsÆ withdrawal requests. On the contrary, contractual savings institutions such as pension funds and life insurance companies can afford to hold huge investments in long-term securities, considering the long-term nature of their liabilities.
  The interest rates in each of these markets will depend on the demand and supply of funds in that market. Assuming investors have funds to invest in short-term securities in the money market and at the same time we have companies which require long-term funds and therefore issue corporate bonds. Based on the seg- mented markets theory, interest rates will behave as follows. The effect of an increase in the supply of funds (by investors) in the short-term market will lead to

92	4  Determination of Interest Rates

a reduction in short-term interest rates. Also, the effect of an increase in the demand for funds (by borrowers) in the long-term market will lead to a rise in long-term interest rates. This combined effect of the changes in supply of short-term funds and demand for long-term funds will generate an upward sloping yield (normal) curve. On the other hand, if we now have investors who are interested in investing in the long-term market, the increase in the supply of long-term funds will result in a reduction in long-term interest rates. If we also have companies needing short-term funds, the increase in demand for funds in the short-term market will result in a rise in short-term interest rates. In effect, the changes in the supply of long-term funds and the demand for short-term funds will produce a downward sloping yield (inverted) curve.
  One important consideration of the segmented markets theory is that securities with different maturities are not substitutes; therefore, the expected yield from investing in a particular security with a given maturity does not have any effect whatsoever on the demand for another security with a different maturity. The reason why securities with different maturities are not substitutes is that, as we have mentioned earlier, investors will have a preference for say short-term securities and not long-term securities based on their own preference. Therefore, they would be more interested in the expected yield on the short-term securities that they have a preference for.



4.5.3 Liquidity Premium and Preferred Habitat Theory

The liquidity premium theory suggests that investors generally prefer to invest in short-term securities compared to holding long-term securities because of the low risk associated with investing in short-term securities. They would however opt to hold long-term securities provided they are offered adequate compensation or a liquidity premium to entice them. Short-term securities are more liquid in the sense that they can easily be offloaded on the market for cash. Long-term securities, on the other hand, tend to be less liquid; thus, they carry high interest rate risk. Investors may be prepared to hold risky long-term securities if they are adequately compen- sated for the additional risk (risk premium) associated with investing in long-term securities. Following from this explanation, we can comfortably adjust the pure expectations theory to reflect the inclusion of the risk premium in the equation, describing the relationship between interest rates of long-term and short-term secu- rities. Therefore, including the liquidity risk premium to Eq. (4.6), we can state the liquidity premium theory succinctly as:
11t + ie1t+1 + ie1t+2 +ù ùù+ ie1t+nù1



where

int =

n	+ LRPnt	(4.9)

4.5  Term Structure of Interest Rates	93

  LRPnt = the liquidity risk premium on n-period security at time t and this has a positive relationship with the term to maturity of the security. Generally, liquidity
risk premium increases with the term to maturity of the security; thus, the yield on long-dated securities has a higher liquidity risk premium compared with the yield on short-term securities.
  The liquidity premium theory indicates that long-term securities must offer liquidity risk premium above that of short-term securities, and this tends to explain the normal shape of the yield curve. There are, however, rare instances where the yield curve may be flat or inverted. A flat yield curve would suggest that interest rates are expected to drop, and an inverted yield curve would mean that the market is expecting a significant reduction in interest rates.
  The liquidity premium theory can also be linked to the preferred habitat theory (Mishkin 2013). This was proposed by Culbertson (1957) and Modigliani and Sutch (1966) and is considered a modern theory. The preferred habitat theory suggests that investors have a preference for securities with respect to particular terms to maturity compared to other securities with different terms to maturity. In other words, investors will typically prefer a particular maturity (preferred habitat) to another in terms of investments. They will not opt for investments outside their preferred habitat unless they can receive appropriate compensation for doing this. Generally, since investors prefer short habitat (habitat of short-term securities) to long habitat (habitat of long-term securities), they may be prepared to invest in long-term securities once they will earn higher expected returns. This shows an upward sloping (normal) yield curve. Though the preferred habitat theory explains the normal yield curve, it can also explain the flat, humped and inverted yields curves.
  The preferred habitat theory does not regard a risk premium as arising from the liquidity premium, but the premium is the result of demand for and supply of funds of different maturities. This suggests that the risk premium for a security may not necessarily be related to its maturity. The theory recognises the fact that the markets are segmented, but this segmentation is only partial. If there is a mismatch between the demand and supply for certain types of securities, given the appropriate expected returns, investors will be ready to switch from one segment to another in order to correct the imbalance. The preferred habitat theory extends the pure expectations theory by explaining that both term to maturity and return matter for bond investors. The thinking behind the preferred habitat theory, therefore, results in the same Eq. (4.9) as depicted by the liquidity premium theory with a term premium, which normally increases with term to maturity.



4.5.4 Importance of the Term Structure

The term structure of interest rates is useful in forecasting interest rates, forecasting recessions and making investment and financing decisions.

94	4  Determination of Interest Rates

Forecasting Interest Rates The shape of the yield curve can be used to forecast yield/profitability by ascertaining whether interest rates will increase or decrease. As mentioned earlier, a normal or an upward sloping yield curve signals the fact that interest rates will rise, while an inverted or downward sloping yield curve suggests that interest rates will decline. We need to be careful, however, in taking into consideration the liquidity premium investors demand as well as any maturity preferences.
Forecasting Recessions The yield curve is useful for forecasting recessions. A flat or inverted (downward sloping) yield curve may signal a recession in the near term or future. The implication of an inverted yield curve is that lower interest rates are expected in the future. When demand for loanable funds reduces, this will lead to lower interest rates. Deficit units will demand less loanable funds when they expect the economy to slow down or experience a recession.
Making Investment Decisions The yield curve is relevant for making investment decisions. With an upward sloping yield curve, some investors with funds for a short period may wish to invest in long-term bonds in order to benefit from the higher rates associated with the long-term bonds. An investor, with excess funds that are only available for 1 year, may consider investing in the long-term securities at the higher rate and then offload them after 1 year on the secondary market instead of investing in short-term securities at a lower rate. The expectations of higher interest rates, however, suggest that the prices of long-term bonds are exposed to possible declines. Investors should therefore consider this strategy if they believe that the market consensus forecast of interest rates is wrong. This is because the higher yield in relation to investing in long-term securities may be offset by a decline in the price of the securities.
  The yield curve also has implications for the asset-liability management of banks or financial institutions. We will discuss in Chap. 5 how interest rate movements directly impact banksÆ profits and equity positions.
Taking Financing Decisions The yield curve may also be used by businesses that intend raising finance through the issue of bonds. By gauging the yield curve or interest rates on securities with different maturities, businesses are able to determine what interest rates to pay on bonds with various maturities. This should assist them to ascertain the maturities for their bond issues.



4.5.5 Estimating Forward Rate

The forward rate represents the marketÆs consensus for future interest rates. It is the rate that is implied from the yield curve.
  Recall that in Eq. (4.4), comparing the interest rate on a 2-year security and a 1-year security, we had:

4.6 Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we examined the determination of interest rates by discussing the theories of interest rates, the economic factors affecting interest rates and the risk and term structure of interest rates. Specifically, we have mentioned that:
1. Interest rate is the price paid for borrowed funds for a specified time period. The loan amount provided by the bank or lending institution constitutes the principal and the additional amount paid by the borrower to the lending institution is the interest amount. The interest rate is typically expressed as a percentage of the principal or remaining balance of the loan. It is also known as the price of credit.
2. There are four important theories that explain interest rates. These are the FisherÆs classical theory, the loanable funds theory, the KeynesÆ liquidity preference theory and McKinnonÆs complementarity theory.
3. Irving Fisher proposed the FisherÆs classical theory, which seeks to answer how interest rates are determined in an economy. The interaction of supply of funds and demand for funds determines the equilibrium interest rate. The supply of funds is determined by the marginal rate of time preference, by income and by reward for saving. The demand for funds is also determined by the marginal productivity of capital and the rate of interest.
4. The loanable funds theory suggests that the market interest rate is determined by the interaction of the demand for and the supply of loanable funds. The demand for loanable funds consists of borrowing in the domestic market by households, businesses and governments as well as borrowing in the domestic market by foreign entities. The supply of loanable funds is also made up of domestic savings (by households, firms and governments), lending in the domestic market by foreign entities, negative hoarding of money balances (dishoarding) and credit creation by banks.
5. John Maynard Keynes developed the liquidity preference theory. The theory analyses the equilibrium interest rates based on the interaction of the publicÆs aggregate demand for holding money and the supply of money. Keynes

References	97

explained that people hold their wealth in money or cash balances (including demand deposits) or in bonds.
6. The development of McKinnonÆs (1973) theory is based on the constraint of internal financing to capital formation in developing countries and that high deposit rates encourage savings for financing physical capital.
7. Interest rates are affected by various economic factors such as inflation, eco- nomic growth, monetary policy, fiscal policy and external flow of funds. These economic factors can cause changes in the demand for and supply of loanable funds and therefore influence interest rates.
8. The risk structure of interest rates is the relationship that exists among interest rates of securities or bonds that have the same maturity. It depicts the interest rate behaviour of securities with the same maturity. The risk structure of interest rates can be explained by three important factors, the default or credit risk, liquidity and income tax status.
9. The term structure of interest rates is the relationship between the yield on bonds or securities with the different maturities but the same default risk, liquidity and income tax status. A plot of the term structure is referred to as the yield curve and can take various shapesùnormal, flat, humped or inverted.
10. The theories that explain the term structure of interest rates include the pure expectations theory, the segmented markets theory and the liquidity premium and the preferred habitat theory. The term structure of interest rates may be used to forecast interest rates, to forecast recessions and to make investment and financing decisions.
