5.1 Introduction

Banks offer different financial services to their clients by using their assets and liabilities in order to maximise net interest income. In the process of providing these services, they face different types of risks, including interest rate risk, credit risk, liquidity risk and foreign exchange risk. Banks need to adopt an integrated approach to manage their asset and liability portfolio in such a manner that it maximises their net interest income and overall profitability at acceptable levels of risks. This integrated approach is referred to as asset and liability management (ALM).
  In this chapter, we examine banksÆ overall approach to managing assets and liabilities. We discuss the strategies for managing liquidity, assets, liabilities and capital. The chapter then considers managing interest rate risk using gap analysis and other market value-based models, including maturity and duration analysis.



® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_5

101

102	5 Asset-Liability Management: Interest Rate Risks, Maturity and Duration Gaps

5.2 Assets and Liability Management Strategies

The asset and liability management approach to managing a bank or financial institution is a modern philosophy that emphasises the simultaneous determination of both the asset and the liability sides of the balance sheet for a given financial institution. It is the management of a bankÆs assets and liabilities so as to earn the highest return at an acceptable risk level and thereby maximising shareholder value (see Choudhry 2007; Rose and Hudgins 2013; Mishkin 2013; Arnold 2014).
  Assets and liability management is an important aspect of modern banking and is typically handled by the Assets and Liability Management Committee (ALCO). ALCO comprise senior management of a bank and operate just below the Board of Directors of the bank. A typical ALCO membership may comprise a Managing Director (Chairman), a Treasurer, a Finance Director, a Head of Corporate Banking, a Head of Retail Banking, a Risk Director, a Head of Market Risk, etc. Other members can be invited to attend meetings as and when needed. The committee meet periodically, say once a month, to discuss the management of the various aspects of the bankÆs balance sheet.
  In order to earn the highest return possible, bank managers must ensure that the assets and liabilities of the bank are managed very well. There are four main concerns in this regard (see Rose and Hudgins 2013):
ò The need to make sure the bank has enough ready cash to pay its depositors when there are deposit outflows (that is when customers make withdrawals and demand payments). This process is known as liquidity management.
ò The need to pursue an acceptably low level of risk by acquiring assets that have a low rate of default and by diversifying assets holdings. This is known as assets management.
ò The need to acquire funds at a low cost is known as liability management.
ò The need to decide on the appropriate level of capital a bank should maintain and acquire is called capital adequacy management.



5.2.1 Liquidity Management

As explained earlier, liquidity management is ensuring that the bank has enough cash on hand to pay for its customersÆ withdrawals. We shall begin by looking at the

5.2.2 Assets Management

To maximise its profits, banks tend to get the highest returns possible on loans and other assets, minimise their risk and make sufficient provisions for liquidity by investing in liquid instruments or assets. Banks attempt to accomplish this by:
1. Finding borrowers who will pay high interest rates and are unlikely to default on their loans. This is achieved by seeking out loan businesses by advertising their borrowing rates and approaching corporations directly to solicit loans. The credit department of the bank will then screen the potential applicant to ensure that when the loan is finally granted, the customer will repay the principal with interest. Banks are typically conservative and will want a loan default rate not exceeding 1%, because the higher the amount of default the greater the chance of bank failure.
2. Purchasing securities with high returns and low risk. Banks seek to invest in instruments that will generate high returns, but they need to do this taking into consideration the level of risk associated with such investments. Investments in treasury bills/notes, for instance, carry virtually no risk and are therefore a good investment option for banks if the rates are high.
3. Thirdly, in managing assets, bank managers attempt to lower their risk by diversifying. They construct a portfolio by purchasing different types of assets and by approving different types of loans to a number of customers across various sectors of the economy. In short, banks seek not to put æall their eggs in one basketÆ. Benefitting from diversification requires selecting assets that are less correlated. Banks also avoid over-exposure to particular sectors or minimise their concentration risk by restricting the proportion of their assets in one area of activity. This involves setting limits in terms of the proportion of assets in sectors and also in countries.
4. Finally, banks must manage the liquidity of their assets so that they can satisfy their reserve requirement without incurring huge costs. Thus, bank managers would hold liquid assets, even if they offer lower returns than other assets. In this regard, banks normally would choose to hold government securities such as treasury bills, even though rates on these securities are most of the time lower than loans to customers.

106	5 Asset-Liability Management: Interest Rate Risks, Maturity and Duration Gaps

5.2.3 Liability Management

The essence of liability management is to ensure that banks acquire funds at the lowest possible cost. Banks need to take control of their liabilities so as to be able to better manage assets. It involves making a decision regarding the composition of liabilities as well as adjusting interest rates on borrowed funds in order to obtain the target mix of borrowing and the desired cost of funds. Banks needs to diversify by opening up to different sources of funds. The strategies banks can adopt in liability management include:
ò Offering different kinds of bank liability products.
ò Use an appropriate pricing mechanism to suit their liquidity needs, that is, raise bankÆs deposit rate if the bank needs more funds.
ò Borrow funds from the market, especially on the overnight inter-bank market, if the need arises.



5.2.4 Capital Management

The goal of capital management is to ensure that banks have enough capital at all times in order to absorb the shocks in the loan market. In banking, if customers refuse to pay off their loans, the loan amount is written off against the bank capital. Hence, it is important for a bank to have enough capital to absorb these shocks from time to time.

5.3 Interest Rate Risk

One may ask: how does interest rate risk come about? Interest rate risk is the risk incurred by a bank or financial institution when the maturities of its assets and liabilities are mismatched. It is a potential loss in the bankÆs net interest income as a result of the position taken by the bank.

5.3 Interest Rate Risk	107

  LetÆs suppose ABC Bank has a pending application from a corporate client that wants to borrow US$100 million for 2 years at a rate of 10%. To fund this loan, ABC Bank borrowed fixed term for 1 year from a customer at rate of 9%. In this example, the bank has æshort-fundedÆ its assets. That is, it has borrowed for 1 year but has given out a loan, which matures in 2 years.
  In the first year of the loan, the bank has locked 10% on its asset (loan) and 9% on its liabilities (deposit). Therefore, the profit on this transaction in the first year is (10û9%) Î 100 m = 0.01 Î 100 = US$1 million.
In the second year, the bankÆs profits are uncertain, as the level of interest rate
could change. Suppose the interest rate increases to 11% in the second year, the bank cannot change the rate on the assets because it is fixed rate loan.
  On the liability side, the bank will have to borrow at the current market rate for the next year at 11%. Thus, the bankÆs profit will be shown as follows:

Interest received on loan = 0.10 Î 100 million = 10 million Interest paid on deposit = 0.11 Î 100 million = ù11 million Net Interest Income =	ù1 million


  The negative profit will erode the +1 million profit gained in the first year. In this example, if rates rise by more than 1%, the actual profit on the loan will be negative. Thus, the bank has exposed itself to refinancing risk as a result of holding longer- term assets relative to liabilities. Refinance risk is defined as the risk that the cost of rolling over or re-borrowing funds will rise above the returns being earned on asset investments.
  On the other hand, letÆs suppose ABC Bank has to borrow US$100 million for 2 years from a customer and has to lend US$100 million for 1 year. LetÆs assume further that the rate on the customer loan is 10%, while the bank could borrow at 9%. Here, the bank has borrowed a longer-term liability and given out a short-term loan and is facing interest rate risk. That is, it faces uncertainties about the interest rate at which it can invest funds in the second year.
In the first year the bank has locked the returns as calculated below:

Interest received = 0.1 Î 100 million = 10 million Interest paid = ù0.09 Î 100 million = ù9 million Net Interest =	ù1 million


  In the second year, suppose rates fall to 8%, the bank cannot review rates on the borrowing. The asset has matured in the first year and therefore could only be invested at the current market rate of 8%. The bankÆs position will look as follows:

108	5 Asset-Liability Management: Interest Rate Risks, Maturity and Duration Gaps

Interest received =
Interest paid =0.08 Î 100 million =
ù0.09 Î 100 million =8 million
ù9 millionNet Interest =ù1 million
  Hence, the loss in the second year will wipe away the profit in the first year giving the bank a negative return. In this case, the bank suffers from reinvestment risk by holding shorter-term assets relative to liabilities and faces uncertainty of/in reinvesting the assets in the second year. Reinvestment risk is the risk that the returns on funds to be reinvested will fall below the cost of funds.
  Apart from potential refinancing and reinvestment risks, mismatching assets and liabilities also exposes the bank to market value risk. The market value of an asset or liability is the present value of future cash flows of the investment. If the market interest rates rise, the discount rate used to discount the future cash flows also rises and therefore its market value falls. If rates fall, the discount rate falls and therefore the market value rises. Thus, in a situation where a bank has more longer-term assets than liabilities, a rise in market rates will lead to a greater fall in assets than liabilities and expose the bank to economic losses and potential risk of insolvency (failure).

  There are models that banks can use to measure interest rate risk, and these include:
ò The gap analysis (repricing model)
ò The maturity model
ò The duration model



5.4 Gap Analysis

The gap analysis is also known as the repricing model or the funding gap. The model is defined as the accounting cash flow analysis of the repricing gap between the interest revenue a bank or financial institution earns on its assets and the interest it pays on its liabilities over a given period of time. A repricing gap is known as the difference between rate-sensitive assets (i.e. assets, which will have their interest rate repriced or changed over some future period) and rate-sensitive liabilities (i.e. liabilities, which will have their interest rates repriced or changed over some

5.4 Gap Analysis	109

Table 5.8 Computation of bank gap


Time bandAssets (A) (million dollars)Liabilities (L) (million dollars)Gaps (A ù L) (million dollars)
Cumulative gapsLess than 1 month5070ù20ù201û3 months2040ù20ù403û6 months15105ù356û12 months25205ù301û5 years1007525ù5Over 5 years10550Total220220

future period). Rate sensitivity means time to repricing (Sinkey 2002; Rose and Hudgins 2013; Saunders and Cornett 2014).



5.4.1 Computing the BankÆs Gap

Under the gap analysis, a bank reports the gaps in each maturity bucket by calcu- lating the rate sensitivity of each assets (RSA) and liability (RSL) on its balance sheet. By rate sensitivity, we mean assets or liabilities which will be repriced at or near the current market interest rate within a time period, that is, how long a bank manager must wait before he/she can change the interest rate on the assets or liabilities before they mature. Suppose an asset has 2 years to mature. If a bank manager can change the rates in a yearÆs time depending on the market rates prevailing at that time, then the asset qualifies to be designated at repricing over the 2-year time band. However, over the next 6 months, we cannot call it repricing, because the interest rate on it cannot be changed.
Assume that ABC Bank has the following mismatch Table 5.8 (repricing gap).
  In the above example, the bank has a negative gap in the time band of less than one (1) month. That is, it has more liabilities than assets, and therefore, a rise in the 1 month interest rate would lower the bankÆs net interest income. The bank faces refinancing risk, because a rate increase will lead to the bank paying more interest expense than receiving by leading to a lower net interest income. The opposite effect holds in the 3û6 monthsÆ time bucket.



5.4.2 Effect of Interest Rate Changes on Net Interest Income

To calculate the impact of a rate change on the net interest income of a bank, the following formula can be developed:
Let ?NII = change in net interest income in the ith bucket

Gapi = dollar size of the gap between book value of RSA and RSL in the maturity bucket i
?Ri = change in the level of the interest rate impacting assets and liabilities in the ith bucket
Then,
?NII = (Gapi) ?Ri = (RSAi ù RSLi)?Ri
  In the above example, suppose market interest rates rise 1% on the less than 1 month bucket, then the impact on net interest income is as shown below:
?NII = (ù20 million) Î 0.01 = ù200,000
  Also, we can use cumulative gap (CGap) to calculate the impact on the net interest income over a time period such as 3û6 months and 1 year. In the example, the one cumulative gap is given as:
CGap = (ù20) + (ù20) + 5 + 5 = (ù30)
  If ?Ri is the average interest rate change affecting assets and liabilitiesÆ repricing within a year, then the cumulative effect on the bankÆs interest income over 1 year is
?NII = (CGap) Î ?R
?NII = (ù30 million) Î 0.01 = ù300,000

Answer 5.1
For the ABC Bank, within the period of 1 year:
RSA = Assets that can have their interest rate change over the next 1 year. RSL = Liabilities that can have their interest rate change over the next 1 year.
1. RSAs include the following:
ò Short-term loans with 1-year maturity. Here, interest is reset at maturity and therefore qualifies to be RSA = US$40 million.
ò Long-term loans with 6 months interest rate repricing. Though these loans are
longer than 1 year, every 6 months interest rate can be changed and is therefore an RSA over the next 1 year = US$50 million.
ò 91-day T-bill = US$30 million; these are repriced every 3 months at maturity.
ò 25-year mortgage repriced over a 1-year period = US$50 million. Although this is for 25 years, the interest rate on it can be changed every year, and therefore, it is
a rate-sensitive asset over the 1-year period.
Therefore, the total RSA for ABC Bank over the 1-year period is

RSA = (US$40 million + US$50 million + US$30 million + US$50 million)
= US$170 million.
2. RSLs include the following:
ò 3-month fixed deposits = US$60 million. Interest on this is reset at maturity.
ò 1-year time deposit = US$40 million. Interest on this is also reset at maturity.
  The rest of the liabilities do not qualify to be included in the 1-year RSLs. This is because their interest rates will not change over the next year.
Therefore, total RSL for ABC Bank is
       RSL = US$40 million + US$60 million = US$100 million We can now calculate the funding gap as:
Funding Gap = RSA ù RSL = US$170 m ù US$100 m = US$70 m.

3. The gap ratio is defined as  CGap
  In this question, the cumulative gap over the 1-year period is US$70 million, and total assets are US$250 million.

112	5 Asset-Liability Management: Interest Rate Risks, Maturity and Duration Gaps

Therefore, Gap Ratio = US$70 million/US$250 million = 0.28 = 28%
4. If rates increase by 1%, then the impact on net interest income is given by the formula

?NII = (CGap) Î ?R
From the above, CGAP = US$70 million. Hence,
?NII = US$70 million Î 0.01 = US$7 million
  Thus, a 1% rise in market interest rates over the next 1 year will lead to a US$7 million increase in the net interest income of the ABC Bank. This is because ABC Bank has more assets than liabilities and will therefore receive more interest income than payments for interest expense.
5. For unequal rate changes,

?NII = (RSA Î ?RA) ù (RSL Î ?RL)
= Interest revenue ù Interest expense
= (US$170 million Î 1.5%) ù (US$100 million Î 1.2%)
= US$2.55 million ù US$1.2 million
= US$1.35 million
The following deductions could be made concerning the gap model:
ò The larger the absolute value of the CGap, the larger the expected change in NII.
ò If CGap is greater than zero, a change in net interest income is positively related to a change in market interest rates. That is, an increase in interest rate will lead to an increase in net interest income.
ò If CGap is less than zero, an increase in rate will lead to a reduction in net interest income and vice versa.
ò In both cases, the interest rate should change equally on both liabilities and assets.

5.4.3 Weaknesses of the Repricing Model

The following are weaknesses associated with the use of the repricing model:

5.4 Gap Analysis	113

ò Market Value EffectsùWhen interest rate change, the repricing model does not take into account the changes in the market value of assets and liabilities. That is, the model ignores the present value of assets and liabilities and hence only partially measures the extent of interest rate risk exposure.
ò Over-aggregationùThe repricing model ignores information regarding the dis- tribution of assets and liabilities within the various time buckets. This is because when rates change, assets and liabilities may be repriced at different times, even within the same time band. The model does not take into account what happens in terms of interest rate repricing within each of the time bands.
ò Run-offsùThis is defined as the periodic cash flows of interest and principal payments on long-term assets, such as conventional mortgages, which can be reinvested at market rates. In practical terms, banks do not wait for the entire loan amount granted initially to be repaid at maturity, but receive regularly cash flows in the form of interest and principal payments throughout the life of the assets. The repricing model in its simplest form does not take these cash flows into account. The bank manager can adjust these cash flows by adding the estimated amount of the run-offs to the RSAs or RSLs before calculating the gap.
ò Cash Flows from Off-Balance Sheet ActivitiesùThe repricing model ignores cash flows from off-balance sheet items. When interest rates change, it impacts on the off-balance sheet items as well and therefore must be included in estimat- ing the funding gap.

5.5 Market Value-Based Models: Maturity and Duration Analysis

In this section, we look at maturity and duration analyses. These two approaches are based on the market values of assets and liabilities.



5.5.1 The Maturity Model

When interest rates change, the market value of assets and liabilities also changes. The maturity model considers the gap between the maturities of assets and liabilities. The market value of an asset is defined as the sum of the present value of all cash flows related to it (Saunders and Cornett 2014). Mathematically, the market value of an asset is given by:
Market value =   C1   + 	C2	 + 	C3			Cn	

(1 + r)

(1 + r)2

(1 + r)3

(1 + r)n

where C1, C2, ... , Cn are cash flows from the asset,
r is the market interest rate, and
n is the number of years left for the asset to mature.

ò The longer the maturity of a fixed income asset or liability, the larger the decrease (increase) in market value given interest rate increases (decrease).
ò The reduction in the value of a longer-term security increases at a diminishing rate for any given increase interest rate.

5.5.1.1 The Maturity Model with Portfolio of Assets and Liabilities

We can extend the single asset case discussed above to include a number (portfolio) of assets and liabilities. In this context, we will calculate the maturity of a bankÆs assets as a weighted average of its individual assets.
Let MA = weighted-average maturity of a bankÆs assets ML = weighted-average maturity of a bankÆs liabilities Such that
Mi = Wi1Mi1 + Wi2Mi2 + ... + WinMin
where
Mi = weighted-average maturity of a bankÆs assets or liabilities, i = A or L 
Wij = importance of each asset (liability) in the asset (liability) portfolio as measured by the market value of that asset or liability position relative to the market value of
all assets (liabilities)
Mij = maturity of the jth asset (or liability), j = 1, 2, ... , 3 
  The effect of interest rate rise/fall on the market value portfolio is the same as that of an individual asset/liability. Thus, in the maturity model the bank manages its interest rate by managing the maturity gap between its assets and liabilities. The maturity gap in this regard is defined as the difference between the weighted-average maturity of the bankÆs assets and liabilities.
  That is, maturity gap = MA ù ML. The gap could be positive, zero or negative. Consider the case where MA ù ML > 0. Here, the weighted average of the maturity of assets is longer than that of liabilities. Therefore, a rise in interest rate
will lead to a greater fall in the market value of assets than liabilities. This means, in a rising interest rate regime, it is not advisable for the bank to write long-dated assets against short-dated deposits.
Let us assume the balance sheet of ABC Bank is as follows (Table 5.10):
  The above balance sheet items are shown in market values. The balance sheet identity Equity ? A ù L holds. Therefore, as interest rates change, the changes in the

Hence, ?E = ?A ù ?L.
  That is, Change in Equity = Change in the value of assets ù Change in the value of liabilities.
  In the above balance sheet, suppose there is a 1% rise in interest rates of both assets and liabilities, the market value of both the assets and liabilities will be as shown below:

  Thus, a 1% rise in interest rates has led to ABC BankÆs equity falling from US$10 million to US$7.92 million (i.e. US$10 million ù US$3.08 million). A percentage fall of 30.8%! The maturity gap in this example is 3 years (i.e. MA = 5 years, ML = 2 years), so that maturity gap = MA ù ML = (5 ù 2) years = 3 years. This

118	5 Asset-Liability Management: Interest Rate Risks, Maturity and Duration Gaps

means that a bank whose maturity gap is really large could suffer greatly with a rise in interest rates, which could wipe off all its equity and result in bank failure.
  Also, if the maturity gap is less than zero, then a fall in interest rates will adversely affect the bank. This is because a negative maturity gap implies more long-dated liabilities than assets. When interest rate declines, the value of liabilities will rise faster than that of assets, leading to a greater fall in the equity of the bank.
  The maturity model therefore suggests that for a bank to protect or immunise itself against rising/falling interest rates, bank managers should manage their matu- rity gap profile. We can infer from the above example that the best option is for the bank to strive for a maturity gap of zero, i.e. MA ù ML = 0. However, this does not always protect against rising/falling interest rates.


5.5.1.2 Weaknesses of the Maturity Model

There are two main weaknesses with this model, and they are:
ò The model does not account for degree of leverage in the bankÆs opera- tionsùThat is, some of the bankÆs operations may be funded by equity. But this is not captured in this model. Thus, the deposits being used to fund the assets of the bank may not fully match the assets because of the presence of equity.
ò The model also does not account for the timing of cash ?ows from the bankÆs assets and liabilitiesùEven though both assets and liabilities may have the same maturities, the timing of cash flows in the form of interest received or paid may differ. In this case, the bank will not be fully protected against interest rate risk, though both assets and liabilities may have the same maturity tenor.

5.5.2 Duration Analysis

Duration is the weighted-average time to maturity on a loan, using the relative present values of the cash flows as weights. On a time-value basis, it is measured as the time period it takes to recover the initial loan investment. For example, if a bank gives a loan, all cash flows received before the period of the loanÆs duration reflect the recovery of the initial loan investment, while any cash flows that are received after the period of the loanÆs duration and before maturity are considered as profits or returns the bank receives on the loan. Duration analysis weights the time at which cash flows are received by the importance in present value terms of the cash

5.5 Market Value-Based Models: Maturity and Duration Analysis	119

flows arriving at each point in time (Sinkey 2002; Rose and Hudgins 2013; Saunders and Cornett 2014).


5.5.2.1 The General Formula for Duration

The duration for any given fixed income security that pays interest annually can be calculated using the formula below:

P CFt Î DF1 Î t	P PVt Î t

CFt Î DFt
i=1

i=1

where
D = duration measured in years.
CFt = cash flows received on the security at the end of the period t.
DFt = discount factor	1 t , where R is the annual yield or current level of interest
rates in the market.
PVt = present value of cash flows at the end of the period t, which equals CFt Î DFt.
  The above formula can be modified to accommodate the number of times interest is received on a security in a year. In this case,


N

  CFtÎt (1+R/m)mt

where m = number of times interest is received in a year.

5.5.2.2 Features of Duration

1. The duration of a zero-coupon bond is the same as that of its maturity. Note that a zero- coupon bond is one which does not pay any interest before the maturity of the bond. These bonds are sold at a discount and mature at face value; an example is the 91-day and the 182-day treasury bills. That is,

MB = DB
Maturity of bond = duration of bond
2. The duration of a consol or perpetuity is given as

DC = 1 + 1
R
  A consol is a bond that does not have a maturity date. Interest is paid on the bond forever and is fixed.
3. Duration increases with the maturity of fixed income assets or liabilities but at a decreasing rate. The longer the maturity of the asset, the bigger the duration. However, for a 1-year increase in the maturity of the asset, the change in the duration figure will be less than 1 year. This is expressed mathematically as

 ?D	?D2
?M > 0, ?M2 < 0

4. Duration and yield. The duration of a bond decreases as the yield on the bond increases. This is because higher yield discounts will later weigh cash flows more

122	5 Asset-Liability Management: Interest Rate Risks, Maturity and Duration Gaps

heavily, and the relative importance or weights of those later cash flows decline compared with earlier cash flows on an asset or liability.

?D < 0
?Y

5. Duration and coupon interest. The higher the coupon on the bond, the lower its duration. That is, the investor recoups initial investment faster when payments are large.

?D < 0
?C



5.5.2.3 Economic Meaning of Duration

Duration is seen as a measure of the average life of an asset or liability in terms of cash flow. It is also a direct measure of an assetÆs or liabilityÆs interest rate sensitivity or elasticity. This means the larger the duration of an asset, the larger the sensitivity of the asset to interest rate rise or fall. For example, if the duration of an asset A is 5 years and that of B is 2 years, then asset A is more sensitive to interest rate movement than B. This relationship can be expressed mathematically as:


dP	1
dR =ù 

[P Î D]

(1 + R)
or
dP = ùD  dR

The above expression may simply be explained as:

Percentage change in price of asset = Duration of asset
Î Present value of change in interest rate.
  Hence, the duration D in the above equation is the interest elasticity of the asset and it measures the percentage change in price of a bond as a result of a percentage change in interest rate. Also, the equations show that the price of bond and interest rate movements are in opposite directions. That is, a rise in the market interest rate will lead to a fall in the price of the bond. The gain in the bond price as a result of an interest rate fall is usually the same as a loss in the bond price as a result of an interest

5.5 Market Value-Based Models: Maturity and Duration Analysis	123
rate rise. As a result, we say that the gains and losses in bond prices due to rate movement are symmetrical.
  We can further rearrange the above equation to the modified version of an assetÆs sensitivity to rate movement as:

where

dP
P = ùMDdR


MD =	1
(1 + R)

This equation is called the modified duration.
  The equations above hold for annual compounding interest rates. For semi-annual interest rates, we use the following formula:
dP = ùD" dR #

5.5.2.4 Duration and Immunisation

Duration can be used to protect (minimise) a bankÆs balance sheet or some subset of the balance sheet against interest rate risk. We illustrate how this can be done in two ways. The first is by immunising against the future cash flows of a single asset or liability in the future, and the second is by immunising the whole balance sheet.


5.5.2.5 Immunising Future Cash Flow Payment

This is more relevant to an insurance company who is expected to pay a lump sum (or amount) to a policyholder at some cut-off date in the future. Suppose we are in 2010, and the insurance company has to make a guaranteed payment to a policy- holder in 5 yearsÆ time; the insurance company can invest in both a 5-year maturity and a duration zero-coupon bond or in a coupon bond with a 5-year duration. In both cases, the cash flows from the investment will be enough to pay for the lump sum (amount) to the policyholder come the maturity date.

126	5 Asset-Liability Management: Interest Rate Risks, Maturity and Duration Gaps

5.5.2.6 Immunising the Whole Balance Sheet

Apart from immunising individual assetsÆ cash flows against interest rate shocks, we can also immunise the whole balance sheet of the bank against adverse movements in interest rates. We do this through the measure of the duration gap of the bankÆs balance sheet. The duration gap is a measure of the overall interest rate risk exposure of a bank. To estimate the duration gap, we first need to determine the duration of the bankÆs asset portfolio (A) and the duration of its liabilities (L).
These can be calculated as

DA = X1ADA + X2ADA + .. . + XnADA

and

DL = X1LDL + X2LDL + ... + XnLDL
1	2	n
where
X1 j + X2 j + ... + Xnj = 1, j = A, L
  The Xijs in the above equation is the market value projections of each asset or liability held in the respective asset and liability portfolios. Thus, if 2-year bonds form 10% of a bankÆs asset portfolio and have duration of 1.5 years, then X1ADA = 0.1 Î 1.5 = 0.115. In simple terms, the duration of a portfolio of an asset or liability is a market value weighted average of the individual durations of the assets or the liabilities on the bankÆs balance sheet.

5.5.2.7 Difficulties in Applying the Duration Model

In practice, it is difficult to apply the duration model. Some of the difficulties encountered when applying the duration model are:

130	5 Asset-Liability Management: Interest Rate Risks, Maturity and Duration Gaps

ò Duration can be costlyùUsing the duration model to immunise a bank against interest rate risk is time-consuming and costly. This is because it involves restructuring the whole balance sheet, which can sometimes be large and com- plex. However, with the introduction of financial innovation instruments and derivatives on the market, this problem can be solved to some extent.
ò Immunisation is a dynamic problemùImmunising the whole balance sheet using the duration method is a dynamic strategy. In theory, it requires the bank to rebalance the balance sheet continuously to ensure that the duration from the asset or the liability portfolio exactly matches. Continuous rebalancing may not be easy to do, as it involves costly transaction fees.
ò Large interest rate changes and convexityùDuration is useful for accurately measuring the price sensitivity of fixed income securities for small changes in interest rates. If the interest rate change is large, duration becomes a less accurate predictor of how much the prices of the securities will change and therefore less of a measure of interest rate sensitivity. Convexity is the degree of the curvature of the price-yield curve around some interest rate level. A high convexity means that for equally large changes of an interest rate (up or down), the capital gain effect of a rate decrease will more than offset the capital loss effect of a rate increase.
Quick Quiz
1. What does duration mean? How does duration differ from maturity?
2. Why is the duration model considered a more complete measure of an assetÆs or a liabilityÆs interest rate sensitivity than the maturity model?
3. Discuss some of the difficulties associated with using the duration model.




5.6 Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we discussed asset-liability management strategies, interest rate risk, gap analysis, market value-based models and maturity and duration analysis. We learnt that:
1. Management of the assets and liabilities is the responsibility of the Asset Liability Committee (ALCO) of the bank. The ALCO ensure that the bank stays liquid, earns the highest return on its assets given risk, borrows at the least possible cost and secures its capital position.
2. Changes in interest rates could affect the net interest income of a bank. Banks are exposed to refinancing and reinvestment risks. Refinancing risk occurs because the bank may be uncertain as to the rate at which it can raise new liabilities in the future. Reinvestment risk occurs because the bank is uncertain as to the rate at which it can reinvest assets upon maturity.

5.6 Summary and Conclusions	131
3. Interest rate changes are negatively related to the market value of assets and liabilities. When interest rates increase, the market value of assets and liabilities falls and vice versa. The extent of the fall or rise in assets and liabilities depends on their maturity. Assets and liabilities with a longer maturity will experience larger gains or losses for a given change in interest rates compared to assets and liabilities with shorter maturity.
4. Interest rate changes affect a bankÆs net interest income, and this can be examined by using gap analysis. Rate sensitivity means assets or liabilities which will be repriced at or near the current market interest rate within a time period; that is, how long a bank manager must wait before he/she can change the interest rate on the asset or liabilities before they mature. For equal rate changes, the change in net interest income for equal changes in interest rates on assets and liabilities is given as:
?NII = (Gapi)?Ri = (RSAi ù RSLi)?Ri
For an unequal rate changes on assets and liabilities, the change in net interest income is given as:

?NII = (RSA Î ?RA) ù (RSL Î ?RL)

5. A rise (fall) in interest rate generally leads to a fall (rise) in market value of an asset or liability. The longer the maturity of a fixed income asset or liability, the larger the fall (rise) in market value for any given interest rate increase (decrease). The fall in the value of a longer-term security increases at a diminishing rate for any given increase interest rate.
6. Maturity gap is = MA ù ML, the gap could be positive, zero or negative. A bank is adversely exposed if it has a positive maturity gap and interest rates are expected
to rise. This is because a rise in interest rate will lead to a greater fall in the market value of assets than liabilities. This means if the interest rate is rising, it is not advisable to write long-dated assets against short-dated deposits. Also, if the maturity gap is less than zero, then a fall in interest rates will adversely affect the bank. This is because a negative maturity gap implies longer-dated liabilities than assets. When interest rate declines, the value of liabilities will rise faster than that of assets leading to a greater fall in the equity of the bank.
7. Duration is the weighted-average time to maturity on a loan using the relative present values of the cash flows as weights. It measures the period of time required to recover the initial investment on a loan. In addition to being a measure of the average life in the cash flow sense of an asset or liability, duration is also a direct measure of the interest rate sensitivity or elasticity of an asset.

Asset-Liability Management: Using Hedging Techniques









Learning Objectives
After completing this section, you should be able to:

6.1 Introduction

Bank managers are constantly seeking innovative ways of immunising their balance sheet against interest rate risk. In the previous chapter, we looked at how banks manage interest rate risk using gap analysis and other market value-based models, including maturity and duration analysis. Another important approach to managing interest rate risk is the use of the derivatives. A derivative is a financial instrument whose value depends on the value of an underlying security. Examples of derivatives include forwards, futures, swaps, caps, floors, etc.
  In this chapter, we look at how forwards and futures as well as swaps are used to protect banks against interest rate risk.


6.2 Using Futures and Forwards

To fully understand the nature of forwards and futures, we will compare them to a spot contract. A spot contract is an agreement between a buyer and a seller at time zero (i.e. t = 0), when the seller of an asset agrees to deliver it immediately and the buyer of the asset agrees to pay for that asset immediately. In short, a spot contract is


® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_6

135

136	6 Asset-Liability Management: Using Hedging Techniques

an agreement involving the immediate exchange of an asset for cash. In practice, however, it takes up to two business days for a spot contract to be settled (Sinkey 2002; Rose and Hudgins 2013; Saunders and Cornett 2014).
  A forward contract is an agreement between two parties (i.e. a buyer and a seller) to exchange a given asset for cash at an agreed price on a predetermined future date. For example, in a 6-month forward contract for a bank to buy a 5-year bond, the bank (buyer) and the seller agree on price and quantity of today, but delivery of the bond for cash takes place in 6 monthsÆ time. If the current 6-month forward price of such a bond is US$50,000 in 6 monthsÆ time, this is the price that will apply, no matter what happens to the spot price between the time the contract was agreed and the time of delivery.
  A futures contract is an agreement between a buyer and a seller at time zero to exchange a standardised pre-specified asset for cash at a later date. Futures contracts are very similar to that of forwards, but the main differences between the two contracts are:
ò Futures contract takes place on an organised exchanged market, while forward contract is usually over-the-counter transactions.
ò The price of the forward contract is fixed throughout the life of the contract. However, in a futures contract, the price is marked to the market daily, and the losing side is required to settle cash throughout the life of the contract. Mark to market is the process by which the prices of outstanding contracts are adjusted to fully reflect current market conditions.
ò Forward contracts are tailor-made contracts, while futures are standardised. There is therefore flexibility with the use of a forward contract, especially on a bilateral basis (i.e. between two parties).
ò Forward contracts are subject to counterparty and default risk because of its bilateral nature. On the other hand, default risk on the futures contract is signif- icantly reduced since they are traded on an exchange, which guarantees the protection of the counterparties against credit or default risk.



6.2.1 Hedging Interest Rate Risk with Forward Contract

A forward contract can be used to hedge against an expected interest rate risk on the balance sheet. Suppose ABC Bank has a US$1million 10-year asset on its balance sheet with duration of 5 years and current price of US$860,000. The bankÆs research team has predicted that the interest rate will rise by 3% from the current level of 12% over the next 3 months.
  We know from the previous section that a rise in interest rate will lead to a fall in the price of the bond. We can calculate this change in price, using the formula developed earlier as:

6.2 Using Futures and Forwards	137
P = D Î P Î  ?R 
1 + R

So that ?P = ù5 Î 860,000 Î	0.03
1 + 0.12

= ù115,178.57

  Thus, the bank is expected to incur a capital loss of US$ ù115,178.57, and the price of the bond will fall to (US$860, 000 ù US$115, 178.57) = US$744, 821.43. To offset this loss, the bank manager may hedge the bankÆs position by taking an
off-balance sheet hedge. In this case, the bank manager may sell US$1 million 10-year bond at face value for forward delivery in 3 months. Suppose the price agreed today for 3-month delivery is US$860,000. If the rate prediction proves to be true, the bank stands to lose US$ ù115,178.57 relating to the on-balance sheet asset. On the forward, the bank will purchase the bond on the market at US$744,821.43
and sell to the counterparty for US$860,000, the price agreed on from the beginning of the contract. The profit in the forward contract is US$860, 000 û US$744,
821.43 = US$115, 178.57, which exactly offsets the loss on the on-balance sheet position. Thus, by using the forward transaction, the bank manager has fully
immunised itself against any interest rate risk.
  In this example, for every change in interest rates, a loss/gain on the on-balance sheet is offset by a gain/loss on the forward contract. The bankÆs net interest income is fully protected.



6.2.2 Hedging Interest Rate Risk with Futures Contract

Most banks hedge interest rate risk exposure using futures contracts. This they do, either at the micro level (called micro hedging) or at the macro level (known as macro hedging). Before going into details, letÆs spend some time explaining some important concepts.
Micro Hedging A bank is using micro hedging when it employs futures or forward contracts to hedge a particular asset or liability risk. In micro hedging, the bank manager often tries to pick a futures or forward contract with the underlying deliverable clearly matched to the asset (or liability) position being hedged.
Macro Hedging This occurs when the entire balance sheet duration gap is hedged using futures or other derivative securities. This is different from hedging of individual assets. Macro hedging takes the whole portfolio view and allows for individual asset and liability interest sensitivities or duration to net each other out.
Routine Hedging Routine hedging occurs when a bank reduces its interest rate risk or other risk exposure to the lowest level possible by selling enough futures to offset the interest rate risk exposure of its whole balance sheet or cash position in each asset and liability. Reducing interest rate risk also reduces expected return and, thus, shareholder wealth. Therefore, a manager will weigh the interest rate risk exposure

138	6 Asset-Liability Management: Using Hedging Techniques

carefully before deciding to go for a routine hedge. There is a trade-off between expected return and risk, and hence a minimum risk fully hedged portfolio will have to be weighed against the shareholderÆs interest, since such a portfolio will result in a lower return.
Selective Hedging This is partially hedging of the balance sheet duration gap or of an individual asset and liability. It involves selectively hedging a proportion of the balance sheet since a fully hedged portfolio means a lower expected return.
Quick Quiz

6.2.2.1 Macro Hedging with Futures

The number of futures contracts a bank will buy or sell in a macro hedge depends on the size and the direction of the interest rate exposure and the return-risk trade-off from fully or selectively hedging that risk. We know that if interest rates change, the bankÆs net worth is affected, and the magnitude can be estimated using the formula:
?E = ù[DA ù kDL] Î A Î  ?R 
1 + R
  The objective of a bank manager to fully hedge the balance sheet exposure will be fulfilled by constructing a futures position such that if interest rates do rise/fall, the gain/loss made on the futures position just offsets the loss/gain on off-balance sheet transactions. Also, when interest rates rise, the price of the futures is affected, since the price of the underlying deliverable is impacted. Thus,

?F = ùDF Î  ?R

6.2.3 The Problem of Basis Risk

The basis risk is the residual risk that occurs as a result of the fact that movement in the spot assetÆs price is not perfectly correlated with the movement in the price of the asset delivered under a futures or forward contract. This lack of perfect correlation is called the basis risk. Basis risk occurs for two reasons.

6.3 Using Swaps	141

  First, the balance sheet asset and the liability being hedged are not the same as the underlying security on the futures contract. Secondly, the difference between move- ments in spot versus futures rates also contributes to the basis risk. This is because the spot transactions for financial instruments and futures transactions take place in different markets.
  To solve the basis risk problem, we can modify the number of futures contract to buy by using the formula shown below:

NF = (DA ù kDL) Î A
DF Î PF Î br
where br = basis risk
Example 6.2
Assume that a bank has assets of US$250 million and liabilities of US$200 million. The assets have duration of 6 years, and the liabilities have duration of 3 years. The price of the futures contract is US$115,000, and its duration is 5.5 years. Determine the number of futures contracts needed for the bank to construct a perfect hedge.
Solution

NF = (DA ù kDL) Î A
DF Î PF
NF = [6 ù (0.8)3] Î US$ 250,000
5.5 Î US$ 115,000
= 1423 contracts

6.3 Using Swaps

A swap is an agreement where two parties decide to exchange their cash flows according to specified rules at a specified future time. There are various types of swap contracts. They include interest rate swaps, currency swaps, credit swaps, commodity swaps and equity swaps.
  Banks often arrange interest rate swaps between counterparties. This may involve two parties agreeing to periodically exchange interest payment on a specified notional amount of principal. The bank receives a transaction fee for its services. If it guarantees payments to both parties, it is exposed to default risk (i.e. the possibility that one of the parties will default on its obligation). In that event, the bank must assume the role of that party and fulfil the obligation to the other party (Rose and Hudgins 2013; Saunders and Cornett 2014).
  At this point, we are interested in examining the mechanics of interest rate swaps and their use in hedging interest rate risk exposure on the balance sheet of banks.

142	6 Asset-Liability Management: Using Hedging Techniques

  An interest rate swap is an exchange of fixed-rate interest payments for floating- rate interest payments by two counterparties. The most common kind of swaps is the plain vanilla, fixed-for-floating interest rate swap. In a swap, the swap buyer agrees to make payments to the swap seller based on a fixed interest rate on periodic settlement dates. The swap seller, on the other hand, agrees to make payments to the buyer of the swap based on a floating rate on the same periodic settlement dates. The two counterparties involved in a swap transaction generally have a comparative advantage to the respective positions taken in the swap.
  In undertaking swap transactions, the bank that agrees to be a fixed-rate payer (i.e. swap buyer) is seeking to transform the variable- or floating-rate nature of its liabilities into fixed-rate liabilities in order to better match the fixed returns earned on its assets. On the other hand, the bank that is a variable-rate payer (swap seller) seeks to turn its fixed-rate liabilities into variable-rate liabilities to better match the variable returns on its assets.
  Suppose we have two banks, A and B. Bank A has borrowed US$100 million 4-year medium term notes with 10% fixed coupon rates. The funds were used to write commercial loans that have rates indexed to LIBOR (London Interbank Offered Rate). As a result, Bank A has a negative duration gap, as duration of assets is shorter than that of liabilities.
Thus, DA ù kDL < 0
To hedge the interest rate exposure of the bank against falling interest rate, Bank
A can reduce the duration or interest rate sensitivity of its liabilities by changing them to short-term floating-rate liabilities that better match the characteristics of its asset portfolio. The bank can achieve this change either on the balance sheet or through the off-balance sheet. If the bank chooses to use the off-balance sheet, it could sell an interest rate swap by entering into a swap agreement to make the floating-rate payment side of the transaction.
  The second party to this transaction is Bank B. Bank B has invested US$100 million in fixed long-term rate on residential mortgages. The bank is relying on a short-term variable-rate deposit of 1-year repricing (i.e. rate change every 1 year). Thus, Bank B has a positive duration gap, so then:
DA ù kDL > 0.
  Bank B can hedge its exposure to the interest rate by transforming its short-term floating-rate liabilities into fixed-rate liabilities to match the fixed-rate nature of its assets. In this case, the bank can enter into a swap to pay fixed-rate and receive floating-rate payments.
  The opposing nature of the balance sheets of Bank A and Bank B makes it possible for the two banks to enter into an interest rate swap agreement with Bank A making floating payment and B making fixed payment. Thus, Bank A is the seller and Bank B the buyer in this swap transaction.

6.3 Using Swaps	143

Example 6.3
Assume the notional amount of a swap is US$100 million (equal to the size of liability of Bank A and with 4 years to maturity). The annual coupon cost of these note liabilities is 10%. Bank AÆs problem is that the interest rates may rise, in which case the returns from its assets will fall and will not be enough to pay for the cost of the funds. On the other hand, the fixed returns on Bank BÆs mortgage portfolio may be insufficient to cover interest payment cost of its liabilities should interest rates rise. Thus, Bank B takes a position in the swap agreement that requires it to send a fixed-rate payment to Bank A and Bank A agrees to send a variable-rate payment to Bank B. 6.3.1 Macro Hedging with Swaps

The duration model, discussed earlier on futures, holds for swaps as well. A swap arrangement can be valued in the same manner as an equivalent valuation of a bond. In swaps, the fixed-rate side can be viewed as a T-bond with a fixed payment. Similarly, the floating-rate payments can be viewed as floating coupons on a bond
where coupons are linked to LIBOR every year. Thus, a change in the value of the swap, ?S, when interest rates rise will depend on the relative sensitivity of the fixed- rate bonds and the variable-rate bonds. In duration terms, this is (Dfixed ù Dfloat). Hence in general,
?S = ù(Dfixed ù Dfloat) Î Ns Î  ?R 
1 + R
where
?S = change in market value of the swap contract
(Dfixed ù Dfloat) = difference in durations between a government bond that has the maturity and coupon fixed payment side of the swap and a government bond that
has the same duration as the swap payment interval (e.g. annual floating payments)
Ns = notional value of swap contracts
 ?R  = shock to interest rates 1 + R
Note that as long as Dfixed > Dfloat, when interest rates rise, the market value of the
fixed payment will fall by more than the market value of the floating-rate payments;
in market value terms, the fixed-rate payers gain when interest rates rise and lose when rates fall.
  The loss on the balance sheet position should be enough to offset the gains from the swap contract.
Mathematically, ?S = ?E
Therefore, substituting and solving for the number of swap contracts to purchase,
we have:

Ns = (DA ù kDL) Î A
Dfixed ù Dfloat

Example 6.4
Assume DA = 5 years, DL = 3 years, k = 0.9 and asset, A = US$ 100 m. Suppose the current duration of the 10-year fixed rate on the swap is 6 years, while the duration of the floating-rate bond reprices annually (1 year). Calculate
the number of swap contracts to buy to fully hedge the balance sheet position.

6.4 Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we discussed how to use forwards, futures and swaps to hedge against interest rate risk. We mentioned that:
1. A forward contract is an agreement between a buyer and a seller at time zero to exchange a pre-specified asset for cash at a later date. A futures contract is an agreement between a buyer and a seller at time zero to exchange a standardised pre-specified asset for cash at a later date.
2. Banks can hedge interest rate risk exposure using futures contracts. This, they do either at the micro level (micro hedging) or at the macro level (macro hedging).
3. The number of futures contracts a bank will buy or sell in a macro hedge depends on the size and the direction of the interest rate exposure and the return-risk trade- off from fully or selectively hedging that risk.
4. The basis risk is the residual risk that occurs as a result of the fact that movement in the spot assetÆs price is not perfectly correlated with the movement in the price of the asset delivered under a futures or forward contract. This lack of perfect correlation is called the basis risk.
5. A swap is an agreement where two parties decide to exchange their cash flows according to specified rules at a specified future time. There are various types of swap contracts. They include interest rate swaps, currency swaps, credit swaps, commodity swaps and equity swaps.
6. Banks often arrange interest rate swaps between counterparties. This may involve two parties agreeing to periodically exchange interest payment on a specified notional amount of principal. The bank receives a transaction fee for its services. If it guarantees payments to both parties, it is exposed to default risk (i.e. the possibility that one of the parties will default on its obligation). In that event, the bank must assume the role of that party and fulfil the obligation to the other party.
7. An interest rate swap is an exchange of fixed-rate interest payments for floating- rate interest payments by two counterparties. The most common kind of swap is the plain vanilla, a fixed-for-floating interest rate swap. In a swap, the swap buyer agrees to make payments to the swap seller based on a fixed interest rate on periodic settlement dates. The swap seller, on the other hand, agrees to make payments to the buyer of the swap based on a floating rate on the same periodic settlement dates. The two counterparties involved in a swap transaction generally have a comparative advantage on the respective positions taken in the swap.
8. In undertaking swap transactions, the bank that agrees to be a fixed-rate payer (i.e. swap buyer) is seeking to transform the variable- or floating-rate nature of its liabilities into fixed-rate liabilities to better match the fixed returns earned on its assets. On the other hand, the bank that is a variable-rate payer (swap seller) seeks to turn its fixed-rate liabilities into variable-rate liabilities to better match the variable returns on its assets.

Off-Balance Sheet Activities

7.1 Introduction

The efforts of banks to regain a significant level of the market share that their traditional banking activities could not make possible have led to the influx of off-balance sheet activities (non-traditional banking activities) in the banking sector for more than a decade now. Off-balance sheet (OBS) activities are activities that affect banksÆ profit but are not formally captured in the financial statements. They encompass a variety of items including certain loan commitments, letters of credit and guarantees. They also include derivative transactions such as forwards, futures, forwards and options and swaps, whose notional values are carried off-balance sheet, but whose fair values are recorded on the balance sheet. OBS activities are also known as contingent assets and liabilities. OBS assets are items that, when a contingent event occurs, are captured on the asset side of the balance sheet, while off-balance sheet liabilities are items that, when a contingent event occurs, are captured on the liability side of the balance sheet.
  OBS assets and liabilities have complex contingent claims that make their exact market valuation very difficult. Managers of banks and financial institutions need to appreciate the general features of the risk exposure associated with each major OBS


® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_7

149

150	7  Off-Balance Sheet Activities

asset and liability as well as how each one can impact the return and profitability of a bank or financial institution (Saunders and Cornett 2014).
  In this chapter, we discuss the various types of off-balance sheet activities, the rationale and importance of off-balance sheet activities, risks associated with off-balance sheet activities, management and regulations of off-balance sheet activ- ities and how to report off-balance sheet activities.


7.2 Types of Off-Balance Sheet Activities

Capitalising on their individual competitive advantages, banks are able to generate some fee incomes by reducing transaction costs (transaction-cost advantage) for their customers, while reducing information that is made available to their deficit units or borrowers (information-cost advantage).
  In order to generate fee income through the reduction of transaction costs, banks may engage in quite a number of activities such as providing customised services
e.g. æprivatised bankingÆ, to a small but growing wealthy niche around the world. Other services may include providing securities to their customers backed by mortgages. A typical example of this will be the æspreadÆ, where the banks take up funds from surplus units and lend such funds to deficit units at a cost (interest rate), such that at the end of such a period, the funds given out plus interest rates are given back to the surplus units, minus a fee.
  On the other hand, banks tend to reduce the information made available to their deficit units borrowers, while engaging in off-balance sheet lending activities. The types of off-balance activities include guarantees (e.g. letters of credit), loan com- mitments, loan sales and derivatives.


7.2.1 Guarantee

This is an undertaking by a bank (the guarantor) to stand in to cover the current obligations of another entity should that entity fail to do so. It is also an acceptance of an obligation by a bank to pay the face value of a bill of exchange on maturity, normally by covering the sale of goods. Letters of credit is an example of guarantee.
Letter of Credit (LC) In an event where a bank client is assessed as being credit worthy, the bank can act as a guarantor to a contractual commitment between that client and a third party, whereby the LC issued by the guarantor (the bank) serves as a guarantee of payment or a payment of last resort. Banks earn fee income by charging a fee usually between 1 and 10% of the LC value for every year that the letter remains valid. Therefore, we define letter of credit as a document a bank issues on behalf of its customers, authorising a third party to draw drafts on the bank up to a certain specified amount on stipulated terms and conditions. It is a conditional

7.2 Types of Off-Balance Sheet Activities	151

commitment on the bankÆs part to provide payment on drafts drawn in accordance with the document terms. Of all the trade payment methods, this is the most secure, as the bank substitutes its own creditworthiness for that of the buyer/applicant. There are two types of letters of credit. These are the standby letter of credit and the commercial letter of credit.
Standby A standby letter of credit (SBLC) is an irrevocable commitment by the issuing bank to make payment to a particular beneficiary. The bank issuing the SBLC is obligated to guarantee for the benefit of a third party. SBLCs can either be financial-oriented or performance-oriented. In the case financial-oriented SBLCs, the account party is responsible for making payment to the beneficiary, whereas in the case of performance-oriented SBLCs, the account party performs a service. SBLCs should contain certain descriptive information and a separate binding agreement with the account party. SBLC should be labelled æstandby letter of creditÆ limited in amount to cover a specific time period and including relevant information. The bank is not a party to the contract between the account party and the beneficiary. SBLCs should not be worded to involve the bank in making determination of law. SBLCs, like loans, can be participated in and syndicated. However, the sale of an SBLC participation does not diminish the total contingent liability of the originating bank. Syndication represents a legal apportionment of liability. If one of the banks fails to fulfil its obligation under an SBLC, the remaining banks are not liable.
Commercial A commercial letter of credit is issued for the purpose of facilitating trade or commerce. Drafts are generally drawn when the transaction involved is consummated as planned. A commercial letter of credit is not sold for cash, but it represents a contingent liability. The difference between a commercial letter of credit and a standby letter of credit (SBLC) is that the commercial LC facilitates the sales of goods and is expected to be drawn upon by the beneficiary, while the SBLC is not, unless the account party defaults in meeting the beneficiary obligation.
Revocable or Irrevocable A revocable letter of credit may be amended or cancelled by the issuing bank at any time with or without prior notice to the beneficiary. An irrevocable letter of credit cannot be cancelled without the agreement of the issuing bank, confirming both bank and the beneficiary.
Letter of Credit Payment Terms An LC must state whether it is available by the following: Sight: it is paid when the document fully complies with the terms and conditions specified in the LC. Deferred: a deferred payment undertaking is given that the LC will be paid at maturity, for example, 90 days from the bill of lading shipment date. Acceptance: payment is made after an agreed term via a draft accepted by an issuing bank. Negotiation: the giving of value for draft or documents is by a bank authorised to negotiate.

152	7  Off-Balance Sheet Activities

7.2.1.1 Risk Associated with Guarantee

The main risk associated with a guarantee is default risk. The bank would have to make good on the loan in case of a default by the third party.



7.2.2 Loan Commitments

These are arrangements where, for a defined period, banks decide to commit to extending loan facilities or a specified sum to borrowers, for a specific purpose and at a specified fee. For instance, a bank may have entered into an agreement to lend US
$5 million during the year to a company. The company may have drawn down US$4 million. This means it can still draw another US$1 million if it requires funds. The US$1 million represents an unused commitment. This off-balance sheet item could come on to the balance sheet at a later date. The bank simply agrees to provide a line of credit or an overdraft facility, but has the option to withdraw such a facility under certain circumstances, such as a fall in the credit quality of the potential borrower. Borrowers also have the right to decide whether or not they want to take the loan.
  Loan commitments could be irrevocable or revocable. Irrevocable commitments are commitments that cannot be revoked or cancelled by the bank under any circumstance. The bank is therefore under obligation to fulfil its promise to the potential borrower. Revocable commitments are commitments that can be cancelled by the bank and the bank is not under any obligation to honour its promise to the borrower.
  The participating bank earns a fee, which is split in two parts: upfront fee and non-usage fee.
Upfront Fee This fee is charged to the borrower by the bank as a payment for its service before the commitment is executed. The payment of this fee is made one time only.
Non-usage Fee Since the bank has committed a specified sum to this cause, the funds cannot be used in generating other incomes for the bank (non-interest bearing). Hence, a periodic fee is charged to the borrower for the part of total sum unused. Since the commitment is mostly based on the borrowerÆs creditworthiness, the bankÆs commitment to lend ceases if the borrowerÆs financial condition deteriorates below some specified level or is later found to pose as a significant credit risk.


7.2.2.1 Risks Associated with Loan Commitments

The bank may be confronted with a number of risks in relation to loan commitments, including:

7.2 Types of Off-Balance Sheet Activities	153

Liquidity and Funding Risk The bank faces liquidity risk if all the borrowers to whom such commitments are made decide to borrow the money at the same time, which could result in excessive liquidity pressure on the bank.
Interest Rate Risk Interest rate risk is a contingent risk that arises as a result of the bank pre-committing to make available loans to the loan applicant over the stipulated time period at either fixed interest rate as a fixed-rate loan commitment or at a variable rate as a variable-rate loan commitment.
Credit Risk Banks also face a degree of contingent credit risk in setting the interest rate on a loan commitment. The interest charged could be based on the risk premium. However, with time, the credit rating of the borrower could fall. This could lead to an excessive loan default.
Take Down Risk In making loan commitments, the bank must always stand ready to provide the maximum of the loan amount, but the borrower is under no obligation to borrow that amount. This results in a risk to the bank because the excess amount that the borrower does not take could have been invested to generate returns for the bank.



7.2.3 Loan Sales

In securitisation, financial intermediaries such as banks sell loans or securities directly to investors (buyers) through markets, instead of holding them. A loan sale, also known as secondary loan participations, is a financial contract by which a bank agrees to sell the future returns from an underlying bank loan to a third party, without a clear contractual remedy, insurance or any credit improvement. In selling loans, banks put their reputations on the line rather than their equity capital. The investors may buy the loans without recourse to the bank, and in the event that the actual loans go bad, the loans sales do not become off-balance sheet contingent liability, since the investors bear the default risk on the loans. However, if the loans are sold with recourse, then the bank (seller) bears the default risk on the loans and the loans sales are deemed off-balance sheet contingent liability. This means, in the event that the loans go bad, the investors have recourse to the bank and can put the loans back on the bankÆs balance sheet.


7.2.3.1 Risk Associated with Loans Sales

The main risk associated with loan sales is contingent credit risk. Loans sold with recourse present a long-term contingent credit risk to the seller (bank). This is because, if the credit quality of the loans falls, the buyer holds the option to sell the loan back to the bank that originated the loans.

154	7  Off-Balance Sheet Activities

7.2.4 Derivatives

Derivatives are financial instruments whose values are derived from the values of underlying asset. The underlying asset may, for example, be oil or a companyÆs stock. The common types of derivatives include futures contracts, forward contracts, options and swaps. They are widely used to speculate on the future expectations or to reduce risk.
Forward Contracts Forward contracts are arrangements by two parties to engage in a financial transaction at a future point in time. These contracts are normally negotiated with a commercial bank that allows a customer to purchase or sell a specified amount of a particular security at a specified rate on a specified future date. Forward contracts are private agreements between two parties, and because they are customised, it is very difficult to resell to someone else.
Futures Contracts Financial futures contracts are standardised agreements to deliver or receive a specified amount of a specified financial instrument at a specified price and date. Financial futures contracts are usually traded on an organised exchange. A futures contract is a forward contract that has been standardised and sold through an organised exchange. A futures contract stipulates that the seller (i.e. the party that takes the short position) is supposed to deliver a given quantity of the commodity or financial asset to the buyer (i.e. the party that takes the long position) on a specified date, known as the settlement or delivery date, at an agreed price. No payments are made initially when the contract is agreed upon. The seller, who takes that short position, benefits from a fall in the price of the underlying asset, while the buyer, who takes the long position, benefits from a rise in the price of the asset.
Options Contracts Options contracts give the owner the right and not the obliga- tion to purchase or sell a specified financial instrument for a specified price within a specified period of time. There are two forms of options contractsùcall or put options. The call options contract grants the owner the right to buy, while the put options contract grants the owner the right to sell the underlying security at a specified price and within a specified period.
Swaps These are contracts between two parties who have agreed to exchange future obligation at a specified rate. It is also an agreement between two parties to exchange exposure to a specific risk.


7.2.4.1 Risks Associated with Derivatives

The risks associated with derivatives include interest rate risk, foreign exchange risk and credit risk.

7.3 Reasons and Importance of Off-Balance Sheet Activities	155

Interest Rate Risk When interest rates are changing, the value of the derivatives is affected because interest rates are inversely related to the value of the financial instrument.
Foreign Exchange Risk Changes in the foreign exchange rate could affect the value of currency futures, forwards and swaps.
Credit Risk Credit risk occurs because of the potential for the counter party to default on the payment obligations under a derivative contract. This arises in a situation that would require the bank to replace the contract at the current market prices and rates, potentially at a loss.



7.3 Reasons and Importance of Off-Balance Sheet Activities

There are reasons accounting for the growth of off-balance sheet activities in banks. First, there is increased volatility, resulting in firmsÆ demand for risk management instruments from banks. The continuous changes in interest rates and exchange rates expose banks, businesses and individual investors to interest rate and exchange rate risks. Such risks may give rise to increased demand for services from banks to manage the risks leading to the growth of off-balance sheet activities.
  Another reason is banksÆ interest in saving capital and circumventing reserve requirements. Central banks usually require that banks meet capital adequacy requirements in order to continue operation. To meet such requirements, banks have to deposit a certain percentage of their deposits, which are on-balance sheet items with the central bank. Off-balance sheet activities enable banks to increase their funds in the form of fees, which do not fall under capital adequacy requirement, thus enabling them to save more capital.
  Also, reduction in the spreads in traditional lending activities of banks is pushing many of them to engage in more profitable off-balance sheet activities. Since banks can generate income from off-balance sheet activities, they are in a position to reduce their interest rates on loans and can, therefore, use the additional income to cover part of their operating expenses. Seeking more profitable off-balance sheet activities makes banks more competitive since they do not have to pass on all their costs to the customers.
  Off-balance sheet activities contribute significantly to a steady increase in incomes generated by banks. Contemporary banks that use off-balance sheet activ- ities are in a position to generate higher profit. In Africa, by moving towards the deregulations of most financial systems, banks are now developing other sources of income through off-balance sheet activities. The most dominant off-balance sheet activities are fees and charges on financial transactions. Most African countries are moving towards competitive banking, and it is expected that off-balance sheet activities will increase as a result.

156	7  Off-Balance Sheet Activities

7.4 Management and Regulations of OBS Activities

Off-balance sheet activities in banks are regulated just like all other activities of banks. However, the nature of the regulation may differ from one jurisdiction to the other. In the USA, for instance, the banking industry is regulated at both the state and federal levels. In Ghana, the banking industry is regulated by Bank of Ghana under the Bank of Ghana Act 2002 (Act 612) and the Bank of Ghana (Amendment) Act 2016 (Act 918), the Banks and Specialised Deposit-Taking Institutions Act 2016 (Act 930) as well as the individual bankÆs internal governance and control and systems. Since the off-balance sheet activities are not stated in the financial state- ments, but constitute a much greater significance of banksÆ assets than traditional banking does, it is prudent that the credit risks associated with these activities be dealt with.
  The central bank can increase the level of required reserves of banks in order to reduce their pool of excess reserves from which they engage in off-balance sheet activities. Subsequently, banks should be required to include off-balance sheet activities in their comprehensive management risk systems and set risk limits in the arguments/claims for conducting such activities. Furthermore, the central bank would have to ensure that banks comply with the generally accepted accounting principles (GAAP) and the Securities and Exchange CommissionÆs (SEC) require- ments in properly disclosing information on such off-balance activities in their statement of financial position (SoFP).



7.5 Ways of Reporting OBS on General Ledger of Banks

Off-balance sheet fees can improve the earning ratios at a faster pace than on-balance sheet fees. The volume and risk of the off-balance sheet activities are considered in the evaluation of capital adequacy, but the regulatory issue is off-balance sheet can subject a bank to certain risk, including credit risk as a result of default by clients. Derivative contracts, for instance, must be reported at a fair value basis on the balance sheet. An evaluation of off-balance sheet lending activities should apply the same technique as the evaluation of direct loans.



7.6 Summary and Conclusions

The chapter examined off-balance sheet activities. Banks, in the event of seeking profit and increasing ownersÆ wealth, have gone beyond traditional banking activi- ties leading to the influx of off-balance sheet activities (non-traditional banking activities). Off-balance sheet activities encompass a variety of items including

References	157

certain guarantees, like letters of credit, loan commitments, loan sales and deriva- tives. We discussed that:
1. In order to generate fee income through the reduction of transaction costs, banks may engage in quite a number of activities such as providing customised services.
2. The reasons accounting for the growth of off-balance sheet activities are increased volatility, giving rise to demand for risk management by companies, interest rates changing continuously, etc.
3. Off-balance sheet activities contribute to a significantly steady increase in incomes generated by banks.
4. There are risks associated with the extra income earned by banks through their various off-balance sheet activities.
5. The central bank can increase the level of required reserves of banks in order to reduce their pool of excess reserves from which they engage in off-balance sheet activities.
6. Off-balance sheet fees can improve the earning ratios at a faster pace than on-balance sheet fees.
7. The volume and risk of off-balance sheet activities need to be considered in the evaluation of capital adequacy.
Discussion Questions and Problems
1. Explain and give examples of off-balance sheet activities.
2. Discuss the importance of off-balance sheet activities.
3. What are the trade-offs in off-balance sheet activities?
4. Discuss the management and regulations of off-balance sheet activities.
