8.1 Introduction

Banks, like other depository institutions, are in the business of accepting deposits and giving out loans to their customers. The loans given to these customers, including firms, governments and individuals, constitute the bankÆs loan portfolio (which are part of its assets), and the interests charged on these loans represent their main source of revenue (Arnold 2014). Providing loans to customers, especially to the private sector, is important for financing investment in the sector to enhance economic growth. The process of lending to these customers is, however, associated with huge risks, as evidenced by the global financial crisis. Also, in Africa, a number of banks have gone under due to bad management of these loans resulting in high non-performing loans in the books of these banks. In Ghana, the takeover of UT Bank and Capital Bank by GCB Bank was due to huge bad loans in the books of the two banks leading to these banks becoming financially distressed. Proper review and monitoring of banksÆ loan portfolio is extremely important in protecting these assets. In this chapter, we look at bank loans and the factors that affect the growth and mix of bank loans. We also examine the risks associated with lending, banksÆ


® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_8

161

162	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

lending process and how credit applications are analysed. The chapter also focusses on loan agreements and the resolution of problem loans or loan workout.



8.2 Bank Loans

Banks provide all kinds of loans to their customers for various purposes, ranging from the buying of vehicles, furniture and fittings and financing education to the expansion of a business. These bank loans can be categorised as follows:
Individual loansùThese are loans given to individuals to finance the purchase of vehicles and home appliances and to cover personal expenses such as medical care.
Corporate loansùThese are loans granted to businesses to finance investment projects, acquire fixed assets, purchase inventories and cover certain expenses such as wages and salaries, administrative costs and other operating expenses.
Agricultural loansùThese loans are granted to farmers to help them in their farming activities such as planting, harvesting and storage of crops as well as caring for and feeding of livestock.
Financial institution loansùThese are loans given to banks, insurance companies, finance houses and other financial institutions to help facilitate their operations, expand their services and meet their obligations to their customers.
Mortgage/Real estate loansùThese are loans backed by real estate property such as land, buildings and other structures. They include loans for the construction of buildings and land development and for financing the purchase of residential or commercial property.
Lease financing receivablesùThis is a financing arrangement where a bank buys equipment or vehicles and leases them to its customers. Lease financing helps firms to acquire the needed machinery and equipment for their operations.



8.2.1 Determinants of the Growth and Composition of Bank Loans

As mentioned earlier, banks give out a variety of loans. The composition of loans held by one bank differs from what is held by another bank and this is due to a number of factors, including:
ò Size of the bank
ò Official loan policy of the bank
ò Experience and expertise of management in making different types of loans
ò Return and risk profile on the type of loan
ò Profile characteristics of the market area the bank serves

8.3 Regulating Bank Lending	163

  Size of the bank is an important determinant of loan portfolio mix. The size of a bank determines how much loan can be made to the borrower. Larger banks tend to have huge capital and therefore can give out a greater proportion of their loans in huge amounts to corporate organisations and high net worth households. In contrast, smaller banks normally focus on providing retail credit in smaller amounts to households as well as small businesses.
  Another key determinant of loan portfolio composition is the official loan policy of the bank. Some banks decide as a matter of policy to give out certain types of loans. A bank may decide to specialise in mortgage lending. This may prevent the bank officials from also giving out some other types of loans.
  The experience and expertise of management in making different types of loans is also important in influencing the mix or composition of the bankÆs loan portfolio.
  The bankÆs loan composition is also dependent on the expected returns on each type of loan vis-Ó-vis the risk associated with the particular loan. A bank may grant more of a particular type of loan if the expected return on the loan is higher with a reasonable level of risk compared to other types of loans or assets the bank could invest in.
  Finally, the profile characteristic of the market area the bank serves also contrib- utes in determining the bankÆs loan portfolio composition. A bank serving a sub-urban area with a large number of single family homes and small retail stores is likely to have its portfolio consisting of real estate, car and personal loans. However, a bank situated in a central business district surrounded by office buildings and manufacturing companies will have more of its bank loans made up of business loans.
Quick Quiz
1. Discuss the different types of loans banks provide.
2. What factors account for the growth and composition of bank loans?



8.3 Regulating Bank Lending

Banks and other financial institutions that are in the business of lending are usually heavily regulated because of the considerable impact their activities tend to have on the domestic economy as well as the global economy. The loan portfolios of banks are closely regulated and reviewed by the regulatory authority in order to minimise risk and improve safety in the banking industry. In line with that, the nature and extent to which regulation is implemented largely influence the composition, quality and yield of the loan book of a given bank. The regulator may restrict or even prohibit certain loans, and the bankÆs loan policy may also restrict credit officers from granting other loans (Rose and Hudgins 2013).
  There are many examples of such restrictions. Firstly, banks are often not allowed to grant loans for which the shares of those same banks are used as collateral. If not,

164	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

the default on loans of that nature may cause the sale of the shares, which would in turn adversely affect the value of the bankÆs shares and the bank as a whole. Also, the bankÆs legal lending limit requires that a loan that is more than a certain per cent of the unimpaired capital and surplus account of a bank must not be granted to a single customer. The percentage may vary depending on whether the loan is secured or unsecured. This is meant to reduce the risk exposure from a single customer. For instance, in Ghana, Section 62 of the Banks and Specialised Deposit-Taking Insti- tutions Acts 2016 (Act 930) requires banks not to lend more than 25% of the net owned funds of the bank to a person or group of connected persons. Where the loan is unsecured, the total lending shall not exceed 10% of the bankÆs net owned funds. There are also restrictions on lending to bank staff. The bank may be restricted from lending on preferential terms to an employee of the bank unless the lending is part of an approved employee package. The regulator may require loans to bank staff to be priced at market value as opposed to their being subsidised by the bank. See for instance the Banks and Specialised Deposit-Taking Institutions Acts 2016 (Act 930) in Ghana, the Banks and Other Financial Institutions Act (BOFIA) in Nigeria, the Banking and Financial Services Act No. 7 of 2017 in Zambia, the US Sarbanes-
Oxley Act of 2002 and other national banking laws).
  Loan regulation is also necessary as a way of preventing discrimination against some customers trying to seek credit. Such regulation may prevent banks or lending institutions from denying loan applicants credit by virtue of their age, gender, ethnicity, religion or political affiliation. (See the Equal Credit Opportunity Act (1974) in the USA.)
  Disclosure laws require banks to make known to the borrower all costs associated with a loan, including the interest rate on the loan, commitment fees and all required charges, before the loan agreement is signed.
  Central banks may also institute certain regulations, especially for banks that are into international lending, because of the political and business risks these banks face in foreign markets. The regulations may require banks to disclose their credit exposure to a single country when this is more than a certain percentage of their capital or assets. Such regulations are necessary in order to reduce the risk exposure that may arise when loans are made to foreign entities.
  In reviewing banksÆ lending, the regulatory authority is often particular about the quality of loans as well as the soundness of banksÆ lending policies. The review process typically entails each bank being assigned a numerical rating based on the quality of the bankÆs loan portfolio. The rating system has the following: (1) connotes strong performance, (2) stands for satisfactory performance, (3) is fair performance,
(4) is marginal performance and (5) suggests unsatisfactory performance. Banks that score very high rating with respect to their loan quality are less, frequently subject to the central bankÆs examination.
  As part of their examination, officials from the central bankÆs banking supervision department classify the loans into standard or current loans, specially mentioned loans, substandard loans, doubtful loans and loss loans (Sinkey 2002; Rose and Hudgins 2013).

8.3 Regulating Bank Lending	165

Standard loansùthese are loans for which the borrower is up to date (i.e. current) with repayments of both principal and interest. Such loans are fully secured by cash and cash substitutes.
Specially mentioned loans or other loans especially mentionedùthese are loans protected by adequate security, as to both principal and interest, but they are potentially weak and constitute an undue credit risk. It is a warning to manage- ment to monitor the credit carefully and to ensure that the bankÆs risk exposure is minimised.
Substandard loansùthese loans display well-defined credit weaknesses that jeopar- dise the liquidation of the debt. These include loans to borrowers whose cash flow is not sufficient to meet currently maturing debt, loans to borrowers who are significantly undercapitalised and loans to borrowers lacking sufficient working capital to meet their operating needs. Substandard advances are not protected by the current sound worth, nor the paying capacity of the customer.
Doubtful loansùloans that have the same weaknesses as the substandard loans with the added characteristics that the loans are not well-secured, and the weaknesses make collection or liquidation in full, on the basis of currently existing facts, conditions and values, highly questionable and improbable.
Loss loansùadvances that are considered uncollectable and of such little value that their continuation as recoverable advances is not warranted.
  The officials will typically multiply the total of current loans by, say 1%, total of other loans especially mentioned by 10%, total of substandard loans by 25%, total of doubtful loans by 50% and total of loss loans by 100%, and then find the weighted sum and compare that with a bankÆs loan loss provision. If the weighted sum is too high in relation to the loan loss provision, the supervision officials may require either a revision in the bankÆs lending policy or an increase in the loan loss provision by the bank.
  The quality of a bankÆs loan portfolio is an important element of its asset quality, which is part of the bankÆs performance. The central bank assigns numerical ratings to six dimensions of performance: the capital adequacy, asset quality, management quality, earnings record, liquidity position and sensitivity to market risk exposure. These six aspects of banksÆ performance are combined into what is known as the CAMELS rating. The CAMELS rating is used to evaluate the overall performance of banks and to determine their strengths and weaknesses. Using the CAMELS rating system, the banking supervision officers or examiners rate the banks on a scale of one (1) to five (5), where one (1) is the best and five (5) is the worst. The higher the rating assigned to a bank, i.e. one (1), two (2) or three (3), the less frequently it will be subject to review and examination by the central bank, while banks with overall rating of four (4) or five (5) are examined more frequently. The CAMELS as an acronym is defined as follows.
ò Capital adequacyùThis measures the bankÆs capacity in handling the losses as well as meeting all obligations towards customers of the bank without stopping its operations. It can be met by the amount and quality of capital the bank can access.

166	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

The capital to risk weighted assets ratio can be used to determine the capital adequacy of the bank.
ò Asset qualityùThis measures the quality and performance of all the assets of the bank including current and fixed loans, investments, real estates and off-balance sheet transactions. The ratio of gross non-performing loans to gross advances is an important criterion for evaluating the effectiveness of the bankÆs lending decisions.
ò Management qualityùThis measures the effectiveness of the bankÆs board of directors and management in ensuring efficient management of the bankÆs oper- ations. It examines managementÆs ability to ensure the safe operation of the bank as it complies with the necessary regulations, both internal and external. Assess- ment of management quality is important in determining whether the bank is in a position to react to financial stress, which is reflected by the capacity of manage- ment to manage and control risks.
ò EarningsùThis measures the bankÆs level of efficiency in generating income to cover its expenses/commitments.
ò Liquidity positionùThis measures the bankÆs ability to convert assets into cash. The ratio of cash maintained by banks and balanced with the central bank to total assets is a measure of the liquidity of the bank.
ò Sensitivity to market exposureùThis measures the bankÆs sensitivity towards changing market conditions, such as how adverse changes in exchange rates, interest rate, asset prices and commodity prices affect the bank. It covers how particular risk exposures can affect the bank.
  The CAMELS rating is useful for evaluating the overall financial position of the bank and for the necessary corrective actions, if any, to be taken (Rose and Hudgins 2013).



8.3.1 Elements of a Written Loan Policy

The board of directors of a bank have the ultimate responsibility for all loans made, and regulating the lending process requires establishing a written loan policy. A written loan policy outlines the specific guidelines and principles for the bankÆs lending activities (i.e. the kind of loans it can give out and what the lending process should entail as well as the actual composition of the bankÆs loan portfolio). A written loan policy enables loan officials to know the procedure they must follow in giving out loans. Through a loan policy, the bank is able to obtain a portfolio, which is in line with other set objectives of the bank such as promoting profitability and controlling its risk exposure. A loan policy varies from one bank to the other but must contain the following important elements:
1. Statements of Lending: A statement specifying the loan portfolio in terms of the type of loans, maturities, quality and the size of loans.

8.4 Stages of the Bank Lending Process	167

2. Establish a Lending Authority: This should clearly define what type of loan and maximum loan amount each loan officer or committee is authorised to approve.
3. Establish Lines of Responsibility: It is ensuring that assignments are made, and all the information is reported to its department.
4. Operating Procedure: Specification of appropriate operating procedures for soliciting, reviewing, evaluating and making decisions on customer loan applications.
5. Required Documents: Relevant documents to accompany each loan application must be obtained and properly filed.
6. Lines of Authority: It should define who has the responsibility for maintaining and reviewing the bankÆs credit files.
7. Guidelines: There should be proper guidelines regarding how to take a loan, evaluate it and perfect the loan collateral.
8. Policies and Procedures: Policies and procedures are required for setting interest rates, fees on loans and repayment of loans.
9. Establish Quality Standards: A statement of quality standards applicable to all loans. A loan application should be declined if the applicant does not meet the standards.
10. Establishing Upper Limit to Loans: A statement specifying the upper limit to a loan beyond which a loan cannot be granted.
11. Define its Community: A statement describing the bankÆs principal trade area where it may want to give out loans.
12. Trouble Loan: An indication of the procedures for detecting, analysing and working out problem loan situations.


8.4 Stages of the Bank Lending Process

The lending process of banks involves various stages, and these include:
1. Identifying the Prospective Loan Applicants
The first stage in the lending process involves finding prospective loan customers. Mostly, in the case of loans to individuals, the customers themselves approach banksÆ loan officers who ask them to complete loan application forms. Business loan applications, however, are mostly due to loan officers approaching firms to prospect for new business. Most loan officers fill out a customer contact report when they visit a prospective customerÆs place of business, and this report is updated after every visit to the prospective customer. This exercise provides the next loan officer with vital information regarding the prospective customer.
2. Appraising Loan ApplicantÆs Character and Purpose of the Loan
After a prospective client takes a decision to apply for a loan, the loan officer arranges a meeting with the customer. This meeting gives the customer the oppor- tunity to explain his or her reason(s) for deciding to take the loan. To the loan officer, the meeting gives him/her the opportunity to assess the prospective clientÆs character

168	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

and sincerity of purpose. If the loan officer determines that the customer is insincere in indicating the reason for the loan and is unlikely to comply with the terms of the loan, this could be used as a good reason for declining the loan application.
3. Regularly Visiting the Loan ApplicantÆs Premises to Ascertain Credit Record A firmÆs loan application may require the loan officer visiting the firmÆs premise to make certain observations. This may include the firmÆs operations, the riskiness of the firmÆs operations and an assessment of the firmÆs likelihood of success. These site visits would enable the loan officer to assess the customerÆs location and the condition of the property in the business and to seek some clarifications. Other providers of credit to this customer may be contacted for further information about the creditworthiness of the customer. The bank may want to find out whether the customer adhered to all terms in previous loans acquired, and whether all the required payments were made. This information about the customer from previous credit providers will help the bank assess and predict the customerÆs character, sincerity of purpose and sense of responsibility and likelihood to default.
4. Assessing Loan ApplicantÆs Financial Conditions
When the prospects of a loan application look bright, at this stage, the customer is asked to submit the necessary documents that the bank will require in evaluating the loan application. Documents required by the bank include financial statements and, in the case of a company, board of directorsÆ resolution authorising the negotiation of a loan with the lender. The bankÆs credit analysis department then conducts a thorough financial analysis of the applicantÆs financial statements. This is to deter- mine whether the applicant has sufficient cash flows and backup assets to repay the loan. After all the necessary documents have been submitted, the credit analysis department analyses and evaluates the customer based on the documents received, as well as any other information. The recommendations are then made to the appropri- ate loan committee as to whether the loan request can be granted or not.
5. Evaluating Collateral Supporting the Loan and Signing the Loan Agreement If the customerÆs loan application is approved, the loan officer or credit committee checks to make sure that the collateral pledged to the loan is in good condition and that its value is enough to pay for the loan in case of default and assesses whether the bank has immediate access and title to the collateral in case of default. This is known as perfecting the lenderÆs claim to collateral. To the extent that the loan officer and committee are satisfied that the loan and the proposed collateral are sound, all parties to the agreement then sign all documents that constitute the loan agreement.
6. Monitoring to Ensure Loan Agreement Is Complied with
The lending process does not end after the signing of the loan documents. The agreement is rather monitored to ensure that the terms of the loan agreement are being complied with and that payment of principal and interests is being made as expected. In the case of corporate loans, the loan officer makes regular visits to the customer to ascertain how he or she is utilising the funds for the intended purpose, in order to avoid moral hazard. The visit is also useful in finding out any needs of the

8.4 Stages of the Bank Lending Process	169

borrowing firm. This information is summarised into a customer profile and is very helpful in assessing the customerÆs progress and other financial requirements.
Quick Quiz
1. What are the stages involved in the lending process?
2. What are the elements of written loan policy?
3. How do you determine capital adequacy of a bank?


8.4.1 Risks Associated with Lending

Banks at every point in time grapple with different types of risks, including credit risk, market risk, interest rate risk, liquidity risk, price risk, foreign exchange risk, transaction risk, compliance risk, strategic risk, reputation risk and country risk. Risk is an uncertainty that can adversely affect banksÆ capital reserves and/or earnings. Therefore, banks need to engage in risk management practices which involve identifying potential risks and taking actions to mitigate them, should they occur. Some risks are easily identifiable or predictable but others not so much. It therefore behoves on these banks to put in place structures to mitigate external and internal shocks that result from these risks (ComptrollerÆs Handbook 1998). We discuss how these types of risks are related to the lending activities of banks.


8.4.1.1 Credit Risk

Banks are in the business of providing loans to borrowers or deficit units who need funds for investment. The business of lending raises a challenge, when borrowers are unable to pay back the loan. This brings about credit risk. Other forms of credit risk include borrower risk, industry risk and portfolio risk. There is always some level of risk of default, since banks cannot determine with 100% accuracy a borrowerÆs intention. This leads to adverse selection and moral hazard. Credit risk is not linked only to loans but also to other bank products like overdrafts, letters of credit, derivatives and some off-balance sheet activities. Banks adopt a credit rating mech- anism to mitigate this risk, but it comes with its own challenges. For example, loan applicants with good credit ratings are not immune to unforeseen external shocks that could make them default on the loan. Other methods used by the banks include incorporating prudent underwriting standards, performing due diligence, hiring competent staff and setting up polices for lending and others. After a loan is granted, the bank can still manage its credit risk by properly structuring the loan and restructuring when it becomes necessary to ensure the loan is paid. When this is left undone, it could affect the loan repayment even if it was a good loan. It is also important for banks to focus not only on individual loans but also on the entire loan portfolio as well to have a holistic view. This will improve the banksÆ management of risk.

170	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

  To effectively manage the credit risk associated with the loan portfolio, the bank as an organisation must identify its risk profile. The board of directors and manage- ment must assess the risk level and risk culture of the bank. To do this, management must perform a thorough assessment of the bankÆs loan portfolio by examining the composition and possible risk factors that may occur. Management must understand fully all-inherent risks and internal and external shocks that can affect the loan portfolio. They must also understand the concentration of the loan with respect to industry and geography, risk levels and other macro factors. They must ensure the soundness of the policies, processes and practices necessary to control the risks associated with the loans and that credit officers comply with these. Credit officers need to be properly trained to implement the credit policies of the banks.


8.4.1.2 Market Risk

Market risk arises due to change in cash flows of a financial instrument resulting from changes in market prices. This has been mainly attributed to the modernisation of banks. In the quest for banks to expand and improve on the intermediation function, they began facing risks such as fluctuations in interest rates, exchange rates and other market variables. Market risks comprise interest rate risk, liquidity risk, price risk, currency risk and transaction risk. These are further elaborated below.


Interest Rate Risk

Changes in interest rate affect the bankÆs lending practices. The composition of the bankÆs loan portfolio and the terms of its loans, like maturity, rate structure and embedded options, expose the bankÆs revenue stream to interest rate fluctuations. Ideally, when interest rates fall, borrowers will refinance their loans, thus paying off the old loans and taking on a new one at the prevailing rate, which affects the banksÆ cash flow stream, as future interest payments are curtailed. Interest rate changes are difficult to predict and if interest rates are high, people borrow less, all things being equal. Therefore, banks have to well manage their loan pricing and portfolio maturity to mitigate interest rate risk. To do this, banks can stress test their lending portfolio to see their sensitivity to interest rate changes. Pricing, lending and portfolio maturity decisions should be made while factoring in interest rate effect. Caveats should be stipulated in the loan policy that protects the bankÆs interest in interest rate fluctuations. The Asset and Liability Management Committee (ALCO), made up of the banks staff, are typically responsible for assessing the bankÆs interest rate risk and the management of its risk profile. They have the documentation on the bankÆs loan portfolio, pipeline and maturing loans as well as trends, which they use to aid in their assessment. In so doing, banks are able to hedge against this risk. It is also not uncommon for banks to shift interest rate risk to their borrowers by

8.4 Stages of the Bank Lending Process	171

structuring loans with clauses that protect them in the onset of any fluctuations. Some loans are also issued at variable interest rates.


Liquidity Risk

Liquidity is simply the ability of an institution to meet its short-term obligations as and when they fall due. Others have added that it is not only the ability to meet short- term debts, but to do so without a significant loss in value. Liquidity risk is the oppositeùthat is the inability to meet short-term obligations without a significant loss in value. In the context of banking, liquidity risk is the inability of a bank to meet withdrawal requests without much loss in value. Banks, in as much as they need to give out more loans to make profits, must not end up giving out all its capital in the form of loans, considering that deposits are short term in nature and depositors may come making withdrawal requests. In a situation where a bank is unable to meet its current liquidity needs, it would send a wrong signal to the market and that can have a ripple effect on the whole financial market. This has the tendency to cause bank runs. During the global financial crises, there were a number of bank failures, as banks could not meet the high liquidity requirement caused by bank runs. Since then, there have been more stringent measures put on banks to ensure they can meet their liquidity needs. These include regulations like Basel I, II and III. Also, it is now very important for banks to engage in liquidity risk management. This involves strategies banks use to assess their ability to meet short-term obligations without having to dispose of long-term assets at a cost lower than would be expected. They assess how much cash or collateral they can make available on a short notice. They also put in place policies, procedures and actions to help mitigate the risk associated with liquidity.
  A bankÆs liquidity management should include identifying loans that can be converted into cash with ease. A loanÆs liquidity depends on factors such as the quality, pricing and maturity of the loan. It also depends on how easy a bank can sell the loan to other entities and terms involved. The level of loan commitments made and how much borrowers actually draw against such loan commitments can also affect a bankÆs liquidity. The bank needs to have a system that tracks such commit- ments and the amount borrowers eventually use or draw. The bank needs to have adequate knowledge of the types of loan commitments, deals in the pipeline, normal usage levels and historically high usage levels in order to ascertain whether its liquidity position is good for normal, seasonal or emergency needs. Appropriate management of information systems should help in distinguishing between loan commitments that are legally binding and those that are not.


Price Risk

Bank lending activities in the past were not directly affected by price risk, as banksÆ main form of business did not involve trading at an agreed price. Bank loans were

172	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

usually held to maturity, requiring book value accounting treatment. Over time as the market developed, banks also started to engage more in active portfolio manage- ment. With expansion in the loans market, loan portfolios became sensitive to price risk. Securitised loans, for instance, that are being processed can suffer from price risk if they take long at the pipeline stage. Most price risk in banks occurs at the pipeline stage when banks have agreed upon the price of a service and conditions change, making the price unfavourable to the banks. Banks involved in international lending are mostly affected by price changes in the secondary market for such loans.


Foreign Exchange Risk

Foreign exchange risk or currency risk is that risk associated with devaluation or depreciation of a currency or fluctuation in exchange rates. This affects the value of banksÆ foreign assets and investments, interest payments and debt size denominated in a foreign currency. Fluctuations in foreign exchange affect bank loans that are issued in foreign currency. Banks also suffer foreign exchange risk when they engage in multicurrency transactions. Changes in foreign exchange can have a significant effect on account balances, which can greatly hamper the banksÆ revenue. Also, macroeconomic shocks like political instability, election uncertainty and financial crises can cause the exchange rate to fall. This implies that banks will have to readjust prior financial commitments between them and their customers. This could have adverse consequences on the banks. To hedge against this risk, banks usually adopt hedging techniques to peg the currencies at an agreed level.


Transaction Risk

Transaction risk is a form of exchange rate risk that occurs within the banks in the processing, filling and approval of transactions. When banks perform a service like granting a mortgage loan, the prevailing rate is adopted for the transactions. But within the time of processing to delivery of the service, the market rate may have changed. The level of transaction risk depends on the adequacy of information and on technological systems, policy and controls, the level of expertise of the human resource and the swiftness of the organisational process. When these structures are inefficient, the bank could lose a significant amount of money. For example, when a bankÆs computers are slow, it will mean fewer transactions are processed and more time is wasted. This is cost to the bank. Also, inefficiencies in the keeping of documentations like customer records, signature records and collateral documents can also have an adverse impact on the bank.

8.4 Stages of the Bank Lending Process	173

8.4.1.3 Compliance Risk

This is the risk of legal or regulatory sanctions, financial loss or damage to reputation as a result of non-compliance with regulatory requirements. The banking sector is arguably the most regulated industry, particularly in developing economies. This is partially because such economies are mainly bank based rather than stock market based. For banks to meet this compliance may mean more financial commitments into infrastructure, capital and others. Failure to comply with rules may also mean hefty fines, lawsuits and withdrawal of licences. Some of this compliance banks must observe includes maximum limits given to a single borrower, requirements of deposits to the central bank, capital adequacy, minimum capital requirement, etc. Banks are also subject to regular supervision, consumer protection and community reinvestment regulations, among others. To be up to date with regulatory compli- ance, banks can set up regulatory committees to ensure their internal procedures are compliant with these requirements.


8.4.1.4 Strategic Risk

Banks like any other institution formulate their strategic goals and objectives, and there arises a challenge when their operations, especially loan decisions, are not in line with other objectives and goals. Other forms of strategic risk include poor decision-making, inappropriate strategic or tactical decisions about loan portfolio growth, wrongly executed tasks and the problem of making substandard policies. For example, when a bankÆs long-term goals do not align well with short-term goals, that bank is likely to face a strategic risk. An example could be a situation where a bank funds money laundering when it does not align with its long-term goals. To mitigate this problem, banks can engage in strategic risk management. Strategic risk management would involve setting up internal audit committees to identify the broken links between strategy and execution within the banks and come up with recommendations that would help mitigate such problems.


8.4.1.5 Reputation Risk

Reputation is key in the banking industry. It is only when clients trust a bank that they put their money into it. It is therefore important for banks to maintain good reputation in the eyes of their clients. Anything that brings the bankÆs name into disrepute becomes a reputation risk. One main way by which a bank may lose its credibility and reputation is when clients are not able to access their monies on time. This can result in a bank run, an event where dissatisfied clients, because of fear and panic, cause concerned clients of other banks to believe in an eminent collapse, thereby rushing to withdraw their monies. Some other means through which banks can face reputational risk include un-innovative attitudes, notoriety for poor

174	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

standards and customer service, insufficient credit facilities, inefficient loan delivery systems and frequent lawsuits. Bad reputation could lead to bank collapse, loss of goodwill, share price depreciation, customer disloyalty and others. Some ways to improve bank reputation include adopting positive standards of service, reconstituting the board of directors with people of integrity, adopting positive social values and incorporating Environmental, Social and Governance (ESG) factors into investments.


8.4.1.6 Country or Sovereign Risk

Country or sovereign risk is the inability of the country to meet its bond obligations as and when they fall due. In a broader sense, country risk is the risk that a countryÆs economic, social and political conditions may change unfavourably to affect the ability of doing business in the country. In the case of banks facing country risk, they may lose their foreign assets or foreign clients may not be able to repay their debt obligations to them. Some situations that might cause a country risk include political and social instability, change of government to an unfavourable one, nationalisation and expropriation of assets, government repudiation of external debt, loose exchange controls and currency devaluation or depreciation. In extreme cases, banking oper- ations may come to a halt. Banks involved in international banking tend to be more exposed to country risks than domestic banks. Insurance could be one way of managing this type of risk.



8.5 Credit Analysis

In evaluating a loan request, every bank does a credit analysis of borrowers to assess their creditworthiness. Based on this analysis, the credit department makes recom- mendations as to whether the loan request should be granted or not. The bankÆs credit department usually carries its analysis based on some key criteria. It is for the banks to critically consider these in order to reduce the risks associated with the lending.



8.5.1 Creditworthiness of the Borrower

An important issue to address is whether the borrower can first and foremost pay back the loan together with its interest on time. Most banks evaluate their borrowers using the six (6) Cs of credit evaluation, and these are character, capacity, capital, collateral, condition and compliance.

8.5 Credit Analysis	175

Character Character refers to a set of qualities (truthfulness, honesty and consis- tency) that distinguish one person or entity from the other. Here, the loan officer must convince himself or herself that the customer has a responsible attitude towards the repayment of the loan. The loan officer wants to be sure the customer is truthful about the purpose of the loan and has a past record of paying back all loans collected and doing so on time. The loan officer must be convinced that the customer has a well-defined purpose for requesting bank credit and a serious intention to repay.
Capacity This is the assurance that the customer making a loan request has the authority to do so. In the case of a firm, the loan officer must make sure that the representative applying for the bank loan has indeed the mandate of the firmÆs board of directors to apply for a loan and to sign any loan agreement. This can be ascertained by obtaining a copy of a companyÆs resolution passed by the companyÆs board of directors for the company to take credit. Also, where it is a partnership, the bank must request the firmÆs partnership agreement to determine which persons are authorised to borrow on behalf of the partnership firm. Thus, the person who enters into the credit agreement must have been authorised to do so and must be in good legal standing to sign a binding loan agreement. This is because a loan agreement signed by unauthorised persons (for example, minors) could result in the bank or credit institution making huge losses since collecting loans under such agreements might be difficult.
Capital Another key issue banks try to assess when performing a credit analysis is in the area of capital or cash adequacy. Thus, the bank tries to find out whether the borrower has the ability to generate enough cash to repay its loan. Generally, borrowers can draw up funds to meet their financial obligations from three sources, including cash flows from normal operations, sale of assets and funds raised through the issue of debt or equity. However, bankers mostly prefer cash flows as the main means of loan repayment as opposed to the other ways of raising funds. This is because the sale of assets weakens a borrowerÆs balance sheet, while raising of funds through debt or equity by borrowing, the customer makes the bankÆs position as a lender riskier.
Collateral Collateral is a property promised to the lender as compensation if the borrower defaults. The collateral lessens the effects of adverse selection since it reduces the losses associated with a loan default. In assessing a borrowerÆs collateral as a way of determining his or her creditworthiness, the bank tries to find out whether the borrower or borrowing firm has assets of good value and quality to be used in case of default to settle the loan and its interests. The loan officer must make sure that the asset used as collateral is in good shape and that its value will not be lost by the time the loan repayment is due. Other factors to be considered by the bank in terms of collateral include the durability, marketability and stability of value of the collateral pledged.
Conditions Credit analysts need to be abreast with current economic trends such as interest rates, exchange rates, inflation, GDP and recent developments in the bor- rowing companyÆs line of operation or industry. A review of economic conditions

176	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

and the business environment within which a firm operates is critical as these factors could impact the quality of the loan. Such information can be accessed from the Internet, newspapers, etc. Knowledge of these issues would enable the bank ascer- tain which industry to grant the loan to and the amount of interest to charge.
Compliance In assessing the creditworthiness of a borrower, the credit department needs to take into account the compliance or control factor. This factor borders on changes in laws and regulations, and whether these changes may affect the borrower or lender in any way. The bank must make sure that the loan being given is in accordance with the regulatory requirements of the country. Compliance also ensures that the loan request being granted is in line with the bankÆs existing loan policy.


8.5.2 Structure and Documentation of Loan Agreement

The next issue to consider in the credit analysis is the structure and documentation in the loan agreement. The bankÆs loan officer has a responsibility towards the bor- rower as well as the depositors and shareholders of the bank and therefore must be interested in ensuring that their demands are met at all times. Seeking to satisfy a customerÆs demands, for instance, may entail drafting a loan agreement with a more convenient loan repayment schedule. This is important in ensuring that the quality of the loan has implications for also safeguarding shareholdersÆ investments as well as depositorsÆ funds. When a major customer defaults on a loan repayment, this clearly affects the bankÆs loan portfolio. Therefore, the bank can advise on and adjust the amount of the loan requested by the customer so as to accommodate the customer (since some customers may not know what their real financial needs are). Structuring the loan agreement is necessary to ensure that the bank is protected and may entail imposing certain loan covenants on the activities of the borrowing customer. If these restrictions are not imposed on the borrowerÆs activities, they may adversely affect the quality of the loan and pose as a threat on the bankÆs ability to recover the loans. The bankÆs loan recovery process needs to always be detailed in the loan agreement.


8.5.3 Collateral Requirement

The borrower providing collateral to support the loan application is also an important criterion for reducing the bankÆs risk. Collateral provides the means by which the bank is able to recover all, or a substantial part, of the loan given out in the event of default. In all, there are three sources from which borrowers can repay loans, including cash flows, assets and guarantees:
ò The firmÆs cash flows or profitability. Where the bank assumes the borrowing firm as a going concern, cash flows would be considered more important.

8.5 Credit Analysis	177

ò The assets in the balance sheet pledged as collateral that can be sold to settle the loan in the case where the cash flow is inadequate. The assets may include real property, accounts receivable and inventory.
ò Guarantees from the firmÆs owners or directors where their personal assets are pledged against loans taken. In the case of subsidiaries, the parent company typically serves as guarantor.
  In situations where the borrowerÆs cash flow is not enough to pay for the loans, the collateral pledged behind a loan and other assets owned by the borrower can be used as the second line of defence against loan default. This implies that the lending bank must take the following into consideration:
ò The bankÆs ability to get a clear title to the asset available in order to pay for the loan
ò Whether the assets can easily be sold in order to raise funds to pay for the loan
ò Whether the bank has exclusive rights to assets used as collateral
ò Whether the assets have been pledged to someone else. In the case where the bank grants mortgage loans, it has to ensure that the deeds to the property have been filed with the government authorities and that the borrower has a clear title to the property.



8.5.4 Loan Covenants

Another important issue to consider by banks is the requirement for the borrower to comply with a number of loan covenants. Loan covenants are restrictions imposed by a lender that prevent the borrower from undertaking certain activities until the debt has been paid. A bank can introduce loan covenants as part of the requirement for the loan application, and the rationale is to ensure that the borrower is in a position to fully honour its indebtedness to the bank without difficulty. Loan covenants may take different forms, including:
ò Restriction of dividend paymentùPayment of dividends to shareholders reduces the firmÆs cash flows and makes interest and principal repayment difficult to honour. The bank will also want to restrict such dividend payments to improve the firmÆs cash position to fulfil its debt obligation.
ò Restrictions on acquiring certain assetsùThe bank may restrict the borrower from acquiring additional assets as this has the tendency of constraining the borrower from fulfilling its debt obligation to the bank.
ò Restriction on the disposal of assetsùThe bank could also restrict the borrowing firm from disposing of certain fixed assets. Retaining such assets reduces the risk associated with the loan.
ò Limit on additional debt issueùWhen a bank grants a loan facility to a borrower, it would have done so taking into consideration its existing capital structure in determining its risk profile. So allowing the firm to continue borrowing increases

178	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

the risk on the loan given by the bank. In this case, the bank will be interested in limiting the amount and type of additional debt (especially senior debt).
ò Restriction on entering into merger and acquisition contractsùEntering into mergers and acquisitions (M&As) also has the tendency of reducing the borrow- ing firmÆs cash flow, especially in the case where it is acquiring another firm. The lending bank may therefore restrict the borrower from undertaking such deals.
ò Requirement on financial ratiosùThe lending bank may insist on certain finan- cial ratios, such as interest cover, working capital ratio and debt ratio being met by the borrower. For instance, the bank may require an interest cover of say five times. This means the firmÆs earnings before interest and taxes (EBIT) should be five times its interest expense. When the firm breaches this financial ratio and defaults on the loan, the bank will have the right to terminate the loan agreement and discontinue making funds available to the firm. In some cases, the bank may even ask for repayment of the loan already granted.
  It is worth noting that though covenants cannot guarantee that the lending will be completely risk free, they are important in influencing the behaviour of the borrow- ing firmÆs management in order to reduce the credit risk.


8.5.5 Sources of Information About Loan Customers

The bank relies primarily on outside information when assessing the creditworthi- ness of their loan customers. Generally, the following constitute sources of infor- mation to the lending bank:
ò Information provided by borrower when granting loan request: This includes the amount of funds needed, purpose of loan, previous creditors, assets backing the loan request, the nature of business in case of a company, etc.
ò Other lenders to the borrower: These institutions may be of immense help in answering questions such as were all scheduled repayments made and were they made on time?
ò Financial statements. Banks can seek information from financial statements and annual reports to assess firmsÆ profitability, liquidity, asset management posi- tions, marketability, etc.
ò Information from local and regional credit bureaus to ascertain the borrowerÆs credit history. Examples of the credit rating agencies in the USA are Standard & Poor, MoodyÆs, etc. Most African countries have begun establishing credit reference bureaus and examples in Ghana include Dun & Bradstreet, HudsonPrice Data Solution and XDS Data Ghana Limited. What is available is a credit reference bureau. The other sources of information are newspapers, magazines, journals, other publications and institutions that provide industry ratios.
ò Information about the economic trends in the local area, country and international standing would also be needed in determining whether to lend or not to lend and

8.5 Credit Analysis	179

how much to be lent. The activities of many loan borrowers are sensitive to economic trends or fluctuations in business activities. For instance, demand for some products may be seasonal. The credit analyst must therefore determine whether the borrowerÆs business is in a period of boom or recession when making decisions about loan requests. This implies that they must assess the vulnerability of their borrowerÆs business to the cyclical changes and incorporate those findings in their loan making decisions.
Quick Quiz
1. What factors are considered when assessing the creditworthiness of a borrower?
2. Identify the sources of information for assessing loan customersÆ creditworthiness



8.5.6 Credit Culture and Risk Profile

A good appreciation of the credit culture and the risk profile of banks is key to the success of their loan portfolio management. Considering the relevance of the bankÆs lending activities, its credit culture tends to affect other activities of the bank. The bank is also confronted with a multiplicity of risks in its operations. It is therefore important for the bankÆs staff to have a good understanding of the credit culture and risk profile of the bank. The credit culture of a bank is made up of its credit values, beliefs and behaviours. The credit culture affects the bankÆs lending and credit risk management. A bankÆs credit practice also deals with the type and frequency of credit the bank gives to its clients. If a bank goes beyond this level, it risks collapse.
  The bankÆs risk profile identifies the amount of risk the bank can take at any point in time. It is more measurable than the credit culture. Management of the bank needs to incorporate the risk profile when formulating strategic plans and other long- and short-term plans. The risk profile evolves from the credit culture, strategic planning and daily activities of managing the credit. Risk profiles differ from bank to bank, even if their products and services are similar. For instance, two banks may have identical levels of loans with completely different risk profiles. One bank may have its loans fully perfected and granted to its customers in the domestic market, while the other bank may have unsecured loans given to foreign customers. Also, assume how much more a non-performing loan (NPL) of US$5 million loan would adversely affect a US$600 million bank than a US$6 billion bank. A bankÆs risk profile also changes over time and is affected by both internal issuesùlike capital, portfolio size, type of loans, etc.ùas well as externalùchange in macroeconomic conditions such as interest rate and exchange rate.
  Credit culture may also vary from one bank to the other. Some banks have a very conservative approach to credit, by focusing on financially strong, well-established borrowers. Banks that are growth oriented may also have an aggressive approach by lending to risky borrowers. These cultural differences are based on the bankÆs objectives for asset quality, growth and earnings. Banks that have an aggressive

180	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

growth strategy would probably need a more detailed credit policy than others, which are more risk averse. Thus, risk culture, risk profile and credit practices are related. In a situation where there is a disconnect, management may not be efficient in managing the banks risks. For example, in a situation where a bankÆ risk culture and profile are not aligned with its credit practices, the bank is not protected from risk and eminent collapse. So, management has to play a balancing role to ensure these three are always linked.


8.5.7 Credit Management Information Systems

Credit risk management is an important aspect of managing a bankÆs risk. Banks with effective credit risk management information systems are better able to assess their risk profile, take corrective actions and make meaningful predictions and forecast that are more accurate. Good credit risk management information system provides more forward-looking information from the banksÆ data on loans on which independent conclusions can be drawn. Their information is current and in real time, and it brings out only the sufficiently needed information in a form that the board of directors and senior managers can understand. For example, a report presenting the level of classified assets has limited value; however, if the report contains historical information and shows the classified asset position relative to capital, it becomes more useful. Similarly, reports on numbers of exceptions to policy are not very useful, but reports on aggregate exceptions as a percentage of industry or specialised lending portfolios may signal a change in risk assumption. Summary data presented in a concise format generally satisfy managementÆs needs. All redundant information is taken out. It is also analytical in nature and contains not just figures but figures with meaning that management can understand. It is also well formatted so that users can easily extract the information they need. It also contains key summaries that bring out key information. It contains key metrics for measuring performance such as charge-off rates, delinquency ratios, nonaccrual loans and restructured loans. Banks with sound credit risk management information tend to be more proactive than reactive. They are able to make more timely and relevant policies and their underwriting practices are robust. Also, their loan portfolios usually perform well. The complexity of the information from the credit management information system may be determined by the size of the bank and the kind of transaction it deals in.
  It is also important to note that the data that feeds into the information system should be accurate and free from errors. As technology churns out what it receives, the ægarbage in garbage outÆ analogy holds here. It is key for the data to be free from any kind of error; else the information extracted would be inaccurate. Not only is error a problem, if data are not regularly updated, information drawn becomes obsolete. So, if old information were fed into the credit risk management information system, decisions made based on such information would not reflect current market conditions and therefore would not be the best decisions. For the management information system to function properly, data must be regularly updated. Thus, it

8.6  Loan Agreement	181

should be timely and up to date. If these conditions were not met, the credit risk management information system would not be as efficient and effective as it should be.



8.6 Loan Agreement

A loan agreement is a contract, which sets out the terms of a loan. A loan agreement details all the terms of the loan contract and is usually in written form. Every loan agreement has different parts, and these are note, loan commitment agreement, collateral, representations and warranties, restrictive covenants, events of default and termination and repayment and conditions subsequent.
Note The borrower signs this and it specifies the principal amount of the loan, interest charged and the repayment schedule (i.e. the date and amount of each instalment). In addition to this, clauses, which set out the type of loan facility, the purpose of loan and other fees to be paid, are provided.
Loan Commitment Agreement This is usually in the case of larger business loans or mortgage loans. Here, the bank promises to make credit available to the borrower for a particular period up to a maximum amount and charges a commitment fee for that service.
Collateral Loan agreements normally require collateral to be pledged when a loan is being taken. This is to protect a bankÆs interest, especially in the case where the borrower defaults in repaying the loan. Details about the collateral such as its location and value and how the bank can claim the collateral are indicated in the loan agreement. The collateral could be accounts receivable, inventory, marketable securities, real property and equipment.
Representations and Warranties These are also very important in loan agree- ments. The borrower must guarantee that the information supplied for the loan request is true and correct. Even though collaterals are provided, the borrower may sometimes pledge personal assets as a way of guaranteeing for the loan. The main idea behind this is to identify the person or firm taking the loan and is responsible for repaying the loan.
Restrictive Covenants Loan agreement also contains restrictive covenants, and these are conditions in the agreement to protect the bankÆs interest and also are a way of minimising the credit risk. Restrictive covenants are of two types:
ò Affirmative covenant: affirmative covenants may be information or financial restrictive covenants. Information covenants may mandate the borrower to peri- odically file its financial statements. Financial covenants would require the borrower to maintain certain levels of liquidity, adequate loan/property insurance or stay within defined financial parameters.

182	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

ò Negative covenants: these covenants may restrict/forbid the borrower from taking certain actions such as payment of dividends to shareholders, acquiring certain assets, disposal of certain assets and borrowing from other sources or entering into merger contracts.
Events of Default and Termination Most loan agreements include clauses, which indicate specific activities or actions when there is a breach of the terms of the loan. The agreement may include a statement on the action(s) the bank can take when there is a breach of contract in order to recover its funds. The events of default clause also show who bears the collection cost, as well as court and attorney fees that may arise from litigation of loan agreement.
Repayment and Conditions This part of the loan agreement shows the means by which the amount borrowed can be paid.
Quick Quiz
1. What is a loan agreement?
2. Identify and explain the parts of a loan agreement.


8.7 Loan Review

Once a bank or lending institution and a borrower sign a loan contract, the contract is subjected to periodic monitoring and review. The contract is not ignored until the loan falls due, since such a practice would generally be very risky. The conditions under which a loan is made to a borrower keep changing from the time the loan agreement is signed up to the time the loan obligations fall due and payments are made. Thus, those varying circumstances may affect the borrowerÆs financial posi- tion and ability to make loan payments. Some borrowers may lose their jobs or sound health making them less likely to pay back their loans. Similarly, some businesses may need more credit or witness declining profits in the midst of economic instability.
  As a result of these developments, lending institutions have to be on the lookout and review all loans on a periodic basis until they fall due for repayment. Loan review procedures vary from one bank or lending institution to another. In spite of that, there are a number of generally used procedures common to almost all lenders. These procedures include the following:
1. Periodically reviewing all types of loans.
2. Ensuring that all the important features of every loan are checked by properly planning the review process. Those important features include:
(i) The condition and quality of any asset pledged as collateral security in the loan contract.
(ii) A proper assessment of whether or not the borrowerÆs financial situation and forecasts have changed over time.

8.8 Resolution of Problem Loans: Loan Workouts	183

(iii) The record of loan payments made by the borrower to ensure that they are made in line with the provisions of the repayment schedule outlined in the loan contract.
(iv) The completeness of the paperwork of the loan to ensure that the lending institution has access to the collateral security of the loan and is empowered by the contract to sue the borrower in the courts of law if the need arises.
3. Carrying out the most frequent reviews on the largest loans since the borrowerÆs failure to honour such loan obligations could adversely affect the financial position of the lending institution itself.
4. Fast-tracking the loan review processes if the need arises.
5. Conducting reviews on troubled loans more often and increasing the frequency of the reviews as the extent of trouble of the loans worsens.
  It is imperative for lending institutions to conduct loan reviews to ensure that they function soundly and efficiently. This practice enables management of lending institutions identify loans that have weaknesses and problems. Apart from that, it helps management find out whether officers responsible for giving loans actually comply with institutional lending policies. To make sure that these regular checks are done properly, some lending institutions separate the loan department from the loan review department. This process also makes it possible for lending institutions to gauge their overall risk exposure and their future need for capital.



8.8 Resolution of Problem Loans: Loan Workouts

It is important to understand that even though most banks or lending institutions put in place preventive measures to ensure that the loans do not go bad, recovering some loans may be difficult or troubling. The borrower may decline in the payment of some instalments, or the collateral pledged against the loan may have depreciated in value. The following can be identified as common signs of problem loan situations:
ò Irregular or delinquent loan payments
ò Frequent amendments in loan terms or agreement
ò Customer relying on non-recurring sources of funds as a means of paying loans
ò Adverse changes in borrowing customerÆs share price
ò Adverse changes in the borrowing firmÆs capital structure, liquidity and activity levels
ò Losses in one or more years as measured by return on equity, return on assets or the firmÆs earnings before interest and tax (EBIT)
ò When actual cash flows differ from the cash flow expected or projected at the time of the loan request
ò Sudden, unexpected, unexplained changes in deposit balances maintained by the borrower
ò Unusual or unexpected delays in filing financial statements with the bank

184	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management

ò A sudden or unusual change by borrowing firm in its accounting principles and procedures and pricing policies
ò Poor loan renewal record
  How then should a bank or lending institution handle problem loan situation? How can the bank recover its funds from such a situation? The following are important recommendations banks can consider in loan workout:
ò Loan workout specialist can meet with the borrower to find out the possible causes of the problem and come out with solutions such as cutting down on expenses, increasing cash flow and improving management control and entering into new markets or product lines. Specialists could also suggest inducing new capital, finding additional collateral, a merger or filing for bankruptcy.
ò Bank personnel can also evaluate the quality, competence and integrity of management and visit the site to check on the condition of the firmÆs properties or equipment.
ò Early and rapid detection and reporting of problems associated with loans.
ò In the case of a suspected loan default, the bank can seek, with court approval, an immediate prepayment of the loan.
ò The task of recovering the loan should be kept separate from the lending function. This means officers in charge of recovering loans should be different from those approving loans.
ò The bank must estimate which assets or resources are available to cater for the loan.
ò The loan workout personnel can conduct an investigation to find out whether the borrower or borrowing firm has other financial obligations to meet, which may compete with the claims of the bank.



8.9 Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we discus portfolio and credit management. We specifically discus the types of bank loans, regulations and lending processes and credit analyses. We also examine loan agreement, lending to business firms and pricing of bank loans. We learn that:
1. Banks provide all kinds of loans to their customers for various purposes, ranging from the buying of vehicles, furniture and fittings and financing education to the expansion of a business. The types of bank loans include individual loans, corporate loans, agricultural loans, financial institutions loans, mortgage loans, leasing financing, etc.
2. The factors that determine the growth and composition of bank loans include size of the bank, official loan policy of the bank, experience and expertise of management in making different types of loans, return and risk profile on the type of loan and the profile characteristics of the market area the bank serves.

8.9 Summary and Conclusions	185

3. BanksÆ loan portfolios are closely regulated and reviewed by the regulatory authority in order to minimise risk and improve safety in the banking industry. In line with that, the nature and extent to which regulations are implemented largely influence the composition, quality and yield of the loan book of a given bank. The regulator may restrict or even prohibit certain loans and the bankÆs loan policy may also restrict credit officers from granting other loans.
4. The board of directors of a bank has the ultimate responsibility for all loans made and regulating the lending process requires establishing a written loan policy. A written loan policy outlines the specific guidelines and principles for the bankÆs lending activities (i.e. the kind of loans they can give out and what the lending process should entail as well as the actual composition of the bankÆs loan portfolio).
5. The lending process of banks involves identifying the prospective loan appli- cants, appraising loan applicantÆs character and the purpose of the loan, regu- larly visiting the loan applicantÆs premises to ascertain credit record, assessing loan applicantÆs financial conditions, evaluating collateral supporting the loan and signing the loan agreement and monitoring to ensure loan agreement is complied with.
6. Banks at every point in time grapple with different types of risks, including credit risk, market risk, interest rate risk, liquidity risk, price risk, foreign exchange risk, transaction risk, compliance risk, strategic risk, reputation risk and country risk. Risk is an uncertainty that can adversely affect banksÆ capital reserves and/or earnings.
7. The bankÆs credit analysis is necessary in evaluating a loan request. Based on this analysis, the credit department makes recommendations as to whether the loan request should be granted or not. The credit analysis will entail ascertaining the following: creditworthiness of the customer, structure and documentation of loan agreement, collateral requirement, loan covenants and sources of informa- tion about loan customers, credit culture and risk profile and credit management information systems.
8. Loan agreement is a contract, which sets out the terms on a loan. The parts of a typical loan agreement include note, loan commitment agreement, collaterals, representations and warranties, restrictive covenants, events of default and termination and repayment and conditions.
9. As conditions change from time to time, it is important for lending banks to always monitor and review all loans on a periodic basis until they fall due for repayment.
10. Banks may put in place preventive measures to ensure that the loans do not go bad. However, recovering some loans may be difficult or troubling. The bor- rower may decline in the payment of some instalments or the collateral pledged against the loan may have depreciated in value. It is important for the bank to put in place measures to work out the loans.

186	8  Loan Portfolio and Credit Management
