Chapter 9
Corporate Lending







Learning Objectives
By the end of this chapter, you should be able to:
ò Discuss banksÆ lending to business firms
ò Identify the types of loans banks make to business firms
ò Analyse business loan applications using financial ratios
ò Show how bank loans are priced
ò Explain the various loan pricing models


9.1 Introduction

Businesses require loans from their banks to finance their operations. Lending to businesses or corporate lending involves banks giving out different types of loans to businesses, ranging from short-term to long-term loans. Short-term loans are loans which mature in 1 year or less, while long-term loans are loans which mature in more than 1 year. Most banks prefer giving out short-term loans because of their relatively low chances of default compared to long-term loans.
  In this chapter, we examine the various types of short-term and long-term corporate loans. We discuss the evaluation of corporate loan applications. We also examine the different models of pricing corporate loans.


9.1.1 Lending to Businesses

Bank loans remain an important source of financing businesses, especially in African countries, which tend to have bank-based financial systems. Bank loans may be more attractive to firms compared to other forms of debt finance due to the following reasons (see Arnold 2014):


® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_9

187

188	9  Corporate Lending

ò Low legal and administrative chargesùBanksÆ lending entails direct negotiation between the lending bank and the borrower, and it is therefore associated with lower cost compared with a bond issue, which involves costs of engaging a bookrunner, distribution expenses, arrangement expenses, regulatory costs, etc.
ò SpeedùBank loans can be contracted more quickly than bond issues. A typical bank loan application can be approved, and funds made available in the bor- rowerÆs account in few days and in some cases hours, whereas, in the case of bond issues, it may take months for the borrowed funds to be available to the issuer.
ò FlexibilityùThe borrower has more flexibility in renegotiating the terms of a bank loan during the tenure of the loan if its economic situation changes than in the case of a bond issue. For instance, if the borrowing firmÆs economic circum- stances improve and it is in the position to repay the loan before maturity, banks are in a position to accommodate and alter the terms of the loan. This may be difficult to achieve in the case of bonds with fixed redemption dates or even with lease financing with fixed terms.
ò Accessibility to SMEsùBanks may be able to provide loans to SMEs, whereas the bond market may target only large companies. A number of banks in Africa and other developing countries have now set up SME desks focusing mainly on lending to the SMEs.


9.2 Corporate Loans

We now discuss the various types of short-term as well as long-term loans given to corporate institutions.


9.2.1 Short-Term Corporate Loans

Self-Liquidating Loans These are loans given to support ongoing business opera- tions or day-to-day activities of the firm. These loans are used in purchasing raw materials or finished goods for onward sale in order to take advantage of the time lag between when the firm needs funds for acquiring inventory to the point when funds from sold finished goods are received (cash cycle). The cycle starts when cash (borrowed from bank) is used in acquiring inventory, the raw materials are processed and finished goods are put up for sale. The cash received from the sale of the goods is used in paying for loan taken from the bank. Self-liquidating loans can support the business until cash flows are generated.
Working Capital Loans Working capital loans, which are similar to the traditional self-liquidating loans, are often used for purchasing raw materials. They are also very useful during the peak periods in a business production or credit needs. For instance, if a company expects a heavy demand for its products in a particular

9.2 Corporate Loans	189

season, it will need short-term funds in purchasing raw materials to produce its products. The firmÆs bank can set up a line of credit for the company depending on the firmÆs estimation of need for funds. A line of credit is a more flexible way of financing a business, as it allows the business to borrow up to a specified amount for a specified period. Working capital loans are normally secured or backed by the firmÆs accounts receivables or inventory, and they carry floating interest rates on the amounts actually borrowed.
Letters of Credit A letter of credit is a legal document, which backs a customerÆs obligation to a third party. This implies that the bank would bear the cost if the customer were not able to meet the obligation. These letters of credit are often used in international trade. In the situation where an importer imports goods, the exporter would want a guarantee that the goods will be paid for when they are shipped and will therefore request a letter of credit from the importerÆs bank in order to guarantee payment.
Interim Construction Financing Interim construction financing/loan is used to support the construction of office buildings, shopping centres, other permanent structures or building projects. This form of financing affords builders or contractors with funds needed in hiring workers, renting or leasing equipment and purchasing building materials. However, as soon as the building project is completed, the bankÆs loan is usually paid off with a longer-term mortgage loan given by another lender.



9.2.2 Long-Term Corporate Loans

Term Loans Term loans are long-term loans which are used in acquiring fixed assets and in funding long- and medium-term projects. With term loans, the bank gives out a specified amount for a specified period and purpose. The amount applied for is normally based on the projected cost of the project or cost of asset. Such loans have a maturity of between two (2) and ten (10) years. The loan is paid in instalments using cash inflows from the project being undertaken. This implies that the projected cash inflows of the project being undertaken should be taken into consideration when deciding on the amount of each instalment.
Long-Term Loans This form of credit is used in financing the construction of fixed assets which will be generating some cash inflows in the future. These projects include ports, harbours, oil refineries, pipelines, mines, petroleum exploration and driller equipment. It is the riskiest of all business loans. This is because such projects require very large amounts of funds, and cash flows are uncertain since there may be delays in the schedule of profits. Moreover, the laws and regulations of the country where the project will be undertaken, as well as changes in economic variables such as inflation and interest rates, may adversely affect the success of the project. Due to the size and risk of project loans, they are normally shared among various borrowers and several lenders. This is to minimise the risk associated with giving out such loans.

190	9  Corporate Lending

Revolving Line of Credit This type of credit facility allows a borrower to borrow funds to a specific amount, repay all or part of the funds borrowed and re-borrow more funds as and when the need arises till the credit line reaches maturity. In other words, if a borrower is granted a revolving line of credit, the bank is obliged to offer funds for a specified period. The bank charges commitment fee for the obligation. This credit is normally granted without collateral to secure the loan. The advantage about this credit facility is that the borrower can borrow extra funds during reces- sionary periods and pay for the loans during peak periods when cash is generated.
Loans Used in Leverage Buy-outs A leveraged buy-out (LBO) is acquiring a firm and financing the acquisition with debt. Buy-outs become necessary when some firms identify poorly managed firms, acquire them with borrowed funds and manage to later sell the firms at a higher price. On the other hand, managers within a firm who believe they can restructure the firmÆs operations to improve cash flows may attempt an LBO with the view of improving the firmÆs performance. This makes the firm highly leveraged. A firm financed in this way has a potential of generating high returns but with greater risk. If the firm does not perform well, for instance in recessionary periods or when interest rates are high, the firm might not be able to pay for its debt. Due to the risky nature of such loans, they require high interest rates to compensate for the risks involved.
Project Finance Loan This involves securing a long-term loan for financing major projects through project financial engineering, based on financing against cash flows generated by the project alone. A project company or special purpose vehicle (SPV) is usually created to build and operate a project, such as energy, by sponsors who provide equity. The project finance loans may be provided by a bank or through the issue of bonds. The unique feature is that the loan repayment is based on the cash flows from the project rather than the assets of the sponsor. Project finance is structured finance, which is based on off-balance sheet financing. The activities of the project company are separated from that of the sponsoring firm, and therefore, if the project is unsuccessful, the bank and other project creditors have no (or very limited) recourse or claim on the sponsoring firmsÆ assets and cash flows.
  After discussing the various types of short-term and long-term bank loans, we shall now turn our attention to analysing business loan applications.


9.3 Evaluation of Corporate Loan Applications

Businesses raise funds from bank loans in order to finance their day-to-day opera- tions. Banks are also in the business of making loans, among others. However, banks would want to assure themselves that the loans they give out will not become bad debt and as such will employ various tools in analysing such business loan applications.
An important tool used by banks in analysing corporate loan applications is a
financial ratio analysis of the borrowing firmÆs financial statements. The various

9.3 Evaluation of Corporate Loan Applications	191

financial statements assessed include the borrowing firmÆs balance sheet, income statement and cash flow statements. In analysing the financial statements, the bank assesses the firmÆs control over expenses, efficiency and marketability of the cus- tomerÆs product, profitability and liquidity (Sinkey 2002; Rose and Hudgins 2013). We take these one after the other.



9.3.1 Corporate CustomerÆs Control Over Expenses

The efficiency and effectiveness with which a firm controls and monitors its expenses and protects its profit is very important. In trying to analyse a firmÆs ability to control its expenses, the firm can analyse its financial statement by taking each of the expenses and expressing them as a percentage of net sales over a period of time. The ratios to be calculated include the following:
ò Wages and salaries/net sales
ò Overhead expenses/net sales
ò Cost of goods sold/net sales
  Comparison can be made with previous yearÆs ratios or with the industryÆs ratios. In the situation where expenses are rising with no increase in sales, the firm may be said not to be monitoring and controlling its expenses well.



9.3.2 Activity/Efficiency

Activity or efficiency ratios measure how well the firm uses its assets such as inventory, fixed assets and accounts receivables and how well the business firm utilises these assets to generate sales and cash flows for the firm. These ratios also measure the speed with which the firm converts its sales into cash. The activity ratios include:
ò Inventory turnover ratioùmeasures how quickly the firm sells its goods or the number of times within a year it takes a firm to roll its inventory over. It is calculated by dividing the cost of goods sold by inventory. That is,

Inventory turnover ratio = Cost of goods sold
Inventory

ò Inventory holding periodùmeasures the number of days it takes for a firm to roll its inventory over. It is calculated by dividing the number of days in a year by the inventory turnover ratio. Thus,

192	9  Corporate Lending

Inventory holding period = 365/inventory turnover period.
ò Average collection periodùevaluates the firmÆs credit and collection policies and measures the time it takes for the firm to receive cash for goods sold on credit or the time lapse between credit sales and payment. It is calculated as:

Average collection period = Accounts receivable
Average sales per day

Average sales per day are calculated by dividing annual sales by the number of days in a year.
Average payment periodùevaluates how long it takes the firm to pay its suppliers. It is calculated as:

Average payment period =	Accounts receivable
Average purchases per day

ò Fixed asset turnoverùmeasures the efficiency with which a firm employs its fixed assets. The ratio shows how much sales the firm is generating from the use of its fixed assets. It is computed as:

Fixed asset turnover =	Sales
Net fixed assets

ò Total asset turnoverùshows the efficiency with which the firm uses all of its assets to generate sales. All things being equal, lenders will be favourably disposed towards higher total asset turnovers since that would be an indication of more sales and increasing cash flows. It is calculated as:

Total asset turnover ratio =	Sales
Total asset




9.3.3 Marketability of the CustomerÆs Product, Service or Skill

One factor that banks consider when giving out loans is the ability of the firm to generate cash flows to repay loans. The firmÆs ability to generate cash flows depends, to a large extent, on how well the firm markets its products, services and skills, and

9.3 Evaluation of Corporate Loan Applications	193

this can be measured by growth in sales revenue, profitability levels and market share.
  The profitability measures include net profit margin, gross profit margin, operat- ing profit margin, basic earning power, return on assets and return on equity.
ò Net profit marginùmeasures the percentage of each dollar cash sales remaining after all costs and expenses, including interest, taxes and preference share divi- dends, have been deducted. A lending bank would be willing to make loans to a borrowing firm with increasing net profit margins over a period or may want to compare the firmÆs profit margin with the industryÆs ratios. It is calculated as:

Net profit margin = Net profit
Sales
ò Operating profitùit is calculated as revenue minus expense, excluding tax and interest. The shortcoming of this profitability measure is that it does not give the measure in relation to the assets or equity that generated the profit.
ò Return on equityùthis is the rate of return on shareholdersÆ investment and it is the ratio of net income to equity. Return on equity (ROE) is usually used in comparing firms in the same industry. The disadvantage about this measure is that ROE can be artificially inflated by reducing the book value of equity through share repurchase. As a result, the ROE is increased without an improvement in profits. It is therefore recommended that for a more realistic view, the ROE of a firm is examined over a longer period of time.
ò Return on total assets provides an idea of the overall return on investment earned by the firm. It is calculated by finding the ratio of the net income to the total assets of the firm. A low return may result from the firmÆs low basic earning and its above-average use of debt, both of which cause its net income to be relatively low. This measure gives a measure of the efficiency of a company in generating returns from its assets, without being affected by management financing decisions.
  A firmÆs market share can be measured using market ratios such as price/earnings ratios and market to book value ratios. These ratios relate to the firmÆs market value as measured by its current share price to certain accounting values. These ratios provide investors with information on how the firm is performing.
Price earning (PE) ratio is measured as : Market price per of share
Earnings per share

A high PE ratio is an indication of rapid growth of firmÆs future earnings. Thus, a
firm with a high PE is likely to have its loan request granted.
ò Market/book ratio assesses how investors view the firmÆs past and particularly its likely future performance. Stocks with higher expected performance are likely to sell at higher market/book values than those with less attractive prospects.

194	9  Corporate Lending

9.3.4 Liquidity Indicators

Liquidity ratios measure a firmÆs ability to meet its short-term obligations as and when they fall due. Since financial distress and bankruptcy normally follow low or declining liquidity, liquidity ratios are good indicators of cash flow problems. Liquidity can be measured using current ratios or quick (acid test) ratios.
ò Current ratioùmeasures how the firm finances its short-term obligations using its current assets. It is calculated by dividing current assets by current liabilities. It is calculated as:

Current ratio = Current assets
Current liabilities

ò Quick ratioùmeasures how the firm is able to meet its short-term obligations using current assets without relying on inventory. Inventory is not factored in this measure because it is considered the liquidity of all current assets. It is calculated as:

Quick ratio = Current assets ù inventory
Current liabilities

  It must be noted that how high or low the current or quick ratio is depends on the industry the firm operates in and on the costs and benefits of having too much or too little liquidity.
ò Another measure of the firmÆs liquidity is net working capital, which is calculated by subtracting the firmÆs current assets from its current liabilities.



9.3.5 Leverage or Capital Structure Indicators

These indicators measure the extent to which a firm uses funds from creditors to finance its operations rather than using funds from shareholders. The leverage indicators also show how much debt the firm has already taken in addition to the loan requests. These indicators assess the ability of the firm to meet such obligations. The lending bank would want to know how indebted the firm is. This is because the more indebted a firm is, the higher the likelihood of the firm to default in paying the loan and interest. The leverage or capital structure indicators can be measured using the following ratios:

9.4 Pricing of Corporate Loans	195

Debt-to-equity ratio = Total debt
Equity
Debt ratio = Total debt
Total assets
Asset-to-equity/equity multiplier = Total assets
Total equity
Capitalisation ratio =	Long-term debt
Total long-term liability and net worth
Debt-to-sales ratio = Total liabilities
Net sales



9.3.6 Contingent Liabilities

There are other potential claims, which are usually not shown on the firmÆs balance sheet but are useful in assessing a borrowerÆs likelihood to repay loans. They include guarantees and warranties behind the firmÆs products and services, litigation or pending lawsuits against the firm, unpaid taxes and limiting regulations. These contingent liabilities, when they turn into actual claims, can reduce the firmÆs ability to repay loan since its funds would now be directed to settling those claims.

firmÆs financial statements?




9.4 Pricing of Corporate Loans

Loan pricing or deciding on how much interest to charge on loans given out is one of the most difficult tasks in lending. This is because the lending bank would want to charge a loan rate high enough to generate enough returns as well as to compensate for the bankÆs risk. At the same time, the bank would want to give out loans whose price is low enough to enable the borrower to comfortably pay and not move to other lenders.
  Over-pricing can lead to borrowers turning to other banks, while under-lending can result in low profits by the lending bank given the level of risk. Pricing loans to

196	9  Corporate Lending

accommodate the needs of both the borrower and lender could therefore be very challenging. However, regulations by central banks through their policy rates and base rates have made pricing a bit easier. Generally, banks take into consideration their cost of borrowing, the risk they incur or are likely to incur and their profit margins, in addition to other factors as determined by the credit department when pricing loans.
There are various pricing models used in pricing bank loans, and these include:
ò Cost-plus loan pricing
ò Price leadership model
ò Below-prime market pricing (mark-up model)
ò Loans bearing maximum interest rates
ò Consumer profitability analysis



9.4.1 Cost-Plus Loan Pricing

This is the simplest loan pricing model. In pricing a corporate loan, the bank takes into consideration the cost of acquiring funds such as accepting deposits, borrowing in the money market as well as the operating cost for running the bank. Thus, the bank must be able to make a good estimate of all the costs involved to ensure reasonable pricing. Under this pricing policy, the formula for loan interest is written as:
Loan interest rate = the cost of raising the loanable funds
+ the bankÆs non-funds operating costs such as wages and salaries of loanpersonnel,
cost of physical facilities and materials in granting loans
+ compensation for risk of default + a desired profit margin.

Illustration 9.1
Suppose a bank receives a loan request for US$10 million; the credit department will have to make the right estimate of the various costs in order to price the loan. If the cost of raising funds is estimated to be 8%, non-funds operating cost to be 4% of the US$10 million request, default rate of borrower to be 3% and profit margin to be 2%, the price of the loan will be:
Loan interest rate = 8% + 4% + 3% + 2% = 17%
  One of the shortcomings of this model is that it does not take into consideration competition from other lenders and assumes that banks know their costs accurately.

9.4 Pricing of Corporate Loans	197

9.4.2 Price Leadership Model

This model attempts to overcome the shortcomings of the cost-plus model. With this model, an index rate, e.g. the lowest rate charged on loans granted to the creditwor- thy customer, is set and forms the basis upon which the loan interest rate is set. The formula for the loan charged is expressed as:

prime rate or other	premium for	premium for
Loan interest rate =	base rate including	+ default risk	+	term risk desired profit margin

Illustration 9.2
LetÆs assume a bank receives a long-term loan request for US$8 million for one of its major customers. If the rate charged to a creditworthy customer is 10% (which includes bankÆs profit margin), a rate of default of 4% and a term risk premium (premium paid for taking loan for a longer period) of 4%. The interest rate of this loan will be:
Loan interest rate = 10% + 4% + 4% = 18%




9.4.3 Below-Prime Market Pricing (Mark-Up Model)

Under below-prime pricing, banks lend at low money market interest rates plus a margin to compensate for risk and to provide profit. According to this pricing model, the loan rate is the sum of the interest cost of borrowing and a mark-up for profit and risk. The formula for the loan charged is expressed below:
Loan interest rate = interest cost of borrowing in the money market
+ mark-up for risk and profit.

Illustration 9.3
Suppose a corporate borrower makes a loan request for 20 days and the cost of borrowing is 10%. The bank estimates its risk and profit mark-up to be 7%. This results in a loan interest rate of 17%.
Loan interest rate = 10% + 7% = 17%

198	9  Corporate Lending

9.4.4 Loans Bearing Maximum Interest Rates

Cap rate is an agreed upon limit of a loan contract rate which implies that the interest to be paid by the borrower will not be above the set ceiling, irrespective of the future changes in interest rates. The bank, however, performs this service for a fee. Loan caps, to some extent, protect the borrowing firm in the event of increase in interest rates, since it will not be paying above a particular interest rate. This implies that banks must be cautious when setting cap rates since they could be adversely affected in periods of very high interest rate.
Illustration 9.4
Assume a borrower is offered a prime rate plus 2% floating rate with a cap of 5% above the initial loan rate. This means that if the prime rate at the time the loan was given was 10%, the initial loan rate would be 12%. With a cap of 5%, the borrowing firm would not pay any rate higher than 17 % (i. e. 12 % + 5%) regardless of any increase in future interest rates.



9.4.5 Consumer Profitability Analysis

The customer profitability analysis (CPA) is similar to the cost-plus loan pricing. The main difference is that it assumes that banks should consider all the revenues and expenses arising from the entire lender-borrower relationship. This implies that all revenues and expenses associated with a particular customer are taken into consideration when pricing a loan. The formula for CPA is given as:
Net before-tax rate of return to the lender from the customer relationship Revenues from loans and other services provided to the customerù
Expenses from providing loans and other services to the customer
=	Netloanable funds used in excess of this customer'sdeposits	.
  Revenues from the customer are those loans and other services provided to this customer. They may include interest on loans, commitment fees, fees for cash management services and data processing charges. Part of the deposit account balances may consist of float and legally required reserves set by the central bank. Thus, in computing the amount of revenues derived from the customer relationship, the bank should deduct legally required reserves from the customerÆs deposit account balances before determining the returns made by investing deposit account balances. It is important to do this, because the full amount of the customerÆs deposits is not usually available to the bank for granting loans or investment. Expenses from providing loans and other services may include wages and salaries of bank staff, credit investigation costs, interest on customer deposits and costs of reconciling and processing accounts. Net loanable funds are the amount of loans

9.4 Pricing of Corporate Loans	199

made to the customer minus their average collected deposits adjusted for required reserves set by the central bank.
  A positive net before-tax rate of return to the lender from the customer relation- ship means that the bank will make a gain over all expenses incurred. Hence, the loan is likely to be granted. However, if it is negative, then the bank may decline the customerÆs loan application. Alternatively, the bank may increase the interest rate on the loan or charge higher fees for other services the customer may request. These strategies are used to maintain a profitable lender-borrower relationship if the computed net before-tax rate of return is negative. Generally, the more the perceived risks associated with a particular customer, the higher the net before-tax rate of return that is expected to accrue to the bank.
Illustration 9.5
A bank has received an application to extend a US$2 million line of credit to Oil Angola Ltd for 1 year. Oil Angola Ltd uses the credit facility and deposits an amount equivalent to 18% of the credit line with the bank. The revenues and expenses from the entire customer relationship are shown below:

Sources of revenue expected to be provided by the borrowing customerUS$11% Interest income from the 12-month loan220,0001% Loan commitment fees20,000Customer deposit management fees58,000FundsÆ transfer charges7000Fees income83,000Total annualised revenues expected388,000Costs expected to be incurred in serving this customer
8% Interest paid on the customerÆs deposit28,800Costs of funds raised to lend to this customer92,000Activity costs for this customerÆs accounts28,000Cost of funds transfer for this customer2100Loan processing costs4200Record keeping costs1800Total annualised expenses156,900Net amount of the bankÆs reserves expected to be drawn upon by this customer this year
Average amount of credit committed to customer2,000,000Minus: Average customer deposit balances net of required reserves324,000Net amount of loanable reserves supplied to customer1,676,000

Net before-tax rate of return to the lender from the customer relationship =
Revenues expected ù Costs expected

Net loanable funds provided to this customer

200	9  Corporate Lending

(US$388, 000 ù US$156, 900)
=	US$1,676,000
= 0.1379
= 13.79%
  The above computation produced a positive net before-tax rate of return from the entire customer relationship, so the loan request put in by the customer is acceptable. This is due to the fact that the revenues cover all the associated costs. If the net rate of return is negative, it suggests the loan to be granted to the customer is not adequately priced. It stands to reason that where the bank perceives the risk on the loan to be high, it would require high net rate of return.

9.5 Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we discus corporate lending. We also identify and examine the various types of loans banks can offer to businesses. We also examine how business loan applications are analysed using financial ratios, how bank loans are priced and the various loan pricing models. The chapter focuses on the following issues:
1. Banks give out different types of loans to businesses. These range from short- term to long-term loans. Short-term loans are loans which mature in 1 year or less, while long-term loans are loans which mature in more than 1 year. Most banks prefer giving out short-term loans because of their relatively low chances of default compared to long-term loans.
2. Businesses raise funds from bank loans in order to finance their day-to-day operations. Banks are also in the business of making loans, among others. However, banks would want to assure themselves that the loans they give out would not become bad debt and as such they will employ various tools in analysing such business loan applications.
3. An important tool used by banks in evaluating corporate loan applications is
financial ratio analysis of the borrowerÆs financial statements.
4. Loan pricing or deciding on how much interest to charge on loans given out is one of the most challenging tasks in lending. This is because the lending bank would want to charge a loan rate high enough to generate enough returns as well as to compensate for the bankÆs risk. At the same time, the bank would want to give out loans whose price is low enough to enable the borrower to comfortably pay and not move to other lenders.
5. There are various pricing models used in pricing bank loans, and these include cost-plus loan pricing, price leadership model, below-prime market pricing (mark-up model), loans bearing maximum interest rates and consumer profitabil- ity analysis.

10.1 Introduction

PeopleÆs material and social needs, standard of living and traditions usually affect their spending and saving behaviour. Moreover, the unlimited nature of human needs and the scarcity of resources necessitate the need to look for extra funds to finance some pressing needs which a personÆs available resources cannot cater for. Economic units with idle resources, on the other hand, give money, material goods and properties to individuals and families in need as loans in exchange for future repayments, which include the principal and finance charges. The concept of con- sumer loans is not new. However, the fast rate at which these loans are growing makes it worth discussing.
  In this chapter, we discuss what consumer loans are, classification of consumer loans and their growth in Africa. We also discuss consumer loan applications, the importance of consumer loans in the development of an economy as well as default risk and its management. The chapter concludes with some current trends in the banking sector with regard to consumer loans.




® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_10

204	10  Consumer and Retail Lending

10.2 Consumer Loans

Consumer loans are loans that are given to people for personal or household purposes by banks and other lending institutions. Consumer loans can also be called consumer credit or consumer lending. Commercial banks, which in times past used to lend to only businesses or corporate bodies, have joined other non-bank financial institutions like finance houses, microfinance institutions, credit unions and savings and loans companies, among other lending institutions, in providing loans to indi- viduals and households. Research suggests that consumer loans form part of the most profitable services offered by banks (Van der Walt and Prinsloo 1993). Consumer loans are loans used by clients for household electronic appliances, education expenses, home improvements, vehicles and vehicle maintenance, rent advance and other personal expenses.



10.2.1 Characteristics of Consumer Loans

A consumer loan is a kind of credit that is granted purposely for personal, family or household use. The targeted market for this product is the low-income, middle- income and self-employed people. The amounts involved are normally not huge, but are dependent on the purpose for which they are sought. They are usually unsecured and are granted mostly on a short- or medium-term basis. In some instances, however, they can be secured and granted on a long-term basis. Consumer loans are lent based on the borrower's integrity and ability to pay. Although customers go through some laid down procedures and provide various documents to acquire such credit, emphasis is laid on the faith/trust the lending institution has in the borrower. Moreover, the terms of a consumer loan, just like any other credit, are agreed upon by the parties involved in the transaction, before the exchange of the requested amount. In some cases, the bank or lending institution requires collateral, which has to be outlined properly in the loan documentation. Lending institutions also have provisions regarding the maximum amount of interest and the length of time for the loan repayment. Consumer loans have proven to be a profitable source of income to lenders. Nonetheless, they are also among the most risky financial products that financial institutions offer. This is because individualsÆ and familiesÆ financial standings are highly unstable compared to those of corporate organisations. Trage- dies such as illness, accidents and loss of employment can easily hamper individual clientsÆ ability to repay loans granted. As a result of this sensitivity, consumer loan portfolios are monitored by government regulatory agencies for their compliance with certain regulations.

10.2 Consumer Loans	205

10.2.2 Classification of Consumer Loans

BanksÆ client needs fall into different categories. For this reason, there are also different kinds of loans to meet the varying needs of customers. These loans can be grouped into secured and unsecured loans, with the secured loans requiring collateral and the unsecured loans having no need for collateral. We can also classify consumer loans as being term or revolving. Term loans are paid off in equal periodical instalments over a given period of time, whereas revolving loans can be spent, repaid and spent again. Yet another mode of classifying loans is according to purpose:ùwhat the funds will be used for by the borrower, or type:ùhow the borrower will repay loan. Consumer loans can generally take the following forms:
Home/Mortgage Loans These are credits or advances obtained by individuals and households to finance the purchase of a house or for the refurbishment of an existing private residence. These are usually long-term loans spanning about 25 years or even more, depending on the repayment arrangement. They are secured by the property itself. Also, the interests on these loans are either fixed or floating, with a specified base rate, say the treasury bill rate. A commitment fee, which is a percentage of the loan amount, is paid upfront by the borrower and the remainder is then paid by the financial institution granting the loan. The amount involved in the loan is dependent on the income of the customer and the value of the property involved. Some banks and mortgage finance companies are also involved in home loans.
Vehicle Loans These are loans advanced to individuals to finance the purchase of private cars. They are usually medium-term loans spanning less than 5 years, although some run for longer times. The loans are secured by the vehicle itself. The loans carry either a fixed or a floating interest rate. In some instances, a commitment fee needs to be paid up front by the borrower and the bank granting the loan pays the remainder. The amount involved in the loan is dependent on the income of the customer and the value of the vehicle involved.
Personal Loans These are loans made out to individuals for personal use, such as paying for rent, car repairs, utility bills and medical bills. They are unsecured and easy to solicit. They are repaid either in a fixed term manner with fixed monthly instalments or with a lump sum on a specified date. Individuals make use of personal loans because they offer an instant remedy to their financial predicaments.
Educational/Student Loans Some students, at some point in time, may have to borrow directly from banks and other lending institutions to finance their education. Some parents may also borrow to pay the fees of their wards in school. There are different packages of educational or student loans available to finance higher education. They are unsecured and interest rates on the loans are lower than on other consumer loans due to partial subsidies on interest payments by government. Also, students who use such loans can defer the start of loan payments to a later date, normally some months after graduation or after they are employed.

206	10  Consumer and Retail Lending

Overdrafts Overdrafts are a special kind of credit granted by banks to their cus- tomers holding current accounts. Overdrafts are unsecured and they enable cus- tomers to withdraw funds well above the amount deposited into their accounts. The part of the funds, which is above the customerÆs balance, constitutes the overdraft or the credit granted to the client and interest is charged accordingly.
Credit Cards Another special kind of credit granted by banks to their customers is access to loans via credit cards. VISA and MasterCard, for example, offer such credit avenues. Credit cards offer customers with a convenient, unsecured, revolving line of credit as and when they need it. Interest is charged on the debt balance that accrues on the card. In spite of this, there are extra risks to banks, emanating from channels like stealing and other fraudulent uses of peopleÆs credit cards as well as from credit cards users who are delinquent borrowers.
Quick Quiz
1. What are consumer loans?
2. How different are consumer loans from commercial loans?
3. What forms do consumer loans take?




10.3 Rapid Growth of Consumer Loans and Consumer LoansÆ Accessibility

The high standard of living and inflation in developing countries, coupled with the slow rate at which peopleÆs disposable incomes are rising, have in recent times propelled many individuals to borrow to finance their existence. The increase in competition among financial institutions to fulfil such needs of customers and to boost their profit levels and customer base has, in turn, made consumer loans highly accessible. High levels of loans and advances on the balance sheets of financial institutions in Africa attest to this fact (Mutero 2014). Moreover, the period when commercial banks were lending to only business organisations is also long gone. Commercial banks have now widened their lending activities to cover households, which have expanded consumer loansÆ accessibility even more. This inclusion of consumer loans in bank portfolios is because banks have also found it advantageous to allocate a good portion of their total amounts in loans to individuals or households.
  Periods of low inflation and low lending rates within the banking industry help lending institutions to grant more loans to individuals. Thus, in times of high inflation and high lending rates, loans become more costly, and although individuals and households may have need for loans, they will shy away from accessing credit due to the high cost involved. Venkatesan (2014) suggests that banks are gradually tightening their lending to both consumers and businesses due to challenges in the macroeconomic environment. Notwithstanding the high rate of interest in recent

10.4 Factors that Influence ClientÆs Choice of a Lender for Consumer Loans	207

times, customer consumer loans are still among the fastest growing products offered by financial institutions. Personal loans, for example, are an easy option for people with financial needs, since such borrowers go through less stringent procedures, and virtually no collateral is required to obtain these loans.
Quick Quiz
1. How accessible are consumer loans in Africa currently?
2. Is there the need to control the rate at which consumer loans are growing? (Discussion)



10.4 Factors that In?uence ClientÆs Choice of a Lender for Consumer Loans

There are various reasons why individuals need loans. A request for a loan by an individual usually implies that the individual is ready and willing to sacrifice his/her future consumption to achieve some level of satisfaction, which is presumed better today than tomorrow. By so doing, the individual commits to forfeiting some future benefits and accepts to settle current debts with future incomes. The current needs of an individual determine the kind of loan he or she will demand. However, individ- uals are also influenced by some factors or conditions to access loans from a particular financial institution. Moreover, these conditions are put in place by the financial institutions to attract their customer base. Some of the driving forces that influence a clientÆs choice of a particular institution for consumer loans are:
ò The ease in obtaining loan: this has to do with the procedure an individual who needs a loan has to go through to acquire a loan. A cumbersome, time-consuming and highly demanding or stringent procedure deters applicants. For this reason, lending institutions have over the years tried to ease the process of acquiring loans. Individuals favour institutions with easy procedures but that could also be detrimental to lendersÆ ability to properly assess the creditworthiness of appli- cants. While easing the loan originating process, there is also the need for lenders to follow laid down regulations and procedures as much as possible, thus balancing the twin requirements of client comfort and organisational prudence.
ò The need for no collateral: consumer loans, as noted earlier, are usually not secured, that is, they do not require collateral. Notwithstanding this characteristic, a few loan facilities such as mortgage and loans for some durable assets, like vehicles, do require collateral. The demand for a collateral by lenders before granting loans deters clients in accessing loans. For this reason, individuals prefer loans from institutions which do not require collateral before granting loans. However, in the case of loans which require collateral, there is the need for lending institutions to insist on using those assets for which the customer needs the loan as the underlying collateral for granting the loan.

208	10  Consumer and Retail Lending

ò Administrative/processing fees: loans come with administrative fees, which are charges for processing the loans. Borrowers prefer lower charges and banks with lower processing fees are better able to attract many customers. However, with such fees being another source of income to the lending institutions, lenders need to check the mix of potential income and customer size to find the right balance.
ò Costs of credit (interest): the interest component of loans is the extra burden customers bear for choosing to borrow for present spending and to repay with future disposable income. Conversely, interests on loans generate the income earned by lenders for granting loans. High interest on loans, however, constitutes a disincentive to borrowers as it usually means a higher repayment amount or a longer term for the loan. Consequently, financial institutions with high interest loans are not likely to attract clients.
ò Terms of loan repayment: this refers to the arrangements regarding the loan being granted. These include the repayment period, repayment mode and repayment amount. Customers generally prefer loans with more flexible terms. In essence, financial institutions with friendlier terms of loan repayment are more likely to enjoy a larger share of marketÆs portfolio of loans.
Quick Quiz
1. What are the factors individual borrowers take into account when selecting where to solicit for a loan?
2. What are some of the enticing means by which lenders can draw borrowers?


10.5 Consumer Credit Rating/Scoring

Before granting loans to customers, it is typical of banks to first rate the creditwor- thiness of the customers to assess whether they qualify for the loans or not. Feldman (1997) explained credit scoring as the means by which a single quantitative measure is assigned to a loan applicant or a would-be borrower and this represents an estimate of the borrowerÆs ability to repay the loan in the future. Some of the ways by which a lender rates customers to determine their ability to repay loans before granting them are:
Accounting-Based Model Accounting ratios are widely used by banks in a bid to limit adverse selection and moral hazard problems in loan advancements. Financial ratios derived from balance sheets, profit and loss accounts and the debt-to-dispos- able income are regarded as good predictors of default.
Survival-Based Credit Scoring Model This model measures loan response or loan repayment time. It is used for checking the time when loans granted or being granted to customers are likely to go æbadÆ. This is crucial for effective credit management policies of lenders.
  The models mentioned are more business-based than customer-based. Also, the liquidity and financial activities of customers are considered the most crucial factors

10.6 Loan Application Process	209

in determining creditworthiness of clients. Lenders usually focus on anticipated changes in the income of consumers, as well as their ability to use future income to settle the credit being made available to them. The FICO scoring system is one of the current and widely used scoring systems around the world.
The FICO Scoring System It is a type of credit scoring system created by the Fair Isaac Corporation and it is used to estimate the likelihood of default based on historical data on loan performance and personal data of a borrower. Banks and lending institutions use borrowing customersÆ FICO scores in addition to other information on the borrowing customersÆ credit report for assessing their credit risk and determining whether or not to grant the loan facility. FICO scores take into consideration information such as the history of the borrowerÆs payments, the length of the credit history, amount currently owed, the types of credit already accessed and new credit accounts. FICO scores predict credit risk with a scoring range of 300 to 850. An applicant with a higher value represents lesser credit risk and has a higher probability of timely repayment of loan advanced than an applicant with a lower score. Generally, customers with scores above 650 suggest a very good credit history and loans granted to them are classified as æprime loansÆ. But cus- tomers with scores below 620 mostly have difficulty obtaining loans on favourable terms. To ascertain the creditworthiness of borrowers, lending institutions take into account the FICO score of borrowers as well as other relevant information such as age, religion, race, salary, marital status, employment status and residential details.
Quick Quiz
1. What crucial factors or variables do lenders consider before granting loans to consumers?
2. What systems do lenders use to rate the creditworthiness of potential borrowers?


10.6 Loan Application Process

The fundamental object of banks is to take funds from depositors and lend them out to borrowers. The business of lending comes with some risk. In fact, banksÆ main source of funds is deposits from customers and these funds are payable as and when these customers need them. Banks are thus very careful when lending the funds deposited by some customers to others and follow some principles in lending to customers. Below are some of those principles:
ò Liquidity: customers can request funds deposited with banks anytime. Therefore, it is imperative that even as banks seek to grant loans to other customers who are in need, there is also the need to keep in mind that they as banks are supposed to be liquid enough to provide depositors with their funds whenever they need them. Due to this, banks usually lend for short durations of time and engage in other investment activities to ensure that they can easily recoup some funds to meet their depositorsÆ withdrawal requirements.

210	10  Consumer and Retail Lending

ò Safety: this refers to how safe funds lent are or should be. In essence, the borrower should be in a position to repay the loan with interest within the stipulated time and at the agreed intervals without any failure.
ò Diversity: this principle has to do with the maxim of ænot putting all your eggs in one basketÆ. As much as banks know that consumer loans are profitable, they also need to keep in mind that the majority are unsecured and for that matter all their funds need not be channelled into any one class of loans.
ò Profitability: this is supposed to be the cardinal principle when granting loans to customers. It is important for banks to lend where it can earn sufficient profits; however, they should only grant a loan to an individual or an economic entity with high probability of repaying.
  When banks abide by these principles, they ensure the safety of depositorsÆ and shareholdersÆ funds.
  Again, because most consumer loans granted do not require collateral, lenders need to properly scrutinise clients to assess their creditworthiness before loans are granted in order to reduce the probability of loan defaults. Despite the need for proper checks before granting loans, regulations regarding lending and borrowing of nearly every country prevent financial institutions from discriminating against cli- ents on any basis except on their creditworthiness. However, a personÆs creditwor- thiness is dependent on some personal characteristics, most notably, the personÆs income, record of previous loans collected and repayment and the personÆs relation- ship with the institution granting the loan.
  The time taken to access a consumer loan is not long. Typically, they take a minimum of one day and a maximum of about five days (Rose and Hudgins 2013). Others like the home and vehicle loans, however, take a bit longer. The applicant provides documents that detail the essential financials and other information about the borrower. The lenderÆs decision to lend is based on the information taken from the borrower. The applicant is expected to specify the loan amount and purpose, the period and means of repayment and guaranties and/or collateral offered (if needed). There is usually a standard form for the applicant to fill in all the information needed. A loan application is neither a pledge by the applicant nor a commitment by the lender to grant the loan. Until all the parties have signed, none of the parties is bound to fulfil his or her obligations. Some of the requirements in the loan application process include national ID card, confirmation letter from employer, last three monthsÆ payslips, six months bank statement, tenancy agreement (for rent advance), collateral document, where applicable (this includes post-dated cheques), passport size pictures, letter of introduction from employer (where applicable), residential details and guarantor(s) (where applicable).
  The key factors considered by lenders in granting consumer loans are the borrowerÆs character and his or her ability to pay. A lender assesses the risk profile of a borrower using the information taken, and based on the risk profile of the borrower, a loan is granted or denied. A borrower therefore has higher chances of qualifying for a loan if he or she earns relatively high and/or has a stable income. In addition, if the borrower has a good amount of deposits with the lending institution,

10.7 Consumer Loans and Economic Development	211

he or she has a better chance of accessing credit from that institution. Also, if the applicant has held his or her employment for quite a long time, he or she stands a better chance. Moreover, a foreign resident of a country, or someone with quite a long-term residence status, stands a better chance of qualifying for a loan. Besides the above, if the applicant has a property like a building or a car, the person stands a better chance of accessing credit, since that could serve as collateral, although it may not be required.
  The challenge lending institutions face in granting consumer loans is that they are difficult to evaluate. This is because individuals can conceal important information compared to businesses. Moreover, individuals are not able to easily adjust to shock like injury, ill health and financial setbacks compared to business units. The key characteristics of consumer loans, which gives lenders an escape route, are that they are normally smaller amounts.
Quick Quiz
1. What cardinal principles guide financial institutions when providing loans to consumers?
2. What are some of the key issues financial institutions consider before lending to a borrowing customer?
3. Briefly describe the consumer loan application process.


10.7 Consumer Loans and Economic Development

Consumer loans benefit both the lenders and the borrowers. The lenders take the risks and for that matter enjoy the benefits. These benefits accrue from profitably managing the deposits of their customers by lending them out as loans while making funds available to the depositors when they want to withdraw their deposits. By so doing, they receive interests and other finance charges which boost their profits and their overall operation in terms of expanding to cover unreached areas. On the other hand, credit availability makes it possible for individuals and households to get extra funds above their current disposable income to spend on things they urgently need today and will pay later with future incomes. In a nutshell, consumer loans help consumers make future purchases now.
  In terms of the economy, borrowing to finance consumption can propel the growth of an economy. Higher profits for financial institutions mean higher taxes for government, which can be used to develop the economy of a country. Moreover, the increase in consumption will also increase the growth of GDP, which is a key indicator of growth in an economy. Also, expansion in financial institutions in terms of opening of new branches can help improve financial inclusion in an economy.
  Notwithstanding all the above-mentioned benefits, the relationship that interplays among inflation, interest rate, household disposable income, savings and debt is a cause for concern, in that high levels of debt can impede future spending of households, and therefore, in the long run, economic growth and development

212	10  Consumer and Retail Lending

may slow down. This is true in the sense that high inflation spikes interest rates, which translates into high household debts, which are supposed to be offset by future disposable incomes. This will decrease household savings, which will in turn reduce how much banks will have at their disposal to further lend to customers. This will stifle banks, and some might even collapse. Barba and Pivetti (2009) attest to this fact.
Quick Quiz
1. How beneficial are consumer loans to borrowers, lender and the economy?
2. Can consumer loans be bad for an economy? At what stage?



10.8 Default Risk and Default Risk Management

Financial institutions grant consumer loans based on trust in the borrowersÆ ability to repay. As a result of the keen competition in the banking industry and the desire of banks to improve their profitability with the high interest rates they charge on consumer loans, banks are lending bigger sums to individuals and households than the deposits they receive from them. Due to this lending side of business, banks are liable to the risk of defaults by customers. MoodyÆs, a global rating agency, defined default as any missed or delayed payment of interest and/or principal. Default contributes to the buildup of high levels of bad debts and non-performing loans (NPLs). It can result in huge losses to financial institutions and may cause some of them to be sold or be merged with other larger institutions or even collapse. This will negatively affect the entire banking system of the country and the economy as a whole.
  The reason for default in consumer loans is largely because borrowers are individuals, and for that matter, a shock to their financial stability in the form of loss of employment, business downturn for the non-salaried workers or ill health impacts greatly on their ability to repay their debt. For this reason, banks must take adequate steps in addition to what they do generally to manage their risk exposure and reduce the rate of default among borrowers of especially consumer loans.
  Without a doubt, credit risk management plays a very important role in the banking sector. Banks and lending institutions can consider the following steps to ensure the reduction in the rate of defaults among borrowers:
ò Improved eligibility criteria to ensure proper screening of clients before granting loans: procedures for soliciting, examining, accessing and making decisions on customer loan applications should be strictly followed to eliminate unworthy borrowers.
ò Repayments could be collected through deductions from payroll of borrowers who are salary earners.
ò Lenders can also request customers to include their own life insurance policy and pension benefits to their loan applications. For instance, in Ghana, the National

10.9 Currents Trends in the Consumers LoansÆ Market	213

Pensions Act 2008 (Act 766) and the National Pensions (Amendment) Act 2014 (Act 883) allow for the use of a memberÆs second-tier benefits to secure a mortgage to acquire a primary residence. Thus, lenders can make use of that to reduce the risk of default.
ò Lending institutions should also strengthen their credit administration and recov- ery procedures.
Quick Quiz
1. How does the risk of default arise with consumer loans?
2. How can lenders mitigate default risk?



10.9 Currents Trends in the Consumers LoansÆ Market

Banks and lending institutions, in their bid to capture larger markets, are repackaging their consumer loans. They are adopting easy means of granting loans on highly flexible terms of payment in order to attract many customers. Some of the offers they have come up with include:
ò Facilitating faster loan processing
ò Lowering administrative/processing fees
ò Entering into new arrangements of credit with institutions for their employees
ò Opening more branches at convenient places to customers
ò Removal of down payments before granting loans
ò Allowing borrowers to start paying instalments after some months after taking loan
ò Increasing loan amount after some months of consistent repayments of existing loans
ò Waiving the need for collateral for some loans which previously required collaterals
  Another current issue of concern in todayÆs consumer loans market is the issue of predatory lending and subprime loans. This is a practice where some banks and lending institutions lend to customers with below-average credit record. Such customers have a record of default in loan repayment or bankruptcies. They are mostly associated with home loans. Due to their low credit rating, interest rates on loans granted to such borrowers are high, and as a result, some of them run the risk of losing their properties owing to defaults. Moreover, borrowers with lower incomes are not able to access such loans because of the high interest rates. Although such loans are difficult to regulate and are seemingly unethical, they open up avenues for low credit-rated borrowers to access credit.

214	10  Consumer and Retail Lending

10.10 Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we discus consumer and retail lending. We look at the types of consumer loans, the growth of consumer loans, the consumer loan application process, consumer loans and economic development, default risk and default risk management and current trends in consumersÆ loans market. We mentioned that:
1. Consumer loans are loans that are given to people for personal or household purposes by banks and other lending institutions. They serve as a good means by which individuals and families can benefit from future consumption today and repay with future income at a charge.
2. Consumer loans can generally take the form of home/mortgage loans, vehicle loans, personal loans, educational/student loans, overdrafts and credit cards.
3. Some of the driving forces that influence a clientÆs choice of a particular institu- tion for consumer loans include the ease in obtaining the loan, the need for no collateral, reasonable administrative/processing fees, affordable costs of credit (interest) and easy terms of loan repayment.
4. Banks need to rate the creditworthiness of the customers first to assess whether they qualify for the loans or not. Some of the models used by lending institution to rate customers and ascertain and/or determine their creditworthiness include accounting-based model, survival-based credit scoring model and the FICO scoring system.
5. Consumer loans benefit the banks and the borrowers. Consumer loans offer banks an opportunity to earn more profit from the interest and other finance charges they get on advances made to individual borrowers. Borrowing to finance consump- tion can propel the growth of an economy; however, defaults in loans granted could ruin the survival of the banks that grant them as well as cause a decline in the growth of an economy. There is therefore the need for banks to take adequate steps to reduce the probability of default.
6. Banks and lending institutions can repackage their consumer loans to capture a larger market by facilitating faster loan processing, by lowering administrative/ processing fees, by entering into new arrangements of credit with institutions for their employees, by opening more branches at convenient places to customers, by the removal of down payments before granting loans, by allowing borrowers to start paying instalments after some months after taking loan, by increasing loan amount after some months of consistent repayments of existing loans and by waiving the need for collateral for some loans which previously required collaterals.

Chapter 11
Mortgage Banking











Learning Objectives
After reading this chapter, you will understand:
ò What mortgages are
ò The mortgage origination process
ò How lenders assess a mortgage loan application
ò The types of mortgage products offered
ò Risks from mortgage origination, mortgage lending and mortgage investing
ò The challenges of mortgage markets in Africa


11.1 Introduction

A mortgage is a loan used to acquire a property, and the property usually serves as collateral for the loan. This means that if the borrower is unable to pay, the lender can seize the property and sell it to recover the loan granted. This is termed as foreclo- sure. The lender is also called the originator of the loan. Mortgage banking is the process of originating mortgages. Mortgage originators are usually financial institu- tions. The types of financial institution that originate mortgage loans, however, vary by country. Popular mortgage originators, however, include commercial banks, savings and loan associations, credit unions, insurance companies and pension funds. The mortgage may be required for a home. This is known as a residential mortgage. Any other mortgage is a non-residential mortgage. Non-residential mort- gages are usually taken on commercial properties. The buyer of the property is referred to as the mortgagor while the lender is the mortgagee.
  The mortgage contract, among other things, will include the duration of the loan, the rate on the loan, how often repayments are expected and any collateral required by the lender. Most mortgage products are targeted at employees in the formal sector, and this has led to a huge housing gap, because most employees in Africa are found in the informal sector.


® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_11

217

218	11  Mortgage Banking

  In this chapter, we discuss the primary market for mortgages, fixed versus adjustable rate mortgages, factors that affect the value of a mortgage, secondary market for mortgages, risks from mortgage origination, risks associated with mort- gage lending, risks from mortgage investing, challenges confronting the mortgage/ housing markets and how to improve the mortgage and housing markets in Africa.


11.2 Primary Market for Mortgages

Mortgages are originally issued in the primary market and can subsequently be traded in the secondary markets. In essence, the mortgage loans are granted in the primary market. The primary market for mortgages is the market where the mort- gages are originated. It involves the borrower applying for a mortgage loan from a mortgage originator, and this process is known as origination. The principal mort- gage originators include mortgage banks, commercial banks and mortgage finance companies. In some developed financial markets, principal mortgage originators include life insurance companies and pension funds.
  The originator charges a number of fees to originate a mortgage loan. These fees include origination fees, application and processing fees and a service fee. The origination fee is usually a percentage of the loan granted and has to be paid up front by the borrower. This fee is usually between 0.5% and 2% in the USA. The fees are much higher in developing and emerging countries because these markets tend to be less efficient. Lenders are usually willing to take a lower fee for large volume loans due to economies of scale. For example, the origination fee for a US
$1,000,000 loan may be 2%. A borrower taking a US$10,000,000 loan may be able to negotiate a lower rate, say 0.5%. Lenders are also usually more willing to charge a lower origination fee on expensive loans than to less expensive loans. The lender may even require the borrower to pay a fee before the application can be considered. This represents the application and processing fee. The fee is to enable the lender conduct basic credit checks and property appraisals and cover other administrative costs.
  The payment of the application fee does not guarantee that the borrower will receive the loan. The service fee is charged by the originator to cover the adminis- tration of the loan. The administration function includes sending the borrower periodic statements, sending reminders when the required payments have not been made and, in extremis, foreclosing the loan. As we will see later, the originator does not have to necessarily perform the servicing function. This function can be outsourced to another institution that will then receive the service fee. Some lenders may give the borrower a commitment letter. The commitment letter may have a duration of 1û2 months. The letter commits the lender to lend if the borrower decides to take up the loan. Thus, though the letter obligates the lender to lend, it places no such obligation on the borrower. The borrower thus has an option to take the loan or not. For this flexibility, the borrower will pay a commitment fee. The commitment fee is on the undisbursed amount of the loan that the lender has committed to lend.

11.2 Primary Market for Mortgages	219

  Once you take a mortgage loan, the lender will require that you make periodic payments on the loan. There are various types of payments that can be made. The basic ones are discount loans, interest-only loans and amortising loans. For a discount loan, the amount the borrower receives is at a discount from the face or maturity value of the loan. The borrower pays the face value of the loan upon maturity. For an interest-only loan, the borrower pays only interest and at maturity pays the face value of the loan. An example of an interest-only loan is a balloon loan. For an amortising loan, the required payments made by the borrower comprise both interest and principal repayments.


11.2.1 How Do Lenders Assess Credit Applications?

There are two main indicators used by lenders to assess a mortgage loan application. These are the payment-to-income ratio (PTI) and the loan-to-value ratio (LTV). The PTI ratio compares the monthly payment required on the loan to the borrowerÆs monthly income. The lender usually prefers a lower PTI ratio because the lender is exposed to lower risk. For example, assume that Kwaku Manu wants to take a mortgage loan from HFC Bank. The monthly payment required on the loan is US
$2500. Kwaku Manu earns a monthly income of US$4000. This means that the payment on the loan represents 62.5% (2500/4000) of KwakuÆs income. LetÆs also assume that Ama Mansah also wants to take a mortgage loan from HFC Bank. The loan amount and monthly payments are the same as those of Kwaku Manu. Ama, however, earns US$10,000 a month. This means that the payment required forms only 25% of her monthly income (i.e. 2500/10,000). HFC Bank will incur lower risk if it lends to Ama compared to lending to Kwaku. Put differently, all else being the same, Ama should find it easier to pay the loan compared to Kwaku. Most lenders have a policy on what the maximum PTI can be. LetÆs assume that the PTI for HFC Bank is 40%. This means that the bank will reject KwakuÆs application because his salary is too low to service the required payments on the loan. He has to use 62.5% of his salary to service his loan, whereas the bank requires that a borrower should not use more than 40% of his salary to service a loan. A high PTI is also not good for the borrower. If the borrower has to use say 80û90% of his salary to pay for a loan, then he is likely to struggle to make the payments and is more likely to default.
  There are two types of PTIs. These are the front and back ratios. The front ratio is based only on the payments required on the mortgage. Thus, the payments required on the mortgage are divided by the income of the borrower (pre-tax income). For the back ratio, the mortgage payments and payments on other debt facilities, such as car and personal loan payments, are included. Thus, the total repayment that a borrower is required to make is divided by their income (pre-tax income). Front and back ratios between 28% and36% are considered as prime loans. Prime loans are high- quality loans, while subprime loans refer to low-quality loans.
  The loan-to-value (LTV) ratio compares the loan that the borrower is taking to the value of the property that the borrower wants to purchase. For example, Deborah

220	11  Mortgage Banking

Narh wants to buy a house at East Legon Hills costing US$800,000. Stanbic Banks has approved DeborahÆs loan request of US$600,000. This means the loan-to-value ratio is 75% (600,000/800,000). This means that Deborah has to make a down payment of US$200,000. Banks usually will prefer a lower LTV because it exposes the bank to lower risk. For example, if Korkor Narh was purchasing a similar property worth US$800,000 but has to borrow US$750,000, the bank is taking more risk with this loan compared to the earlier loan. The LTV for KorkorÆs loan will be 93.75%. The lower LTV is due to the fact that it will be easier to recover US
$600,000 compared to recovering US$750,000 if the borrower cannot fulfil her obligation on the loan, especially when housing prices are declining. Further, there are lower agency problems when the LTV is low. When the LTV is lower, it suggests that the borrower has invested much more in the property than when the LTV is high, all else being the same. That is, the borrower has more equity in the property. The borrower is therefore much more likely to see the property as his and therefore is more likely to take care of the property and maintain it. The borrower will also not like to lose his home and is therefore more likely to service the loan. If the LTV is very high, say 100%, then the borrower is not likely to see the property as his or hers and is less likely to take good care of the property and maintain it. Mortgage originators are likely to have a policy on the LTV. If the lending bankÆs policy on the LTV is 80%, then Deborah will qualify for the loan; however, Korkor will be denied. This is because the LTV for Deborah is 75%, which is lower than the maximum LTV of 80% set by the bank. On the other hand, KorkorÆs LTV of 93.75% is higher than the bankÆs maximum LTV of 80%. Usually, LTVs lower than 95% are considered as prime loans.
  In addition to using these ratios, banks can check the credit history of the borrower. In developed markets, lenders rely on credit scores to evaluate the health of a borrower. These scores are based on the FICO methodology (as discussed in Chap. 10). Higher scores indicate higher credit quality or a lower risk of default. In Ghana, a bank can find out the creditworthiness of a borrower from any of the registered credit reference bureaus (CRBs). Furthermore, banks are likely to check the employment history of the potential borrower. This has become important, especially because aggressive lending by some banks that gave loans to people who were not employed has been identified as one of the factors that contributed to the global financial crisis in 2007.


11.3 Fixed Versus Adjustable Rate Mortgages

With a fixed rate mortgage (FRM), the interest rate on the loan is fixed for the entire duration of the loan. On the other hand, the rate on an adjustable rate mortgage (ARM) varies with changes in interest rates and is not fixed over the duration of the loan. In the case of ARM, the interest rate may be reset, say every 6 months, and is tied to a benchmark interest rate such as the treasury bill rate. The loan contract will indicate the frequency of adjustment as well as how rates will be determined.

11.3 Fixed Versus Adjustable Rate Mortgages	221

Typically, the interest rate on an adjustable rate mortgage will be lower than the rate on a fixed rate mortgage at the inception of the mortgage contract. This is to compensate borrowers for assuming the interest rate risk. The interest rate risk exposure of either the bank or the borrower depends on how interest rates change and whether the mortgage is an FRM or an ARM.
  LetÆs start with an FRM. For an FRM, if interest rates rise, the bank loses because it cannot reset the rate on the loan. The borrower gains because he or she will be paying a rate which is lower than prevailing interest rates. Indeed, an increase in interest rates can lead to the insolvency of a bank, especially when the bank has borrowed short and lent long. If interest rates decrease, the lender gains and the borrower loses. This is because the lender will be earning rates that are higher than rates on current loans. The borrower loses because the rate she or he will be paying will be higher than current interest rates. The borrower is therefore likely to refinance by borrowing money from another lender at current low interest rates to pay off the loan from the original lender. The process of refinancing, however, is not costless. The borrower is likely to pay a penalty to the original lender to terminate the mortgage contract. Also, the borrower will incur an origination fee that will have to be paid to the new lender or originator. It is likely, however, that a borrower may assess these costs to be lower than the potential savings derived from terminating the original mortgage. In essence, this becomes a positive NPV decision for the bor- rower because the costs (termination fee and origination fees) are lower than the potential benefits (interest cost savings).
  For an ARM, interest rates on the loan increase when market interest rates increase and decline when market rates reduce. The borrower is therefore exposed to rising interest rates and benefits when interest rates reduce. An ARM is also usually better for a financial institution because the return on its loans will move in the same direction as the cost of its funds. This makes the spread of the financial institution more stable. Generally, the ARM is fair to both the lender and the borrower.
Example 11.1
Assume that Poloko Ncube wants to buy a property at Tyger Valley Estate. The property is a three-bedroom apartment going for US$450,000. 3. Find the loan-to-value ratio. Will Poloko qualify for this loan if the lender requires a PTI of 90%?

However, the interest savings are almost 30% of the original interest. This is because the interest being paid by the borrower for the 15-year loan forms 70% (864,533/1,230,871) of the original interest payment. The monthly payment jumps from US$6795.30 to US
$7025.19. This is necessary so that we can pay the same loan in 15 years instead of 20 years. Thus, borrowers may want to pay off their loans over a short time period if they can afford to do so. Shorter maturities are also beneficial for the lender. This is because in this example, the lender will be exposed to interest rate risk and other risks such as credit and prepayment risk for only 15 years as compared to being exposed for 20 years.

This is because the interest being paid by the borrower for the 10-year loan forms 43% (527,627/1,230,871) of the original interest payment. The monthly payment jumps from US$6795.30 to US
