Shorter maturities are also beneficial for the lender. This is because, as in this example, the lender will be exposed to interest rate risk and other risks such as credit and prepayment risk for only 10 years as compared to being exposed for 20 years.

  Most mortgage loan products in many African countries are priced in US dollars. This is because these institutions usually obtain their funds offshore. Thus, they give their loans in dollars and other foreign currencies such as the pound to reduce their exposure to foreign exchange risk. They are able to reduce foreign exchange risk because both their cost of funds and cash flows are in dollars and other foreign currencies such as the pound.
  Usually, the borrower is required to pay back the loan once the property is sold or disposed of. This is because the property has been used as collateral. Essentially, once the property is sold, the lender can no longer fall on it in the event of default.



11.3.1 Other Mortgage Products

Some other mortgage financing products apart from FRMs and ARMs are discussed in this section. Some of these products are home purchase mortgages, home equity mortgages, home completion mortgages, home improvement mortgages, graduated payment mortgages, growing equity mortgages, second mortgages, balloon-payment mortgages and shared appreciation mortgages.
  A home purchase mortgage is given to the borrower to buy or purchase an identified property. Some mortgage lenders also give the borrower funds to build their own house from scratch. With a home equity mortgage, a homeowner is able to release equity or obtain money from their property. Thus, they take a loan from a lender using their property as collateral. A borrower can take a home completion and a home improvement mortgage to complete a building that they have started and/or to improve/renovate an existing building, respectively.
  Graduated payment mortgages (GPMs) are mortgages whose payments graduate or increase over the life of the mortgage and level off after a certain period of time. Thus, for a 15-year mortgage for example, the payments may increase till the tenth year. After the tenth year, the payments level off or no longer increase. Growing equity mortgages (GEMs) are similar to GPMs. The only difference is that the payments on a GEM never level off. Thus, the loan may be paid off before the scheduled maturity. Borrowers who expect their salaries to increase over time may opt for a GPM or a GEM.
  A second mortgage is taken by a borrower to complete a first or primary mortgage. A lender or seller of a house may offer the borrower a second mortgage. A seller of a house may provide a second mortgage to the borrower to make the home more affordable. The rate on a second mortgage will usually be higher than the rate on the first mortgage. This is to compensate the lender for the additional risk that they are taking. This is because the first mortgage has priority over the second in terms of payment, for example, if default should occur. Usually, the maturity of the

11.4 Factors that Affect the Value of a Mortgage	229

second mortgage is shorter than the maturity on the first or primary mortgage. This reduces the risk exposure of the second lender.
  Balloon-payment mortgage is similar to the payments on a bond. The borrower pays interest only and is required to pay the entire principal upon maturity. In essence, the required payments increase or balloon. Most borrowers may have to refinance the mortgage loan because they have not saved enough to pay the ballooned amount or principal. For a shared appreciation mortgage, the lending institution lends to the borrower at below market interest rates. In exchange, the lender acquires an equity stake in the property. This stake is negotiated between the borrower and lender. The equity stake entitles the lender to a share of rents and the value of the property if it is eventually sold.



11.4 Factors that Affect the Value of a Mortgage

A mortgage or mortgage security is valued just like any financial asset. The value of a mortgage is the present value of the anticipated cash flows from holding the mortgage or mortgage security. The cash flows are mainly interest and principal repayments. Thus, the value of a mortgage is the present value of the expected interest and principal repayments from holding the mortgage. The value of a mortgage is expressed as follows:
PV or value of mortgage = X Interest + Principal
n=1
  The formula shows an inverse relationship between rates and the present value or the value of a mortgage. An increase in rates will reduce the present value or value of the mortgage. On the other hand, a reduction in interest rates will lead to an increase in the value of the mortgage.
  Factors that impact the rate will have an effect on the value of the mortgage. The rate a borrower obtains is a function of the risk-free rate and a risk premium. Factors that influence the risk-free rate will therefore have an effect on the value of a mortgage (Madura 2014). An increase in inflationary expectations will lead to an increase in the risk-free rate and, therefore, a reduction in the value of mortgages. An increase in economic growth will lead to an increase in the risk-free rate and consequently a reduction in the value of mortgages. An increase in money supply generally leads to a reduction in interest rates, assuming it does not stoke inflationary expectations. The reduction in interest rates due to an increase in money supply will therefore lead to an increase in the value of mortgages. Finally, an increase in government budget deficits exerts an upward pressure on interest rates due to the excess demand for loanable funds by the government. This increase in demand for loanable funds thus leads to an increase in interest rates and consequently a reduction in the value of mortgages.

230	11  Mortgage Banking

  Some of the factors that account for the risk premium include default and liquidity risk. When credit scores are low, for example during a recession, the interest rates on mortgages are likely to increase, leading to a reduction in the value of mortgages. A less liquid mortgage security poses higher risk to investors and thus deserves a higher interest rate, which should translate into a lower value.


11.5 Secondary Market for Mortgages

Mortgages issued in the primary market can be sold in the secondary market, which provides the opportunity to the originator to lay off the mortgage asset from its balance sheet. The secondary market facilitates mortgage activities because it enables the originator to sell old loans to obtain new funds that can be used to give out new loans. Assuming GHL Bank originates a 15-year mortgage loan in the primary market. It has the option of selling this loan to another financial institution before maturity. Investors in mortgage securities now fund the old loans. Further- more, without an active secondary market, the primary market will be highly constrained because once a loan is issued, the originator has to hold the loan asset to maturity, thus bearing the risk and rewards associated with the asset. A risk-averse financial institution may issue fewer loans in this situation.
  Nowadays, most banks or lending institutions decide not to keep all the loans they have granted on their balance sheets. They sell them to other institutions or investors through a variety of means. The loans may be sold individually or in a pool of loans through a securitisation process. In an asset securitisation process, the issuer sells an asset, which is usually loans and issues securities based on these assets. When mortgage loans are securitised, the resultant securities are called mortgage-backed securities (MBSs). Other loans such as student loans and credit card loans can also be securitised. In the asset securitisation process, the assets are sold to a special purpose vehicle (SPV), which may be set up for that purpose. The issuer therefore receives cash in exchange for selling the loans. To finance the purchase of the loans, the SPV sells securities, usually bonds, to investors in the capital markets. In exchange for these securities, the investors pay cash to the SPV. The SPVs provide credit enhancement and therefore enable the issuer to sell the securities at a lower rate or cost. Due to the fact that the loans are sold to the SPV, other creditors cannot lay their hands on these loans. This makes the position of the investors in securities safer or more secure. Another form of credit enhancement is purchasing insurance against the probability of default on these securities. These insurance contracts used to be sold by insurance companies and were referred to as credit default swaps (CDS). When the issuer receives the interest and principal payments on the loans, because it is no longer the beneficial owner, it would pass through these payments to investors. These securities are also therefore referred to as pass-through securities.
  There are several potential benefits to a lender if it decides to securitise its loans. A lender can use asset securitisation to reduce its risk. When the assets are securitised, the risks inherent in the loans are now borne by investors. These risks

11.5 Secondary Market for Mortgages	231

include interest rate risk, prepayment risk and credit risk. A lender will be able to diversify its source of funding and reduce its cost of capital. If the lender maintains the servicing rights, it can still earn the service fee, though it no longer funds the loan. A lender can use the securitisation process to manage its regulatory capital. Once the loans have been securitised or offloaded from the balance sheet, the lender is not required to hold regulatory capital against those loans. Borrowers also benefit from the asset securitisation process. Borrowers can get a lower rate because the lender is able to reduce its cost of funding through the securitisation process. It can therefore pass on some of the cost savings to its borrowers. There are also benefits available to investors who purchase securities issued through the asset securitisation process. Investors who purchase these securities are able to invest in an otherwise illiquid asset (a mortgage loan with a long maturity). In addition, investors are exposed to lower credit risk since the loans are pooled and are therefore diversified. There are a number of disadvantages associated with the asset securitisation process. The banks issuing the securities may not have an incentive to ensure that the loans originated are of high quality. This may have been one of the factors that contributed to the global financial crisis. Furthermore, there are concerns that the asset securitisation process can lead to financial disintermediation and reduce the profitability of financial intermediaries such as banks. This is because the funds lent
are now provided by investors in the capital markets.
  Some mortgages have been sold as derivative-style instruments through what is termed collaterised debt obligations (CDOs). Unlike a bond issue, where risk is spread thinly between all the bondholders, with CDOs, risk is concentrated in investment layers or ætranchesÆ.



11.5.1 The Structure of CDOs

The typical structure of CDOs is as follows:
Tranche 1 (highest risk), known as the æequityÆ tranche, normally comprises 5û10% of the value of the mortgages in the pool. Throughout the life of the CDOs, the equity tranche absorbs any losses arising from the default by the mortgage holders, up to the point where the principal that underpins the tranche is fully exhausted. At this point, the investment becomes worthless (see Ryan 2009).
Tranche 2 (intermediate risk or æmezzanineÆ tranche) is made up of about 10% of the principal and absorbs any losses not absorbed by the equity tranche until the point where the principal is also exhausted (see Ryan 2009).
Tranche 3 (AAA or æseniorÆ tranche) is made up of the balance of the pool value and absorbs any residual losses that may arise. Institutional investors who want safe assets are likely to invest in this tranche (see Ryan 2009).

232	11  Mortgage Banking

11.5.2 How Asset Securitisation or the Housing Market Contributed to the Global Financial Crisis

After the dot.com bubble burst in the USA in the late 1990s, the Federal Reserve began to reduce interest rates to stimulate the US economy. These low interest rates forced institutional investors to begin looking for alternatives to US Treasury securities. These investors were therefore looking for securities, which offered higher rates. They began investing in higher risk securities such as MBSs. Some of these securities were highly rated by the credit rating agencies, thus providing comfort to these institutional investors. Little did they know that some of these highly rated securities would eventually default. The rating agencies wrongly assumed that housing prices across states were lowly correlated. Thus, they believed that defaults in one state should not lead to defaults in other states. Further, after the crises, rating agencies were accused of intentionally understating the risk of these securities because they were paid by the issuers to rate the securities. Giving a bad rating could mean that the rating agency would not receive further business from the issuer.
  In addition, the low interest rates meant that borrowers who otherwise could not take a mortgage could now afford to do so. Those who already qualified could also take bigger loans. Lending institutions also lowered their credit standards, thus giving loans to people who under normal circumstances should not obtain a loan. Some mortgage loans had no documentation, some had low or teaser rates and some were given to individuals with no income, no jobs or no assets (so-called NINJA loans). These loans were referred to as subprime mortgages. The lenders assumed wrongly that housing prices would continue to increase, and thus, they could sell the properties to recover their loans even if the borrowers defaulted. Beginning from 2005, housing prices began to fall because of reduced demand, given the very high prices. The Federal Reserve had also started increasing interest rates. A lot of borrowers, especially subprime borrowers who had taken ARMs, began to find it very difficult to pay off their required instalments, leading to defaults. Once these borrowers defaulted, the holders of MBSs/CDOs could not receive their anticipated cash flows. Even holders of the highly rated (senior tranches) experienced default. The insurance companies who were supposed to reimburse investors when default occurred also defaulted and could not honour their obligations under the credit default swap (CDS) contracts, which they had issued.
  A series of events and bankruptcies were significant during the global financial crisis in 2007. We catalogue some of these events. On 14 September 2007, Northern Rock experienced a bank run and had to be bailed out by the Bank of England. Northern Rock was taken into state ownership in February 2008. On 11 March 2008, the Fed announced the creation of a 200-billion-dollar Term Securities Lending Facility, which would lend against an expanded set of collateral. In March of 2008, Bear Stearns was acquired by JP Morgan with the help of the New York Fed. By September 2008, Freddie Mac and Fannie Mae had been placed into state conser- vatorship. AIG, an insurance company and a huge issuer of CDSs, received an $85

11.6 Risks from Mortgage Origination	233

billion-dollar bailout package from the Fed for 80% of its equity. Also, in September of 2008, Lehman Brothers filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy, while Merrill Lynch was acquired by Bank of America. In the same month, Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley transformed themselves to bank holding companies. The Emergency Eco- nomic Stabilisation Act of 2008 allowed then Treasury Secretary, Henry Paulson, to spend up to $700 billion dollars on ætoxic assetsÆ and to provide direct cash to financial institutions. In February 2009, President Obama signed a 787-billion-dollar economic stimulus package in the form of the æAmerican Recovery and Reinvest- ment Act of 2009Æ.
  During the crises, the Treasury Bill Eurodollar Difference (TED) spread, which is a measure of credit risk and the health of the banking industry increased signifi- cantly. The TED spread is the difference between the 3-month London Interbank Offer Rate (LIBOR) and the yield for the 3-month US Treasury Bill. Further, over the course of the crises, the Fed consistently reduced interest rates. As on September 2007, the Fed funds target rate was 4.75%. By December 2008, the Fed fund rate target had been cut to 0%û0.25%.
  One of the legacies of the global financial crisis was the disappearance of traditional investment banks in the USA. They were acquired or sold to commercial banks.



11.6 Risks from Mortgage Origination

Originating mortgages is not a riskless process. The risk associated with mortgage lending is known as pipeline risk. You can view the mortgages as being in a pipeline until it is eventually issued to the borrower. During this process, the lender faces price risk and fallout risk. If the lender commits to lend to the borrower at a particular rate and rates change, it could have an adverse effect on the lender. LetÆs assume that between the time that the lender commits to lend to the borrower and the time that the funds are disbursed, rates increase by 100 basis points or 1%. The lender cannot change the rate it promised the borrower; thus technically, the increase in interest rates will lead to a lower present value or proceeds if the lender decides to sell the mortgage. If the lender maintains the mortgage in its loan book, it would incur an opportunity cost since the income coming in will be lower than prevailing market rates.
  Another aspect of pipeline risk is fallout risk. Fallout risk occurs because the borrower does not close or take the loan. This is because the lender may give the borrower the right, but not the obligation, to close. In essence, this is an option like a contract. Thus, the borrower may decide not to close or æfall outÆ of the pipeline. This could be because interest rates have declined, and therefore, it is cheaper to the borrower to obtain the loan from another lender. Furthermore, the loan may have been predicated on an increase in income that may not have materialised. Also, a bad inspection report on the property can also lead to a fallout risk.

234	11  Mortgage Banking

11.7 Risks Associated with Mortgage Lending

There are a number of risks associated with giving a mortgage loan. These risks include credit risk, prepayment risk and interest rate risk.



11.7.1 Credit Risk

Credit risk is the risk that a borrower will pay late or default entirely on the mortgage. The probability that a borrower will default depends on a number of factors. Generally, economic conditions can lead to a borrowerÆs default. A borrower is more likely to default during a recession or a downturn in the economy. A borrower with a low credit score or bad credit history is also more likely to default compared to one with a high credit score or good credit history. The borrowerÆs income is also a key determinant of the probability of default. Borrowers who earn more are less likely to default. Borrowers with a higher income are more likely to have a lower payment-to-income ratio (PTI), all else equal. They are thus less likely to default. The loan-to-value ratio (LTV) or the proportion of debt compared to the value of the property can also affect default, as we have discussed earlier. Higher loan-to-value ratios, or lower percentage of equity contributed by the borrower, are likely to lead to higher defaults. There are several mechanisms that a bank or lender can use to mitigate credit risk. The bank can insure mortgage loans that it gives to its clients. In addition, the bank can sell or securitise the loans upon origination. The credit risk is now borne by investors in the secondary market.



11.7.2 Prepayment Risk

Prepayment risk is the risk that a borrower will pay off the loan before maturity. Borrowers usually prepay their loans, especially on fixed rate mortgages, when general interests drop. This is because it is cheaper to take a new loan because interest rates have dropped. Borrowers thus usually refinance their loans in an environment of declining interest rates. They can borrow from a new lender at the prevailing low interest rates and pay off the higher rate loan from the original lender. Why does this represent a risk to the original lender? The bank or lender receives the cash flows earlier than anticipated and is likely to find that it can only relend at a lower interest rate because interest rates have dropped. To overcome prepayment risk, a lender can issue ARMs. Borrowers will have no incentives to prepay because the rates they pay drop when interest rates decline. The lender can also securitise or sell the loan in the secondary market to avoid prepayment risk. Prepayment risk will now be borne by investors in the loan.

11.7 Risks Associated with Mortgage Lending	235

11.7.3 Interest Rate Risk

A bank incurs interest rate risk because changes in interest rates can affect the bankÆs spread or even its equity position. Thus, changes in interest rate can lead to a reduction in a bankÆs spread and can also lead to the bank being technically insolvent (negative equity).
  Interest rate risk occurs because a bank may borrow short and lend long or lend long and borrow short. LetÆs explain how interest rate risk manifests given these two scenarios. Here, we focus on how changes in interest rates affect the bankÆs spread. When a bank borrows short, it means that it takes funds from depositors on a short- term basis. Lending long means the bank transforms these short-term deposits into longer dated loans. Thus, for example, a bank may take a 1-year deposit at the rate of 5%. The bank then gives a 3-year loan at the rate of 15%. This implies that the spread in the first year is 10%. At the end of the first year, the 1-year deposit would have matured. Thus, the bank needs to pay the depositor. However, the funds have been lent for 3 years. Therefore, the bank can issue a new 1-year certificate of deposit at the end of the first year. However, interest rates may have increased or decreased. LetÆs assume that rates have increased to 8%. This suggests that the spread for the second year would be 7% (15û8), meaning the bankÆs spread shrinks from 10% to 7% because of the increase in interest rates. If rates decline, however, then the bankÆs spread will increase. LetÆs assume that at the end of the first year, the bank is able to obtain funds by issuing a 1-year certificate of deposit at the rate of 3%. This means that its spread will now be 12% (15û3). However, the bank may not enjoy this spread because borrowers are likely to prepay its long-term loans in an environment of declining interest rates. Thus, by borrowing short and lending long, the bank probably expects interest rates to decline. An increase in rates will adversely affect the spread of the bank.
  When a bank borrows long and lends short, it is also exposed to interest rate risk. For example, say a bank takes a 15-year deposit at the rate of 5% and invests in a 1-year security at the rate of 12%. The spread for the first year will be 7% (12û5). At the end of 1 year, the 1-year security would have matured. The bank needs to find another investment. If at the end of the first year, rates on 1-year securities have declined to 10%, then the bankÆs spread reduces to 5% (10û5). On the other hand, if the rate on the 1-year securities rises to 15%, then the bankÆs spread is now 10% (15û5). Thus, the bank is exposed to declining interest rates and benefits from increasing interest rates when it lends short and borrows long.
  A bank can reduce interest rate risk by selling or securitising its loans. It can also reduce interest rate risk by using ARMs. Using ARMs means that the bankÆs return on mortgages will move in line with its cost of funds, thus stabilising the spread.

236	11  Mortgage Banking

11.8 Risks from Mortgage Investing

Risks from investing in mortgage-related securities include credit risk, prepayment risk, price risk and liquidity risk.



11.8.1 Credit Risk

When borrowers default, it means that holders of any mortgage-related securities will not receive some or all of the promised principal and interest payments.



11.8.2 Prepayment Risk

We have explained prepayment risk earlier in relation to mortgage lending. When interest rates drop, borrowers prepay their loans. Holders of MBSs will therefore also receive payments earlier than anticipated. Any principal prepayments are paid at face or par value.



11.8.3 Price Risk

Related to interest rate risk is price risk. Changes in interest rates cause changes in the value of the mortgage loan or security. Increases in interest rates lead to a reduction in the price or value of the mortgage loan or security. On the other hand, decreases in interest rates lead to an increase in the value or price of the mortgage or mortgage security. This stems from the inverse relationship between rates and present values.



11.8.4 Liquidity risk

Mortgage loans are by their nature illiquid. This is because they have a long tenor, 10û30 years. By securitising a mortgage loan, this otherwise illiquid asset becomes liquid. However, even mortgage-backed securities (MBSs) are not as liquid as other tradable debt securities. MBSs usually have a higher bid-ask spread compared to other debt securities.

11.10 Improving the Mortgage and Housing Markets	237

11.9 Challenges Confronting the Mortgage/Housing Markets

There are a number of challenges that confront mortgage markets in Africa. These challenges include:
ò Lack of long-term funding sources. Due to the underdeveloped nature of bond markets in Africa, long-term funding for mortgage financing is a challenge for most financial institutions.
ò Macroeconomic instability such as high inflation rates and exchange rate insta- bility have hindered the development of the mortgage market in Africa.
ò Non-existence of a secondary market for mortgages. Once the secondary market is non-existent, activity in the primary market is likely to be reduced. This is because mortgage originators will find it difficult to offload mortgages from their balance sheet.
ò Interest rates in Africa are stubbornly high, thus affecting the ability of the citizenry to go in for mortgages. Interest rate volatility also makes it difficult for financial institutions to issue long-term loans at fixed rates. Floating-rate loans may also not be viable in such an environment.
ò Africans prefer to build their own homes rather than take a mortgage to purchase a house. The average family takes between 5 and 15 years to complete their home, thus locking up capital and increasing costs.
ò The cost of building materials and labour is very high. However, the cost of building is lower in Africa compared to the developed markets.
ò Physical and residential planning is poorly done in Africa. Most neighbourhoods evolve ænaturallyÆ rather than evolving according to a well-laid-out plan.
ò Related to the previous point is lack of appropriate infrastructure. Most commu- nities do not have adequate roads, water, telephone connection and electricity supply.
ò Income levels in Africa are generally low, thus limiting the ability of individuals to purchase a home using a mortgage.
ò The land tenure system poses many challenges to the housing and mortgage markets. There are various land disputes and the same plot of land can be sold to several people at the same time.
ò Unfortunately, African judicial systems have been characterised by long delays in disposing of cases such as land litigation.


11.10 Improving the Mortgage and Housing Markets

The following recommendations would be useful in improving the mortgage and housing markets in Africa:
ò A stable macroeconomic environment, which attracts external funds.
ò The development of an active secondary market for mortgages.

238	11  Mortgage Banking

ò A strong legal and regulatory framework, comprising a consistent and holistic set of laws, is required (in areas such as property rights, collateral and foreclosure) for the housing sector. Also, there is the need to intensify efforts aimed at enhancing land administration.
ò Reforming the land tenure and title registration system is likely to be beneficial to the mortgage and real estate sectors.
ò Establishment of land courts to handle and clear the huge backlogs of land-related cases in the courts, which are inimical to the housing industry.
ò Promoting a cost-effective construction industry and programmes to promote local building materials.
ò Governments should subsidise cost of infrastructure to developing sites, which would help reduce the final prices of houses in the long run.
ò Governments should consider establishing land banks for property developers to build affordable housing units, considering the fact that land acquisition comes at a high price, which tends to affect prices of housing units.
ò A well-defined and integrated system of housing finance is needed to fund the construction of new structures and to finance the trading of existing properties.
ò Mortgage banks can go on a promotion drive to sell their products and explain mortgages to the general public and niche groups who are likely to purchase mortgages.
ò Mortgage banks should be flexible. They can be innovative in providing flexible payment mechanisms so that more people can take mortgages, especially those in the informal sector.
ò GovernmentsÆ involvement in the market may be beneficial. African governments can provide a mechanism where funding is channelled to mortgage banks and mortgage finance companies. The rates provided by governments are likely to be lower than what private lenders will provide. This may lead to lower costs for borrowers. Government involvement may also lead to the provision of long-term financing and the establishment of an active secondary market.



11.11 Summary and Conclusions

This chapter examined mortgage banking by discussing the primary market for mortgages, fixed versus adjustable rate mortgages, factors that affect the value of a mortgage, secondary market for mortgages, risks from mortgage origination, risks associated with mortgage lending, risks from mortgage investing, challenges confronting the mortgage/housing markets and how to improve the mortgage and housing markets in Africa.
1. A mortgage is a loan used to acquire a property and the property usually serves as collateral for the loan.
2. Mortgages are originally issued in the primary market and are subsequently traded in the secondary markets. The primary market for mortgages is the market

11.11 Summary and Conclusions	239

where the mortgages are originated. It involves the borrower applying for a mortgage loan from a mortgage originator and this process is known as origina- tion. The principal mortgage originators include mortgage banks, commercial banks and mortgage finance companies.
3. There are two main indicators used by lenders to assess a mortgage loan appli- cationùthe payment-to-income ratio (PTI) and the loan-to-value ratio (LTV). The PTI ratio compares the monthly payment required on the loan to the borrowerÆs monthly income. The loan-to-value (LTV) ratio compares the loan that the borrower is taking to the value of the property that the borrower wants to purchase.
4. The main types of mortgages are fixed rate mortgage (FRM) and adjustable rate mortgage (ARM). Other mortgage financing products include home purchase mortgages, home equity mortgages, home completion mortgages, home improve- ment mortgages, graduated payment mortgages, growing equity mortgages, sec- ond mortgages, balloon-payment mortgages and shared appreciation mortgages.
5. The value of a mortgage is in essence the present value of the expected interest and principal repayments from holding the mortgage. Inflation, economic growth, money supply and government budget deficits impact interest rates, which in turn affect the value of mortgages.
6. Mortgages can be sold in the secondary markets either individually or in a pool of loans through a securitisation process.
7. There are risks associated with mortgage origination, lending and investing, including pipeline risk, credit risk, prepayment risk and interest rate risk.
8. Mortgage markets in Africa are confronted with a number of challenges and these include lack of long-term funding sources, macroeconomic instability, non-existence of a secondary market for mortgages, high interest rates, low-income levels, land tenure systems and weak legal regime.

Chapter 12
Management of Bank Capital











Learning Objectives
By the end of this chapter you should be able to:
ò Explain what constitutes bank capital
ò Discuss bank capital requirement and its role in bank regulation
ò Explain how much capital banks need
ò Understand how to use risk-based capital ratios to determine minimum capital requirement for banks
ò Explain how banks strategise to raise funds to meet their capital needs in the wake of regulatory pressures


12.1 Introduction

Banks need capital because of regulatory requirements. Bank managers, under normal circumstances, will hold less capital relative to assets because of the high cost of holding capital. Banks are therefore tempted to hold capital exactly according to the amount required by the regulatory authorities. Capital is, however, important in bank management because it serves as a safety buffer against risk and encourages public trust in the long-term sustainability of the bank. In addition, capital has become the main driver of bank regulations. We will discuss in detail the importance of bank capital, its regulatory requirements and the important role it plays in the management of banks.
  In this chapter, we look at bank capital and risk, regulation and bank capital and the size of bank capital. We also discuss risk-based capital ratios and the Basel agreements and also how banks strategise to meet their capital needs.





® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_12

241

242	12  Management of Bank Capital

12.2 Bank Capital and Risk

Bank capital or net worth of a bank is the difference between its assets and liabilities. That is:
Total asset ù Total liabilities = Capital
  A bankÆs assets comprise its cash, loans, mortgages and securities. Loans are monies given to individuals, firms or other banks at a rate that yields interest to the bank. Mortgages are debt instruments secured by individuals or firms with a real estate property as collateral. Securities as assets to a bank are government treasury bills and bonds that the bank invests in for capital gains and interests. A bankÆs liabilities are usually made up of customersÆ deposits and debts the bank owes.
  Bank capital refers to the funds shareholders or owners contribute to running the bank. This may take the form of fresh purchases of shares and annual earnings that the owners reinvest in the bank. ShareholdersÆ funds are considered permanent capital because the owners do not have the right to withdraw their funds from the bankùthough they have the option of selling their shares to other investors (Arnold 2014). Banks normally hold capital for three reasons. First, bank capital prevents bank failure. Bank failure is the condition in which the bank is not able to satisfy its obligations to pay its depositors and other creditors and so goes out of business. Secondly, the quantum of capital influences the returns to the owners of the banks. Lastly, banks keep capital to meet regulatory requirements. Bank capital is the fund that owners can fall on should the bank liquidate. In effect, bank capital serves as a cushion to the bankÆs failure. Owners subject their capital to risk in anticipation of higher returns. The risk that the bank could lose value on its capital is termed capital risk. Banks are exposed to different kinds of risks that threaten their stability and capital standing. Liquidation is normally the result of mismanagement of bank risk, which puts pressure on the bankÆs capital (shareholdersÆ wealth).
  The types of risk a bank is prone to are credit risk, interest rate risk, market risk and liquidity risk. Credit risk or default risk is associated with the inability of borrowers to fulfil their obligation of loans and interest payments. This occurs when the future cash flows that borrowers expect to use in payment of their debt are not realised. Perceived credit risk by banks can sometimes lead to increasing interest rates on loans or withholding lending (credit crunch). Interest rate risk is the varying of interest rates before the maturity of an investment or loan. Interest rate risk reduces the gains spread when rates are going down in an investment or going up in a loan. Market risk or systematic risk is when a financial institution or a bank finds itself experiencing shocks that affect performance of the industry. Systematic risk cannot be avoided by a bank through diversification, but it can be insured. Some of the shocks of systematic risk are political instability, disasters, terrorism and reces- sions. Liquidity risk is the risk that the bank may be unable to meet its short-term financial commitments. If the bank is not able to meet its depositorsÆ withdrawals due to liquidity problems, it is likely to lose such customers to its competitors, and

12.3 Regulation and Bank Capital	243

this could adversely affect its earnings. If the bank is unable to meet its liquidity needs, it stands the risk of a bank run and its eventual collapse.
  A bank has an obligation to depositors to meet short-term withdrawal requests, and the inability of the bank to meet this obligation is a signal of illiquidity of the bank. Liquidity risk, if mismanaged, makes a bank a candidate for possible takeover. Banks have a responsibility to manage risks associated with the banking sector to avoid liquidation and loss of shareholders wealth. Banks, as a result of inefficiencies in providing financial services to their customers, face operational risk. The oper- ational risk may be due to old and faulty computers, hiring of unqualified staff, managerial failures and breakdowns in internal systems, human errors, etc. The operational risk has the tendency to affect revenue generation, operating cost and hence the value of ownersÆ wealth. Most economies in Africa are import driven with high volumes of foreign exchange transactions through the bank. This exposes banks to exchange rate risk due to their dealings in foreign currency for themselves and their customers. The risk is due to hostile movement of price at both the demand and supply sides of the foreign exchange market.
  Banks can adopt several defensive measures to protect ownersÆ capital. Some of these measures include diversifying the bankÆs portfolio of assets and venturing into other locations, attracting quality management, buying deposit insurance, etc. The ownersÆ capital absorbs all the losses of the bank, which allows the bank to continue operating until it is able to recover such losses. Thus, it serves as the final defence against risk and bank failure.


12.3 Regulation and Bank Capital

Banks are regulated both within the economy they operate and by international regulatory bodies to serve the interest of its customers and thus maintain stability towards economic growth. Banks serve as the intermediaries between economic players by accepting deposits and giving out loans for production leading to eco- nomic growth. This activity by banks calls for regulation. The central bank is the main regulator of banking activities within an economy, but in recent times, the international banking regulation accords, Basel I and II, have a provision for bank regulation, especially with capital management. Regulatory capital is set by regula- tors for banks to be held against credit and market risks.
  In the past 20 years, African economies have witnessed that regulatory capital requirement improved and widened to capture various types of risks, to distinguish between asset classes of diverse risks and to permit a different method in determin- ing the risk weights to be applied to each asset category. By so doing, the rules have become increasingly broadened to allow for the recent complexities in modern banking. The significance of the adequate capital requirement was emphasised during the economic downturn. For instance, in 2006, the Central Bank of Nigeria (CBN) imposed a higher capital requirement (from two billion Naira to 25 billion Naira) on commercial banks. This was a reform agenda aimed at improving the

244	12  Management of Bank Capital

stability of the financial system in Nigeria. This led to a reduction in the number of banks from 90 to 25. Notwithstanding all these reforms and interventions, the CBNÆs detailed audit of commercial banks showed that many banks were under-capitalised. The CBC had to spend almost US$3 billion to bail out and recapitalise some of these banks between 2008 and 2009.
  Most African countries that use the Basel I regimes for regulatory capital ade- quacy keep to the guidelines of Basel I, except for Ghana, which has adopted a tailored version of Basel I and is currently implementing Basel II. South Africa and Mauritius have adopted Basel II, and Angola is in Basel I equivalent. Due to the stringent requirement of Basel III, which is meant to prevent another banking crisis by increasing capitalisation and improving credit policies, only South Africa is prepared to implement this requirement fully.
  Generally, most regulatory and supervisory authorities in African are still tied to the Basel I framework, while countries in other parts of the world are discussing the implementation of Basel III. It is quite baffling to have stability in AfricaÆs banking system improve while the continent struggles to implement the various Basel rules. In the 2015 Africa Economic Brief, the African Development Bank Group indicated that while most African countries are struggling to implement Basel II, Mauritius and South Africa have done so. A brief explanation may be that the Basel rules are less important for African Banks, looking at their structure and their operations. A detailed observation is that African banks are not well integrated into the global financial architecture; therefore, the Basel II and Basel III frameworks are too complex for the African banking systems. African banks are characterised with low or no derivatives markets, high liquidity and improved governance systems. These characteristics seem enough to guarantee the stability of the banking system in Africa. Considering the complexities with Basel II and III and the fact that even banks from the developed world are struggling to adopt these risk models, African banks will obviously lack the human resource capacity and information technology to implement them.


12.4 Size of Bank Capital

In order to ascertain whether the ownersÆ wealth is being well managed at the bank, one needs a good appraisal of banksÆ profitability. The fundamental measure of banksÆ profitability is the return on asset (ROA). That is, the net profit after tax per dollar of assets, given by:

ROA = Net profit after taxes
Assets
  The evidence of how the bank is being managed resourcefully is determined by the return on asset, since it measures how much on average each dollar of asset is converted to profit. On the other hand, bank owners are interested in how their equity

12.5 Risk-Based Capital Ratios and the Basel Agreements	245

investment is being converted to profits. This is provided by another measure of profitability known as return on equity (ROE), which provides an indication of net profit after tax per dollar of banksÆ equity capital. This is written as:

ROE = Net profit after taxes
Equity capital

  The return on equity has a relationship with the return on asset through the equity multiplier (EM). The equity multiplier is the measure of sensitivity of how well the ownersÆ investment is doing relative to (ROE) and how resourcefully or efficiently the bank is managed. We can express this as:


Net profit after taxes	Net profit after tax

Assets

Equity capital	=
of which, by definition, it produces:

Assets	Î Equity capital

ROE = ROA Î EM
  The formula above indicates that when a bank holds a small amount of equity capital for a given return on asset, it boosts the returns to the owners of the bank. The question is: how do you determine the bankÆs capital requirements? The next sections seek to answer this question taking into consideration the Basel Accords.



12.5 Risk-Based Capital Ratios and the Basel Agreements

The Basel Accords are regulations set by the Basel Committee on Bank Supervision (BCBS) to guide banks on capital and risk management. The aim of the accords is to help banks meet their obligations of short-term withdrawals by customers and some unexpected losses the banks might face in their operations. From Basel I in 1988 to Basel III in 2010, the BCBS has revised the regulations to meet the recent challenges faced by banks with the recent revisions based on the 2008 financial crisis.
  Basel I, issued in 1988, was the first of BCBS regulations targeted at reducing a bankÆs credit risk. Basel I groups assets of a bank into five categories and places weights on each of them, depending on the creditworthiness of the debtor. High-risk assets are assigned high weights and low-risk assets are given low weights (Table 12.1).
  As part of its requirements, Basel I requires banks (international) to use risk-based asset ratios to set capital. The sources of capital of banks are divided into two tiers, that is, Tier 1 and Tier 2. The ratio is the sum of Tier 1 and Tier 2 capital divided by the risk-weighted assets of the bank. Tier 1, or core capital, is made up of equity capital and retained earnings (disclosed reserves). Tier 1 capital is also recognised as

Table 12.2 Risk-weighted bank assets

unredeemable and noncumulative in nature. It is the true capital used as cushion for the banking operations. Examples of Tier 1 capital include ordinary shares and surplus, undivided profit, qualifying non-cumulative perpetual preference shares, etc. The Tier 2 capital includes undisclosed reserves, revaluation reserves, loan loss reserves, convertible bonds and cumulative preference shares. The Tier 2 capital is supplementary to the core capital in the running of banksÆ operations. The risk- weighted assets are computed based on the five categories of bank assets according to Basel I.
Basel risk-based asset ratio = Tier 1 capital + Tier 2 capital
Risk-weighted assets

  Basel I has been criticised for many reasons. The measure of capital, that is, equity, was debated as not being a good estimation of capital since banks in different countries have different access to the stock market. This argument developed into the question of the difference between Tier 1 and Tier 2 capital and whether to use book values or market values of capital. This comes at the heels of the ambiguity in the components of Tier 1 and Tier 2, as different standards apply in various countries. This has triggered innovative ways for banks in accumulating capital without interfering with the set regulations. Basel II, also known as the second Basel or the Revised Capital Framework, was an amendment of Basel I to deal with the criticisms and shortfalls. A paper by the African Development Bank indicates that developing

248	12  Management of Bank Capital

countries impose more regulations on capital compared to developed countries and that North Africa imposes more regulation than any other region in the world.


12.6 Strategies for Meeting Capital Needs of Banks

Banks, over the years, have realised the need for planning their prospective long- term capital needs since they face regular regulatory pressures to keep adequate capital levels. Banks tend to raise capital from both internal and external sources (see Rose and Hudgins 2013). Raising capital both internally and externally comes with advantages and disadvantages.


12.6.1 Internal Source of Capital

Internal capital is less expensive to raise and does not dilute shareholdersÆ value and earning per share of stock. Internally generated sources of capital are mainly earnings retained by the bank rather than paying it out to the owners of the bank. The bank also avoids cost of raising money from the open market, when it can raise capital internally. If the bank decides to sell shares to raise capital, and despite the fact that existing shareholders have some preemptive right on new issues, some shares will spill over to new shareholders, who will then be entitled to part of the future earnings generated by the bank. The internal capital is normally exposed to taxability risk and other changing macroeconomic conditions, which the manage- ment of the bank might not have any control over.
  The board of directors and management at the bank must determine the appro- priate retention and payout ratio in order to grow internal earnings to meet their capital needs. The decision to retain earning or payout earning as a dividend plays a major role in determining the bankÆs internal capital. The retention ratio is the ratio of the current retained earnings to the after-tax net income, while the dividend payout ratio is the ratio of the current dividend to the after-tax net income. The sum of the two ratios is equal to one. That is,
Retention ratio + Dividend payout ratio = 1
  The importance of the retention ratio cannot be overemphasised. If the retention ratio is set too high (low dividend payout ratio), it will lead to a reduction in dividend income to shareholders. On the other hand, when the retention ratio is kept too low (high dividend payout ratio), it will result in slower growth of internal capital, and this may expose the bank to failure risk and also retard expansion of asset. Slower growth in capital has the tendency to limit the funds available for the bank to build its asset base through loans and investments. It is imperative to note that a lower dividend payout ratio will reduce the market value of the shares issued by the bank. Therefore, optimal dividend policy is needed to maximise the value of the

12.6 Strategies for Meeting Capital Needs of Banks	249

shareholdersÆ investment. That is, the dividend level that will be able to help the bank satisfy its capital needs attracts new shareholders and retains existing shareholders.
  The main driver of managementÆs decision on appropriate retention ratio and dividend payout is to increase the speed at which the bankÆs assets grow to protect current ratio of capital to asset from wearing down. The expression below guides management in addressing the issue about the growth rate of earnings that shields the capital-to-assets ratio when the bank continues to smoothen its dividend payout.
Internal capital growth rate =
Retained earnings
Equity capital	= ROE Î Retention ratio


Retained earnings		Net income Equity capital	= Equity capital Î

  The above equation reveals that capital from the internal affairs of the bank can be increased by increasing earnings, the retention ratio or both simultaneously. The earnings of the bank can be increased by higher profit margins, equity multiplier and the asset utilisation ratio. Increasing the retained earnings relatively to the net income is a way of increasing the retention ratio of the bank.
  Assuming bank managers agree to retain 40% of their net earnings when the return on equity is 20%, the internal growth rate of capital will be 8%. This suggests that the bankÆs assets cannot grow beyond 8% based on the conditions established under the internal capital generation. Anything beyond 8% will force the capital-to- assets ratio to fall and further trigger regulatory capital requirements to increase.
Internal capital growth rate = 0.2 Î 0.4 = 0.08
  When the growth rate of the assets is known, management must be able to establish a combination of return to equity rates and retention ratios that preserve the capital-to-assets ratio. 12.6.2 External Source of Capital

Apart from raising capital internally, banks can also raise capital from external sources by issuing shares (both ordinary and preference), acquiring debt, engaging in sale or lease of non-current assets and the swapping of shares for debt securities. The option that a bank chooses to raise capital externally at any point in time depends on the risk the bank is exposed to, the conditions prevailing in the securities market, the regulations on raising capital and, above all, the shareholdersÆ interest (returns). External sources of capital, such as issuing fresh shares, pose a threat potential to shareholdersÆ returns. That is, comparatively, increasing the number of owners dilutes a bankÆs earnings.
  In an attempt to raise capital, the bank can issue additional shares to existing shareholders or prospective shareholders. Shares offered to the former do not normally affect control, but the latter increase the ownership of the bank, which affects control and shareholder earnings unless the bank makes more returns from the additional capital. Issuing shares is one of the most expensive ways of raising external capital because of the cost associated with floating shares. On the other hand, share issue boosts the bankÆs capacity to borrow, as the number of owners increases. Issue of shares is also a way of controlling the debt stock of a bank. Ordinary shareholders receive dividends after preference shareholders have been paid. It means preference shareholders have the first claim on the bankÆs earnings. Although ordinary shareholders are the true owners of the bank, there is currently an avenue for preference shareholders to convert their preference shares to ordinary shares by holding convertible preference shares.
  Another way of raising capital for the bank is through the issue of debt securities such as debentures, bonds and subordinated notes. Debt capital is a booster to banksÆ earnings when the cost of the debt is less than the gains from usage. Unlike dividends, interest payments on debt securities are tax deductible. However, accu- mulation of high debt can be detrimental to the performance of banks.
  In recent times, banks sell some of their non-current assets (facilities) and lease them back in order to have some inflow of cash to meet their capital investment needs. Leasing back facilities can be very profitable when the write-off process is swift. These transactions normally occur in times when economic conditions are favourable for banks, such as rising inflation causing high market value of non-current assets over their book values coupled with increasing economic growth rates. In situations where the bank has a lot of risky assets, the bank sells some of these assets and invests in less risky investments to control their risk exposure. Some banks also sell assets in an attempt to improve their capital-to-assets ratio.

12.6 Strategies for Meeting Capital Needs of Banks	251

  Swapping shares for debt has become another option for banks in raising capital. Banks that undertake swaps have the advantage of avoiding sinking fund payments for debt securities and can save the bank some future cash outflows that can be invested in other activities. Swaps are profitable when the market value of a debt is less than the book value, in that the bank will issue shares of the same amount (current market value) to buy back the debt. The bank is better off after the transaction, because it removes a high amount of debt (book value) and replaces it with a lesser amount in the form of shares.
  With the motive of increasing shareholdersÆ wealth, banks must critically analyse the various alternatives available for them to raise capital and their effects on earnings. As illustrated in Table 12.1, a bank is considering to raise capital of $60 million externally. Given that the bank has assets of $3 billion, outstanding 24 mil- lion shares of ordinary shares at $4 per share at par and $180 million equity capital, the conditions under operations are such that the bank can generate total revenue of
$300 million with expenses of $240 million with about $45 million after-tax earnings. In choosing an option for raising the capital, should the bank opt for an ordinary share issue of six million shares at $10 per share? The earning per share for an ordinary shareholder will be $1.5 (Table 12.5).

252	12  Management of Bank Capital

  The second alternative is to sell preference shares for $20 per share at 8%. This option will attract dividend of $4.8 million annually for the preference shareholders, which would otherwise have been earned by ordinary shareholders. Meanwhile, the issue of preference shares will better the earnings per share of ordinary shareholders from $1.50 to $1.68. Finally, the bank has a third option to sell debt in the form of capital notes at 10% for $60 million. In this trade, the bank will incur interest expense of $6 million annually, which reduces the tax burden on the bank. The debt capital option leaves the ordinary shareholders with $1.70 earnings per share. Apart from the issue of ordinary shareholders, the other two options do not dilute the ownership and hence the control. Considering shareholder value as the main motive of bank managers, the selling of debt capital is the best option for raising the $60 million capital externally.



12.7 Summary and Conclusions

This chapter highlighted the need for bank management. We have learned that capital is important in bank management because it serves as a safety buffer against risk and encourages public trust in the long-term sustainability of the bank. In addition, capital has become the main driver of bank regulations. Some of the flagship points on the chapter are:
1. Bank capital refers to the funds shareholders or owners contribute to running the bank. This may take the form of a fresh issue of shares and/or annual earnings that the owners reinvest in the bank.
2. Banks hold capital for three reasons: to prevent bank failure, the quantum of capital influences the returns to the owners of the banks and to meet regulatory requirements.
3. Banks are exposed to different kinds of risk that threaten their stability and capital standing, including credit risk, interest rate risk, market risk and liquidity risk.
4. When a bank holds a small amount of equity capital for a given return on asset, it boosts the returns to the owners of the bank.
5. Banks are regulated both within the economy they operate and by international regulatory bodies to serve the interest of its customers and stability towards economic growth.
6. The international banking regulation accords, Basel I and II, have a provision for bank regulation, especially for capital management.
7. The Basel Accords are regulations set by the Basel Committee on Bank Super- vision (BCBS) to guide banks on capital and risk management. The aim of the accords is to help banks meet their obligations of short-term withdrawals by customers and some unexpected losses the banks might face in their operations. From Basel I in 1988 to Basel III in 2010, the BCBS has revised the regulations

References	253

to meet the recent challenges faced by banks with the recent revision based on the 2008 financial crisis.
8. Developing countries impose more regulations on capital compared to devel- oped countries, and North Africa imposes more regulation than any other region in the world.
9. Banks have, over the years, realised the need for planning their prospective long-term capital needs since they face regular regulatory pressures to keep adequate capital level.
10. Banks tend to raise capital from both internal and external sources. Raising capital internally and externally comes with advantages and disadvantages. Internally generated sources of capital are ploughing back into retained earn- ings. External sources of capital include issuing ordinary and preference shares, acquiring debt, engaging in sale or lease of non-current assets and swapping of shares for debt securities.
Discussion Questions and Problems

6. What are the determinants of the choice of various sources of external capital?
7. The sources of capital of banks are divided into two tiers, and these are Tier 1 and Tier 2. Discuss.

13.1 Introduction

Banks prepare financial statements to measure and evaluate how well they have performed in obtaining good returns and in delivering value to their shareholders. Preparing financial statements is also necessary for banks to examine the assets and liability position at any point in time (Arnold 2014). The International Accounting Standards Board (IASB) reissued its International Accounting Standard (IAS 1) on the presentation of financial statements in September 2007. According to IAS 1 reissued, the balance sheet is now known as the statement of financial position and the profit and loss account as the statement of comprehensive income. We therefore use these new terminologies.
  This chapter introduces the financial statement of banks. We discuss the state- ments of financial position (balance sheet) and comprehensive income (profit and loss account) of a bank. In doing this, we will examine the main components and what they tell us. We also look at the measuring and evaluating of bank performance.



® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_13

257

258	13  Measuring and Evaluating the Performance of Banks

13.2 Statement of Financial Position

The statement of financial position shows the bankÆs sources and uses of funds (Sinkey 2002). This shows the assets, liabilities and equity of the bank. It is important to quickly define these. An asset is any resource that an entity controls resulting from a past event from which future economic benefits are expected to flow to the entity. A liability is an entityÆs present obligation as a result of a past event from which resources embodying economic benefits are expected to flow out of the entity. Equity is the difference between assets and liabilities, that is, the residual interest in a firm. Therefore, for an asset, what matters is that the entity can control its use by restricting access to others. Consequently, ownership is not required, so far as the entity has control over the asset; an example is finance lease. Legally, the asset is not owned by the entity; however, the economic substance of the transaction is that the asset belongs to the entity. For a liability, the entity must have a present obligation as a result of a past event.
The basic equation is stated as follows:
Assets = Capital (Equity) + Liabilities	(13.1)
A = C + L	(13.2)
Uses of funds = Sources of funds	(13.3)
  Therefore, equity and liabilities are sources of funds for a bank. The assets represent the use to which the bank has put those resources (sources of funds).
  The IASB has moved financial reporting towards a fair value approach, as against historical cost accounting. Historical costs represent the cost of buying an asset or incurring a liability. Fair value is the price at which an asset can be gained or a liability settled between knowledgeable willing parties in an armÆs-length transac- tion. Fair values are more relevant but may involve a lot of estimates and therefore are less reliable. Historical costs are more reliable but less relevant.
  Table 13.1 shows the statement of financial position of a hypothetical bankù BANK ABC. We will go through this statement to give you a feel of what a bankÆs financial statement looks like and to prepare the ground for the analysis of these statements in a later section.

13.2.1 Assets

The following components are some of the usual assets you will find on a bankÆs statement of financial position.
Cash and Cash Balance with the Central Bank
This represents the cash balances of the bank. These balances include cash kept in the bankÆs vault. They also include cash balances with the central bank. The balance with the central bank is what is known as the primary reserve. The treasury

department must try to balance the needs of being liquid against too much liquidity. This is because if liquidity is kept in the form of cash, then it will not earn any return though it is the most liquid asset the bank can keep.
Government Securities
These include investment in treasury bills and government bonds. These instru- ments, on initial recognition, must be classified as fair value through profit or loss, available for sale, loans or receivables or held to maturity. With fair value through profit or loss, gains or losses are sent directly into income, while available for sale,

260	13  Measuring and Evaluating the Performance of Banks

gains or losses are recognised in equity, that is, as an unrecognised gain in the statement of comprehensive income. Those held as loans and receivables and held to maturity must be measured using amortised cost.
Dues from Other Banks and Financial Institutions
These represent deposits or amounts that the bank has placed with its corresponding banks or institutions. Some of the correspondent banks may not be based locally. Therefore, the amounts held with foreign correspondent banks may be in foreign currency. These amounts, therefore, need to be translated according to the rules of IAS 21 (The Effect of Changes in Foreign Exchange Rates).
Items in Course of Collection from other Banks
This usually represents uncollected cheques that have not yet been cleared.
Investment in Securities
These represent investment in bonds, notes and other instruments such as commer- cial papers. These are all classified according to the rules in IAS 32 (Financial Instruments), IAS 39 (Financial Instruments: Recognition and Measurement) and IFRS 7 (Financial Instruments: Disclosures), which deal with the presentation, measurement and recognition and disclosures for financial instruments. These clas- sifications are:
ò Fair value with gains and losses through profit and lossùthese are instruments held for trading purposes. Derivatives are always classified under this section unless they are used for hedging purposes. As the name implies, gains and losses are shown in the income statement.
ò Available for sale financial instrumentsùthese are financial instruments that the entity intends to hold forever. Gains and losses are shown in the statement of comprehensive income in the statement of recognised income and expense (SORIE). This is where unrecognised gains are recorded.
ò Loans and receivablesùthese are instruments that are not tradable and are measured at amortised cost.
ò Held to maturityùthese are instruments that the entity could sell but does not intend to unload. They are also measured at amortised cost.
Investment in Associate Companies and Subsidiaries
These represent investments in associate companies and subsidiaries. An associate is a company in which the investor has significant influence over its activities. This is usually assumed with a shareholding of between 20% and 50%. Significant influence is the power to influence the operating and financial decisions of the company, but not to control or have joint control over its activities. An entity is a subsidiary, if the investor has a controlling interest in the bank. Control represents the power to determine or manage the operating and financial decisions of the investee company. This is usually assumed with a shareholding of more than 50%.
Loans and Advances
These are the main assets of a bank. These are the assets that generate income for the bank. In 2016 and 2017, Bank ABCÆs loans and advances formed 47 and 56%,

13.2 Statement of Financial Position	261

respectively, of the total assets of the bank. In Ghana, there have been periods, such as the early 2000s, where banks had invested more of their assets in treasury securities rather than giving loans to the private sector. This was because interest rates (treasury bill rates) were very high, and therefore, there was no incentive for banks to take the risk of lending to the private sector.
  Banks give various kinds of loans. These include, but are not limited to, agricul- ture, forestry and fishing loans, mortgages, construction loans, mining and quarrying loans, commerce and finance loans, services loans, electricity, gas and water loans and transportation, storage and communication loans.
Other Assets
These include accrued income receivable and prepayments for items that the bank has made.
Property and Equipment (Non-current Assets)
These represent investment in branches, furniture and fittings and equipment like computers. For banks, these do not form a significant portion of their total assets. In 2016 and 2017, Bank ABCÆs property and equipment formed 3.3 and 4.3%, respec- tively, of its total assets. Banks rely more on their human capital and financial resources (used to make loans) than their physical assets like buildings and equip- ment. This is unlike a typical manufacturing firm that will have more of its assets in the form of equipment and buildings. Do not be surprised therefore if you compute the non-current (fixed) asset turnover for a bank and find it to be low. Banks do not have as much non-current assets as manufacturing companies, and they do not work those assets as hard as manufacturing companies do.
Intangible Assets
These are assets that cannot be seen or touched. They are governed by IAS 38ù intangible assets. There are criteria that must be met before an intangible asset can be recognised in the statement of financial position. Basically, these are that the cost of the intangible assets has to be measured reliably and that the future economic benefits are expected to flow to the entity. An example of a common intangible asset is goodwill. Banks may also capitalise specialised software as intangible assets. Intangible assets with an indefinite life must be tested for impairment. Impairment occurs where the carrying value of the asset is higher than the greater of its fair value less costs to sell or value in use. The carrying value is what used to be called the net book value on the statement of financial position. The fair value is the amount that can be recovered from an asset through an armsÆ-length transaction between knowl- edgeable and willing parties. The value in use represents the value of the asset on a discounted cash flow basis. If the carrying amount is greater than the value in use or fair value less costs to sell, then the carrying value on the statement of financial position must be reduced to the higher of these two (impairment). Assets with a definite life must be amortised (just like depreciation) over their useful life.
We now turn our attention to the subject matter of liabilities.

262	13  Measuring and Evaluating the Performance of Banks

13.2.2 Liabilities

The following liabilities can be found on a typical bankÆs statement of financial position.
Deposits
These are the main liabilities that you will find on a bankÆs statement of financial position. They provide most of the resources that a bank uses to fund its loans. Banks are one of the most levered (employing a lot of debt) businesses, if not the most leveraged business that you can find. This does not apply to just one bank or to a particular geographical area. Banks all over the world rely a lot on debt. In Ghana, the capital adequacy for banks is 10%. This means that the owners of a bank have to contribute only about 10% of the total assets of the bank. The Basel Accord even allows a lower amount. Using Basel, Tier 1 Capital should be 4% and Tier 2 Capital 8%. Why are deposits a liability? They are the liability of a bank because the bank is liable to pay anytime depositors want to withdraw their funds. The funds represent an asset for the depositor but a liability for the bank.
  There are various types of bank deposits. The most common ones are the current account, time deposits and savings deposits. With the current account, the customer can draw an unlimited number of cheques on his or her account (provided he or she has the money to do so). The downside, however, is that most banks will not pay the account holder any interest on a current account. A current account will also incur service charges due to the service that the bank is providing the customer by keeping the account for him or her. With a savings account, as the name implies, the account holder is keeping the account with the bank for savings purposes (to accumulate money and to meet a rainy day). However, the interest rates offered on savings accounts are usually very low. Some banks will allow the account holder to write a cheque on his or her savings account, but this will usually not go through the clearing system, and the number of cheques that a person can write will be severely limited. With time deposits, the customer covenants with the bank to keep his or her funds for letÆs say six months. If the account holder happens to need his or her money before six months, the bank will discount the instrument for the client to enable him or her earn interest that reflects the period for which the deposit has been held. A good example of a time deposit will be a fixed deposit account. The interest rate on a time deposit will usually be more than that on a savings account. However, time deposits are less liquid compared to a savings account.
Due to Banks and other Financial Institutions
You remember that we said that amounts due from other banks and financial institutions represent an asset for the bank. This time around amounts that are due to (the bank has to pay to) other banks and financial institutions will represent a liability to the bank. These amounts represent deposits that other banks have placed with a bank.

13.2 Statement of Financial Position	263

Non-deposit Borrowings
A bank may borrow money from non-deposit sources if it needs more liquidity and has a lot of loans to fund. Non-deposit borrowings are more expensive compared to deposits. Despite being more expensive, they can be arranged very quickly (elec- tronically or through a phone call). Non-deposit sources of borrowing include overnight funds borrowed from other banks, borrowings from the central bank, loans from multilateral agencies (such as the African Development Bank) and repurchase agreements (where a bank can borrow money using investment securities that it has as collateral).
Tax Liabilities
Tax liabilities include the current yearÆs taxes owed as well as deferred tax liabilities. IAS 12 deals with taxes. The IASB and the Financial Accounting Standards Board (FASB in the USA) have adopted a statement of the financial position or temporal difference approach towards the recognition of deferred tax. The UK, however, adopts a timing difference approach. A deferred tax liability arises because of timing or temporal differences. Temporal differences are due to the fact that the carrying value of the asset (what is recorded on the statement of financial position) may be different from what the tax authorities have in their books. If the carrying value in the books of a bank is higher than that in the tax authoritiesÆ books, then a deferred tax liability will arise. On the contrary, if the carrying value is less than what the tax authority has in its books, then a deferred tax asset will arise.
Off-Statement of Financial Position Items
These are items that are not on the statement of financial position. However, they add to the total exposure of a bank. We identified off-balance sheet (off-statement of financial position) items in Chapter Seven to include standby letters of credit, unused loan commitments, loan sales and derivatives. Off-statement of financial position items are very important since they may indicate that the bank is carrying a lot more risk than is visible on the statement of financial position. They provide banks with a lot of fee income. They have also recently increased in size and are sometimes bigger than the assets on the statement of financial position itself.
Quick Quiz

2. List and explain five liabilities on a bankÆs statement of financial position. Why are these items considered as liabilities?

(a) Cash
(b) Deposits
(c) Loans and advances
(d) Government securities

13.3 Statement of Comprehensive Income

In this section, we will discuss the statement of comprehensive income of a bank. The statement of comprehensive income used to be known as the income statement. The statement of comprehensive income includes the income statement and other comprehensive incomes. The main components on a bankÆs statement of compre- hensive income will be its interest income, interest expense, non-interest income and non-interest expense.
  Table 13.2 shows the consolidated comprehensive income for Bank ABC. As we did for the statement of financial position, we will go through the statement of comprehensive income as well. The IASB has adopted a statement of financial position approach towards the recognition of income and expense in the statement of comprehensive income. Income can be described as an increase in economic benefits over the reporting periodùapart from contributions from shareholders. Income could result from an increase in assets or from a decrease in liabilities. Expenses can be described as a decrease in economic benefits over the reporting periodùapart from distributions to shareholders. Expenses could result from an increase in liabilities or a decrease in assets.
Interest Income
This represents income that a bank earns on loans. For all banks, this will form the biggest portion of its income. LetÆs illustrate this. Interest income in 2017 was US
$64,256,000 while non-interest income was US$17,134,000; therefore, total income will be US$81,390,000. Interest income therefore represents 79% of total income. Also, the bank can earn interest income on its investment securities. However, income from loans and advances will usually outweigh income from investment securities. Why? This is because the main business of a bank is to give loans to productive sectors of the economy, not to invest in securities. Loans will earn the bank more income compared to securities. However, they entail more risk for a bank.
Interest Expense
The bank incurs interest expenses on the liability side of its sources of funds. These include interest on current accounts, savings accounts, time deposits, overnight borrowings and call accounts and other borrowings. Interest on deposits is lower than interest on time deposits and overnight borrowings.
