266	13  Measuring and Evaluating the Performance of Banks

Net Interest Income
The net interest income is the difference between interest income and interest expense. It is also sometimes referred to as the bankÆs interest margin. Ideally, the bank will be trying to maximise interest income while reducing interest expenses. Thus, a bankÆs assets (loans and advances, securities, etc.) will generate income for it. The bankÆs liabilities (current accounts, savings accounts, time deposits and other borrowings) will generate interest expenses.
Fees and Commissions Income
These represent non-interest income for the bank. They include service charges on customer accounts, commissions earned on foreign transfers, fee/commission income on letters of credit and other guarantees and other fees and commissions.
Fees and Commissions Expense
These are the expenses that arise in an attempt to earn commissions and fees.
Net Fees and Commissions
This is the difference between fees and commissions income and fees and commis- sions expenses.
Trading Income
This represents income on foreign exchange transactions (due to translation and transactions) and equities that are held for trading purposes.
Operating Income
Operating income is the sum of net interest expense, net fees and commissions, net trading income and other operating incomes. Therefore, it represents all the income less all expenses, that is, interest income, non-interest income, trading income and other operating incomes less interest expense and non-interest expense.
Total Operating Expenses
These comprise expenses on salary costs, administration and general expenses, depreciation and amortisation, auditorÆs remuneration, legal expenses and all other expenses.
Quick Quiz
1. What proportion of Bank ABCÆs total income represents fee income in 2016? What about interest income?
2. A bank has interest expense of US$50,000 and interest income of US$80,000. What is the bankÆs net interest income?
3. Generally, banks rely more on interest income compared to non-interest income. Why may this be so?

13.4 Measuring and Evaluating Bank Performance	267

13.4 Measuring and Evaluating Bank Performance

In this section, we will discuss how to measure and evaluate a bankÆs performance. Why do we need to evaluate a bankÆs performance? You may be a depositor in a bank and would want to know that your funds are safe. You may also be a chief financial officer with a big institution like a pension fund, which has excess funds to lend to a bank at a reasonable return. You may also be an employee or potential employee of a bank that wants to know whether your dream job is exactly what it seems to be. You might also have bought some shares of the bank and will want to know how well the bank is doing. Other equally interested stakeholders in a bankÆs performance are tax authorities, investment analysts, credit rating agencies and bank regulators. In essence, there are various users of banksÆ financial statements and they will all have their particular interests in assessing the bankÆs performance. However, a general-purpose financial statement (statement of comprehensive income and statement of financial position) will be the starting point for all these varied stake- holders who are interested in the bankÆs financial performance.
  The key objective of most firms, including banks, is to maximise shareholdersÆ value by pursuing policies that are beneficial for the stock price of the firm. How do we find the value of the bankÆs stock? It is simply the present value of its future cash flows. We will discuss some of the most important ratios that pertain to a typical bank. Below are hypothetical financial statements of Mercantile Royal Bank (MRB) and Land Sovereign Bank (LSB) from 2015 to 2017. We will use these financial statements as we discuss the ratios.

13.4.1 Performance Indicators

We will now look at some of the very popular performance indicators for banks. These indicators differ a bit from bank to bank. The financial statements of banks differ from those of manufacturing firms. Their analyses and evaluations, therefore, are also peculiar.
Net Profit Margin
This is defined as (Table 13.7):

Net profit after tax Interest income
  In 2017, Mercantile Royal Bank made a net profit margin of 35.56%, meaning it made a net profit of 0.3556 per dollar of interest income. Land Sovereign Bank made a net profit margin of 26.56%, meaning it made a net profit of 0.2656 per dollar of interest income. A plot of these figures will show that the net profit margin for Mercantile Royal Bank lies above that of Land Sovereign Bank over the 3-year period. This means that the net profit margin of Mercantile Royal Bank is always higher than that of Land Sovereign Bank over the 3-year period.

Expense Control Efficiency
Next, we look at the expense control efficiency measure. This is defined as (Table 13.9):
Profit before tax Interest income
  This tells us how much of the bankÆs interest income it gets to keep as profit before tax. When you move down from interest income to profit before tax, you incur expenses. Therefore, the higher the ratio, the better the bank is performing, because it suggests that the bank is keeping a tight lid on expenses.
  For 2017, Mercantile Royal Bank kept 49.89% of its interest income compared to Land Sovereign Bank, which kept 34.50% of its interest income. Alternatively, for Mercantile Royal Bank, for every dollar of interest income, it made a net profit before tax of 0.4989 while for Land Sovereign Bank, for every dollar of interest income, it made a profit before tax of 0.3450. This suggests that Mercantile Royal Bank performed better in controlling its expenses in 2017. In 2016 and 2015, it also performed better in controlling expenses and therefore had a higher ratio compared to Land Sovereign Bank.
Equity Multiplier
This tells us how leveraged the bank is and, by implication, the risk it is taking. Higher leverage, signified by a higher equity multiplier, means that the bank is taking on more risk since it carries much more debt on its statement of financial position. The formula for the equity multiplier is given as:

Asset Utilisation
This tells us how efficiently management is using the assets under its control. The asset utilisation is also known as the asset turnover. How do we find the bankÆs asset turnover? Asset turnover is given as:
   Sales Total assets
  However, the sales of a bank are represented by its interest revenue. Therefore, the formula becomes:
Interest income Total assets
LetÆs find the asset turnover then (Table 13.12).
  In 2017, Mercantile Royal Bank had an asset utilisation of 8.22% while Land Sovereign Bank had an utilisation ratio of 10.30%. Alternatively, we can say that for every dollar in assets, Mercantile Royal Bank generated 0.0822 in interest income while Land Sovereign Bank generated 0.1030 in interest income. Therefore, Land Sovereign Bank had higher asset utilisation than Mercantile Royal Bank. Mercantile Royal Bank has a larger statement of financial position (balance sheet); therefore, it

274	13  Measuring and Evaluating the Performance of Banks

may be finding it more difficult to use those large assets. In 2016, the asset utilisation ratios for both banks were quite similar while Land Sovereign Bank had a higher utilisation ratio in 2015. Generally, it seems Land Sovereign Bank is making better use of its assets.

Return on Equity (ROE)
The ROE is one of the key ratios that shareholders look out for. This tells them how much returns that management has made in net profits after tax for every dollar contributed in equity.
The ROE is defined as:

Net profit after tax
Total shareholderÆs funds (equity)
  However, as we broke down the ROA, the ROE can also be broken down to identify areas that are good as well as areas that may need improvement.

Earnings Spread (ES)
The earnings spread is defined as:

Total interest income	Total interest expense
Total earning assets ù Total interest bearing liabilities

  Earnings spread measures how much the bank earns on its assets and the cost it incurs on its liabilities. The spread brings together the yield on assets and the cost of funds for the bank. Naturally, every bank will want the spread to be as high as possible. However, competition can limit how much the bank can earn on its assets. A bank cannot be charging average interest rates of 35% while its competitorsÆ rates range around 20%. If that were to happen, it will lose some of its prime customers to its competitors. Market conditions too are likely to determine the cost on a bankÆs interest bearing liabilities. ThereÆs a huge debate in Africa concerning the earnings spread for banks in the continent. Banks seem to charge high interest rates on their loans while they pay out very little to depositors. The business of banking, however, would be very difficult if banks had to pay market interest rates for all the funds they borrowed. In fact, in the USA, due to the high competition for funds between banks, in the 1930s, banks saw their cost of borrowing rising very sharply. This caused a lot of problems for banks, and bank regulators in the USA had to prohibit the payments of interest on current accounts.
  For the earning assets of the banks in question, we use the government securities that are due from banks and other financial institutions, loans and advances and investment in other securities. For interest bearing liabilities, we use customer deposits, due to banks and other financial institutions, borrowings and long- term debt.

280	13  Measuring and Evaluating the Performance of Banks

Earnings Per Share (EPS)
The calculation of earnings per share is governed by the accounting standard IAS 33ùearnings per share. The EPS is the earnings earned per each share owned. Rightly or wrongly, a lot of analysts focus a lot on this single figure. If managersÆ bonuses are linked to this figure, they may be tempted to play games and engage in creative accounting to increase the EPS. However, such behaviour is unethical, to say the least, since it misleads users who place reliance on any such financial statement. The EPS is calculated as:
Profit or loss attributable to ordinary shareholderÆs Number shares in issue
  Remember that if shares are issued during the year you would have to weigh the shares issued. You would also have to adjust for bonus issues and rights issues.
  The diluted earnings per share looks at what will happen to the earnings per share figure assuming dilutive contracts crystallise (e.g. share options are exercised and become actual shares). Diluted EPS, as it is calculated, is not forward-looking, as it looks at current and not at future earnings.
  The table below shows the number of shares that were in issue for both banks during the period of our analysis.

  EPS really standardises everything by relating the earnings of the bank to its shares on issue. Over the 3-year period of our analysis, Mercantile Royal Bank has clearly delivered more earning per share to its shareholders compared to Land Sovereign Bank.

Loan Rate
The loan rate measures the loans given by a bank as a proportion of its assets. The loan rate is defined as (Table 13.20):
Net loans Total assets
  Mercantile Royal Bank does not have a substantial portion of its assets in earning assets (loans). The loans it gives out are less than 50% of its total assets. This suggests that the bank can do better by giving out more loans. A review of the statement of financial position shows that a lot of assets are kept in government securities and in amounts due from other banks. The loan rate, however, has been increasing for Land Sovereign Bank since 2015. Keeping a lot of assets in govern- ment securities means that loans to the private sector will be reduced. Over the 3-year period, Land Sovereign BankÆs loan rate was higher than that of Mercantile Royal Bank. That means it gave out more loans compared to Mercantile Royal Bank, taking the size of their statement of financial position into account.

The loan loss ratio is defined as (Table 13.21):

Provisions on loans Total loans and leases
  This tells us that in 2017, Mercantile Royal BankÆs provisions on loans were 2.73% of its loans and leases. Land Sovereign BankÆs loan loss ratio was 3.73% of its loans and leases.   In 2017, for every dollar in deposits, Mercantile Royal Bank gave out 0.67 in loans and advances, while for every dollar in deposits, Land Sovereign Bank gave out 1.03 in 2017. The situation with Land Sovereign Bank may seem surprising since banks are supposed to keep a portion of the deposits they receive (the reserve ratio). That is, a bank cannot give out all the deposits it receives as loans by regulatory requirement. However, the situation for Land Sovereign Bank may be explained by that it might have given out non-deposit sources of finances as loans to supplement the loans given from deposits. These may include equity and borrowings from the money market.
  How does the loan rate tie in with the advances to deposit ratio? Land Sovereign Bank is giving out more loans for every dollar received in deposits, and it also has a higher loan rate compared to Mercantile Royal Bank.



13.4.2 Non-financial Performance Indicators

We have just looked at how to analyse banksÆ financial statements. Do bear in mind that a ratio analysis of this nature may not tell you everything you need to know about the bank. You may find it useful to interview the bankÆs managers as well as visit its business premises and observe how it conducts its business. These are things that the hard figures may not reveal.

13.4 Measuring and Evaluating Bank Performance	285

  In fact, in recognition of how soft issues have become important, the IASB issued a discussion paper on Management Commentary in October 2005. The Management Commentary is known in the UK as the operating and financial review (OFR). This discussion paper provided guidance on what should be reported in the management commentary. The management commentary is an opportunity for management to discuss issues pertaining to the firm. These include factors that contributed to the entityÆs present performance as well as those that will be important for future performance.
  You also need to look at non-financial performance indicators to give you a holistic picture of the performance of the entity in question. To help you generate ideas as to these non-financial performance indicators, we list a few below:
ò Market share
ò Number of new products developed
ò Productivity per employee
ò Employee morale and motivation (staff turnover could give a good indication of this)
ò Customer satisfaction
ò A strong brand name
  A good place to start a broader assessment apart from financial measures is to look at KaplanÆs balanced score card and Fitzgerald and MoonÆs building block model. The balanced score card has four measures. These are financial, customer, internal and innovation and learning. The financial perspective looks at what the company should do to succeed financially and appear good to their shareholders. The customer perspective looks at how the company should appear to its customers to achieve its objectives. The internal business processes look at what business pro- cesses the company must excel at to satisfy its clients and shareholders. Innovation and learning look at how the company can continuously improve and be innovative to achieve its objectives.
  Fitzgerald and MoonÆs building block model have the following three blocks. These are dimensions, standards and rewards. The dimensions represent critical success factors (CSFs). The standards are key performance indicators (KPIs) derived from the critical success factors. The rewards are incentives to motivate managers to achieve the KPIs.

13.5 Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we look at the statement of financial position of a bank. We identify some of the assets you may find on a bankÆs statement of financial position. We also identify some of the typical liabilities that you may find on a bankÆs statement of financial position. Importantly, you should be able to tell why a particular item is an asset or a liability. We also look at off-statements of financial position items and how these have become important. In addition, we discuss the components of the statement of comprehensive income.
  We also look at various financial performance indicators for banks. We discuss how to break these ratios down. By breaking ratios into their various components, the analyst can understand which areas contributed to good or bad performance and, by implication, which areas need attention and can be improved. We also discuss non-financial performance indicators. We stressed that financial indicators alone may not be enough to understand a bank fully. Non-financial performance indicators add to the analystÆs understanding of the financial performance of a bank, what has led to its current performance and what is likely to influence performance in the future.

Chapter 14
Strategy in the Banking Industry







Learning Objectives
At the end of this chapter you should be able to:
ò Identify strategic business units within a bank
ò Identify alternative directions for bank strategy
ò Discuss corporate strategy and diversification
ò Explain international strategy in banking
ò Describe innovations in banking
ò Discuss mergers and acquisitions in banking


14.1 Introduction

The term strategy comes from the Greek word æstrategosÆ which means the æart of the generalÆ and was originally used in the context of warfare. It simply means a game plan for success. The business environment in banking may thus be characterised as a war zone and the leader of a business entity (its generals) must design a game plan in order to succeed in this environment. This game plan is what is known as business strategy. As businesses, banks must formulate their own game plan for success within the industry. Strategy in the banking industry is concerned with the decisions that a bank makes about its future and how it responds to the influences and pressures in the modern business environment. Formulating strategy is one thing and implementing it is another, because strategy is formulated for the æidealÆ scenario but reality is different from the ideal situation. A bank must therefore be mindful of the realities that pertain to the process of translating its strategies into action because these realities can become constraints on the strategic choices that the bank will ultimately consider. In this regard, banks can make three broad strategic choices.
  First and foremost, a bank must decide how it wants to position itself in relation to its competitors. In the modern banking industry, a bankÆs competitors may include other financial institutions, which offer competing products. Thus, banks must


® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_14

289

290	14  Strategy in the Banking Industry

decide how they want to compete in the market or in the various markets in which they operate, that is, in terms of the markets for the various products that they offer. For example, if the goal of a bank is to pursue a strategy of expansion in order to achieve superior operational efficiency, will this be on the basis of competitive advantage or by leveraging on technological advances? Questions such as these should be the focus when choosing a strategy to position the bank within the industry.
  Secondly, the bank must choose products it wants to offer and the markets it wants to serve; this will include both scope and scale. Modern banking has transcended accepting deposits and making loans; sustainable innovation is now more important than ever for success in the industry. Thus, the bank must decide whether it wants to focus on just a few products or a few markets. Should the products be diversified in terms of scope and markets? Which market niche can the bank serve more effectively and efficiently? Should the bank focus on this market only or should it target other niches? What should be the timing and mode of product/service delivery? In choosing its products and markets, the bank must not lose sight of its initial strategy on how it wants to position itself in the industry.
  The third choice should be about how the strategies would be pursued. For any of these strategic choices, the core questions that arise are: should they be pursued by organic development, mergers and acquisitions or through a joint venture with other organisations, both within and outside the banking industry? For instance, banks in Sub-Saharan Africa have started offering insurance products in partnership with insurance companies, mobile money services in partnership with telecommunication companies and other joint ventures with other banks.
  The focus of this chapter is to explain and provide the rationale for a wide range of strategic options for banks. In addition, the chapter also takes a look at the criteria and tools that can be used to evaluate strategic choices. We discuss bank corporate strategy and international strategy in banking. We also look at innovations and mergers and acquisitions in banking. Particular focus is given to banks in Africa.


14.2 Bank Corporate Strategy

Strategic business units (SBUs) are very important in corporate strategy. They represent the self-directed divisions within an organisation that are small enough to be flexible and big enough to affect the long-term performance of the organisation. They have autonomous missions and objectives. That is to say that they can function as separate entities on their own but form part of a network that constitutes the bigger organisation and can therefore be useful in helping the bigger organisation to respond to changing economic conditions. These units usually focus on product offering and market segments with clear marketing plans, campaigns and analyses. Banks, like most businesses, have SBUs because their products and services cut across different markets or niches. The SBUs are the different segments for which there are goods and services that are distinct from other segments. SBUs are usually

14.2 Bank Corporate Strategy	291

autonomous enough to ensure flexibility and to exercise control over most or all of the factors that impact upon the bankÆs long-term goals. Examples of SBUs in a typical bank include wholesale banking, retail banking, corporate banking, centre for treasury and corporate/business. Different banks may have different names for their SBUs, but the description given to those in this section does not vary significantly from one bank to another.
Wholesale Banking This SBU is usually the segment of the bank that focuses on organisations rather than individuals. Public and private institutions are the target niche for this business unit. The unit will usually have a relationship management team that is responsible for managing the bankÆs relationship with its corporate clients. The relationship management team is tasked to, among other things, inno- vate and design products that suit the needs of corporate clients across sectors and the different needs within any given sector. Organisations that form this niche include diplomatic institutions, educational institutions, government and public institutions, international development organisations, private firms, other financial institutions, etc.
  Each of these groups of customers may have specific banking needs that require tailor-made products and services. The products and services offered to these organisations may include, but not be limited to, the following: bancassurance products, bank guarantees (e.g. performance bonds, advance mobilisation guaran- tees), investments, e.g. fixed deposits, call accounts, loans (short, medium and long term), project finance and loan syndication, projects and fund management, publicû private partnerships, trade finance, e.g. letters of credit, forex forward products, transaction bankingùincluding transfers and remittances.
Retail Banking This business unit of a bank is concerned with the provision of the full range of banking and financial services, mainly to individuals and some times to small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs). These services are similar to many of those listed for the wholesale banking unit except that here the target niche is private individuals and SMEs. This unit must have a well-trained relationship management team that is competent to handle the specific needs of different types of customers. For their SME customers, it is important to innovate products that suit their specific needs.
Treasury The treasury unit of a bank is responsible for the investment management function of the bank. This includes the money market and forex activities of the bank. This department may also function as a dealer in government securities.
Business Centre The business or corporate centre is the æshop floorÆ of a bank. It is what is known as the æbrick-and-mortarÆ banking hall. The function of the business centre is to serve as a one-stop shop for all the various types of banking products and services. The business centre, among other things, facilitates the following: transac- tional services, e.g. deposits, withdrawal, transfers, local and foreign transactions, trade facilitation services, trade financing, loans and advances, investment services,
e.g. fixed deposits, and call deposits.

292	14  Strategy in the Banking Industry

14.2.1 Bases of Competitive Advantage and the Strategic Clock

We now focus on the bases of competitive advantage and the concept of strategic clock. The different ways of thinking about the competitive strategy of a bank are what we mean by the bases of competitive advantage. Competitive strategy is concerned with the basis on which a business unit expects to achieve competitive advantage in its market. Michael Porter (1985) proposes three generic strategies that an organisation can adopt to achieve competitive advantage. These are æoverall cost leadershipÆ, ædifferentiationÆ and æfocusÆ. Many people often confuse the exact meanings of each of these terms. For instance, æcost leadershipÆ is not intended to mean ælow priceÆ as it may appear. To remove this ambiguity, we shall use the term æmarket-facingÆ strategies proposed by Johnson et al. (2008) and similar to that used by Faulkner and Bowman (1995). The market-facing strategies are represented by what is commonly known as the strategic clock (see Fig. 14.1). This is based on the principle that any competitive advantage to be achieved can be achieved by giving customers what they want more effectively than oneÆs competitors, thus embedding PorterÆs categories. In a competitive environment, customers make choices on the basis of a blend of price and the expected product (or service) benefits. Thus, the strategic clock represents different positions, where a bankÆs customers and potential customers have different expectations in terms of ævalue for moneyÆ. Each position in the strategic clock also represents a generic strategy by which a bank may achieve competitive strategy.
  It can be seen from Fig. 14.1 that each of the strategies acknowledges the importance of cost, especially in relation to a bankÆs competitors, and this makes cost a significant and strategic consideration, regardless of the chosen strategy.

14.2 Bank Corporate Strategy	293

Because these strategies are market facing, it is necessary to explain the factors that are critical for the success of each strategy on the clock. At æno frillsÆ and ælow priceÆ, customers are mainly concerned with price but only to the extent that the product or service being offered by the bank meets their requirements or needs. That is to say that they are more particular about such things as the reliability and convenience of the service being offered than about other product features. On the other hand, at æfocused differentiationÆ, customers want products to be customised to meet their particular needs and they are prepared to pay a premium price for such tailor-made products. There is an emerging trend in banking, where banks offer highly customised services to individuals and organisations as far as allowing the customer to decide which specific staff of the bank should attend to them and the location at which the service will be received. The clock does not denote a process, as each strategy can be pursued independently and by different SBUs within the bank. The appropriate strategy requirement of a bank or its SBUs may also change over time.


14.2.1.1 Price-Based Strategies: æNo FrillsÆ and æLow PriceÆ

The æno-frillsÆ strategy combines low price with low perceived product or service benefits and is usually used for price-sensitive market segments. When customers do not see any differences or do not value the differences in the product being offered by different banks, their emphasis would be on the price. For instance, consider two banks who are both offering ATM services, but one is charging a lower fee per transaction, customers may choose the bank with the cheaper fees if they perceive that there is no difference in the two services to justify a higher fee. Also, there may be a segment of the market that is price sensitive and simply does not care about the quality of the product or service being offered. These customers are usually not able to afford the service or they just choose not to pay so much for a certain service. Banks seeking to serve niches such as these may choose this strategy. This may include low-income customers and students. For example, some banks do not charge any fees to student accounts for a range of basic services such as withdrawals, ATM transactions and payments of school fees. This strategy may work well for markets where the customers have high power or low switching costs. This strategy is useful for avoiding major competitors and may be used by new entrants and smaller banks to carve out a niche or use it as a strategy to attract more customers before adopting other strategies, especially if bigger banks compete on other bases.
  The ælow-priceÆ strategy, on the other hand, aims to achieve a lower price than competitors for a similar level of perceived product or service benefits compared to those offered by competitors. Banks can achieve a competitive advantage by using this strategy to appeal to niches (or sell products) that are unattractive to their competitors so that they can avoid the tendency of competitive pressures eroding price. The danger here, however, is that competitors may wade into the niche over time, which could erode profits significantly for everyone. Moreover, banks which rely on this strategy may have fewer resources available to develop products in the future, and the low cost may even lead to loss of the perceived benefit of the product

294	14  Strategy in the Banking Industry

over time. Thus, neither a æno-frillsÆ strategy nor a ælow-priceÆ strategy may be possible to be achieved in the long run without a low-cost base. Banks must, however, realise that low cost alone is not a viable basis for competitive advantage. They must therefore find ways to reduce costs in ways which their competitors are not able to match, so that pursuing a low-price strategy might give them sustainable competitive advantage.


14.2.1.2 Differentiation-Based Strategies

Dear Customer, Get debit cards in 20 minutes & cheque books in under 4 days. This is guaranteed. And if we fail, we will waive off related charges.ùBarclays Bank, Ghana
  The quote above is a text message that Barclays Bank sent to its customers in September 2017. It is common for banks to take up to one month to issue debit cards and cheque books. This may be unacceptable to many customers in this fast-paced world. By adopting this strategy of offering an existing product faster, Barclays Bank is pursuing a differentiation strategy. Correspondingly, the cost of Barclays BanksÆ debit cards is a little higher than many of its competitors, though not the highest in the market. By guaranteeing delivery, the bank also tells customers and potential customers that it is a reliable bank. This might give the bank an edge over its competition for these products because time is important in todayÆs business environment. Differentiation strategies provide products, and services offer benefits that are different from what other banks are offering and which are valued by buyers. When a bank adopts a differentiation strategy, the objective is often to achieve competitive advantage by offering customers better products or services, either at the same price as competitors or at a slightly higher price. Thus, they seek excellence in their product and service delivery.
  The success of a differentiation strategy for banks will depend on some definite factors. The bank must first identify and understand the æstrategic customerÆ. The strategic customer is the customer the strategy is targeting. The target niche must be well defined so that the strategy can be focused. The strategy must take the needs of the customer into consideration. Information on the needs of the customer can be gotten from a market research. Banks do this by offering avenues for feedback from customers in their banking hall in the normal course of business. The bank must also consider who its key competitors are. For instance, Barclays Bank is not just competing with banks in Ghana but also with banks in other parts of the world. Banks must therefore identify their competitors in each of their product or service offerings and then determine which bases of differentiation will be helpful. This can be done at the level of the various SBUs.
  Differentiation strategies must be difficult to imitate if they are to provide the bank with sustained competitive advantage. For instance, if it takes another bank less than 20 min to offer debit cards at a low cost and this technology can be easily acquired by other banks, Barclays Bank may not be able to sustain this advantage for

14.2 Bank Corporate Strategy	295

long. Differentiation strategies must take into consideration how vulnerable the strategy is to price-based strategies that may be used by competitors.


14.2.1.3 The Hybrid Strategy

As the name suggests, the hybrid strategy is an attempt to combine low-price with differentiation strategies. For this strategy to be successful, banks must be able to deliver enhanced benefits to customers alongside low prices without sacrificing too much of its margins so that they can develop adequate bases for differentiation. This strategy can yield high margins if banks can achieve higher transaction volumes relative to their competitors. It can also be optimal to pursue this strategy if it is possible to cut cost outside the differentiated products. An example is when the cost of advertising products and delivering them can be shared across multiple products in a way that is difficult for competitors to imitate. The hybrid strategy can be used as an entry strategy when there are established competitors already in the market. The new bank can identify one or more of the products being offered poorly by the existing banks and then design a superior alternative at lower fees; when this product is successful, the bank can now move on to pursue differentiation strategies or can do both simultaneously.


14.2.1.4 Focused Differentiation Strategy

This strategy provides products with highly perceived benefits, which justify a substantial price premium to a given niche. Think of this: you enter a crowded banking hall and find a teller with no queue; meanwhile a few customers came into the hall and were served by that teller and they left the hall within 5 min. Those customers are variously called æpremierÆ, æprestigeÆ æroyalÆ and æfirst classÆ. They pay higher fees for receiving this prestigious service. Note that these are individuals rather than institutions. Spending hours in the banking hall may be more expensive for some customers than the fees being charged. The speed offered might be worth the price they pay.
  For banks, they may have to choose between a focused strategy and the broader differentiation. For instance, a local bank that is following a strategy of international growth may have to choose between gaining competitive advantage on the basis of a single product it can offer globally or tailor-make its products to specific niches or local markets. This may be difficult to sustain over the product growth cycle. Moreover, as the bank improves the services offered on other products, the differ- ences between the æpremierÆ and æregularÆ customers will be eroded and so the former may no longer be profitable.

296	14  Strategy in the Banking Industry

14.2.1.5 Failure Strategies

Failure strategies refer jointly to the remaining strategies: increased price/standard value, increased price/low value and low value/standard price. They are so called because they do not provide perceived value for money neither in terms of product features nor in price. It may be unacceptable to charge higher fees for the same value or a lower value or to charge standard fees for lower value. Customers will almost certainly reject the product in any of these three situations. These strategies are therefore doomed to fail unless such situations are warranted by legislation or market conditions.


14.2.2 Sustaining Competitive Advantage

When a bank strives to achieve competitive advantage, it must be the case that it hopes to keep it for a long time. In todayÆs fast-changing financial services industry, it is important to consider whether it is possible to sustain competitive strategy and, if so, for how long. This section seeks to highlight various issues with respect to sustaining competitive advantage along the various types of strategies discussed above.


14.2.2.1 Sustaining Price-Based Competitive Advantage

Banks, which have achieved competitive advantage by pursuing price-based strate- gies, can sustain the advantage through the following ways:
ò The bank has lower margin which can be sustained if it can maintain a high volume of transactions or activities on the products in questions or if it can cross- subsidise one product or SBU with profits from another product or SBU.
ò If the bank has some form of a unique low-cost structure, which has made it possible to successfully pursue a price-based strategy and can maintain this cost structure in a way that its competitors cannot easily beat, then it can sustain the advantage. Banks are rapidly digitising their operations in an attempt to reduce cost and achieve efficiency. Some banks now have ATMs which accept deposits and perform many of the functions, which were hitherto performed by tellers. If by this they are able to achieve some cost-saving advantages, they may be able to sustain this approach insofar as competitors are not able to match up to that strategy.
ò It may be possible for the bank to possess a unique capability or resource which makes it possible for it to keep cost low through the value chain. For banks to be able to continue to keep costs low, they may bear in mind that it is necessary to continue to innovate in cost reduction to be able to survive. It is also important that competitors find it difficult to imitate.

14.2 Bank Corporate Strategy	297

ò The bank must focus on those niches or market segments where customers particularly value low pricing or lower fees. For instance, a bank may keep fees on student accounts or SME accounts low and focus on building a large customer base among these niches. These customers may be unwilling to pay high fees to maintain an account, but their savings when put together can be a huge (and cheap) source of deposit mobilisation, which the bank can benefit from through interest income on loans.
  Notwithstanding the points above, banks hoping to sustain competitive advantage through a price-based strategy should take note of the following:
ò It may be possible for competitors to do the same. This can eventually erode any advantage the bank may be enjoying. In fact, if competitors can easily imitate the strategy, then there is no point to pursue it, because perhaps the basis may be weak.
ò There is a risk that customers may begin to associate low prices with low product benefit. This may erode any advantage as volume of transactions begins to decline.


14.2.2.2 Sustaining Differentiation-Based Strategy

What is the point in trying to be different if others can imitate you easily? Thus, whatever the basis of differentiation is, it must be sustainable. For instance, many banks try to gain competitive advantage by introducing new products, which are copied by their competitors almost immediately. Some of these products are some- times not very different æin factÆ except for the name changes. Others too are just cocktail products, bringing together parts of existing products. No matter how the differentiation is done, if the bases are not strong, it cannot be sustained. Some of the ways in which banks can sustain advantage through differentiation are to attempt the following:
ò Introduce features that make it difficult to copy or imitate.
ò Focus on intangibles, which cannot be gotten elsewhere. Banks can create this through branding, reputation building, advertisement and service excellence, which will make them a bank of choice. If consumers have an indelible positive impression about the bankÆs products, which may be difficult for a competitor to take away, they may sustain their advantage in the product offering or in the industry.
ò Banks can work their way to be intimately linked with the operations of their customers. This works for institutional banking. For example, banks that collect fees on behalf of universities usually have their system connected to the system of the university. All things being equal, it is possible for the bank to tailor-make products that will suit the needs of the university (that is, focused differentiation), and this may make it difficult to lose its business with the university. One reason could be that the switching costs may be high for the university, and there are

298	14  Strategy in the Banking Industry

risks associated with such a switch, which the university may find unnecessary to bear.


14.2.2.3 Strategic Lock-In

A third approach to sustaining competitive advantage is known as strategic lock-in. This can be used for either price-based or differentiation strategies. Strategic lock-in can occur when a bank gets to a point where it is associated with certain unique characteristics (sort of a proprietary position). ADB Bank in Ghana is known to be æthe bankÆ that serves farmers. For them to sustain these positions, banks must note that much will depend partly on the following:
ò Market position: while a bank seeks to attain a proprietary position in the industry for one or more of its products or SBU for the entire organisation, it is important to recognise that it is less likely that others will follow its lead unless they perceive that the bank is a market leader or that the product in question is market leading and that it can be regarded as æa/the standardÆ.
ò First-mover advantage: it is easier to become a/the standard if the product is the first of its kind to be introduced in the market. This is likely to be attained if the first move is accompanied by aggressive marketing, which establishes the product in the minds of customers. What is the point in introducing a product without selling it effectively? There have been instances where a bank has claimed to be the first to introduce a particular product to the market, but no one regards them for the product, simply because other banks have marketed the product with their brand better and so customers associate the product with them rather than the first mover. A first mover always has a chance to dominate the market early in the product life cycle; they must therefore take the opportunity.
ò Self-reinforcing customer loyalty: when customers regard the bank as the stan- dard for a particular product, they will refer new customers, and this can help the bank sustain its advantage and preserve its position as the leader in the product delivery.


14.2.3 Responding to Competitive Threat

Sometimes, while you are trying to preserve competitive advantage, your compet- itors may be targeting your customers on the basis of a different competitive strategy. This can be a serious threat. For instance, while existing banks have built their advantage over time through differentiation, a new entrant to the industry may attempt to gain advantage by charging lower fees than what pertains in the industry. If competitors are maintaining differential as a basis of retaliation, you may build multiple basis of differentiation as a response to their threat. You may have to consider the basis of your differentiation and ensure that it is meaningful. Also,

14.2 Bank Corporate Strategy	299

minimise the price differences for superior products and focus on less price-sensitive niches or products.
  On the other hand, if you find that your competitors who were previously pursuing differentiation decide to set up a low-priced business to compete, you can respond by establishing low-priced products or distinct SBUs to focus on customers who may be won by the low-price option from a competitor. For example, banks can decide to offer lower charges on digital banking products or subsidiaries. This can help them reach a niche that may otherwise not be reached by other more traditional products or SBUs. Besides, they can mobilise funds through the digital or e-banking SBU that they would otherwise not have got. The bank must not just use these alternatives as defensive strategies only, but must allow the SBU or product to compete with competitorsÆ products.



14.2.4 Competitive Strategy in Hypercompetitive Conditions

In a hypercompetitive environment, sustaining competitive advantage may be near impossible. The focus in this section is to take a look at things that banks can do in an environment where achieving sustainability is difficult or impossible. Up until now, we have assumed that banks pursue competitive strategies because they want to achieve and sustain competitive advantage. In todayÆs fast-changing, turbulent and uncertain banking environment, banks are faced with heightened and increased levels of competition, known as hypercompetition. This kind of environment is associated with quick imitation, rapid innovation and fast-changing customer needs which makes it difficult to achieve or sustain competitive advantage. Thus, banks must be dynamic, flexible and highly innovative. The following points relate to how banks may compete in a hypercompetitive environment or respond to hypercompetition.
ò Competitors may undermine the bankÆs competitive strategies by attempting to imitate its product offering or by attempting to attack the bases of its strategic advantage. In such a situation, innovation is essential to sustain leadership within the industry or product.
ò Besides, it is possible for the bank to block a competitorÆs first-mover advantage by responding very quickly to the competitor strategy before the competitor becomes dominant or leading in the product. In trying to overcome this threat, the bank under threat can quickly innovate products that have enhanced features. This will put the bank under threat, a step ahead in the product offering over its competitors.
ò Competitors may have attempted to build barriers to entry into new markets. This can be overcome by undermining competitorsÆ strongholds by adopting methods and tools that have been ignored by them in order to gain access to the market. Innovation can be useful as well, as this may give customers a new experience, which hitherto did not exist. If the nature of the barrier has been the enormity of

300	14  Strategy in the Banking Industry

existing banksÆ resources, collaboration among smaller banks or with other organisations can help overcome the barrier if such collaborations will strengthen their base.



14.2.5 Strategic Challenges in the Banking Industry

Over 70% of financial institutions globally place ôimproving the customer experienceö as one of their top three strategic priorities in 2017ùDigital Banking Report.
  The challenges that players in the banking industry face are multifaceted, ranging from complex regulatory regimes and cost and operations challenges to difficulty in achieving and maintaining a sustainable growth trajectory. Traditionally, banking was limited to the brick-and-mortar or physical buildings of banks. Modernisation is, however, rapidly shaking the foundations of traditional banking with the surge in technological innovations and advances. Thus, the traditional banking methods that were restricted to the physical buildings are fast becoming antiquated. A little over a decade ago, bank customers could not undertake certain transactions outside their branch. Recent trends, however, show that consumers of banking services are shifting towards ease and convenience. Advances in smartphones, mobile technol- ogy and Internet access are making this shift possible and the speed of innovation in these areas is driving the industry. This means that for a bank to survive in the industry, it must keep up with the pace of technological developments in the industry. This has thus become an important strategic concern. In line with these concerns, banks must address the following challenges in the twenty-first century.


14.2.5.1 Augmenting the Technological Experience

Over the years, the primary touch point of engagement between banks and their customers has been the physical bank locations. In the modern banking industry, however, this has got to change if a bank is to survive within the industry. With increased innovation in the banking industry, the explosion of smartphones (and other smart devices) and the development of associated applications, bank customers are now much more inclined to complete their banking transactions online as much as possible. Bearing this in mind, if banks are to retain their customers, especially the youth, then they need to create mobile-friendly banking experiences together with complement applications that will aid their customersÆ financial life. This is impor- tant to enable them retain existing customers and attract new ones. Thus, a technol- ogy strategy is no longer a nice-to-have for banks but a necessity for those expecting to remain competitive.

14.2 Bank Corporate Strategy	301

14.2.5.2 Mobile Payment Regimes

In todayÆs commercial environment, mobile payments are fast becoming more and more widespread throughout the consumer economy than ever before. Payments ranging from groceries, medical bills, donations, religious commitments, school fees and transport fares are commonly made with some form of mobile payment service. The ninth world payment system report by the Royal Bank of Scotland and Capgemini anticipated and valued resources for the payments industry and reported that in the year 2011, the non-cash payments industry grew by as much as 8.8% to reach 307 billion transactions; this increase was led mainly by the developing world by up to 20%, though the developed markets still account for a majority of the transaction value. The total value of transactions had further risen by 8.5% in 2012 to
$333 billion. This shows that there are enormous opportunities in the developing world. It is therefore important for banks to strategise towards offering mobile payment-related services, especially in the developing regions of Asia, Africa and Latin America.
  Existing popular mobile payment platforms such as Apple Pay, Google wallet, M-Pesa, Myghpay, MTN Mobile Money, Vodafone Cash, Tigo Cash, Airtel Money and Express Pay have set the pace for the banking industry in the area of mobile payments, since consumers are increasingly becoming used to these services that are readily offered on their mobile phone devices. The convenience offered by these services coupled with the simplicity it offers has over the years made customers become comfortable with the security of this method of payment. However, many of these products are offered by telecom companies and technological firms rather than banks. Consequently, this has given rise to a strategic challenge that banks have to respond to.
  The drivers of growth of this method are the increased penetration of smart phones and Internet usage, technological advances and product and service innova- tion. In a number of African countries, some banks have attempted to address this challenge by partnering with telecom companies and other tech companies to offer these services, mainly serving as agents. In other parts of Africa such as Kenya, banks and other financial service companies have started launching their own payment platforms. This is an important issue that any serious modern bank needs to pay particular attention to.


14.2.5.3 Address Security and Authentication Concerns

This point builds on the other points made previously. Security and authentication concerns are important strategic challenges that banks will have to address, as consumers continue to adapt to the mobile banking (and payment) ecosystem. This will include the procedure for confirming the identity of customers who are accessing their accounts remotely. Security breaches and cyber and identity fraud are on the rise, and so the protection and security of banking information is chief to

302	14  Strategy in the Banking Industry

instilling or augmenting the trust in the minds of customers (about electronic-based banking services). Banks have to implement workable strategic plans accordingly to ensure the safety of customersÆ financial and personal data.


14.2.5.4 Technological Disruptions and FinTech

It is well known that technology is fast taking over the traditional banking processes, and the technological sector has already started designing alternatives to many of the products and services that are traditionally offered by banks. Although these alter- natives, known as financial technology (FinTech), are not expected to take a significant share from the banking industry, they pose a significant strategic chal- lenge to banks. This challenge is particularly important because of the cost contain- ment and growth struggles banks face. FinTech is a class of software that serves to displace some of the services and products that give traditional banks the most profits. The products and services provided by FinTech include financial advising, loans, alternative payment processing, money transfer and cryptocurrency. To be able to meet this challenge, banks must develop competitive alternatives in-house, or they can acquire these companies to add to their portfolio of offerings.


14.2.5.5 Reaching New Customers: Multichannel Marketing

Banks, like other businesses, need to attract new customers while retaining existing ones. In this regard, banks need to implement a strategy that will help them retain existing customers and attract new ones. This has become a strategic challenge in modern banking because of the heterogeneity of customers with regard to their needs and the medium through which banks can effectively communicate with them. To this end, an important strategy that must be given close attention in this regard is the so-called multichannel marketing. This strategy is projected to yield high return on investment when properly implemented. Multichannel marketing involves innova- tion in a bankÆs customer acquisition strategies by adopting a combination of traditional, digital and social channels to expand their marketing reach and get their message across to the cohorts of new customers that they hope to gain.
  Before banks undertake multichannel marketing, it is important to conduct a comprehensive analysis of data on their target niche of customers and to determine what they may be interested in and then they can craft a message and use an appropriate medium to get it across. Money spent on marketing must be allocated in a manner that will yield the best return on investment.

14.3 International Strategy in Banking	303

14.2.6 Corporate Strategy and Diversification

Up to this point, our focus has been on strategic choices at the level of business units of banks both through pricing or differentiations. We now turn to an important strategic choice that banks must make at the corporate level. This choice includes the decision to enter or exit particular markets or products. In a fast-paced and highly competitive industry, banks have often been left with little choice but to enter into many new product and market areas. As banks add on new products and markets, they eventually tend to be more concerned with strategic choices at the corporate level than at the level of SBUs. The key question this section seeks to address is: How broad and diverse should banks be in terms of their corporate portfolios? That is, what is the scope or breath of the bank as a corporation? Many banks started either as commercial banks, community banks or merchant banks, but now offer products on such a wide range that it is difficult to distinguish them on the bases of their product offerings or markets. Scope is concerned with how far the bank should be diversified in terms of products and markets.
  Diversification is the process of increasing the range of products or markets served by a bank. This can be achieved either vertically or horizontally. Vertical diversification means that the bank expands its scope across its value chain. An example will be a bank that sets up its own printing press to print its own chequebooks and other documents or acquiring a printing press with the capacity to print these books. Horizontal diversification involves banks investing in similar entities (financial companies) by expanding its product line or by acquiring related firms. Some benefits of diversification include reducing risks and obtaining the highest return possible on investments made, gaining entry into new markets and products and increasing market power.



14.3 International Strategy in Banking

International strategy is a situation where banks transact business outside their domestic markets. Banks would want to extend their business beyond the local market because of the potential new opportunities that exist in such environments. Some of benefits of going international include access to larger markets, greater economies of scale, potential greater returns on capital investments, access to relatively cheaper labour and gaining competitive advantage through various means, such as access to vital materials. The drivers of internationalisation may include market factors, cost factors, regulatory or government factors and compet- itive factors.
  Market drivers in banks are the underlying forces that compel consumers to buy products and demand for services and consequently enable markets to develop. Customer demands and government policies are examples of market drivers. Banks like Barclays Bank, Standard Chartered Bank, UBA, Zenith Bank, Standard

304	14  Strategy in the Banking Industry

Bank and Ecobank are some examples of banks in Africa that have extended internationally. Some traditional modes of entry into international markets include licensing/franchising, exports and wholly owned subsidiaries.
  Another important component for going international is cost drivers, where cost can be minimised by operating internationally through the economies of scale and other channels. Additionally, government drivers are those factors that can be pro- moters or inhibitors of internationalisation. Depending on the extent of openness in some countries, banks can establish their branches in different regions and countries. Banks should be able to identify government policies, peculiar in the countries they want to establish in. Lastly, competitive drivers serve as motivating factors for undertaking an international strategy. This is a situation where the presence of global competitors has made banks like Barclays Bank, Ecobank, Standard Chartered, Standard Bank, Zenith Bank, among others, internationalise.
  Competitive advantage is a state in business where a firm operates at a minimum cost as well as it possesses certain advantages or characteristics that makes it more desirable to customers than other competitors. In other words, it is a situation where a bank does or has something better than other banks. Sources of competitive advan- tage include (1) technologyùthe possession of sophisticated machinery or technol- ogy that enables a bank to attain some sort of competitive advantage. Technological prowess gives banks strong edge in foreign markets. For example, harnessing current customer facing technology, especially advances in mobile technology, and providing superior experience across all customer touch points will make the bank stand out among other competitors; (2) peopleùpeople within a particular bank are good at creating and innovating great ideas that will enable an entity to likely achieve competitive advantage, because even if other competitors can imitate the products, they cannot copy that particular bankÆs people or employees, though they might be poached; (3) organisational culture and structureùthe culture of a firm is the shared beliefs, behaviour, mission, habits and norms of that particular entity. Without a common goal, a bank, for instance, would not be able to achieve further heights in comparative advantage. Energies should be channelled towards attaining a common goal and realising meaningful results; (4) intellectual property and productsùthe nature of a bankÆs products can enable it to possess competitive advantage but these products can be replicated by competitors, so intellectual property can be acquired to protect the rights; and (5) practices and processesùa bank that possesses powerful or superior methods of producing results would have competitive advantage.


14.4 Innovations in Banking

We discussed that banks serve as intermediaries between lenders and borrowers. They create new money by making loans through the deposits they mobilise. To effectively and efficiently execute these tasks, there is the need for banks in this contemporary world to constantly innovate and come out with new ideas. They need

14.4 Innovations in Banking	305

to digitise their transactions and businesses. Hence, a lot of resources should be dedicated to digital transformation in the bankÆs IT Budget. It is important to note that creating and innovating should not be done in isolation. A lot of units, people and facilities need to come together to achieve a common objective. Relationships like those among organisations, between companies and customers as well as among industries help to form a formidable team in times of innovations and inventions. Banking is a speedily changing industry, and the biggest paradigm shift that has occurred is the move to digital or electronic banking. Innovation has an effect on price, cost and sustainability. The adoption of e-banking has given customers the flexibility to access banking services at the comfort of their homes or workplaces. In effect, long queues have been shortened and productive hours are utilised effectively in executing other tasks.
  Innovation involves the process of translating an idea or invention into a good or service that creates value. With innovation, the idea must be replicable at an economical cost and must meet a particular need. There are two basic forms of innovation: closed and open innovation. The former takes place within a particular bank using the resources available in that entity to undertake the processes. Some banks adopt this method in order not to draw attention so as to avoid unnecessary competition. It also helps protect their intellectual property. Open innovation, on the other hand, welcomes varying ideas from all angles possible (internal and external) in order to quicken and enrich innovation of the adopters (banks). Some banks sometimes invite the public to undertake several competitions in the bid of obtaining innovative ideas to facilitate their activities. Groups or individuals who emerge winners are usually awarded. Innovation is more involving than invention. With invention, it is the means of creating new things, but innovation invents and finds the applicability of these new products.
  Innovation diffusion is a theory that seeks to explain how, why and at what rate new ideas and technology spread. It is the process by which innovations spread among users. The demand-side factors, as well as the supply-side factors, together influence the pace of diffusion, over which managers have considerable control. With the supply side, product features determine pace.
  Technology is seen as being a great force behind innovation. It aids in creating new things, translating ideas into realities and also facilitating the transfer of new products or processes to the end users. Transformational developments are spinning around the banking industryùas they are in almost every industry. Telecommuni- cation companies are getting into financial services. For example, MTN has mobile money transactions whereby customers can keep money in their wallets, which also attract interest. Customers can also buy treasury bills with the monies in their wallets. Banks should be able to anticipate the needs of customers and meet them accordingly. This can be done by innovating new, effective and efficient ways of meeting customersÆ needs. Creating innovations involves two major factorsùtiming and relationships. The timing aspect captures whether to be a first mover or a fast follower. Either of them has its own advantages and disadvantages. Usually, fruitful innovations come about through collaborative effort, which is referred to as

306	14  Strategy in the Banking Industry

relationship, and it comes in diverse formsùbetween a bank and its customers, between a big bank and a start-up and between banks.


14.5 Mergers and Acquisitions in Banking

One way by which banks pursue growth strategies is through organic development. This involves undertaking a strategy by building on and developing a bankÆs own capabilities. In other words, it is the process of business expansion by which banks develop new products, expand their customer base as well as output and also expand into new markets. All these processes are within an organisation and hence referred to as organic as compared to mergers and acquisitions, which is inorganic growth. Some benefits of adopting organic development are strategic independence, spread- ing investment over time, no availability restraints, knowledge and learning.
  Apart from organic development, banks can also adopt merger and acquisition and alliances in their growth strategies. A merger is the joining of two or more banks, which were hitherto separate entities to achieve greater efficiencies of scale and productivity, whereas acquisition is when a bank takes over the ownership of another firm, which is also known as ætakeoverÆ. Takeovers can be friendly or hostile. Friendly takeover occurs when the acquiring bank merges or acquires the target bank with the help of the board and management of the target bank. In a hostile takeover, on the other hand, the acquiring bank buys the shares of the target bank directly from the shareholders and replaces the board and management of the target bank. Management of the target firm may institute certain measures to forestall possible takeovers, including antitakeover amendments, poison pills, golden para- chutes, white knight, crown jewels and lobster trap. There have been several instances of mergers and acquisitions in Africa; some examples include Ecobank acquiring the Trust Bank (TTB) in Ghana in 2011 and the merger of Absa Group Limited and BarclaysÆs African operations in 2013. Absa, which was originally known as Amalgamated Banks of South Africa, was founded in 1991 following the merger of a number of banks.
  There are instances where the regulator may also get a strong bank to take over banks experiencing capital and liquidity challenges or decide to consolidate a number of such banks. Examples in Ghana include GCB Bank acquiring UT Bank and Capital Bank in 2017 through a purchase and assumption deal and the creation of Consolidated Bank, Ghana, in 2018 to absorb seven insolvent banks. Also in Kenya, the Consolidated Bank of Kenya was incorporated in 1989 as a result of the merger of nine insolvent financial institutions.
  According to Johnson et al. (2008), there are three broad reasons for mergers and acquisitions: First, there is a financial motive which is focused on making maximum use of financial resources for owners of the firm, i.e. tax efficiency, financial efficiency and asset stripping. Second, a managerial motive, which is directed towards managersÆ interests rather than those of shareholdersbandwagon effects and personal ambition. Finally, there is the strategic motive that is focused on

14.6 Summary and Conclusions	307

improving and developing a business, and it is related to competitive advantageù capabilities, extension and consolidation.
  Strategic alliance is where two banks agree to share resources to undertake a particular strategy or set of goals and objectives. This approach is less demanding and less permanent as well, since it is just the pooling together of resources of both banks to create another entity. In this case, each bank maintains its autonomy while gaining a new opportunity. The two major types of strategic alliances are equity alliances (a new and independent organisation is created) and non-equity alliances (usually contract-based). Joint venture and franchising are examples of equity and non-equity alliances, respectively.
  Some motives for alliances are scale (firms combine in order to achieve econo- mies of scale in their production or output and it helps them to undertake a massive project which could not be realised with separate resources), access (firms form alliances so as to access the competences of another firm that is needed to produce or sell its products and/or services), complementary (combining unique resources so that they can bolster each firmÆs gaps or weaknesses) and collusive alliances (firms collude to increase their market power, thereby enabling them to extract lower prices from suppliers and higher prices from their customers). All the aforementioned business ventures have merits and demerits. Thus, banks should do well to examine and identify which of the strategic options to choose in order to best meet their business needs, satisfy customers and maximise shareholdersÆ wealth.



14.6	Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we examine strategy in banking by looking at bank corporate strategy, international strategy in banking, innovations in banking and then mergers and acquisitions in banking. We mention that:
1. Strategy in the banking industry is concerned with the decisions that a banking firm makes about its future operations and how it responds to the influences and pressures in the modern business environment.
2. In formulating strategy, a bank makes three important choices: decision on how to position itself in relation to its competitors, products to offer to the markets it operates in and how the strategies are to be pursued.
3. An important aspect of the bankÆs strategy is competitive strategy, which is concerned with the basis on which it expects to achieve competitive advantage in its market. In a competitive environment, customers make choices on the basis of a blend of price and the expected product (or service) benefits. Thus, the strategic clock represents different positions in which a bankÆs customers and potential customers have different expectations in terms of ævalue for moneyÆ.
4. Each position in the strategic clock also represents a generic strategy by which a bank may achieve competitive strategy and these different strategies include

308	14  Strategy in the Banking Industry

price-based strategies, which are also known as æno frillsÆ and ælow priceÆ, and differentiation-based strategies, the hybrid strategy and focused differentiation.
5. Banks pursue international strategy by extending their businesses beyond their countries of origin because of potential new opportunities that exist in such environments. Some of the benefits of going international are access to larger markets, greater economies of scale, potential greater returns on capital invest- ments, access to relatively cheaper labour and gaining competitive advantage through various means such as access to vital materials. The drivers of internationalisation may include market factors, cost factors, regulatory or gov- ernment factors and competitive factors.
6. In order to effectively and efficiently play their role as financial intermediaries in this contemporary world, there is a need for banks to constantly innovate and come out with new ideas. Innovation involves the process of translating an idea or invention into a good or service that creates value. With innovation, the idea must be replicable at an economical cost and must meet a particular need.
7. There are two basic forms of innovationùclosed and open innovation. Closed innovation takes place within a particular bank, using the resources available in that entity to undertake the processes, while open innovation, on the other hand, welcomes varying ideas from all angles possible (internal and external) in order to quicken and enrich innovation of the bank.
8. Apart from organic development, which involves building on and developing a bankÆs own capabilities, banks can also undertake merger and acquisitions and alliances.
Discussion Questions and Problems

3. Discuss the costs and benefits, to local SMEs, of internationalisation that results in the influx of foreign banks into local financial systems.
4. Explore the drivers of a successful low-cost, differentiation and focused strategy for a low middle-income economy.
5. Discuss the drivers of internationalisation with a view to rank these drivers as they apply in your local economy.
