15.1 Introduction

Sustainable banking and for that matter sustainability of financial services is not about being green; rather, it implies carrying out banking operations and business activities with conscious consideration for the environmental as well as for the social impacts of these activities. In other words, sustainable banking integrates environ- mental, social and governance (ESG) criteria into traditional banking. Banks imple- ment sustainable businesses in two ways: they incorporate their day-to-day activities such as managing their physical branches, human capital, costs, opportunities and risk exposures and their activities relating to external interactions with their clients, as well as incorporate the types of projects they finance into their business model.
  There are several benefits accrued to a banking firm that conducts sustainable banking business. These range from improved reputation to improved investor confidence. Due to these benefits, there is now an increasing shift by banking firms from simply trying to manage their environmental and social risk to proactively seeking new opportunities presented by sustainability principles, while employing these to differentiate themselves in the market. Among other considerations, sus- tainable or ethical banking stems from the fact that corporations are considered moral agents and therefore have a responsibility to the wider society. Certainly,


® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_15

311

312	15  Sustainable Banking

corporations, including banks, are required to abide by the laws and regulations within which they operate. These laws and regulations, however, are not totally comprehensive and do not address every issue that banks may be faced with. Sustainable or ethical banking covers what the organisation is willing to do beyond its legal obligation to stakeholders.
  In this chapter, we provide a broad overview of the theoretical foundation that supports sustainable banking. We first discuss ethics in banking and then examine sustainable banking. In addition to this, we explain corporate social responsibility of banking firms and how corporate governance will help banks operate responsibly.


15.2 Ethics in Banking

Ethics can be defined as the norms (codes) of conduct that distinguish between acceptable and unacceptable behaviour. Ethics refer to rules provided by an external source (e.g. codes of conduct at workplaces or codes of ethics provided by profes- sional associations). Industries, institutions and organisations have their specific codes of conduct, which define for their members what is acceptable and unaccept- able. The banking industry is no exception. As a result of the potential impact the banking sector has on the economy as a whole, ethics is probably more crucial in the banking sector than in any other industry. As mentioned previously, should the trustworthiness of banks become questionable, this may have a significant effect on the banking industry and hence the economy at large.
  The banking industry plays a major intermediation role in an economy by mobilising savings from surplus units and channelling these funds to deficit units for investment in the productive sectors. The key element to achieving this role is the trust bestowed on the banks by the customers. To keep banks functioning, there is a need to maintain, if not increase, this trust. This in turn can be ensured by clearly laying down norms for employees and management to abide by. Considering the growing complexities of the nature of the banking sector, codes of conduct in banking are critical. These codes of conduct define the acceptable way of behaviour in banking, promote high standards in banking practice and provide a benchmark for self-evaluation. Ethics in banking also establish a framework for professional behav- iour and responsibilities, apart from establishing credibility with the public. The application of principles from a code of ethics amplifies public trust and provides the bank with credibility.
  In addition, ethics are important in banking as they guide behaviour and improve or aid decision-making processes. Banks are constantly faced with different cus- tomers with different requirements, which may not all be above-board. Although codes of ethics cannot provide for every possible scenario, they provide a general idea of what is acceptable and can therefore be used in assessing clients and dealing with other stakeholders. In moments of conflict of interests, ethics help banks exercise good judgement and make decisions that will not discredit the profession or organisation.

15.2 Ethics in Banking	313

  Furthermore, ethics play a critical role in protecting the interests of depositors and other creditors. Depositors can be considered lenders to the banks since they provide the banks with funds for financing their activities. In a sense, they are also creditors. In developing countries with lower levels of financial literacy and financial inclu- sion, most creditors are not acquainted with banksÆ potentially risky activities and hence do not take precautions to protect themselves. More so, they tend to keep large portions of their income and other financial resources with banks. Ethical behaviour serves to protect them from risky activities since banks are bound to their codes of ethics. This prevents them from taking advantage of unsuspecting creditors or depositors.
Quick Quiz
1. What are ethics?
2. How can ethical behaviour improve banksÆ performance?
3. What is a code of conduct?
4. How can ethics improve decision-making?


15.2.1 International Codes of Ethics in Banking

Codes of ethics help define roles and responsibilities in various organisations. The codes do not address every ethical issue staff would face and therefore do not substitute for good judgement. However, codes provide a framework to actively consider the independence of every situation. They play a vital role in the industry by placing responsibility on individuals across the banking industry to consistently adhere to a high standard of professionalism and ethics in the conduct of their business. This, therefore, requires organisations to serve the best interest of their customers and clients in an open, clear and transparent manner. The code of ethics, according to the World Bank group, embraces:
Staff Relations An environment where staff members can work together in teams with openness and trust should be fostered in every organisation.
ò Diversity: this is when all staff members are able to contribute to their full potential. A positive, productive and motivating workplace encourages staff to bring a wide range of cultural and personal experience on board.
ò Fair treatment of colleagues: an organisation, which is committed to fair, equita- ble and impartial treatment of all staff, makes the workplace a harmonious place to be at. These conducts should be made clear by managers in their communica- tions and actions that would show that partial treatment or favouritism is unac- ceptable and will not be tolerated to prevent undue advantages and disadvantages.
Conflicts of Interest The interest of the organisation should be of topmost priority to every employee. Potential conflicts of interest are many and come in various forms. To be able to avoid them, one requires alertness and commitment.

314	15  Sustainable Banking

ò Gifts and gratuities: the worldÆs cultures have diverse customs concerning the giving and receiving of gifts. The organisation should try to train employees so as to avoid creating obligations in connection with official duties.
ò Hiring or advancement of relatives: the organisation should have employee rules whereby potential for misunderstandings as a result of hiring, advancement or promotion of relatives is clearly documented and adhered to all the time.
Financial Interests Disclosing personal or business activities or relationships with other organisations, suppliers, contractors, etc., is very important. This is to prevent any embarrassment that may reflect unfavourably on the organisation.
Political Activities An organisation should be committed to serving as responsible citizens and exercising its civic duties. Therefore, political activities outside of work that may compromise employeesÆ duties and responsibilities should be avoided.


15.2.1.1 The Fundamental Code of Ethics

The International Ethics Standard Board for Accountants (IESBA) provides that organisations and individuals across the financial services industry continuously uphold and abide by the ethical principles that are vital to the achievement of a high standard of professionalism and ethics across the industry. The IESBA ensures compliance with five basic principles of ethics including:
ò Integrity: Organisations and individuals across the financial services industry are to be honest and open in all their professional and business dealings. This includes behaving in an accountable and trustworthy manner, avoiding any acts that might damage the reputation or bring discredit to the industry at any time.
ò Objectivity: Organisations and individuals across the financial industry are not to allow bias, conflict of interest and undue influence to cloud business and profes- sional judgements. They are to declare to those concerned all matters that could impair their objectivity.
ò Confidentiality: Organisations and individuals are to protect the confidentiality and sensitivity of information provided to them. This is key, as banks have the personal details of their clients and customers alike. For example, banks and their employees must respect all confidential information acquired as a result of business or professional relationships and not disclosing any of such information without proper and specific authority to do so, using the information for its intended purposes and not divulging information to any unauthorised persons including third parties, without the necessary consent from those involved unless disclosure is required by law.
ò Professional behaviour: Organisations and individuals across the financial indus- try are to comply with relevant laws and regulations and to avoid anything that discredits the profession.

15.3 Sustainable Banking	315

ò Professional competence and due care: Organisations and individuals across the financial industry are to maintain professional knowledge and skill in the execu- tion of their duties and responsibilities.
ò Fairness: Firms as well as individuals operating within and across the financial sector must act fairly, transparently and responsibly. They should treat those with whom they have professional relationships with respect and ensure that they consider the impact of their decisions and actions towards all stakeholders.

15.3 Sustainable Banking

Sustainable banking or ethical banking is a system that focuses on environmental, social and corporate governance (ESG) conscious practices in banking. As banks try to earn profits, they try to do it in a way that is consistent with their banking policies and practices. In essence, sustainable or ethical banks incorporate ESG issues in their banking activities. Sustainable banking is a broad concept in banking that concerns anything from banks such as providing financing to encouraging affordable housing and financing the start-up costs for manufacturers. The key to sustainable banking is deciding on the common set of principles and applying them no matter what.


15.3.1 Sustainable Banking Activities

Sustainable banking permits the banking firm to select the types of policies they wish to engage in and the principles they are willing to pursue. In doing so, the principles and policies of the banking firm are coded in the form of policy documents that are available to the public and their clients. Some banking policies that are documented include:
ò Community involvement: The bank takes an active interest in its communityÆs welfare and well-being to improve the community in which it operates. Among other things, some banks engage in providing credit for building cheaper houses, giving scholarship to needy students, sponsoring community project and holding educational workshop to inform community members about the services of the bank.

316	15  Sustainable Banking

ò Sustainable practices: The banking firm applies environmental friendly practices, while supporting customers who practise, have or engage in environmental friendly policies.
ò Client screenings: The bank investigates its customer in order to avoid doing business with individuals, organisations and corporate entities with a history of unethical and immoral practices. For instance, it may avoid doing business with a company that has a history of using child labour.
ò Consistent internal and external ethics: The bank engages outsiders about its practices internally. This means that the bank uses the same ethical standards for both internal and external activities. For instance, if a bank refuses to deal with a business that does not offer health insurance to its employees, it should not refuse to offer health insurance to its own employees.


15.3.2 Ethical Themes in Banking

In our earlier discussion on fundamental code of ethics, we suggested that the first central issue of ethics in banking is integrity. Integrity helps banks in the develop- ment of their role as protectors of other peopleÆs money. Banks serve as intermedi- aries between the surplus units (savers/depositors) and the deficit units (borrowers). For the depositor, the financial intermediation provided by the bank yields benefits such as liquidity, diversification of risk and expertise in making lending decisions. The pooling of depositorsÆ funds means that many comparatively small savings can be used to make significant loans to industry. This process of mobilising funds brings great material benefits, but it is something that does not depend solely on simple commercial factors. It involves ethics too.
  Deposits are needed for the whole process to get started, and given the exposure or vulnerability of those who deposit their money with the bank, the most obvious door through which ethics enters banking is trust. Depositors are reliant upon not only the technical competence of the bank but also upon its ethical integrity. For example, if a person deposits money with the bank, he or she trusts the institution. The depositorÆs trust in the bank is one of the conditions of modern-day banking.


15.3.2.1 Lending with Responsibility

As banks need to act with integrity towards people who save or deposit their money with them, they need to engage in responsible lending. Banks are expected to lend funds considering the management of risk and return characteristics. These activities are the core function of banksÆ operations. As Cowton (2010) puts it, depositorsÆ interests are broadly aligned with those of shareholders. However, banks can be argued to have many further obligations, including obligations to stakeholders other than just depositors and shareholders. These wider concerns can be termed as æresponsibilityÆ, which indicates the possession of a degree of accountability for

15.3 Sustainable Banking	317

the consequences of oneÆs actions or inactions. Responsibilities may change in nature or emphasis over time. In providing credit to borrowers, banks would traditionally need to develop an appropriate balance between risk and return. How- ever, in attempting to achieve responsible lending, banks have an even more difficult balancing act to accomplish. We briefly discuss the three aspects of banksÆ responsibility.
  First of all, banks have the responsibility not to exclude certain groups. This means that banks have the responsibility to lend fairly. The difficulty associated with this is that banksÆ credit policies may sometimes adversely affect some groups. This might prevent these disadvantaged groups from participating in society in various desirable ways or drive them to what is termed æloan sharksÆ and their likes. Again, these groups (that is disadvantaged groups) may be defined in various ways to include income level or geographical locations. Therefore, lower-income individuals have less opportunity to access financial services. They cannot save or borrow money. It is important to remember that while banks that practise sustainable banking try to serve the disadvantaged, those are not the only services they provide. It is also important to note is that just because a bank is ethical does not mean that banks should not have a profit motive. Furthermore, small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) or start-ups might face problems, particularly if they operate in certain areas.
  Second, having extended credit, the banksÆ responsibility should not be too hasty to foreclose. Even though it is ethical to call a loan so as to preserve depositorsÆ funds as well as shareholdersÆ capital, banks should not be overzealous in seeking to safeguard their position. In other words, they should withdraw lending with some consideration for the consequences of other stakeholders too. The timing and manner of any withdrawal can be as important to the vitality of the small businesses as gaining credit in the first place. It can be even more important, because it is not only the firmÆs concern that is affected, but also its network of suppliers and customers and its employees and other business contacts; hence, the economic and social consequences are widespread.
  Third, as Morison (1995) argues, lending too much is potentially as serious a problem in the ethical sense as lending too little. There is a linkage with the second point, where if too much is lent then foreclosure is much more likely. Nevertheless, the two are worth distinguishing, for a bankÆs approach to foreclosure is not necessarily tied to its approach to lending. Borrowers clearly have a major respon- sibility in deciding how much to borrow, but so do banks, both in how they encourage borrowing and how they exercise their expertise in sanctioning loans. Banks should bear in mind that if they are taking security, they might suffer less than others if things go wrong. We should also note that certain parties or projects being financed by the bank could affect some stakeholders. For example, banks have in recent years been called upon to recognise the environmental impact of their lending (Cowton et al. 2000).

318	15  Sustainable Banking

15.3.2.2 Finance Through Affinity

Customers who have been attracted to the bank because of its stance on various issues are likely to have certain attitudes in common. The bank might have built ethical features not just into its service delivery but also into its products. There are other types of banks, which may seek to go even further in expressing through their product offering a particular set of values or beliefs. In most cases, the bankÆs operation will involve expressing ethical, religious or social values in financial manners. These various initiatives can be described as affinity finance. This term is appropriate for two reasons. First, in various fields it stands for relationship by choice, a mutual attraction or resemblance, and hence, it seems ideally suited for the coming together of like-minded parties around a particular values-based product offering. Second, it is already familiar in a financial context, referring to credit cards, which are associated with a particular organisation. The card is æbrandedÆ with the affinity organisationÆs name and logo, and the card issuer, usually based on initial take-up of the card and usage, makes payments to it. Many types of organisations, including professional bodies and football clubs, are involved, but among the most prominent beneficiaries of these schemes are charities (Cowton 2002).
  For instance, Islamic banking strives to build a society based on social justice, equity, moderation and balanced relationships (Haiwad 2008). As we have men- tioned in Chap. 2, Islamic banking activities seek to conform to Islamic law (Sharia) and so, for example, all dealings in gambling, pornography, alcohol and intoxicants are forbidden (Haram). The most striking feature of Islamic banking, which is the absence of interest (Riba), is substituted by the concept of profits and losses or risks sharing. Comprising both independent financial institutions and subsidiaries of conventional banks, the Islamic financial services sector is growing fast in both Islamic countries and non-Islamic countries, providing an opportunity for ordinary Muslims to ensure that their saving and borrowing activities do not compromise their faith. In England and Wales, for example, the system of Manzil Home Plans allows Muslim to finance property purchases in a manner which is lawful (Halal) for the Muslim.
  Schemes, which emphasise social rather than financial return, pose little threat to conventional banking. Though such schemes are growing significantly and are making a useful contribution, in terms of total finance, they are miniscule (Nienhaus 2011). We indicated that Islamic banking is very prevalent in North Africa, but it is less practised in Sub-Saharan Africa.
Quick Quiz
1. What is sustainable banking?
2. Briefly describe the core themes in bank lending.
3. What is Islamic banking?
4. Briefly explain finance through affinity.
5. Briefly describe client screening in relation to ethics in banking.

15.4 Social Responsibility in Banking	319

15.4 Social Responsibility in Banking

Social responsibility or corporate social responsibility (CSR) is an important issue in sustainable banking. Although there is no universal definition for CSR, one way of understanding it is to perceive it as a business outlook that acknowledges the business as a citizen with responsibilities to its stakeholders. It is a means by which businesses consider the interest of the various stakeholders and do their best to satisfy them. It seeks to further the good of society beyond the firmÆs interests and legal requirements. In recent times, businesses have become more conscious of the direct and indirect effects of their activities on the society and environment. To address this consciousness, they get involved in initiatives that, although may not contribute to profit, benefit society. Thus, CSR prioritises sustainability and the environment. Throughout this chapter, the term CSR in banking and responsible banking will be used interchangeably.
  As business organisations, banks are not excluded from the switch to more socially conscious business practices and active CSR initiatives. CSR implies that the responsibility of banks goes beyond profit maximisation and shows that banks have an obligation to other constituent organisations in society. The need for banks to be more socially responsible is critical as banks play a crucial role in allocating resources in the economy. Banks provide the funding that drives industrial pursuits and discoveries in science and technology. If they are not socially conscious, this might lead to over-exploitation of those less able to defend themselves and the environment. In this way, leaders have had to find a way to encourage businesses to pursue sustainability in their bid to create wealth. It is also necessary in building banksÆ æreputational capitalÆ. Society has grown to expect more from banks than wealth and job creation. In an age of increasing social consciousness, customers would rather conduct their business with banks that share the same ideals. Therefore, banks have to approach their CSR strategically.



15.4.1 Strategic Approaches to Social Responsibility

There are three strategic approaches to CSR activities. These are the business case for social responsibility approach (risk and cost minimisation strategy), public relation approach (to ensure legitimacy and to manage societal impressions about the organisation) and ethical approach (a sense of accountability).
  The business case suggests that strategic CSR yields substantial business-related benefits to the banking firm, in particular by supporting core business activities. This implies that being socially responsible can be mutually beneficial to the bank and its various stakeholders. In the view of the bank, it is posited that banks that are socially responsible will have a competitive advantage over banks that are not. CSR increases the firmÆs ability to attract, retain and motivate quality employees, enhances the reputation of the business, improves the firmÆs goodwill, fosters consumer loyalty,

320	15  Sustainable Banking

promotes sustainable supply chains and attracts investors. Additionally, generating social and environmental reports assists in improving internal control systems and decision-making and contributes to cost saving, which can improve financial per- formance of the bank. CSR can be used as a tool to curtail managerial opportunistic behaviours resulting in a reduction in monitoring costs, bonding costs, search costs, warranty costs and residual losses. It also results in the reduction in the cost of debts and equity capital. According to Post et al. (2002), æthe capacity of a firm to generate sustainable wealth over time, and hence its long-term value, is determined by its relationships with critical stakeholdersÆ and æany stakeholder relationship may be the most critical one at a particular time or on a particular issueÆ. These relationships are the essential assets that managers must manage, and they are the ultimate sources of organisational wealth. Thus, a better response to stakeholder needs by corporations is good for their long-term profitability. Given that investors are more willing to invest in socially responsible banks, a poor social responsibility can lead to sell-outs of the bankÆs shares by large investment funds, which can in turn negatively impact financial performance. CSR helps mitigate the risks associated with negative corpo- rate reputation such as negative press or customer/non-government organisation (NGO) boycotts and adverse political and regulatory risk. Galbreath (2006) refers to this as a reciprocal strategy where CSR strategy is tied to partnerships, such as between the firm and a specific community group or an NGO for the purpose of improving societal welfare and the firmÆs sales and/or reputation.
  The public relations approach to CSR arises when banks use CSR activities as a medium to divert stakeholder attention from activities that they engage in which may be unacceptable and will otherwise be punished by society should they get wind of it. Therefore, bank managers engage in social responsibility activities in order to guard against stakeholder scrutiny. Banks that engage in social responsibility tend to signal that they are good institutions, and this, in turn, draws attention away from activities they are engaged in that may be contrary to the expectations of society. In the words of Slack (2012), æCSR can be largely used as window dressing and serves a strategic purpose of mollifying public concerns about the inherently destructive nature of banking operationsÆ. The focus here is not to get social acceptance or attract good publicity as a result of being accountable, but rather to ward off bad publicity, which may arise from societal scrutiny. For instance, CSR can be used as a window dressing strategy to divert attention away from the bankÆs earnings management behaviour. Also, a bank may engage in CSR to distract environmentally conscious stakeholders from the fact that one of its biggest clients is a company clearing forests to build luxury apartments. The banking sector has been highly criticised for engaging in CSR that does not tackle the deleterious or harmful impact of their activities, but rather mask the very nature of the problems at hand (Luning 2012). Thus, it is argued that CSR is largely used as a propagandist campaign, which contradicts the arguments supporting CSR. This includes the view that there is a business case for embracing it, that banks are held to account for their actions and that it is important in securing a æsocial licence to operateÆ. In summary, a public relations tool is used to divert attention from activities which may be deemed as unacceptable or punishable by stakeholders. CSR, in such cases, stifles stakeholder

15.4 Social Responsibility in Banking	321

scrutiny. As a form of window dressing strategy, CSR appeals to peoplesÆ con- sciences and desires and it helps banks penetrate previously untapped customer bases.
  Finally, the ethical strategic approach to CSR focuses on the recognition and acceptance by the banking firm that it has a social contract with society, and it is accountable to society for its impact on the society and environments. This means that business and ethics are inseparable and that the slim wedge between business and ethics can be bridged by a concept of CSR. Here, CSR results from the shared beliefs and values within the organisation where it feels a sense of accountability and considers the need to strive for the interest of the total good of society in pursuit of its economic goals. Thus, the bank views CSR as morally mandatory and may not necessarily derive benefits from it. A bank must follow its ethical guidelines, no matter what (even in the face of forgone profit), since ethics is concerned with moral standards.
  These approaches to CSR or social responsibility and the increasing societal scepticism over the true intention banking operations have pressured businesses to find a more dependable measure of their impact on the society and the environment. In the light of this, the concept of the triple bottom line was developed.



15.4.2 The Concept of Triple Bottom Line

Customers have grown weary of seemingly shallow CSR reports and are more interested in the real impact of banking activities. Elkington (1998) in his bid to measure the performance of corporate America developed a new framework, which extended farther than the traditional methods of profit as a measure of performance to include social and environmental dimensions. This is known as the triple bottom line or the three æPsÆ, i.e. profit, people and the planet.
  Profit is an obvious motive for the banking business. The profit motive posits that the ultimate goal of a business is to make profit for its owner. Shareholders expect profit as a reward for the risks they have taken, and profitability helps a business to survive. Employee productivity is also driven by profit as more productivity leads to higher profits and therefore higher earnings for employees. Sometimes, however, in order to make more profitable goods, resources are used quickly without any thought about the future. This will result in scarcity of resources in the future. The profit motive is therefore seen as the cause of a lot of corporate misconduct or wrongdoing. With regard to people, the bank needs to be concerned about the interests of people as part of its social responsibility. Is the bank actually contributing positively to the well-being of customers and the society at large? Are employee and people policies fair and favourable? This also includes the bankÆs contribution to education, equity and access to social resources. Some variables to consider are the unemploy- ment rate, female labour force participation, violent crimes per capita, literacy and
health-adjusted life expectancy.

322	15  Sustainable Banking

  Additionally, banks are expected to be responsible in the way their operations affect the planet or environment. Activities should not limit the benefits future generations can get. The potential effect on the quality of air and water, energy consumption and natural resources should be considered. Specific areas to examine include sulphur dioxide concentration, concentration of nitrogen oxides, electricity consumption, fossil fuel consumption, waste management and changes in land use and cover.
  In conclusion, the triple bottom line has given performance measurement a facelift and allows organisations to apply the concept in a way that best suits them and meets their objectives. One challenge with the triple bottom line, however, is the difficulty that arises from trying to measure people and the planet, in terms of cash, the way profit is measured. Another challenge is the difficulty in obtaining data to measure a project or policyÆs contribution to sustainability. In spite of these, the framework gives banks the opportunity to examine the long-term consequences of their choices.
Quick Quiz
1. How do you explain CSR?
2. Briefly explain the public relation approach to CSR
3. What is the business case of CSR?
4. What is the difference between ethical banking and the ethical approach to CSR?
5. Briefly describe the concept of the ætriple bottom lineÆ.



15.4.3 Principal-Agent Relation in Banking

The principal-agent relationship is an arrangement where an entity legally appoints another to act on its behalf. The appointer is the principal while the appointee is the agent. The relationship is known as æagencyÆ and the law governing it is the law of agency. This is an area in commercial law dealing with a set of æfiduciary relation- shipsÆ involving an agent acting on the behalf of a principal to create legal relations with a third party. The terms of the principal-agent relationship specific to the parties involved are specified in a contract. In banking, the managers of the bank are the agent and the shareholders who are the owners of the bank are the principal. So, in carrying out duties, the agent must have no conflict of interest. If the agentÆs interests are not aligned with those of the principal and the principal has no way of properly monitoring the agent, the agent has the opportunity to act contrary to the principalÆs interest without detection. This is referred to as the æagency theoryÆ or the æprincipal- agent theoryÆ. This problem comes about when there are conflicting interests and information asymmetry.
  As discussed earlier, the principal are typically the shareholders or owners of the banks, while the directors and managers act as the agent. The banking industry is rife with principal-agent problems since the monitoring is difficult and risk transfer is

15.4 Social Responsibility in Banking	323

possible. Because of this, ethical standards must be high to prevent agents from being driven by their own interests and neglecting their fiduciary duty to their principal. Directors and management are presented with conflicts arising from the incentives of their compensation contracts and their duty to act in the interests of their shareholders. Directors and managers, who take risks on behalf of shareholders, benefit through their bonuses when the risks pay off. On the other hand, while shareholders benefit from risks taken, they also bear the brunt when the opposite happens. This was evident during the 2007/2008 financial crisis with traders receiv- ing substantial bonuses while their trading activities took down the same banks in which they were in employment. Additionally, since bonuses are tied to profit managers, they make decisions with annual profits in mind rather than the long- term life of the bank. This is known as æshort-termismÆ.
  In spite of the high levels of regulation in the banking industry, banks are still subject to agency costs. Banks are owned and managed by a separate group of people. This may result in managers not pulling their weight and doing the barest minimum to meet targets and receive their compensations. Decisions are made to favour the managers rather than increase bank ownersÆ wealth. The loss of value owing to managers maximising their utility rather than the value of the bank is known as æagency costÆ.


15.4.3.1 Criticisms of the Agency Theory

As with any theory or proposition, the agency theory is not without its critiques. It has been criticised as having a too narrow theoretical scope. Some critics point out that it is na´ve to study the complications and delicacies of a bankÆs corporate governance in terms of principal-agent contracts. They criticise the use of measures such as banksÆ board structure, directorÆs remuneration and compliance with corpo- rate governance principles, which are compared with the corporate performance of a bank. These critics believe that rather than contractual relationships, board behaviour is determined by interpersonal relationships between the bankÆs board members and political intrigue. Other criticisers have discovered that instead of increasing corpo- rate performance, increasing governance compliance may detract from it. Instead, boards that performed better have well-connected executive directors. Another criticism of the agency theory is that it is over-simplistic in practice. In reality, the directors may have invested shareholdersÆ funds in pension funds, which are invested in hedge funds, which invest in private equity firms and on and on it goes. In this case, it becomes unclear who the agents and principals are and how many there are. Finally, the agency theory assumes that the nature of a person is to look out only for him or herself. It assumes that a person does nothing out of altruistic motives and cannot seek the interest of others. This assumption defeats the whole purpose of appointing directors in the first place.
Quick Quiz
1. What is short-termism?

324	15  Sustainable Banking

2. Briefly explain the term æfiduciary relationshipÆ.
3. Briefly explain the termed æagency costÆ.
4. In a typical banking firm, who is the agent?
5. What are the criticisms of the agency theory?



15.5 Corporate Governance

Corporate governance is said to play an important role in addressing the agency problem in banking. It is basically the means by which businesses are run well in the interest of shareholders and the wider community and improve accountability to them. Corporate governance has become increasingly popular since the global financial crisis and the credit crunch that caused widespread recession in various economies. Corporate governance is the mechanism by which investors (especially outside investors) and other stakeholders, such as government and employees, to protect their interests, exercise control over management and other corporate insiders. The question here is: is the corporate governance of bank different from that of non-financial firms?Æ There are two aspects of banks that make them special and that have implications for their corporate governance: the capital structure of banks (the fact that banks are highly leveraged) and the regulation of bank opera- tions. In the case of the capital structure of banks, the high leverage of banks has two important corporate governance issues: the first issue is that excessive leverage can lead to risk taking if the bank owners become dominant in the governance of the bank. This is because the owners/shareholders hold residual claims over the bankÆs assets, and they may have incentives to increase the bankÆs risk. This is also in line with the issue of shareholdersÆ profit maximisation. The other issue, related to shareholdersÆ risk taking, is the issue of government and/or the regulators bailing out the distressed banks. This is what we term as ælender of last resortÆ. To avoid this, banks need a very strong corporate governance system.
  Corporate governance is also important in the banking sector as poor governance may lead to bankruptcy, which raises social costs due to the prominence of banks in the development of economies. In this regard, banks are subject to peculiar regula- tions and supervision to ensure that they are run fairly and with the benefit of all stakeholders in mind. Weaknesses in a bankÆs corporate governance structure in the form of excessive executive remuneration, inappropriate incentive structures, fail- ures in risk management and internal controls, lack of independence of board members and a deficiency of boardroom ethics typically result in its collapse. We have witnessed the collapse of many banks in Africa and even in the developed world due to corporate governance failures. In Ghana, for instance, the bankruptcy of Capital Bank and UT Bank were the result of failure of the board to take full responsibility of the operations of the banks. A number of developing countries

15.5 Corporate Governance	325

are beginning to introduce corporate governance codes and guidelines in a bid to improve corporate governance practices in banking.
  The board of directors constitute an important component of the corporate governance structure and are responsible for the control of banks, while shareholders play the role of appointing the directors and auditors.


15.5.1 Board of Directors

This is the primary level of supervision over a bank, and the boardÆs authority is derived from law and the bankÆs regulations. Good corporate governance in a bank is the responsibility of the board of directors. The board defines the bankÆs objectives, designs its strategy and leads its activities in accordance with applicable regulations. It is non-negotiable that directors must have high relevant qualifications and under- stand clearly the role of the board of directors to the bank to enable them play that part effectively and efficiently. Thus, the effectiveness in carrying out their duties depends on its members, their expertise and their independence. Directors who have no link with the bank are better able to exercise independence in their decision- making. Directors who are executives of the bank and those with other links, such as close family ties to the chairman, representatives of dominant shareholders, past executives and so on, raise questions concerning their independence. In theory, the independence of a board depends on the types and functions of the directors as well as their relationship with the management.


15.5.1.1 Types of Directors

There are two types of directors: executive directors (ED) and non-executive direc- tors (NED). The executive directors are those directors who hold managerial posi- tions in addition to their designation as board members. They are sometimes referred to as inside directors. They are employees of the bank and therefore covered by employment law. On the other hand, as directors, they are also subject to the company law. One danger in connection to this arrangement is that the managers, who report to the CEO, may have great difficulty in disagreeing with their superior boss. Conversely, the NEDs are those directors who may be executives of other companies and serving on the board of the bank. They hold no managerial position in the bank on whose board they serve. NEDs are also known as outside directors. Among NEDs, a further distinction can be made between those who are completely independent of the bank and those who have some link to it in the form of close relationship to the board chairman, being past executives or employees, having links with major trading partners and so on. Inside directors help outside directors obtain a better understanding of management and gain broader exposure to what happens inside the bank. The board, however, is perceived to be independent, when outside directors outnumber inside directors.

326	15  Sustainable Banking

  In discussing the types of board members, we cannot ignore the structure of the board. One board structure is the unitary board. Here, the bank has one governing body, which may consist exclusively of executive directors, or consist of a majority of executive directors, or the majority being non-executive directors or the majority being exclusively non-executive directors. These structures may arise according to the size and needs of the bank. There are situations where the same person plays the role of board chairman and CEO (CEO duality). Typically, in the USA, the executive directors consist of CEO (also the chairman), the chief financial officer (CFO) and the chief operating officer (COO). Such a board may or may not have non-executive directors. This is usually due to an underlying fear that a strong separate chairman may rival the CEO and undermine his or her authority. Conversely, others are of the opinion that this creates a conflict of interest, for instance, where the board chairman is required to evaluate the CEOÆs performance.
  Another board structure is the two-tier board. This consists of a supervisory board, which is made up of only non-executive directors, and a management board, which is made up of executive directors. In practice, the executive directors,
i.e. the management board members, attend the meetings of the supervisory board but have no vote. They present their strategies, management plans and budgets for comments and approval. The supervisory board has the power to remove and appoint members of the management board.


15.5.1.2 Functions of Board of Directors

The functions of board of directors differ from one country to another. However, the roles and responsibilities of a board can be broadly summarised by the managerial duties that may be prescribed for directors. The major duties of directors are:
The Fiduciary Duty This is a duty of trust. Directors are supposed to act with integrity and honesty to benefit shareholders and the wider society. They should act so as to promote the aims of the bank to ensure its success and act strictly within their authority as per its constitution. Directors are primarily supposed to act in utmost good faith to strengthen the economic efforts and values of the bank for the benefit of all. They should not behave in a way that portrays them as the sole beneficiaries, even if they own all the shares of the bank. They have to also recognise and consider the rights of other stakeholders, such as their creditors. They must avoid conflict of interest and not make any secret profit or benefit in any way as a result of their directorial position. It is unrealistic, however, to assume that they always represent shareholdersÆ best interests, as some board members are naturally utility maximisers.
The Duty of Care This is a call for directors to exercise their independent judge- ment with care, skill and diligence. The expectation is for directors to behave as any reasonable person would. This duty recognises the knowledge, skills and experi- ences of the director and gives the opportunity for the director to bring these to bear in carrying out his or her work. Additionally, directors are allowed to delegate some functions to and rely on the work and skills of others such as employees, officers and

15.5 Corporate Governance	327

experts in meeting the objectives of the bank. This, however, does not negate their oversight responsibility.


15.5.1.3 Board Committees

A board of a bank usually creates committees through which it conducts business in order for independent directors to fulfil their oversight roles and to delegate activ- ities, thereby reducing the burden on the board as a whole. These committees are created as standing committees through board policies, which contain formal refer- ences that are regularly reviewed and amended as necessary. Some issues are too complex to be dealt with by the entire board and may therefore take too much time to resolve. Having committees with the relevant expertise in some of these issues increases board efficiency, since a committee can deliberate the issue and make recommendations to the rest of the board. Most boards worldwide have these standing committees in some form.
The Committee of Outside Directors As the name suggests, this committee com- prises the non-executive directors of the bank, and its chairperson is elected by the other directors. In an ideal situation, this committee should receive support and offer advice provided by the office of the CEO and the bankÆs general counsel to staff. This is especially necessary when the issues have legal implications.
Audit Committee The current form of audit committee most banks have originated in the USA. It is now considered a best practice for banks to have audit committees. It is a standing committee comprising outside directors and was established to bridge the gap between the external auditor and the board. The committee is required to be independent in that not only must members not be in management, but they should also not have close ties with management or should not have been part of manage- ment within the last 5 years. This committee is assisted by the CFO and senior accounting managers and has authority to work with the external auditors, senior management, the finance function and the internal audit function. High-profile corporate scandals such as those of Enron, WorldCom and Parmalat, to name a few, have contributed to the increasing importance of audit committees. This is because they work closely with the external auditors and meet with them quarterly to ensure the accuracy of the financial statements and of proper reporting to the outside world.
  In recent times, the audit committee has taken on an oversight role with regard to financial controls, compliance with laws, regulations and the corporate code of conduct and is in addition conducting special investigations. A key area of concern for audit committees is risk management. This involves understanding all the risks that a company faces and determining how best to manage them. It is worthy of note, however, that some banks have separate risk committees since insufficient supervi- sion of this area is the most prominent flaw in the governance of banks.

328	15  Sustainable Banking

15.5.1.4 Nominating and Governance Committee

To prevent the board of directors from becoming an æold boysÆ clubÆ, codes of good practice in corporate governance recommend a nomination committee to identify and recruit new members to the board when openings arise. This keeps the board from selecting only like-minded people with similar backgrounds to the team. A form of checks and balance is thus created to reduce the likelihood of a dominant director, the chairman or CEO, in particular, pushing his or her own favoured candidates, who will support every decision without question. To achieve this, the committee will have to be composed wholly or mainly of independent directors who will have the mandate to nominate individuals for election as directors. These nominees are then elected at the shareholdersÆ annual general meeting. The com- mittee is also responsible for evaluating management and appraising the adequacy of management compensation.
  One setback of the nomination committee is that it does not resolve an underlying impasse. On one hand, the committee can nominate independent, qualified individ- uals to the board and avoid accusations of partiality or æold boyismÆ. On the other hand, to be an effective and focused team, the board must be able to work together. The chairman should know, respect and be able to cooperate with the other directors. In this vein, having directors æimposedÆ on him or her by the nomination committee could be divisive. Furthermore, the nomination committee may feel loyalty to the chairman or CEO who nominated them. This may cloud their independence.
Compensation Committee Consists wholly or mainly of independent directors, charged with the responsibility of deciding on remuneration and benefit packages for top executives and directors. This committee, like the others, derives its authority from its formal terms of reference, relevant laws and corporate governance codes. The committee receives help from the senior human resource officer and may employ the services of external compensation advisors. It ensures that the system of remuneration of top bank executives is in accordance with the corporate objec- tives of the bank. This is of particular importance as managersÆ remuneration packages may impact on the level of risk they are willing to take. An example is where bonuses are tied to annual profits. Certain banks charge the remuneration committee with the management of retirement investment in addition to their traditional roles. If this is not the case, this responsibility may be delegated to a separate committee of directors with investment experience.


15.5.1.5 Board Meetings

Board meetings are important for providing the board with the opportunity to periodically assess the performance of the bank, review its processes and provide the needed direction. In most corporate cultures, board meetings are viewed with some awe, and banks are not different. Outsiders think of board meetings and paint a picture of an omnipotent and omniscient group making profound decisions

15.6 Summary and Conclusions	329

surrounded by a cloud of mystery. They eagerly wait at the edge of their seats to see the white smoke coming from the chimney foretelling some great pronouncement. The fact is that the happenings in boardrooms rarely justify this kind of reverence. After all, directors are fallible human beings trying to communicate and influence each other as they wrestle with issues that are sometimes quite complex. There is a potential for board meetings to be chaotic and complicated, especially when there are difficult questions to address, compounded by conflicting views and interests. As with any deliberative body, passion can and sometimes does override reason. However, the board does perform some routine activities during meetings.
  The most likely barrier to conducting an effective board meeting is the basic asymmetry in the amount of information available to members and management versus non-management board members. When a considerable disparity occurs, which happens frequently, discussions become one-sided. This situation persists unless an effort is made to close this gap and provide all the directors with equal amounts of information.
  Banks have operational periods such as the end of every quarter when the board typically meets. This schedule is not cast in stone and the board may meet more often should they wish to. Furthermore, special meetings may be called to deal with unique situations. Many boards have a regular cycle during which they deal with planning topics. It is the norm to approve of the annual budget at the beginning of the fiscal year. Longer-term plans will be reviewed before the budget is considered, in order to have a more comprehensive deliberation of the budget. The board may use the first half of the year to assess and possibly refine the bankÆs strategy as necessary. Having a regular cycle allows the board to partake in the bankÆs strategic manage- ment process. The boardÆs subcommittees also have a calendar they follow.
Quick Quiz
1. What is corporate governance in a bank?
2. Briefly explain the termed æthe duty of careÆ as used in corporate governance.
3. What are the different types of board of directors?
4. Briefly discuss the essence of a board meeting.
5. Briefly explain the functions of the audit and nomination committees of a board.

15.6 Summary and Conclusions

This chapter discusses sustainable banking by focusing on ethics and the need for ethics in banking. The chapter also looked at sustainable banking, CSR in banking as well as corporate governance in banking. Specifically, we have mentioned that:

330	15  Sustainable Banking

1. The activities of banks are critical for economic development and the past financial crisis demonstrated the devastating impact the banking sector could have on a country and indeed, the world, if performance plunges.
2. The crisis resulted in loss of jobs, and the banking sector has taken years to recover. This has led to heightened sensitivity on the part of banks to incorporate environmental, social and governance criteria into traditional banking. As busi- ness organisations, banks are not excluded from the switch to more socially conscious business practices and active sustainable operations.
3. Sustainable banking implies that the responsibility of banks goes beyond profit maximisation and shows that banks have an obligation to other constituent organisations in society. Banks must, therefore, use money and be mindful of the environmental, cultural and social impacts, and with the support of all, including the depositors and investors who want to make a difference. This they will do to meet the present-day needs without compromising those of future generations.
4. Sustainable banking permits the banking firm to select the types of policies they wish to engage in and those of the principles they are willing to pursue. In doing so, the principles and policies of the banking firm are coded in the form of policy documents that are available to the public and their clients. Some banking policies that are documented include community involvement, sustainable practices, client screenings and consistent internal and external ethics.
5. The need for banks to be more socially responsible is critical as banks play a crucial role in allocating resources in the economy. It is also necessary in building the bankÆs æreputational capitalÆ. Society has grown to expect more from banks than wealth and job creation. In an age of increasing social consciousness, customers would rather conduct their business with banks that share their ideals. Therefore, the bank has to approach its CSR strategically.
6. There are three strategic approaches to CSR activities, and these are: the business case for social responsibility approach (risk and cost minimisation strategy), public relation approach (to ensure legitimacy and to manage societal impressions about the organisation) and ethical approach (a sense of accountability).
7. Banks need to be well governed to ensure proper adherence to their norms. Corporate governance is especially important in the banking sector, as poor governance may lead to bankruptcy which raises social costs due to the promi- nence of banks in the development of economies.

This has been heightened by the downturn in most economies during the 2007/2008 financial crisis. In the light of this, construct a case for ethics in banking.
