Chapter 16
Central Banking and Monetary Policy











Learning Objectives
By the end of this unit, you should be able to:
ò Identify the structure of central banks
ò Examine whether central banks should be independent
ò Discuss the role of central banks
ò Explain how monetary policy is conducted and the tools of monetary policy
ò Understand the money supply process
ò Identify the factors that determine money supply
ò Identify the strategies used in monetary policy
ò Discuss inflation targeting


16.1 Introduction

A central bank, sometimes referred to as reserve bank or monetary authority, is an institution that sits at the apex of the banking system with the mandate of managing the money supply, the interest rates and the currency of a country. Central banks also act as lender of last resort to banks. In most countries, they are also in charge of supervision and regulation to ensure financial soundness and stability of the banking system (Goodhart et al. 2013). The main responsibility of the central bank in many economies is price stability, which is attained by managing inflation towards a set target.
  Central banks are important institutions not just to banks but also to the society as a whole. Their activities affect interest rates, credit availability and money supply, which in turn influence output and inflation in the economy. Apart from these, central banks oversee the activities of banks and ensure that banks do not take excessive risks that will be detrimental to the society. More insight on the role of the central bank will require an understanding of the central bank itself.


® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_16

335

336	16  Central Banking and Monetary Policy

  In this chapter, we examine the general structure of central banks and discuss the arguments in favour and against their independence. We then move on to discuss the role central banks play in developing countries. A key focus in this chapter is the conduct of monetary policy, which is undoubtedly a major role of central banks.



16.2 General Structure of the Central Bank

Central banks, like any other institutions, have a board of directors who are respon- sible for determining the policies of the central bank. The board is usually made up of the Governor, who is normally the chairman, the Deputy Governor, a secretary who is a non-voting member and some other non-executive directors. In many countries, the president appoints the governor. The governor and the other members are appointed for fixed terms. So long as the governorÆs term has not ended, he or she remains a member of the board of directors. The other members, on the other hand, have the opportunity to be reappointed for another term, but the term of office differs from one country to another. In countries where the central bank is independent, there exists a monetary policy committee that is responsible for the conduct of monetary policy. There are also heads of departments who are responsible for various units in the central bank.



16.3 Independence of the Central Bank

The fact that in many countries the president appoints the governor brings to question how independent the central bank is. As most developing countries are now gradually moving away from autocratic rule, activities of the central bank are not completely free of the interference from government. Consequently, central banks in these countries do not have the freedom to pursue policies that will be in the long-term interest of the public. This has sparked a debate on whether central banks should be independent or not. With this debate having persisted for quite some time now, the main question that has been on the table has been whether politicians should have a say in the conduct of monetary policy or whether technocrats from the central bank should have the freedom to conduct their own monetary policy.
  Currently, most countries have given some degree of freedom to their central bank to carry out monetary policy. For example, KenyaÆs Central Bank has seen greater autonomy since the amendment of the Central Bank of Kenya Act in April 1997. Although the Central Bank of Kenya is supposed to provide support to the general economic policies of the government, it needs not subject itself to the powers of the government.
  The independence of the central bank can take the following two forms (see Mishkin 2013).

16.3 Independence of the Central Bank	337

1. Instrument Independence: This is when the central bank can have the freedom to choose whatever instrument it wants to use for monetary policy without any interference by the politicians.
2. Goal Independence: This is when the central bank has the freedom to decide on the goal of monetary policy.
  The extent of freedom varies from one country to another. Most countries have instrument independence but do not have goal independence. Only a few countries, like the USA, have both. Policymakers and people in academia have provided arguments both in favour and against a central bankÆs independence. We discuss the various arguments in the next section.



16.3.1 Arguments in Favour of Independence

Politicians often concentrate on winning votes and would therefore like to pursue policies that will impress voters. From the perspective of macroeconomic policy, a trade-off exists between low unemployment and inflation in the short run; that is, reducing unemployment will mean sacrificing price stability. Since the populace will be happier with a reduction in unemployment rate than they would with low inflation, politicians would like to reduce the unemployment rate, even though this may be at the expense of inflation in future. A central bank that separates its activities from the politicians will take decisions that will be useful over the long run even if the economy will suffer in the short run; that is, independent central banks will choose low inflation over unemployment in this circumstance.
  An argument close to the one raised above has to do with the political business cycles that occur before and after every election if politicians are allowed to interfere in central banksÆ activities. In most developing countries, voters base their decisions on the things they observe close to elections. Politicians observing this trend will capitalise on this and expand the economy close to elections. This will lead to high inflation. They will then tighten the economy just after elections and hope that the public will forget this before the next election. Little progress will therefore occur in the economy because of this instability in economic policies if politicians are allowed to interfere in central banksÆ activities.
  Further, it is also argued that government will push the central bank to finance its fiscal deficits if the central bank is under its control. This the government can do by making the central bank purchase its securities. The end results of this will be high inflation in the economy. An independent central bank may not bow to this.
  Politicians have consistently demonstrated that they lack the political will to pursue policies that will inflict pain on the public in the short term but bring tremendous benefits to the public in the long term, as this may cost them their votes. As such, another argument raised in favour of independence sees central bank activities as too important to be left to politicians.

338	16  Central Banking and Monetary Policy

  Finally, some politicians may like to give independence to the central bank to ease off some of the pressure on them. Since politicians are blamed for everything that goes wrong in the economy, having another institution that takes part of the blame is always useful. The politician can always ride on the back of an independent central bank to pursue the good and longer-term policies that it would otherwise not have been able to pursue without an independent central bank.


16.3.2 Arguments Against Independence

Those who advocate against the central bankÆs independence believe that leaving all activities of the central bank to a tiny elite group in the society is very undemocratic. To them, because central bank officials are not elected, they are not accountable to the public and cannot be held responsible for any wrong decisions that are made.
  Second, in many developing countries, because the majority of the populace is not well-informed, government gets blamed for everything that goes wrong in the economy. The institutions that are supposed to ensure that the activities under them are properly coordinated do not take any responsibility for any mishap. A strong argument raised against the independence of the central bank has been that if government is going to be blamed for everything that goes wrong, they should also have a say in the activities of all institutions, including the central bank.
  Further, others have argued that central banks have abused their powers when they were allowed to operate freely without interference and as such their activities need to be regulated. Closely related to this argument is the fact that central banks have been accused of taking up goals that serve their own interests and not the interests of the public. Giving them independent status will make them pursue their self-interest and not the public interest.
  Up until now, the debate on whether central banks should be made independent or not is still ongoing. It turns out that the preference of people for one or the other depends on how they like the central bank.
Quick Quiz
1. Should central banks be independent?
2. What are the arguments for the central bankÆs independence?
3. What is the structure of central banks?



16.4 Functions of the Central Bank

Central banks conduct monetary policy, issue currency, manage payment systems, serve as lenders of last resort, manage currency exchange rates and provide depositor insurance, among other roles. It is important to note that apart from the conduct of

16.4 Functions of the Central Bank	339

monetary policy, which is common to all central banks in every economy, not all the other functions are performed by each central bank. Many countries establish other institutions to perform some of the tasks outlined here and allow the central bank to focus on a few tasks. For instance, while the central bank in Ghana is responsible for bank supervision and monetary policy, currently in South Africa, the Prudential Authority, which operates within the Southern African Reserve Bank, is in charge of regulating and supervising banks and other financial firms, similar to what the Prudential Regulation Authority of the Bank of England does in the UK. The Southern African Reserve Bank and the Bank of England are mainly responsible for monetary policy conduct and other functions such as issuing currencies, maintaining financial stability and facilitating payment and settlements.
  We discuss the functions of the central bank and examine how one important function of the central bank, which is the conduct of monetary policy, influences the economy.


16.4.1 Bank Supervision

Banks probably borrow more than any institution. The deposits they receive together with the money they borrow from the wholesale markets far exceed the amount their shareholders put into the institutions. A substantial portion of what is raised from all these units is usually lent out. The danger with this is threefold: One is that many depositors would want to have access to their funds immediately they turn up at the banks, without recognising that the banks could have invested their cash in illiquid assets. The second problem is that the bankÆs assets go down while at the same time their liabilities remain the same or even increase, which may result in insolvency, as assets may now be less than liabilities. The two issues just raised point out that banks are prone to both liquidity and solvency risks. Central banks are the institutions responsible for performing oversight activities to limit both liquidity and solvency risk. The third is that individuals who set up banks may be overambitious people, who may like to make huge profits through their speculative activities from depos- itors. This means that they would like to take excessive risks, which may hurt depositors.
  Therefore, an authority should be responsible for vetting them before providing them with licences to ensure that the right people establish banking businesses. What this means is that once new proposals are submitted, they are carefully scrutinised to check their authenticity and the credibility of the individuals who submitted the proposals. The process does not end here; the regulator still monitors their activities once the approval for their establishment is given. It also demands regular reports and frequent visits to the banks to ensure that they are abiding by the necessary regulatory prescriptions. The regulatory authority also ensures they abide by the regulatory requirements. Apart from these, the central bank may consider some consumer protection functions such as whether the bankÆs electronic system is safe from cyber attacks.

340	16  Central Banking and Monetary Policy

16.4.2 Depositor Insurance

When the regulatory role of the central bank, discussed above, fails or if central bank management fails to perform its role very well, a problem arises. Individuals will form long queues at the banks to withdraw their funds, leading to a bank run. The bank with this problem might have carried out some transactions with other banks and may therefore be compelled to curtail those transactions abruptly just because it is under pressure from its depositors. This may cause liquidity problems in other banks too. A run on one bank is bad and the problem may even be worse if the bank run extends to other banks. A way to reduce the risk that banks face is for either the government or a designated body to guarantee that it will repay depositors even if the banks fail to do so. This is the deposit insurance role that some central banks play. Up until now, there is no institution in any African country that guarantees for banks. However, in both the USA and the UK, institutions outside their respective central banks are responsible for insuring deposits. For instance, in the UK, the Financial Services Compensation Scheme compensates depositors up to a maximum of ú 85,000, whereas in the USA, the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation (FDIC) pays up to US$250,000 as compensation to each depositor in an insured bank (https://www.fdic.gov).
  Although having a deposit insurance scheme in existence may improve the confidence depositors have in the financial system, it may also lead to moral hazard issues on the part of the banks. Banks may take huge risks that may fetch them substantial gains when they are successful, but pass on the risk to the central bank when they are unsuccessful. Most countries in Africa do not have the deposit insurance scheme in place because moral hazard issues are still more pronounced than in other regions of the world.



16.4.3 Lender of Last Resort

Another function the central bank performs is serving as lender of last resort to the banks. The central bank usually provides banks with financial support when all other sources of funds for banks fail them. For instance, when the terrorists attacked the World Trade Centre in America in September 2011, many banks could not have access to their cheques. Consequently, the reserves of banks declined. The Federal Reserve supported the banking system through repurchase agreements and discount lending. Also, during the recent financial crises, banks could not tell to which level other banks had been affected by the crises. Following this, they held on to their reserves and hesitated in providing funds to other banks. The Federal Reserve had to step in to provide short-term funds for the banks.
  Interestingly, the lender of last resort function does not provide automatic assis- tance to all banks. Whether a bank will be bailed out or not depends on what led the bank into poor liquidity and solvency position in the first place and also whether or not the bank is too big to fail. As much as this function of the central bank is

16.4 Functions of the Central Bank	341

important, as the lender of last resort and the depositor insurance functions, it also has the danger of creating moral hazard problems, as many banks are likely to engage in excessive risk taking because they may be bailed out. To curb this, most central banks bail out banks whose transactions with other banks may have knock-on effects on other banks. For instance, in the mid-2017, the Central Bank of Ghana did not bail out two banks (Capital Bank and UT Bank) that failed because their trans- actions were not large enough to have serious consequences on GhanaÆs economy. Rather, another commercial bank (Ghana Commercial Bank) acquired the banks through a purchase and assumption deal.


16.4.4 GovernmentÆs Bank and Management of National Debt

The central bank may also act as the governmentÆs bank. By this, we mean the central bank is responsible for carrying out transactions for the government. It receives revenue, for example, taxes, and also makes payments on behalf of the government, for example the payment of government contractors. In some countries, the central bank is responsible for managing the national debt. It raises money for the government through the selling of government bonds. In Ghana, for instance, though the Debt Management Office exists at the Ministry of Finance and is responsible for carrying out this function, yet, we often see the central bank performing an aspect of this role.


16.4.5 Currency Issue

Some central banks are responsible for issuing currency (coins and bank notes). Currency is issued to commercial banks as and when they make a request with the central bank. However, these commercial banks are supposed to give up an equi- valent amount in return at the central bank. Like the debt management function, this role does not cut across all central banks. In some countries, other institutions are responsible for the issuance of currency.


16.4.6 Conduct of Monetary Policy

This is perhaps the most important function of the central bank as it cuts across all central banks. Whether interest rate is too high or too low, it has consequences for the health of the economy. For instance, a very low interest rate can cause inflation resulting in uncertainty in economic activity. A high interest rate, on the other hand, deters borrowing and affects growth in the economy. This means that every

342	16  Central Banking and Monetary Policy

economy needs an institution that will ensure that, at any point in time, the interest rate that exists in the economy is neither too low nor too high. The central bank is the institution responsible for performing this function. It performs this function through its monetary policy. Monetary policy refers to the use of interest rate and money supply to affect the economy. Monetary policy can be expansionary or contraction- ary. When interest rates are too low, causing the economy to heat up excessively, the central bank embarks on a contractionary or restrictive monetary policy to slow the economy down. When interest rates are too high, causing recessions, the central bank embarks on an expansionary monetary policy to put more money in the economy (Arnold 2014). The end result of monetary policy depends on three main actors: the central bank, depository institutions and the non-bank public.
  Monetary policy is conducted to achieve certain goals. These goals include price stability, stable exchange rate, low unemployment, high growth, etc. Unfortunately, central banks cannot directly control these variables. Instead, they have certain instruments they can work through to achieve these objectives. The instruments have the potential of affecting certain indicators (operational targets) like the mon- etary base and the short-run interest rates, which will then affect other variables called targets (intermediate targets), such as money supply and interest rate, and will consequently affect the ultimate goals. We summarise the relationship just established in the schematic diagram below (Fig. 16.1).
  The main question that arises, therefore, is how these targets are arrived at. The targets that are usually chosen should satisfy three criteria: they should be measur- able, controllable and be able to predict the goals with precision. This means that for a target variable to be useful, three things must occur: first, it has to signal quickly that a policy is off track; second, the central bank must have a substantial amount of control over it; and third, the target variable must also have the ability to impact the goal.
  Regarding the goals of monetary policy, different countries may have different goals. Some may have just one goal, while others may have multiple goals. The most important thing to note here is that attempting to achieve more than one goal may lead to incompatibility among the goals. For example, in trying to achieve the price stability goal, a country may find itself doing this at the expense of employment.
  Next, we try to understand how the central bank uses its tools to influence the operational targets for it to eventually affect its intermediate target and ultimately its goals. We start by examining the balance sheet of the central bank. On every balance sheet, there is the asset side and the liability side. To the central bank, reserves and currency in circulation are liabilities to the bank, while securities and loans to financial institutions are assets to the central bank. We discuss each of them and show why they constitute assets or liabilities. It is important to note that assets and liabilities of central banks are not just limited to these. There could be many more of these, but these are the ones that are relevant to the monetary policy process.

Instruments  Operational Targets  Intermediate Targets  Goals

Fig. 16.1 Conduit of monetary policy

16.4 Functions of the Central Bank	343

Assets
Assets of the central bank include loans that have been given to financial institutions and securities that have been bought.
Loans to Financial Institutions Loans that are given to financial institutions by the central bank are assets to the central bank. Remember the central bank is the lender of last resort to the banks. If the banks take loans from the central bank, it represents an asset to the central bank and a liability to the bank.
Purchase of Securities Another form of asset by the central bank is securities, which central banks buy from the government or from other institutions.
  It is the change in these assets that can cause changes in the liabilities (which we will discuss next) and lead to changes in the money supply.
Liabilities
The liabilities of the central bank include reserves and currency in circulation.
Reserves Central banks, in performing their supervisory function, require banks to keep a certain proportion of their deposits as security. This is called required reserve or reserve requirement. What is left out of the total deposits after required reserves and what the banks have used up is what is known as excess reserves. A combination of the excess reserves and the required reserves gives total reserves. These reserves belong to the banks and are therefore liabilities for the central bank.
Currency in Circulation This represents currency in the hands of the public. It includes notes and coins in the hands of the public. The idea that notes and coins are liabilities to the central bank comes from the goldsmith days when receipts were given to people who had given their gold to the goldsmith for safekeeping and upon making purchases would present those receipts to the sellers. The sellers then would have to present the receipts to the goldsmiths for their money. The central bank, in this case, acts like the goldsmith. Once it issues the notes and coins, anytime anyone presents that to the central bank, it is obliged to give something back to that individual. This time however, unlike what the goldsmiths would have done, that is, to give gold back to the presenter of the receipt, the central bank will give the equivalent of that amount in several denominations.
A combination of reserves and currency in circulation is what is known as the
monetary base or high powered money. This can be expressed as:
MB = C + R
where MB = monetary base, C = currency in circulation and R = Reserves
The central bank is the only institution that can change the monetary base. It
changes the monetary base by causing changes in its assets. Changes in the monetary base can influence the size of money supply through changes in the assets of the central bank. It does this by using one of its tools of operations. We show later in this chapter how this can be done using one of its tools. This makes a discussion on the tools of monetary policy imperative at this point.

344	16  Central Banking and Monetary Policy

16.5 Tools of Monetary Policy

Central banks have three main tools they employ in their conduct of monetary policy:
1. Open market operations
2. Discount rate changes
3. Reserve requirement ratio


16.5.1 Open Market Operations

Open market operations (OMO) involve the purchase and the sale of government securities by the central bank. It is currently the most important tool of monetary policy in many countries. When the central bank wants to reduce the amount of money in circulation, it sells government securities to the commercial banks and the general public. When this happens, the commercial banks and the general public will pay for these securities, thus causing a lowering of reserves. When it wants to increase the amount of money in circulation, it buys securities from the commercial banks and the general public. The central bank pays either by cheque or by cash. This increases the reserves of the commercial banks and thus helps to create money.
  To illustrate how central banks can use the open market operations to increase or lower reserves, let us look at the table below. Supposing the central bank wants to put more money in the economy and therefore lowers interest rates, it can now offer to buy say US$1000 worth of government securities from the public. The central bank can either pay by cash or by cheque. If it pays by cash and the public deposits the money into the commercial bank, the reserves of the commercial bank increase by US$1000. If it pays by cheque, the reserves still increase by US$1000 (Table 16.1).
  Remember we mentioned earlier that reserves do not earn any interest. Hence, once Bank A sees an increase in its reserves, it would want to do some lending, and this increases the amount of money in the financial system. If the central bank wants to reduce the amount of money in the system, it will rather sell government securities, which will reduce the amount of money held on the tills and in the vaults, and this will in turn reduce lending.
  The main method used in the open market operation is a repurchase agreement, often called repo. If the central bank wants to put more money into the economy, it can purchase government securities with an initial agreement to sell the securities back to the general public in say a day, a week or 2 weeks. The difference between the selling and the buying price gives the effective interest rate. If, on the other hand,

Table 16.1 Bank balance sheet

16.5 Tools of Monetary Policy	345

the central bank wants to reduce the amount of money in the economy, it will sell securities to the general public and have a prior agreement of buying it back on an agreed date. This method is known as reverse repo. In many other nations, trans- actions in the interbank markets are also used to either increase or lower reserves in the economy. While repos and reverse repos are temporary measures because the opposite transaction is undertaken after a few days, a downright sale or purchase can also be done by the central bank if it wants to effect a more permanent change. Government securities are often used to conduct open market operations. This is because of the low risk attached to government securities; thus, their demand tends to be relatively high. This increases their liquidity. Also, government securities are able to absorb large volumes of transaction without causing excessive fluctuations in their prices compared to other securities.
  How then do open market operations, as a tool of monetary policy, affect reserves/interest rate/monetary base (which is an operational target)? We explain this below using demand and supply of reserves.



16.5.2 The Supply and Demand for Reserves

Banks demand less reserves if interest rates rise. This is because when interest rates are high, banks stand a better chance of making more profit if they lend out their reserves. This means that the opportunity cost of banks holding reserves rises when interest rate increases. Hence, the demand for reserves has a negative relationship with interest rate.
  The supply of reserves is the amount of reserves borrowed from the central bank, and this is considered fixed at any point in time (Fig. 16.2).
  When the demand for reserves equals the supply of reserves, equilibrium is achieved. This leads to the determination of an equilibrium interest rate. Supposing the central bank wants to embark on an expansionary monetary policy, it buys securities and therefore increases the quantity of reserves (Fig. 16.3).
  This shifts the supply of reserves curve to the right and reduces the equilibrium interest rate. The reverse will be true if the central bank wanted to reduce its reserves and sold government securities. The action that has just been described refers to a dynamic open market operation. Under this method, the central bank initiates an action that changes the level of reserves in the economy. Sometimes, however, actions from other parties can occur in the economy, which cause the reserves in the economy to change. If the central bank intervenes when this happens, we say a defensive open market operation has taken place. In effect, a defensive open market operation is used to offset other factors affecting reserves in the economy.

16.5.3 Discount Rate Changes

Another tool used for the conduct of monetary policy is the discount window borrowing. Since the central bank is the lender of last resort, banks can borrow additional reserves from them if they are in need. The rate at which banks borrow from the central bank is known as the discount rate in Algeria and the USA. It is called Central Bank Rate in Kenya, Repo Rate in South Africa, Key Repo Rate in Rwanda, Selic Rate in Brazil and Policy Rate in Ghana. The discount window borrowing is meant to shore up short-term liquidity needs of banks. As a result, loans are often given on an overnight term. In many countries, the fees are generally higher than the rate at which the banks can borrow from each other. This is to prevent banks from using the facility as a solution to their daily liquidity problems. In Ghana,

16.5 Tools of Monetary Policy	347

for instance, banks wanting to borrow from other banks will have to do so at a minus one of the policy rate.
  Discount window borrowing can cause reserves in the financial system to change, and this will cause a change in money supply. When the rate at which banks can borrow from the central bank falls, banks will be able to borrow at a cheaper rate. When the rate at which they can borrow from the central bank increases, it becomes more expensive for banks to borrow. This makes them reduce their lending to clients, as they will not want to borrow at high rates from the central bank. As a result, demand for reserves will have a negative relationship with the interest rate. On the supply side, the supply of reserves curve will be vertical if we make the assumption that central banks supply reserves. Equilibrium will be achieved where the demand for reserves equals the supply for reserves and the equilibrium interest rate remains at E (Fig. 16.4).
  Let us use the example below to examine how the discount-borrowing tool can be used to influence the operational and intermediate targets.
  Supposing now that for one reason or another, banks increase their demand for reserves in such a way that they will now have to borrow from the central bank, the demand curve will shift to the right to D2. If the central bank wanted to increase interest rate, it could move the discount rate from r1 to r2. This will lead to a reduction in borrowing and a decrease in money supply (Fig. 16.5).
  Previously, changes in the rate at which banks borrow from the central bank were the most used tool of the monetary policy. Its disadvantage soon became obvious, as many central banks came to the realisation that it was difficult to accurately envisage the reaction of banks after an increase or decrease in the rate. That is, the central bank can set the rate at which it may want to lend to the banks, but the banks decide on how much they want to borrow at any point in time.

  Today, the rate is used to indicate the direction the central bank wants to take. A higher rate suggests the central bank wants to tighten the economy, whereas a lower rate suggests a loosening.



16.5.4 Changes in the Required Reserve Ratio

Change in the required reserve ratio is another tool that the central bank uses for monetary policy. When the required reserve changes, the amount of reserves that banks keep also changes. Since banks are profit-oriented, a decrease in the required reserve ratio, for instance, means that banks do not by law have to keep much reserves at the central bank. They are therefore able to lend to their clients more easily. The new loans that are made will lead to greater investment, which can feed into the financial system. This, through the multiplier effect we have seen earlier on, increases the supply of money in the economy. One disadvantage of using the required reserve ratio as a tool for monetary policy is that changing it as often as economic conditions change will disrupt the activities of the bank. The open market operation does not have this problem, because as and when changes need to occur in the economy, securities can be sold or purchased without causing any disruptions.
  As discussed earlier in this section, the tools of monetary policy influence operational targets, which in turn influence the intermediate targets before the goals of the monetary policy are affected.
Quick Quiz
1. Discuss the tools a central bank uses to conduct its monetary policy.
2. How do changes in the required reserve ratio affect money supply?

16.6  The Money Supply Process	349

16.6 The Money Supply Process

Now that we understand what goes into the central bankÆs balance sheet, it is easy for us to understand how the items on the balance sheet can change to affect money supply. In this section, we discuss how we can use one of the tools of monetary policy to effect changes in the balance sheet and move on to discuss how this can in turn influence money supply, which is an intermediate target of monetary policy.



16.6.1 Open Market Operations by the Central Bank

Suppose the central bank carries out an open market purchase, it can buy and sell securities. If it sells securities, it is referred to as an open market sale. If it buys securities, it is referred to as an open market purchase. The central bank can buy securities either from the general public or from banks.
Open Market Purchase from a Bank Supposing the central bank carries out an open market purchase from a bank worth US$500, both the bankÆs balance sheet and the central bankÆs balance sheet will be affected. How will these be affected? The central bank will pay the bank with a cheque or cash. Whatever the means of payment is, the bank will deposit this in its vault. This action will increase the banksÆ reserves by US$500. However, because the banks have sold securities worth US$500, the value of their securities will also go down by US$500 (Tables 16.2 and 16.3).
  The outcome of the open market operations worth US$500 has caused reserves to increase by US$500. That is, reserves have increased by the amount of open market operations.
  If instead, the central bank does the open market purchase from the non-bank public, it can also pay by cash or by cheque. If it pays by cheque, the changes that will occur on the balance sheet of the different parties are as follows (Tables 16.4, 16.5 and 16.6).
  As demonstrated above, the results are similar to the open market purchase from the banks. Reserves increase by the amount of the open market purchase.

  If on the other hand, the central bank pays cash to the non-bank public, the outcome will be quite different (Tables 16.7 and 16.8).
  Unlike the scenario in the last example, reserves remain unchanged here. Rather, currency in circulation is what changes. This means that when open market opera- tions are done with the general public, depending on the mode of payment by the central bank, reserves will either change or remain the same. However, because the monetary base is made up of both reserves and currency in the hands of the public, the monetary base will change irrespective of whether cash or cheque payments are made. This suggests that the monetary base is a more stable variable to target than reserves.



16.6.2 Money Creation Process

Now that we understand how the central bank can change the monetary base, we are now in a better position to understand how money supply can change in the economy.

It is clear that apart from the central bank, commercial banks and the non-bank public also have a role to play in the money supply process.
  In Sub-Saharan Africa, the currency ratio has been very high and this has tended to reduce the impact of the money multiplier on the money stock. The high currency ratio for Ghana, Kenya, Zambia, Nigeria and Tanzania during the period 1968û1988 showed that at any time, a greater proportion of money was outside the control of the central bank and this has led to a restrictive monetary policy not meeting desired targets.

16.6.3 Relationship Between Money Supply and Its Determinants

Money supply has a relationship with monetary base, required reserve ratio, excess reserve ratio and currency deposit ratio.
Monetary Base This is made up of total currency in circulation plus total reserves. The monetary base has a positive relationship with money supply. An increase in the monetary base leads to an increase in the money supply and a decrease in the monetary base and this results in a reduction in the money supply.
Required Reserve Ratio This is proportion of deposits that banks are required to keep with the central bank. An increase in the required reserve ratio will reduce money supply and a decrease will increase money supply. Intuitively, an increase in the required reserve ratio, for instance, means banks have to keep more money with the central bank by regulation. This will reduce how much money banks have to lend or invest. Since it is this process that banks use to create money, money supply will reduce. The opposite is true.

16.7 The Monetary Policy Transmission Mechanism	355

Excess Reserve Ratio This is the ratio of reserves in excess of required reserves, funds for loans, investments, creditors, etc., held by banks as a proportion of aggregate deposits. An excess reserve ratio has a negative relationship with money supply. An increase in the excess reserve ratio will reduce money supply. Intuitively, increasing the excess reserve ratio limits banksÆ ability to create money, as little money is available to banks for lending or for purchasing securities. The opposite is true.
Currency Deposit Ratio This is the amount of currency held by people as a proportion of aggregate deposits. The currency deposit ratio also has a negative relationship with money supply. An increase in the currency deposit ratio reduces money supply and a decrease in the currency ratio increases money supply. Specif- ically, increasing the currency deposit ratio means more money will be in the hands of the public, leaving very little money in the banking system for the money creation process. An increase in deposit rates will encourage more deposits and therefore lead to a decrease in the currency deposit ratio, thus leading to an increase in money multiplier and money supply.


16.7 The Monetary Policy Transmission Mechanism

Having understood how the central bank uses its tools to influence its operational and intermediate targets, we now turn to how these affect the economy. The process through which monetary policy decisions affect the economy is what we call the transmission mechanism of monetary policy. These channels include the interest rate channel, the exchange rate channel, the credit channel and other assets price effect channels (also known as equity price channels). We discuss each of these channels one after the other below.


16.7.1 Interest Rate Channel

The traditional interest rate channel emanates from KeynesÆ General Theory (Keynes 1936). It shows how monetary shocks could spur changes in interest rates with pass- through effects on cost of borrowing for firms or individuals, consumption, invest- ment and hence aggregate demand. Therefore, according to this channel, an expan- sionary monetary policy conducted by the central bank results in a fall in the real
interest rate, (r#). The fall in real interest rates also leads to a decline in the real cost of borrowing, which also increases spending on investment (I " ) and on consump- tion (C"). With consumption and investment being components of aggregate demand, this process eventually results in a rise in aggregate demand (Y ad " ).
r # ? I " and C " ? Yad "

356	16  Central Banking and Monetary Policy

  One essential characteristic of the interest rate channel is that it focuses on the real interest rate as the rate that affects consumers and business decisions and not on the nominal interest rate. Also, in considering real interest rate, it is usually the real long- term interest rate that is seen as having a significant impact on consumer or business spending (not real short-term interest rate). The interest rate channel has been supposedly criticised for its empirical failure mainly stemming from the time it takes for interest rate changes to affect the economy. This has stimulated a quest into other transmission mechanisms of monetary policy.



16.7.2 Exchange Rate Channel

The attention of central banks and governments is gradually being drawn to how monetary policy affects the economy through the increasing internationalisation of global economies and also through the introduction of flexible exchange rates. This effect of monetary policy has been observed to affect net exports and aggregate demand. The exchange rate channel involves the interest rate effects, since reduction in a countryÆs local real interest rates results in local currency assets becoming relatively less attractive compared to assets denominated in foreign currencies. Hence, the value of locally priced assets in relation to other currency assets
decreases, and the local currency depreciates (E#). The lower value of the local currency makes local goods relatively cheaper compared to foreign goods, leading to an increase in net exports (NX") and consequently in aggregate demand (Yad").
r # ? E # ?NX " ? Yad "
  Given that many developing countries have their import values greater than their export values, it is likely that this channel may not have the desired effect in developing economies and may therefore not explain the whole monetary policy transmission process.



16.7.3 Credit Channel

The credit channel is broken down into two: the balance sheet channel, which looks at the possible impact of monetary shocks on borrowersÆ balance sheets and income statements, and the bank lending channel, which gives credence on the likely impact of monetary policy shocks on the supply of loans by commercial banks. One vital implication of the credit channel view is that monetary policy tends to have a significant effect on expenditure by small businesses, which depend more on bank loans, than it has on large businesses, which are able to access finance through other means such as stock and bond markets and not through bank loans only.

16.8 How Monetary Policy Affects the Economy	357

16.8.1.1 Bank Lending Channel

The bank lending channel is based on the premise that banks play a special role in the financial system, especially when it comes to solving asymmetric information problems in credit markets. Thus, this mechanism works through the supply of bank loans. So long as no perfect substitution exists for bank deposits with other sources of funds, the bank lending channel of monetary transmission is demonstrated through the expansionary monetary policy, which causes bank reserves and bank deposits to increase when implemented. Consequently, the available quantity of bank loans increases, and since many borrowers are dependent on bank loans to finance their business activities, there is an increase in investment spending; con- sumers may also increase spending as a result.

Bank reserves " ?Bank deposits " ?Bank loans " ?I " ?Yad "
  Similarly, monetary policy tightening reduces bank loan supply for businesses which are bank dependent for funds. These businesses are forced to seek new lenders, which are likely to increase their debt premium, eventually affecting their spending decisions.


16.7.3.2	Balance Sheet (Net Worth) Channel

Similar to the bank lending channel, the balance sheet channel also arises from the occurrence of financial frictions in credit markets. This mechanism is based on the fact that a borrower with a stronger financial position pays a lower debt premium. Demonstrating this channel: when there is an expansionary monetary policy, interest rates fall, causing stock prices to rise (PS) as interest rates have a negative relation- ship with the value of stocks. This raises the net worth of firms and eventually leads to higher investment spending and aggregate demand, since there is a decrease in adverse selection and moral hazard problems.
r # ?PS " ?FirmÆs net worth " ?Adverse selection # , Moral hazard
# ?Lending " ?I " ?Yad "

16.8 How Monetary Policy Affects the Economy

Now that we have understood the channels through which monetary policy can influence the economy, we are in a better position to understand which aspects of the economy monetary policy affects. Changes in interest rates affect demand for goods and services by firms and individuals, output, employment, exchange rate and inflation.

358	16  Central Banking and Monetary Policy

16.8.1 Effect on the Demand for Goods and Services

If both the domestic and international outlooks on the economy point towards a boom, the central bank can come up with a restrictive monetary policy by using the most appropriate tool for monetary policy. Whichever tool it decides to use, interest rate will increase under this circumstance. A high interest rate will reduce lending and investment, leading to people and businesses spending less. Also, higher interest rates will make bonds and other debt instruments more attractive compared to shares. Households with shares in their portfolio will find that the value of their holdings has reduced. This reduces their wealth and makes them reduce their demand for goods and services. If on the other hand, the central bank expects that a recession is imminent, it will reduce interest rate, resulting in low cost of borrowing. Low cost of borrowing will increase lending and investment, leading to an increase in peopleÆs spending. Further, a low interest rate will make ordinary shares more attractive than debt instruments. The value of their portfolio will rise, leading to increased demand for goods and services.



16.8.2 Effect on Output and Employment

The increase (reduction) in the demand for goods and services will occur as a result of a reduction (increase) in interest rate, which will lead to increased (decreased) output and employment. This will in turn increase (reduce) demand for capital goods to expand (lower) the productive capacity of firms. Consumption will also be boosted more because of the benefits from increased employment, while decreased employment will dampen consumption.



16.8.3 Effect on Exchange Rate

Changes in interest rate influence exchange rate. Generally, if interest rates increase (reduce) in an economy, the financial assets of that economy become more attractive (less attractive) to investors compared to other economies. Since investors always want to make high returns, the increase (decrease) in interest rate increases (reduces) the demand for the financial assets of the economy, whose interest rates have increased (decreased). Increased demand in financial assets leads to an appreciation (depreciation) of the currency of this economy relative to other economies. The relationship is not necessarily as straightforward as other variables like inflation rates can impact on exchange rates.

16.9 Inflation Targeting	359

16.8.4 Effect on Inflation

If monetary policy stimulates the economy through a persistent reduction in interest rate, wages and prices will rise. This will eventually lead to higher inflation without necessarily causing output to increase. Further, monetary policy signals to the public the direction the central bank wants to take and informs the public on how they are supposed to respond to this directive. For example, if the central bank wants to embark on an expansionary monetary policy, the reduction in interest rate that will come with this may lead to inflation. Hence, workers will demand increases in wages. This action can lead to inflation, if there is no corresponding increase in output.


16.9 In?ation Targeting

Having discussed earlier the tools of monetary policy, we can now examine how the central bank should pursue a monetary policy that can achieve its goals. To under- stand how the central bank needs to conduct monetary policy, it is important to discuss the goals of monetary policy and also examine the regime it needs to adopt to achieve its stated goals. The central bank pursues a monetary policy that will achieve certain goals in the economy. These goals include price stability, high employment and output stability, economic growth, stability of financial markets, interest rate stability and foreign exchange markets stability. Yet, getting the monetary policy right is often a difficult task. While a monetary policy that is too relaxed can lead to high inflation and impede growth, a monetary policy that is too restrictive also causes a recession and unemployment.
  In this section, we concentrate on inflation targeting which is the main monetary regime the central bank uses to achieve one of its main goals, namely price stability.


16.9.1 Price Stability

People all over the world have increasingly become concerned about the costs of inflation. Inflation, which is defined as a rise in the general price level of goods and services, can lead to uncertainty in the economy, which makes planning difficult. This affects both individual and business consumption. As a result, price stability has over the past few years been seen as the most important goal of monetary policy. Achieving this important goal requires the targeting of important variables, like the inflation rate or the money supply, which will ensure that policymakers will remain in the target. In this case, the targets become what we call a nominal anchor that plays an important role in controlling the dynamic inconsistency problem, which eventually leads to poor economic outcomes.

360	16  Central Banking and Monetary Policy

  The dynamic inconsistency problem has to do with the lack of willpower on the part of policymakers to embark on policies they have promised to embark on. This usually occurs when they pursue discretionary policies instead of committing to rules. For instance, if the monetary authorities have a goal of reducing inflation, it may have to pursue a restrictive monetary policy. However, the general public will not believe in their announcement on embarking on a restrictive monetary policy if policymakers renege on their promises several times. This recognition will make the public revise their expectations on prices and wages upwards, leading to inflation.


16.9.2 Monetary Policy Strategies

In dealing with the dynamic inconsistency problem, central banks have to commit to a rule. Committing to a rule is what has given birth to various monetary policy regimes. Some monetary policy regimes available to developed, emerging and developing economies include monetary targeting, inflation targeting and exchange rate targeting.


16.9.2.1 Monetary Targeting

This involves monetary authorities controlling money supply through the æsupply- sideÆ counterparts of the volume of deposits. The supply-side counterparts include changes in public sector borrowing, sale and purchase of public sector debt from the private sector and changes in bank lending to the private sector. Controlling these items influences the money stock. However, the extent to which these instruments will work depends on a stable demand function and the interest elasticity of demand for credit. In recent times, financial innovation has made the demand function unstable leading to changes in these instruments not having the desired impact.


16.9.2.2 In?ation Targeting

Comparatively, inflation targeting is preferred to the other regimes or strategies because monetary targeting has practically failed to stabilise inflation. Additionally, exchange rate targeting (where the exchange rate is fixed relative to a centre country with an independent monetary policy) has proven less successful. This major disadvantage in the use of monetary targeting has made inflation targeting receive global attention and widespread adoption by central banks.
  As for monetary policy strategy, it is the responsibility of a nationÆs central bank. It involves keeping inflation at a stated target or range, which is usually done by adjusting interest rate. Thus, the central bank under this regime targets a short-term market interest rate as its operating target with the hope that asset prices, lending and

16.9 Inflation Targeting	361

deposit rates and expectations in the economy will be affected. This will then influence both domestic and external demand. Inflation targeting is characterised by:
1. An announced numerical inflation target
2. An implementation of monetary policy that plays a major role in an inflation forecast
3. A high degree of transparency and accountability


Advantages of Inflation Targeting

Inflation targeting has been found to be an ideal strategy for driving down inflation in many countries. For some economists, having several goals to achieve brings inflationary bias. Because central banks are often under pressure to put in measures that will boost the economy, they get criticised by the public for increasing interest rates. Hence, having an explicit target for inflation addresses this problem by making inflation, rather than employment, the ultimate goal.
  Having an explicit target for inflation also controls expectation, which reduces inflation in the short run. Inflation targeting involves targeting either the price level or the rate of inflation, deciding whether to have a specific numerical figure or a range and so on. These will require policymakers to either commit to a rule or use their discretion. Committing to a rule will reduce the flexibility of policymakers but make them more credible in the eyes of the public. This will reduce the dynamic inconsistency problems. Inflation targeting also serves as a measure of central banksÆ performance by enhancing transparency and accountability of the regulatory body. With the success of inflation targeting in New Zealand, an increasing number of countries in developed, emerging and developing economies have also adopted it. Although it has proven successful in countries such as the UK, Canada, Australia and Sweden, policymakers in many developing and emerging countries are beginning to wonder if this is a strategy for them. Inflation targeting works effectively when there are no productivity disturbances. However, for many devel-
oping countries, supply shocks are very imminent.


Disadvantages of Inflation Targeting

Inflation targeting has been criticised on some grounds as well. First, the central bank pursues price stability to the detriment of other broader and equally important monetary policy goals or objectives; i.e. a greater priority is given to maintaining inflation targets at the cost of other pressing economic issues such as unemployment. Second, the imperfect control of inflation makes it difficult to implement and to monitor inflation. Third, it is ambiguous in nature. Due to the lengthy lags of monetary policy, inflation targeting does not provide timely signals to the public about the position of monetary policy. Fourth, it is inconsistent with the objective of the central bank to support financial stability.

362	16  Central Banking and Monetary Policy

16.10 Summary and Conclusions

In this chapter, we discuss the structure and roles of the central bank. We have also discussed issues related to monetary policy and inflation targeting. We have learnt that:
1. The independence of the central bank can take the forms of (1) instrument independence involving the central bank having the freedom to choose whatever instrument it wants to use for monetary policy without any interference by the politicians and (2) goal independence, entailing the central bank having the freedom to decide on the goal of monetary policy.
2. Although most countries are currently making their central banks independent, the downside of independence should also be considered.
3. The role of the central bank includes the conduct of monetary policy, currency issue, lender of last resort, governmentÆs bank and supervision.
4. The central bank can change money supply using the tools of monetary policy, which include open market operations, reserve requirement and discount borrowing.
5. Central banks are not the only ones responsible for the money supply process. The non-bank public and the banking system also play a role.
6. Money supply has a relationship with monetary base, required reserve ration, excess reserve ration and currency deposit ratio.
7. The transmission mechanism process through which monetary policy decisions affect the economy includes the interest rate channel, the exchange rate channel, the credit channel and other assets price effect channels.
8. Inflation targeting helps to resolve the dynamic inconsistency problem by helping policymakers commit to a rule.
9. Although inflation targeting has been successful in many developed countries, its benefits are yet to be realised in developing countries. The reason being that much of developing countriesÆ inflation is caused by supply rigidities.
Discussion Questions and Problems

2. Discuss the roles of the central bank in developing countries.

4. Can money supply be changed by just the central bank?

17.1 Introduction

Governments of emerging and developing economies have created various bodies to supervise the operations of banks in their countries. These are conducted in order to ensure an effective and efficient banking and financial sector. These regulatory bodies define the rules and regulations pertaining to banking operations and ensure that banks operating in these countries comply with these rules. The issue of whether or not to regulate and supervise banking operations was revisited in light of the 2007 global financial crisis. Although developing and African countries may not have many global banks, the need for regulation and supervision is equally important.
  In this chapter, we discuss the importance of bank regulation in the developing and African countries context. We explain the rationale for banking regulation and the forms of bank regulation. We also describe the challenges of bank regulation. We conclude this chapter with bank deposit insurance.








® Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2019
J. Y. Abor et al., Money and Banking in Africa, Advances in African Economic, Social and Political Development, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-77458-9_17

365

366	17  Bank Regulation

17.2 Importance of Bank Regulation

Banks constitute the majority of financial intermediaries in Africa. As such, they screen potential borrowers, monitor customers, provide liquidity and create safe assets among other functions. They reduce information asymmetry by collating data on various customers and investment opportunities and channel resources to pro- ductive ventures. The need for regulation and supervision arises because banks are not perfect in carrying out these functions.
  In developing countries, regulatory authorities ensure that the financial system remains stable. Banks should be closely monitored to ensure that they are performing their functions properly and in compliance with defined rules and regulations. This is especially important because failure to comply could result in economic and finan- cial disasters.
  The regulation of banking operations is important as it provides protection to depositors who together with other creditors act as lenders to banks. Depositors and creditors, especially in developing countries, tend to have less information about banksÆ risky operations. Since depositors keep a large portion of their wealth with banks, a stable banking system is in their interest. The underdeveloped financial markets in these countries further highlight the eminence of banks in developing and African countries and the need for strict adherence to regulation and supervision. However, at the micro level, it is extremely costly to monitor the operations of banks, hence the need for an authority dedicated to carrying out such regulation and supervision exercises.
Quick Quiz
1. List some operations undertaken by banks in developing countries
2. How do banks in developing countries solve the problem of information asymmetry?
3. How does banking regulation protect the interests of depositors and other stakeholders?
4. Why is it that regulatory authorities in developing countries find it difficult to regulate and supervise banks?



17.3 Rationale for Bank Regulation: The Basel II Accord

There are two dominant paradigms for explaining why we need bank regulation and other intermediaries, according to Balling et al. (2012). One form of regulations looks into assets of the intermediaryÆs balance sheet, while the other examines the liability side. Either the assets or the liabilities produce different implications for regulation. With respect to the assets side, banks are viewed as monitoring and/or screening the quality attributes of investment projects. The argument is that without intermediation, such monitoring would be duplicated by the many investors

17.3 Rationale for Bank Regulation: The Basel II Accord	367

involved in funding such projects, or alternatively, investors would have been forced to take larger stakes. On the liability side, there are ex ante identical investors (depositors) who are risk averse and uncertain about the timing of their future consumption needs.
  The astonishing scope and the effect of the 2007/2008 banking crises coupled with strong evidence on the beneficial effects of well-functioning banking systems for economic growth underscore current efforts to reform bank regulation and supervision. It can be recalled that in January 2001, the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision issued a proposal for a Basel II Capital Accord that, once it is com- pleted, it will replace the earlier 1988 Basel I Capital Accord. The Basel II proposal is based on three key pillars: Pillar I deals with improved minimum risk-based (risk- adjusted) bank capital requirements, Pillar II focuses on better supervisory practices and Pillar III focuses on reinforcing greater market discipline through increased information disclosed by banks.
  Pillar I suggests the establishment of minimum risk-based capital requirements with the aim of covering credit, trading and operational risks that well-diversified financial institutions may encounter. Under this pillar, banks are required to maintain a risk-based capital ratio (RBCR) of at least 8%.
  Pillar II, which identifies the role of the national supervisors, has four principles of supervisory review:
ò The regulators are expected to ensure that banks employ appropriate methodol- ogy to determine Basel II ratios and should have a strategy to maintain capital requirements.
ò The regulators must constantly review banksÆ international assessment proce- dures and strategies, taking appropriate action if these fall below standard.
ò The banks must be encouraged by regulators to hold capital above the minimum requirements.
ò Regulators are expected to intervene as early as possible to ask banks restore their capital levels if they fall below the minimum.
  The main purpose of Pillar III is to reinforce the minimum capital requirement and national supervision by providing timely and transparent information. Here, the participating banks are to account and disclose their:
ò Risk exposure
ò Capital adequacy
ò Methods and approach for computing capital requirements
ò All material informationùthat is, information, if omitted or mis-stated, could affect the decision-making of the agent using the information
ò Disclosure should be on a semi-annual basis, quarterly in the case of risk exposure, especially if the bank engages in global activities
  Once the Basel Committee finalises its list of æbest practicesÆ for the regulation and supervision of banks, countries around the world are urged to adopt them. European and Japanese banks began transitioning to Basel II rules in January 2007. Transition in the USA began in 2008. The belief is that the banking sectors

368	17  Bank Regulation

in countries adopting these practices will function better, thereby promoting growth and stability.
The rationale for banking sector regulation includes:
To Maintain the Structure of the Market and Competition
Like other markets, competition among banks may be good for their customers, especially borrowers who might obtain cheaper financing and depositors who are paid higher interest rates, but excessive competition among banks can encourage excessive risk taking by banks (Claessens and Laeven 2004). First of all, competition facilitates the efficient allocation of resources in the financial system. Competitive pressures force banks to improve efficiency, and these efficiency gains are shared with the bankÆs customers in the form of new products and lower fees. Secondly, competition may also lead to instability in the banking system as banks take excessive risks by pursuing riskier borrowers and investments because profit mar- gins in traditional businesses are squeezed by competition. Therefore, banking sector regulation may seek to limit competition by moving barriers to entry, branching restrictions and merger controls. Furthermore, safety might be enhanced by improv- ing the charter values of existing banks. Charter values of existing banks can be enhanced by limiting entry of new banks, so as to increase the economic rents earned by incumbents. Technological advances may have lowered entry barriers in recent years.
To Control Money Circulation Within the Economy
The three major forms of bank regulation relating to monetary control objectives are reserve requirements, the discount window (policy rate) and purchase and sale of government securities (open market operation). In addition, æmoral suasionÆ by the central bank also plays a role. Furthermore, several unconventional monetary policy tools have been used to tackle disturbances on financial markets due to the global financial crisis. The following are some of monetary tools used to regulate banks operating in Africa:
  Legal Reserve Requirements: The cash-asset reserve requirements mandate that the bank to retain a fraction of its deposit liabilities in a non-interest bearing, liquid formùvault cash or deposits, at the Federal Reserve if the bank is a member of a Federal state. For instance, a bank operating in Nigeria may be required to keep a portion of it deposits with Nigeria Central Bank. All banks (especially, depository institutions) in the country are subject to reserve requirements on customer deposits. The reserve requirements vary depending on the type of the deposit. For instance, reserve requirements against time deposits (CDs) are zero, whereas deposits, subject to withdrawal on demand, or net transaction accounts, are subject to a reserve requirement that depends on the level of deposits. Without reserve requirements, banks can be expected to voluntarily hold some cash assets, and deposit expansion and contraction would ensue more or less as it would with legal reserve require- ments. Moreover, reserve requirements have numerous drawbacks: they foster spurious innovation as depository, and they also limit the amount of money available to banks to provide credit to borrowers.

17.4 Forms of Bank Regulation	369

To Ensure Effective and Efficient Credit Allocation
The availability of bank credit affects the pattern of economic activity. Governments are therefore often tempted to influence the allocation of this credit to achieve social and political objectives. Some form of governmental credit allocation is found in almost every country. Some countries make use of tax to direct credit allocation. That is, a country may provide a tax incentive to investors to invest in a particular sector. For instance, tax credits are used to encourage capital investment and greater energy efficiency in homes in the USA. Tax deductibility of charitable contributions has increased the flow of capital to eligible organisations. In Ghana, for example, credit to the agricultural sector attracts a tax rate of 20% as opposed to the general corporate tax rate of 25%.
Quick Quiz
1. How do banks and other intermediaries help investors in decision-making?
2. How does competition among banks benefit customers?
3. What role does bank regulation play in promoting competition?
4. How can bank regulation control money supply?
5. How do bank regulations ensure efficient credit allocation?
