17.4 Forms of Bank Regulation

Given the importance of bank regulation and the broad scope of banking operations, regulatory authorities make use of various tools in order to regulate and supervise the operations of banks in developing countries. These tools include, but are not limited to, restriction of assets and operation, competition among banks, minimum capital requirements and deposit insurance.
Restriction of Assets and Operation One form of regulation that can be applied to banks in developing countries is restriction of asset and operations. As discussed earlier, depositors and creditors tend to be weary of banksÆ risky operations. Regu- lation could include restricting the class of assets that a bank can invest in. This involves keeping the bank from investing in certain risky assets in particular. Banks are also prevented from engaging in certain operations that are considered risky through the formulation of these regulations. These regulations are earmarked to protect the interest of relatively risk-averse depositors and creditors from overly risky operations of banks.
Competition Another way to regulate banking operations is to place limits on the level of competition in the banking industry. In banking, the level of competition has implications for access to finance, allocation of capital funds, competitiveness and development of manufacturing and service industries, economic growth and finan- cial stability (Claessens and Laeven 2004). Competition in the banking sector stimulates innovation, lowers prices and improves the quality of products and

370	17  Bank Regulation

services produced. This, in turn, enhances choice and welfare. However, with an overly intense degree of competition in the banking sector, banks may be more susceptible to engaging in comparable riskier operations in order to sustain or improve profits. This kind of behaviour may provoke an economic crisis and cause disequilibrium in the financial system (Amidu 2013). Limits on competition may take the form of barriers to entry against foreign banks, quotas on the total number of banks in the country, restrictions on number of branches a bank may have and so on.
Minimum Capital Requirements The imposition of capital requirement remains one of the most common ways of regulating banks in developing countries. For instance, the central banks in some African countries, including Ghana and Kenya, have recently increased the minimum capital requirements of banks. In 2017, the Bank of Ghana asked all the banks operating in the country to augment their capital by the end of 2018. More often, this form of regulation is fashioned as a capital adequacy ratio. This regulation serves as a means to ensure that banks are found solvent enough to cushion against unexpected surprises at all times.
Deposit Insurance The use of deposit insurance is another way of regulating banking operations in developing countries. It involves the protection that a govern- ment agency provides to depositors in order to safeguard their deposits with banks against risk of loss as a result of bank failure. The main objective of deposit insurance is to protect the interest of unsophisticated depositors, especially against the risk of failure. The banked population of most developing countries is likely to include people with very basic level of education. This may leave them vulnerable to unscrupulous or high-risk banking operations. The use of deposit insurance reduces the possibility of such occurrences. Additionally, it is possible to encourage banks to correctly price risk by imposing risk-based deposit insurance premiums. We explain deposit insurance in detail later in this chapter.
Disclosure Authorities in developing countries make use of disclosure require- ments to regulate and supervise banking operations. In many African countries, for instance, the adoption of the International Financial Reporting Standards (IFRS) was made mandatory for all banks in 2006. These requirements ensure that banks release enough information to market participants and give them the opportunity to assess the robustness of the banking system.
Quick Quiz
1. List the various forms of bank regulations.
2. The restriction of assets and operations of banks in developing countries is a form of regulation. How does this protect the interests of stakeholders?
3. How is placing restriction on competition an effective form of bank regulation?
4. How do banking regulators protect customers against risk of failure?
5. How do regulators ensure that market participants have enough information about the banking system?

17.5 Challenges of Bank Regulation	371

17.5 Challenges of Bank Regulation

In recent times, many developing countries have experienced a sharp increase in financial integration in the form of cross-border capital flows, equity listing and stock market trading. Cross-border banking in the African continent has also increased during the period. For example, South AfricaÆs Standard Bank currently operates in 15 Sub-Saharan African countries: Togo-based Ecobank operates in 32 African countries, while NigeriaÆs United Bank for Africa (UBA) operates in 20 countries, and MoroccoÆs Attijariwafa Bank has a presence in 12 African coun- tries (KPMG 2013). The rapid extent of such financial integration in developing countries puts pressure on them to make adjustments to accommodate these changes. These changes would have to incorporate institutional capacity building, which requires human and financial capital. However, most of these countries do not have the required human and financial capital to facilitate the effective integration of the financial system. Perhaps policies and action plans focusing on these issues should be drawn in order to address them.
  Another challenge that arises as a result of the globalisation of the financial systems in developing countries is the creation of suitable regulation to cater to the needs of different financial institutions. For instance, regulating subsidiaries may be problematic if regulations in the home country differ from those in the foreign country. Also, corporate governance requirements differ from one country to the other. It may be tempting for regulators in African countries to harmonise domestic regulations with international ones. However, the general regulations will likely not meet the needs of developing and African countries.
  Corollary to the above challenge to financial regulation in developing countries is enforcement. Institutional constraints and limited capacity such as limited and unqualified supervisors, lack of political will, lack of accountability and corruption place a cap on how effective regulations will be in ensuring the effectiveness and efficiency of the financial system in developing countries.
  The increased diversification across and within interest generating income of banks in developing countries makes it more complicated to protect the concern of consumers. The innovation of financial services and products in Africa can pose a challenge to even the most sophisticated consumers. As a result of this, they can be more susceptible to unscrupulous financial operations. The onus then falls on regulators and supervisors to formulate policies and plans that will protect the interests of consumers and ensure they obtain maximum benefit from financial service providers.
  Furthermore, the challenge associated with bank regulations in developing coun- tries and for that matter Africa is the cost involved. The changing of existing regulations and the introduction of new ones involve costs in the form of direct expenditures, enhanced reporting and corporate government requirements, improved internal controls and so on. For example, the adoption of IFRS is expensive for both the banks and the regulatory bodies. While the importance of regulation is agreed on

372	17  Bank Regulation

globally, it must be noted that over-regulation can also lead to additional costs, which will likely be passed on to consumers.
Quick Quiz
1. How has financial integration affected bank regulation in developing countries?
2. What has been the major challenge to effective financial integration in Africa?
3. Will harmonising banking regulations solve the problem of regulating financial institutions with different structures in developing countries?
4. What are some of the institutional factors that make it challenging to regulate banks in developing countries?
5. What is the disadvantage associated with innovation in financial services in developing countries?
6. How will the costs of compliance affect the effectiveness of bank regulation?


17.6 Deposit Insurance

Deposit insurance is a mechanism instituted to protect the funds of bank depositors, either in part or in full, from losses that may arise due to a bankÆs inability to settle debts when they fall due. Deposit insurance makes banks attractive to potential depositors since it provides some level of assurance that their deposits are insulated against risky activities and thereby increase banksÆ ability to offer credit or loans. A governmental agent usually implements it, and claims are paid from a pool of funds into which all banks contribute.
  Deposit insurance has implications for banksÆ risk behaviour. Under deposit insurance, risk is transferred to the insurer. What this implies, therefore, is the assumption that less deposit insurance results in more risk to the bank. In some jurisdictions, there is a limit on the amount of banksÆ deposits or liabilities that can be insured. In so doing, regulators place a cap on the level of risk that banks will be willing to take up, since any downside exposure will fall on them. As banks take on the responsibility for more risk, they will be more rigorous about risk management and cost reduction.
  By mitigating risk-seeking behaviour of banks, deposit insurance helps prevent bank runs. These are common in times of crises or when depositors are under the impression that their funds are at risk. Bank run is a situation where depositors rush to withdraw their deposits as a result of suspected or impending bank failure. There is danger in such a situation, in that banks are forced to liquidate assets at a loss in order to settle depositors, and this sometimes may result in failure. The presence of deposit insurance alleviates the fears of depositors and reduces the likelihood of bank runs or panic withdrawals.
Quick Quiz
1. What is deposit insurance?
2. Explain the motivation behind deposit insurance.

17.7 Summary and Conclusions	373

3. How does the level of insurance coverage affect the risk attitude of banks?
4. How can deposit insurance influence banksÆ cost management?
5. Explain the term æbank runsÆ.
6. Briefly describe how deposit insurance helps prevent bank runs.



17.7	Summary and Conclusions

Banks and other financial institutions play a crucial role in the development of any economy. They serve as a link between surplus and deficit funding units. The eminence of banks and other financial institutions calls for regulation by oversight bodies, especially in light of the role these institutions play in financial crises. In this chapter, we mentioned that:
1. Bank regulations help protect the interests of customers, ensure fair competition and ensure financial stability in the economy and the efficient allocation of resources.
2. The rationales for bank regulation include maintaining the structure of the market and competition, controlling money circulation within the economy and ensuring effective and efficient credit allocation.
3. Bank regulations take various forms, and these include restriction of assets and operations, competition among banks, minimum capital requirements and deposit insurance.
4. Regulators face challenges in regulating and supervising the activities of banks. Some of these challenges include increased financial integration, different needs of different institutions, enforcement, diversification and innovation and the costs involved in administering these regulations. These challenges make it difficult to regulate the activities of banks in developing countries.
5. Deposit insurance is important in protecting depositorsÆ funds from losses that may arise due to banksÆ inability to settle debts when they fall due. It improves the attractiveness of banks to potential depositors by providing some level of assur- ance regarding the safety of their deposits.
Discussion Questions and Problems
1. Banks in developing countries are not large enough to cause shifts in the world economy; however, governments of developing countries have created various bodies to supervise the operations of banks in their various economies. These bodies define the rules and regulations pertaining to banking operations and ensure the compliance of banks to those rules and regulations. Discuss the reasons why, in spite of their relatively negligible effect on the global scale, there is a need for regulation of banking operations in developing countries.
2. Evaluate the various forms of bank regulations that can be instituted by regulatory and supervisory authorities in developing countries.

374	17  Bank Regulation

3. The introduction of bank regulations brings with it some problems with regard to their formulation, implementation and enforcement. Examine the challenges associated with bank regulation in the context of African countries.

A

Abnormal returns The excess returns over the expected returns on an investment in security over a particular time period. It usually represents the difference between the actual returns and the expected returns on the security.
Account party The customer or entity who applies to a bank to establish a letter of credit in favour of another entity or beneficiary.
Accrued interest The interest on a loan or convertible bond that has accumulated since the principal investment, or since the last interest payment date, or since the previous coupon payment.
Adjustable rate mortgages (ARMs) Loans against real property in which the interest rate on the loan varies with changes in interest rates over the duration of the loan.
Adverse selection Bad lending decision with regard to prospective borrower resulting from information asymmetry (lender and borrower not having unequal access to information). This may either involve granting a loan request to a bad borrower or rejecting a loan application from a good borrower.
Affiliated banks A bank or non-bank subsidiary that is owned or controlled by the parent company of a bank holding company.
Affinity (Credit) card This is a credit card that is branded with a bankÆs brand and imagery that is issued to its clients or those who have an æaffinityÆ for that bank. Agency cost The expenses incurred by principals to monitor and bond with agents. Agency offices Banking offices that provide credit and other non-deposit services. Agency problem A problem, usually a conflict of interest that often arises in any relationship where management is expected to act in shareholdersÆ best interests.
Agency theory A supposition or a theory that explains the relationship between principals and agents in business.
Agricultural loans These loans are granted to farmers to help them in their farming activities such as planting, harvesting and storage of crops as well as in caring and feeding of livestock.

376	Glossary

Amortisation schedule This is a table of periodic loan payments detailing the principal and interest amounts which are made up of each payment until the loan is fully paid up at the end of the term of the loan.
Annual percentage rate (APR) This is the annual interest rate on a loan or an investment, expressed as a percentage and represents the actual annual cost of funds over the period of the loan or investment.
Asset conversation A strategy for meeting liquidity needs in which liquid reserves are kept in readily marketable assets that can easily be converted into cash.
Asset conversation loan This is a short-term loan that is usually repaid through the liquidation of an asset such as inventory or receivables.
Asset liquidation An appraisal and sale of a failed bankÆs assets, usually by distributing the failed bankÆs assets to claimants.
Asset management The management of the investments of a client by a financial institution such as an investment bank.
Asset utilisation The ratio of total revenue earned to the total assets a bank owns, measuring the averages yield on assets.
Asset-based loans These are loans secured by a business firmÆs assets (collateral), particularly accounts receivable, inventory and/or other balance sheet assets.
Asset-liability management The process of decision-making to deal with the risk a bank faces as a result of a mismatch between assets and liabilities, either due to liquidity or exposure to interest rate risk.
Assets These are all valuable items a bank or an entity owns (can be fixed or current assets).
Assignments A form of loan sale in which ownership of a loan is transferred to the loan buyer who then has a direct claim against the borrower.
Automated teller machine (ATM) A device for electronic funds transfer in which a customer can access his or her deposit account, make withdrawals and loan payments or obtain information and other transaction services.


B

Balance sheet This is a statement of the financial position of a firm or organisation detailing its assets, liabilities and capital at a specific point in time.
Balanced liquidity management This is the combined use of both asset manage- ment and liability management to cover the liquidity needs of a bank.
Bancassurance This involves an arrangement between a bank and an insurance company where the bank allows the products of the insurance company to be sold through the bankÆs sales outfit.
Bank This is a financial institution that mobilises funds mainly by providing a widest range of financial functions such as current account, savings deposits and time deposits and payment services and also gives out loans.
Bank branches There are two or more offices of a single bank.
Bank capital This is the difference between the value of a bankÆs assets and its liabilities. It represents the net worth of the bank or value to its shareholders.

Glossary	377

Bank closure When a bank is declared insolvent by its chartering agency, which, due to regulatory forbearance, usually occurs after its economic failure.
Bank discount rate The method by which yields on treasury bills and other money market securities are calculated using par value and a 360-day year to determine the appropriate discount rate or yield.
Bank liquidity The ability of a bank to store liquidity on its balance sheet or to purchase it in the marketplace or both.
Bank loans (As compared with bonds) Senior secured, shorter maturity, more covenants, often amended, freely callable and floating rate (compared to unsecured subordinate, longer maturity, fewer covenants, difficult to amend, call protected, fixed rate).
Bank panic This is the simultaneous failure of many banks, which occurs during a
financial crisis.
Bank run This is an event when a large number of customers (depositors) lose confidence in a bank and withdraw their deposits simultaneously, exhausting the bankÆs reserves due to concerns about the bankÆs solvency.
Bank supervision An oversight of commercial banks by the government or central bank.
Bank-customer relationship The relationship between a bank and a customer that distinguishes indirect or intermediated finance from direct finance.
BankerÆs acceptance This is a short-term debt instrument issued by a non-financial
firm and guaranteed by a bank (to pay the holder on a specific future date).
BankerÆs bank Correspondent banks owned by community banks that provide services to other banks but are prohibited from doing business with the general public.
Banking offices The number of banks plus the number of branches.
Banking structure Composition of the banking industry, often defined by the number of banks or by the proportion of unit banks to branch banks and large banks to small, evolving into a æbarbellÆ structure of large and small banks.
Basic (lifeline) banking Low-cost deposits and other retail banking services that are specifically offered to meet the banking needs of customers with limited means.
Basis This is the difference between price movement of a futures contract and the price movement of an underlying asset.
Basis risk This is the risk in relation to imperfect hedging and it arises as the difference between the price of the asset being hedged and asset serving as the hedged, or the difference between the actual selling date of the asset being hedged and the expiration date of the hedged asset (calendar basis risk) or the difference in the location of delivery and sale of the asset being hedged and the asset serving as the hedged (location basis risk).
Bear This is an investor who typically expects the price of a particular security or the general market will decline and attempts to profit from the fall in price.
Bearer bond A bond in which no records are kept regarding ownership. It is owned by the holder as opposed to a registered owner.
Beneficiary The party who is eligible to receive distributions from a trust, will or a life insurance policy.

378	Glossary

Best-effort agreement This is an agreement between a security underwriter (an investment banking firm) and an issuer of securities, in which the underwriter promises to do their best to sell the issuerÆs securities to the investing public.
Beta It is a measure of sensitivity of the asset returns to market returns. It is measured as the covariance between the asset returns and the market returns divided by the variance of the market returns.
Bid quote The most current prices and quantities at which a buyer is willing to pay for securities.
Bid-ask spread The difference between the ask price and the bid price for an asset in the market.
Board of governors This is a group of persons that is tasked with the responsibility of overseeing the running of an entity.
Bond market A financial market for the sale and purchase of bonds. The bonds are initially issued in the primary market and later traded on the secondary market.
Bond prospectus An instrument that details the full terms and conditions of the issue. It is also known as bond indenture.
Bond ratings A grade given to a bond by a private independent party that indicates its credit quality or a bond issuerÆs financial strength.
Bonds Long-term debt instrument issued by a corporate entity or government to investors to raise capital, which is paid back with interest to the investors at maturity.
Branchless banking Banking corporations that have no full-service branch offices but often deliver their services via electronic networks.
Bull This is an investor who usually believes that the market prices of securities in an industry will increase and attempts to profit from the price increase.


C

Callable bond (also referred to as redeemable bond) A type of bond that allows the bond issuer redeem the bond before maturity. It usually involves the issuer paying a higher coupon rate.
Callable certificates of deposit (CDs) Time deposit with a bank or financial institution that can be redeemed by the issuer before the maturity date.
CAMELS rating A rating system that is used to evaluate the overall performance of banks or depository institutions to determine their strengths and weaknesses. It is based on examinerÆs judgement regarding the bankÆs capital adequacy, asset quality, management quality, earnings, liquidity position and sensitivity to market exposure.
Capital Financial assets or long-term funds that the owners or shareholders of a bank or financial institution have contributed. They are mainly made up of stated capital, reserves and earnings.
Capital ratio A comparison between a bankÆs core equity capital and its total risk- weighted assets.

Glossary	379

Capital risk The amount of risk that a bank or financial institution will lose in an investment.
Cash This term is one of the six CÆs of credit, which loan officers should review in any loan application, referring to the generation of income or cash flow by a borrowing customer.
Cash ?ow It is the net amount of cash and cash equivalents that is transferred in and out of a firm.
Cash ?ow analysis An analytical approach to measuring the volume and compo- sition of cash inflows and cash outflows experienced or expected by a borrowing customer.
Cash management services These are services offered by a financial institution to a firm involving cash collections, cash disbursements and investment of any temporary surplus funds until such a time that the funds are needed.
Certificate of deposit (CD) A saving certificate entitling the bearer to receive a specified fixed rate of interest at a fixed maturity date that can be issued in any denomination. It is typically an interest bearing receipt for the deposit of funds in a bank for a specified period of time.

Cheque fraud A major problem faced by banks involving the unlawful use of cheques in order to illegally acquire or borrow funds that are non-existent in the account balance or account holderÆs legal ownership.
Cheque image Means of truncating cheques in which the customer receives a printed or electronic image rather than the cancelled cheque itself.
Collateral This is a property promised by a borrower to the lender as compensation if the borrower defaults. It is provided by the borrower as a way for the lender to secure the loan.
Commercial and industrial loans A type of loan or credit granted to a business or corporation to enable them to purchase inventory, to acquire capital item (e.g. plant and equipment) and to meet operating expenses.
Commercial banks Financial intermediaries that provide banking services to the general public, firms and individuals. They accept customersÆ deposits for safe- keeping and also provide them with loans.
Commercial paper This is an unsecured short-term debt instrument issued by a reputable firm to investors in the money market to raise working capital.
Community banks A depository institution that is typically locally owned and operated predominantly in cities and towns within the local market area.
Compensating deposit balances This is a minimum balance that must be maintained in a bank account, used to offset the cost a bank incurs to set a business loan.
Compliance risk This is the risk of legal or regulatory sanctions, financial loss or damage to reputation as a result of non-compliance with regulatory requirements. Conditional pricing A pricing strategy that establishes a minimum-sized account balance with a lower charges or even zero fee, if the customerÆs deposit balance

climbs above that required minimum but charging a higher fee if the average balance falls below the required minimum amount.
Consumer (Instalment) credit Loans given by banks or other lending institutions to individuals and small businesses including credit card loans, residential mort- gages, car loans and home equity lines of credit.
Contingent-commitment banking The conditional promise of an extension of credit, a form of off-balance sheet banking.
Contractual savings institutions These are non-deposit-taking financial institu- tions that obtain funds at periodic intervals on a contractual basis and promise their clients future compensation.
Consigner A person or company obligated to support the repayment of a loan by a borrower who either has no credit record or has a poor track record of repaying loans.

Cost-plus deposit pricing This entails charging customers for the full cost, or a significant portion of the total cost involved in using deposit services.

Costs of instalment lending Functional cost analysis (FCA) of instalment lending that forces costs onto making a loan, collecting a payment, loss rates, cost of money and other details.
CREDCO Credit committee responsible for reviewing the bankÆs overall portfolio and approving and shaping strategies and tactics recommended by portfolio managers.
Credit analysis This involves evaluating the cash flow and management of a business and its competitive environment to ascertain its creditworthiness.
Credit availability risk The likelihood that lenders may not have the funds to loan or be willing to grant loans to every qualified borrower when credit is applied.
Credit card A payment card issued to users (cardholders) to enable them make purchases and where the cardholder promises to pay the card issuer for the amount used plus other charges.
Credit culture Portion of a bankÆs lending function including such characteristics as organisational design, reporting arrangements, communication practices and incentives.
Credit (Default) risk The risk that a borrower of a loan or issuer of security will fail to meet its obligations as stipulated in the agreed terms and conditions. It is also the probability of loss from a counterpartyÆs default.

Glossary	381

Credit risk methods Analytical tools or programmes that are designed to assess the level of default-risk exposure associated with a borrower of funds.
Credit scoring The use of a statistical analysis performed by lenders to access the creditworthiness of a loan applicant.
Credit spread The difference between a risky loan rate and a comparable risk-free rate or the spread between one risky loan and another. Also called a default-risk premium.
Credit swap Protection against nonpayment through a fee (premium) paid to a third party (counterparty) who compensates the bank in the event of a default.
Credit-swap price Premium for a credit swap, calculated as the credit spread less a credit charge for the counterpartyÆs risk.
Credit unions A member-owned financial cooperative or nonprofit depository institutions that accept deposits and provide loans or other financial services at competitive rates, only to its members.
Credit users Credit card customers who pay their balances in instalments.
Crime risk The danger associated with fraud, embezzlement, robbery or other crimes that could result in loss for a financial institution.
Currency-to-deposit ratio Amount of currency that people hold as a proportion of demand deposits held at commercial banks.

Current liabilities These are financial obligations that are payable within one year. Customer privacy The use of laws and regulations to protect the personal infor- mation that customers supply to their financial service providers so that customers

Customer relationship doctrine A bankÆs management strategy whose first prior- ity is making loans to all those customers who meet the lenderÆs quality standards and from whom positive earnings are expected.

Independent banks Banks or community banks that are not affiliated with a bank holding company.

384	Glossary

Insured deposit transfers A deposit pay-off technique in which the FDIC transfers only insured deposits and secured liabilities of the failed bank to a healthy bank.
Interest income Interest generated on loans, securities and other funds sold.
Interest rate The price paid for borrowed funds for a given period of time. It is also known as price of credit.
Interest rate cap This is the interest rate ceiling imposed on a loan designed to protect the borrower from any increase in the interest cost of the loan.
Interest rate collar An investment strategy that uses the derivatives to hedge an investorÆs exposure to changes in interest rate. It is usually the combination of an interest rate cap and an interest rate floor.
Interest rate ?oor Minimum interest rate below which the interest cost of a loan normally cannot fall, thus protecting the lender from additional lost revenue if market interest rates move lower.
Interest rate risk The risk associated with fluctuations in the interest rates that results in decreasing the value of net worth.
Interest sensitive An asset or liability item held by a bank that is vulnerable to changes in market interest rate and that can be repriced.
Interest-sensitive gap A measure of interest rate risk exposure that shows the difference between the rates of assets and liabilities.
Interest-sensitive gap management Management techniques that usually require a computer analysis of the maturities and repricing opportunities in relation to interest bearing assets, deposits and money market borrowings in order to deter- mine when and by how much a financial institution is exposed to interest rate risk.

Investment bank Financial intermediary such as corporations, pension funds, governments, hedge funds and other financial institutions that issues (under- writes) stocks and bonds for corporate customers and advises customers.
Investment banking services A bankÆs offer to underwrite new debt and equity securities for corporate or institutional customers in order to aid that customer in raising funds.
Investment banks These are financial institutions that assist companies and gov- ernment to raise capital in the financial market.
Investment products Products such as sales of mutual funds, annuities and other non-deposit instruments offered to investors based on an underlying security or group of securities that is purchased with the expectation of earning a favourable return.
Issuer A legal entity, mostly a financial firm or other institution or individual selling financial claims that develops, registers, sells securities, raises new funds and provides letters of credit to finance its operations.

Glossary	385

L

Lending function The origination (underwriting), funding, servicing and monitor- ing loans.
Letters of credit A legal document, which backs a customerÆs obligation to a third party. This implies that the bank would bear the cost if the customer were not able to meet the obligation. These letters of credit are often used in international trade.
Liability management The practice by banks that involves maintaining a balance between the maturities of their asset and their liabilities, typically the use of borrowed funds to meet liquidity needs, and also attracting the volume of liquidity it needs by raising or lowering the rate of interest it is willing to pay on borrowed funds.
Liquidity The degree to which securities or assets can be sold or purchased in the market without affecting the asset prices, usually assets that can easily be bought or that can be converted to cash quickly.
Liquidity risk The risk that an individual or institution may be unable to meet short-term obligations without a significant loss in value.
Loan agreement A contract, which sets out the terms of a loan. A loan agreement details all the terms of the loan contract and is usually in written form.
Loan commitment This is an arrangement where, for a defined period, banks decide to commit to extending loan facilities or a specified sum to borrowers, for a specific purpose and at a specified fee.
Loan commitment agreements Promises made by commercial banks or lending institutions to provide loan to a customer in the future, provided certain condi- tions are met.
Loan covenants Restrictions imposed by a lender that prevent the borrower from undertaking certain activities until the debt has been paid.
Loan loss reserves (LLR) A portion of the capital of a bank that is set aside as an allowance for uncollected loans and loan payments in order to cover potential losses from defaulted loans.
Loan option A device to lock in the amount and cost of borrowing for a designated time period by allowing a customer to borrow at a guaranteed interest rate, regardless of any subsequent changes in market interest rates, until the option expires.
Loan participation Agreement under which a lender shares a large loan with other lenders in order to provide the borrower with the needed funds, thereby reducing the risk any one lender is exposed to.
Loan policy Written statement providing a framework for organising and control- ling bank lending, usually having five main components: (1) general policies,
(2) specific loan categories, (3) miscellaneous loan policies, (4) quality control and (5) committees.
Loan review The process of scrutinising a bankÆs loan portfolio to determine its quality.

386	Glossary

Loan sales A form of investment banking in which the lender trades on his or her superior ability to evaluate the creditworthiness of borrowers and sells some of the loans the lender has made to other investors who value the lenderÆs expertise in assessing credit quality.
Loan strip A share of a long-term loan, usually for a period less than the loanÆs remaining time to maturity, in which the holder receives the agreed upon amount at maturity.
Loan syndication An agreement by a group (syndicate) of banks to provide various portions of a loan, usually a loan that is too large for one bank to make.
Loan workouts Activity within a lending institution that concentrates on delin- quent loans and attempts to design strategies for recovering as much as possible from borrowers with difficulties.
Loanable funds theory A theory that suggests that the market interest rate deter- mines the supply and demand for loanable funds.


M

Market-penetration deposit pricing Pricing strategy where the price on deposit (or a product) is offered at high interest rates (often well above current market levels) or offering a low rate of interest or at zero fees to customers in order to bring in as many new deposit customers as possible.
Merchant banks Banks that often deal mostly in international finance, business loans for firms or credit investments, underwriting and consulting services in an attempt to satisfy all the financial service needs of their clients.
Mobile services delivery A predominant use of portable electronic networks and devices (such as mobile technology) to provide customer services at virtually any location.
Money market deposit accounts (MMDAs) Accounts that represent a cross between a checking and a savings account. Associated with short maturity deposits that have a period of only a few days, weeks, months and on which the offering financial institution can pay any competitive interest rate over a designated short interval of time.
Mortgage banking companies Financial service companies that originate mort- gages or acquire mortgage loans for eventual resale to lenders (e.g. Insurance companies and pension funds) for a longer term.
Microcredit This is the provision of small loans to impoverished individuals, households and other economic entities with little or no collateral to help them become self-employed.
Microfinance This is the provision of financial services (savings account, insurance funds, credit and advisory) to the poor, low-income clients and micro-enterprises so as to help them increase their income, thereby having access to formal financial services.

Glossary	387

Microfinance institutions (MFIs) Financial institutions that provide financial ser- vices to the poor, low-income clients, small businesses and those who have no access to conventional banking services.
Micro-saving A branch of microfinance that deals with financial services that allow the poor and lower income families or individuals to safeguard (store) funds and other valuable items for future use and even earn interest.
Monetary policy The process by which the monetary authority (central bank) of a country controls the supply of money and interest rates to achieve macroeco- nomic objectives such as price stability, exchange rate stability, growth and employment.
Money Anything used as a medium of exchange, a store of value, a unit of account and a standard of deferred payment.
Money market A financial market where short-term securities such as treasury bills, commercial papers, negotiable certificates of deposit, bankersÆ acceptance and repurchase agreements are traded.
Monitoring Continuously auditing of asset values and seizing assets before short- falls occur, or requiring that the posted value of collateral assets equal or exceed promised payments.
Moral hazard Behaviour that occurs when a party to a transaction does not enter into the contract in good faith by providing false information about its assets or credit capacity or may undertake an unusual risk in a desperate attempt to earn a profit before the contract settles.


N

Negotiable certificates of deposit A type of interest bearing instrument issued by banks to mobilise deposits that may be sold to other investors in the secondary market any number of times before it reaches maturity. NCDs specify the interest rate and have a minimum face value and maturity date.
Negotiable order of withdrawal (NOW) accounts A deposit account that pays interest, on which a customer can write an unlimited number of negotiable drafts (cheques) but where the bank reserves the right to insist on prior notice before the customer withdraws the funds.
Net interest income A bankÆs interest income minus its interest expenses.
Net interest margin The spread between interest income generated by banks and interest expense relative to the amount of their (interest earning) assets
Net liquidity position Represents the difference between the volume of liquid funds available and the demand for liquid funds.
Net loan losses Loan amount that the lender considers uncollectible from the borrower and is charged off. It is expressed as the difference between gross loan losses or charge-offs and loan recoveries.

388	Glossary

Net non-interest margin It is the difference between non-interest revenues and non-interest expenses for a bank expressed as a percentage of total assets or earning assets.
Net profit margin The ratio of net income after taxes relative to total operating expenses.
Net worth The difference between the revenue generated from a bankÆs assets and the expenses associated with liabilities payout.
Networking A process that fosters the sharing of facilities for the movement of funds and financial information and ideas between financial service providers and individuals or groups with a common interest.
Nominal interest rate This is the interest quoted rate on a loan, a savings account, bonds and others. It represents how much is earned or paid at the end of the period of the investment or loan.
Non-bank banks Financial institutions that are not considered full-scale banks but are considered as service firms that either offer checking account services or grant commercial loans, but not both.
Non-depository institution Financial intermediaries, usually insurance companies, pension funds, investment companies, securities companies and finance compa- nies, that do not issue deposit liabilities.
Non-interest expense Fixed operating costs of a financial institution other than interest expense and provision for credit losses, such as anticipated bad debt provisions.
Non-interest income This is income a bank derives from non-interest transactions such as fees on deposits, transaction fees, insufficient fund fees, annual fees, monthly account services charges, inactivity fees, cheque and deposit slip fees, etc.
Non-performing loans Defaulted loans for which payments are not current or past due. It is expressed as the sum of loans collected from the bank on which the debtor has not made his scheduled payments for at least ninety (90) days.
Note A written contract between a borrowing customer and a lender, describing the responsibilities of both parties.
Note issuance facility (NIF) A type of medium-term credit where a syndicate of banks agree to buy any notes (short- or medium-term) that a borrower is unable to sell to other investors.


O

Off-balance sheet activities Bank activities, such as trading in derivatives and issuing loan commitments, basically assets or liabilities that do not appear on the bankÆs balance sheet.
Open market operation (OMO) This is a monetary policy activity of a central bank that involves buying or selling securities on the open market in order to increase or reduce the amount of money in the banking system.

Glossary	389

Over-the-counter market A largely unregulated market, whereby geographically dispersed traders who are linked to one another via telecommunication systems and computers trade in securities.


P

Preauthorised payment This is an agreement between a bank and its customer in which the customer grants permission to the bank to automatically debit his or her account with a specific amount on specified time periods through the use of electronic payment system.
Predatory lending The (unethical) practice of lenders taking advantage of certain borrowers (e.g. low-income or uneducated) through deceptive and misleading tactics.
Prepayment risk Uncertainty about the life of a loan associated with loan and deposit contact terms allowing early unscheduled repayment or withdrawal.
Primary capital The sum of total equity capital, the allowance for possible loan losses, mandatory convertible debentures and minority interest in consolidated subsidiaries, minus intangible assets other than purchased loan servicing rights.
Profitability A performance indicator that represents the rate of return generated from using the resources of a business at its command in order to produce and sell services.
Promissory note A negotiable instrument that contains a written promise by the issuer to pay another party (payee) an agreed amount of money upon demand or to pay at a specified future date.


R

Real interest rate This is the nominal interest rate minus inflation. It measures the rate at which oneÆs purchasing power of an investment increases.
Relationship pricing A pricing framework where fees or pricing is determined based on the number of services and the extent to which those services are used by the customers.
Report of condition A quarterly financial statement of a depository institution which lists the assets, liabilities and equity capital (ownersÆ funds) held by or invested in the depository institution at any single point in time; reports of condition must be filed periodically with regulatory agencies.
Report of income A periodic income statement of depository institution which reports how much revenue has been received and what expenses have been incurred over a specific period of time.
Repurchase agreement (Repo) A short-term loan employed in the money market that enables a party (borrower) to sell securities to the lender with the intention of repurchasing the securities at a specified price on a predetermined future date.

390	Glossary

Reserve requirement A portion of total deposits required by law that universal banks are to keep in their vault with the central bank called required reserves.
Reserve requirement ratio This is the ratio of deposited reserves that banks are required to keep with the central bank.
Retail banks A consumer bank that typically offers banking services to households and small businesses.
Retail credit Financing method which provides smaller denomination loans to retail customers (individuals, families as well as smaller businesses) for goods and services.
Return on assets (ROA) This is a measure of profitability of a bank and it is defined as net profits after taxes, divided by total assets.
Return on equity (ROE) This is a measure of return to the owners of the bank and it is defined as net profits after taxes divided by bank capital.
Revolving credit line A financing arrangement that allows a business customer to borrow up to a specified limit from a bank or merchant, repay all or a certain amount periodically and borrow again once it is repaid, as necessary until the credit line matures.
Risk structure of interest rates The relationship that exists among interest rates of securities or bonds that have the same maturity.
ROA Return on total assets as measured by the ratio of net income after taxes to total assets.
ROE Return on equity capital invested in a corporation by its stockholders, mea- sured by after-tax net income.


S

Savings and loan associations These are depository financial institutions that specialise in accepting savings and deposits and focus on providing loans to individuals and small businesses.
Savings deposits Interest bearing funds held at bank or financial institution that provide a modest interest rate for a given period but do not have an expiration date. It usually gives limited withdrawal privileges to depositors.
Self-liquidating loans These are loans given to support ongoing business opera- tions or day-to-day activities of the firm.
Service differentiation Perceptions created in the minds of customers that a par- ticular financial firmÆs services are of better quality, are more conveniently available or differ in some other significant way from similar services offered by competitors.
Servicing rights Contractual agreement where the rights are retained by a lender selling a loan in which the lender continues to collect interest payments from the borrower and that monitors the borrowerÆs compliance with loan terms on behalf of the purchaser of the loan.

Glossary	391

Simple interest method This is a method for calculating the interest on a loan or investment and is computed by multiplying the principal, by the interest rate and by the time involved.
Sources and uses of funds method Approach developed for estimating liquidity requirements that examines the expected sources of liquidity (for a bank, princi- pally its deposits) and the expected uses of liquidity (principally its loans) and estimates the net difference between funds sources and uses over a given period of time in order to aid liquidity planning.
Sources and uses of funds statement Mechanism for providing financial report on a business customer showing changes in assets and liabilities over a given period of time.
Statement of cash ?ows A financial statement constructed by credit analysts or by borrowing customers that summarises the amount of cash and cash equivalents entering and leaving a firm.
Structure of funds method Method of estimating liquidity requirements that depends on a detailed analysis of deposit and loan customers and how the levels of their deposits and loans are likely to change over time.
Subprime loans Loans granted to borrowers whose credit rating is considered to be weak or to people who may have difficulty maintaining the repayment schedule, often due to a prior record of delinquent payments, bankruptcy or other adverse developments
Sweep accounts Bank account that allows financial institution to transfer funds (usually overnight, or amount that exceed or fall short of a required level) out of the customersÆ checking accounts into their savings deposits or into other types of deposits that do not carry any legal reserve.
Syndicated loan A loan or line of credit a group of lenders offers to a borrower in order to reduce the credit risk exposure to any single lending institution.


T

Term loans These are long-term loans which are used in acquiring fixed assets and in funding long- and medium-term projects.
Term structure of interest rates The relationship between the yield on bonds or securities with different maturities but with the same default risk, liquidity and income tax status.
Thrift bank Financial intermediary that focuses primarily on taking deposits and originating home mortgages.
Thrift deposits Accounts whose principal purpose is to provide an interest bearing outlet for customer savings, that is, a place for the customer to store liquid purchasing power at interest until needed.
Tier 1 capital This is a bankÆs core capital that represents the true capital used as cushion for the banking operations, and it includes ordinary shares and surplus, undivided profit, qualifying non-cumulative perpetual preference shares, etc.

392	Glossary

Tier 2 capital This is a bankÆs supplemental capital and it includes undisclosed reserves, revaluation reserves, loan loss reserves, convertible bonds and cumula- tive preference shares.
Time deposits These are deposits for which the customer cannot make withdrawals until at a specified maturity date.
Transaction deposit A deposit service in which cheques or drafts against the deposit may be used to pay for purchases of goods and services.


U

Unit banks A bank without branches, but that offers a wide range of services to its local community from one office.
Universal bank A bank that is engaged in providing a wide range of financial intermediation, including banking, insurance, real estate, brokerage services, commercial and investment services.
Unsecured loan A loan for which the borrower does not provide collateral, but where the lender relies on the borrowerÆs creditworthiness.


V

Vault cash Cash kept on hand inside a bankÆs vault and automated teller machines (ATMs) to meet day-to-day needs of customers and businesses.


W

Warranties A form of guarantee or a section within a loan agreement in which a borrower affirms to the lender that the information provided by the borrower is valid, true and correct.
Wholesale banking A type of banking services provided by large banks to other
financial institutions.
Wholesale banks Large banks that deal with larger clients by offering financial services to large corporations and other banks.
Wholesale lenders Banks or other lending institutions that devote the bulk of their credit portfolios. They usually do not deal directly with customers, but offer larger denomination loans through third parties like mortgage brokers, credit unions and other relatively large institutions.
Working capital This is the difference between a firmÆs current assets (such as cash, accounts receivable, inventory) and its current liabilities (accounts payable).
Working capital loans Loans that have the purpose of financing the everyday operations of a business such as purchasing raw materials.

Glossary	393

Y

Yield This is the returns on an investment in the form of interest income or dividend income. It is typically expressed as a percentage on the cost of investment.
Yield curve A plot of the term structure, indicating the relationship between interest rates and the term to maturity.
